## During inspiration, a sustained negative shift in $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{IP}}$ causes PA to become transiently more negative

During a quiet respiratory cycle-an inspiration of 500 mL , followed by an expiration-the body first generates negative and then positive values of PA. The four large gray panels of Figure 27-15 show the idealized time courses of five key parameters. (T) N27-9 The uppermost panel is a record of $\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{L}}$. The next panel is a pair of plots, $-\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{TP}}$ and $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{IP}}$. The third shows the record of PA. The bottom panel shows a simultaneous record of $\dot{\mathrm{V}}$.

On the right side of Figure 27-15 are the static $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{TP}}-\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{L}}$ curve and the dynamic $\mathrm{PA}-\dot{\mathrm{V}}$ relationship (both copied from Fig. 27-14). On the left side is a series of four cartoons that represent snapshots of the key pressures (i.e., $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{IP}}, \mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{TP}}$, and PA) at four points during the respiratory cycle:
a. Before inspiration begins. The lungs are under static conditions at a volume of FRC.
b. Halfway through inspiration. The lungs are under dynamic conditions at a volume of FRC $+250 \mathrm{~mL}$.
c. At the completion of inspiration. The lungs are once again under static conditions but at a volume of FRC +500 mL .
d. Halfway through expiration. The lungs are under dynamic conditions at a volume of FRC +250 mL .
a. At the end of expiration/ready for the next inspiration. The lungs are once again under static conditions at a volume of FRC.

The $\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{L}}$ record in the top gray panel of Figure 27-15 shows that $\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{L}}$ rises more or less exponentially during inspiration and similarly falls during expiration.

Knowing the time course of $\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{L}}$, we obtained the $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{TP}}$ values in the second gray panel of Figure 27-15 by reading them off the static $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{TP}}-\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{L}}$ diagram to the right and plotted them as $-\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{TP}}$ (for consistency with Equation 27-12). As $\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{L}}$ increases during inspiration, $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{TP}}$ increases (i.e., $-\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{TP}}$ becomes more negative). The opposite is true during expiration. Remember that $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{TP}}$ (along with static compliance) determines $\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{L}}$ at any time.

The $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{IP}}$ record in the second gray panel of Figure 27-15 shows that $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{IP}}$ is the same as $-\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{TP}}$ whenever the lungs are under static conditions (points $a, c$, and $a$ ). However, during inspiration, $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{IP}}$ rapidly becomes more negative than $-\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{TP}}$ but then merges with $-\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{TP}}$ by the end of inspiration. The difference between $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{IP}}$ and $-\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{TP}}$ is PA, which must be negative to produce airflow into the lungs. During expiration, $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{IP}}$ is more positive than $-\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{TP}}$.

The PA record in the third gray panel of Figure 27-15 shows that PA is zero under static conditions (points $a, c$, and $a$ ). During inspiration, PA rapidly becomes negative but then relaxes to zero by the end of inspiration. The opposite is true during expiration. The PA values in this plot represent the differences between the $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{IP}}$ and $-\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{TP}}$ plots in the preceding panel.

We computed $\dot{\mathrm{V}}$ (bottom gray panel) from the relationship in Equation 27-7: $\dot{\mathrm{V}}=(\mathrm{PA}-\mathrm{PB}) / \mathrm{R}_{\mathrm{AW}}$. Remember that PA (along with $\mathrm{R}_{\mathrm{AW}}$ ) determines $\dot{\mathrm{V}}$ at any time. Here, we assume that $\mathrm{R}_{\mathrm{AW}}$ is fixed during the respiratory cycle
at $1 \mathrm{~cm} \mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O} /(\mathrm{L} / \mathrm{s})$. Thus, the $\dot{\mathrm{V}}$ record has the same time course as PA.

The key message in Figure 27-15 is that during inspiration, the negative shift in $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{IP}}$ has two effects. The body invests some of the energy represented by $\Delta \mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{IP}}$ into transiently making PA more negative (dynamic component). The result is that air flows into the lungs and $\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{L}}$ increases; but this investment in PA is only transient. Throughout inspiration, the body invests an increasingly greater fraction of its energy in making $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{TP}}$ more positive (static component). The result is that the body maintains the new, higher $\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{L}}$. By the end of inspiration, the body invests all of the energy represented by $\Delta \mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{IP}}$ in maintaining $\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{L}}$ and none in further expansion. The situation is not unlike that faced by Julius Caesar as he, with finite resources, conquered Gaul. At first, he invested all of his resources in expanding his territory at the expense of the feisty Belgians; but as the conquered territory grew, he was forced to invest an increasingly greater fraction of his resources in maintaining the newly conquered territory. In the end, he necessarily invested all of his resources in maintaining his territory and was unable to expand further.

## Dynamic compliance falls as respiratory frequency rises

In the preceding section, we examined pressure, volume, and flow changes during an idealized respiratory cycle of 5 seconds, which corresponds to a respiratory frequency of 12 breaths/min. The top curve in Figure 27-16A shows a normal $\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{L}}$ time course during an inspiration. As for any exponential process, the time constant ( $\tau$ ) (T) N27-10 is the interval required for $\Delta \mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{L}}$ to be $\sim 63 \%$ complete. For healthy lungs, $\tau$ is $\sim 0.2$ second. Thus, for inspiration, the increase in $\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{L}}$ is $63 \%$ complete after 0.2 second, $86 \%$ complete after 0.4 second, $95 \%$ complete after 0.6 second, and so on. We will make the simplifying assumption that the time available for inspiration is half this time or 2.5 seconds, which represents $>12$ time constants! Thus, if the $\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{T}}$ after infinite time were 500 mL , the $\Delta \mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{L}}$ measured 2.5 seconds after initiation of inspiration would also be $\sim 500 \mathrm{~mL}$ (see Fig. 27-16A, green point on top curve). In Figure 27-16B, we replot this value as the green point at a frequency of 12 breaths/min on the top curve (i.e., normal lungs).

For a respiratory frequency of 24 breaths/min, 1.25 seconds is available for inspiration. At the end of this time, the $\Delta \mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{L}}$ is $\sim 499 \mathrm{~mL}$ (see Fig. 27-16B, blue point on the top curve).

If we further increase the respiratory frequency to 48 breaths/min, only 0.625 second is available for inspiration. At the end of this period, only slightly more than three time constants, the $\Delta \mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{L}}$ is $\sim 478 \mathrm{~mL}$ (see Fig. 27-16B, red point on top curve). Thus, over a wide range of frequencies, $\Delta \mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{L}}$ is largely unchanged. Only when respiratory frequency approaches extremely high values does $\Delta \mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{L}}$ begin to fall off.

The situation is very different for a subject with substantially increased airway resistance. If $\mathrm{R}_{\mathrm{AW}}$ increased 5 -fold, $\tau$ would also increase 5 -fold to 1 second. (T) N27-10 As a result, the trajectory of $\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{L}}$ also would slow by a factor of 5 (see Fig. 27-16A, bottom curve). If we could wait long enough during an inspiration, these unhealthy lungs would

# N27-9 Model of the Respiratory Cycle 

## Contributed by Emile Boulpaep and Walter Boron

The four large graphs with gray backgrounds in Figure 27-15 represent a hypothetical respiratory cycle. We generated the tracings using a rather simple computer model, making the following assumptions:

1. Throughout the respiratory cycle, the static compliance (C) was a constant $0.2 \mathrm{~L} / \mathrm{cm} \mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O}$.
2. Throughout the respiratory cycle, the resistance $(\mathrm{R})$ was a constant $1 \mathrm{~cm} \mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O} /(\mathrm{L} / \mathrm{s})$.
3. The initial lung volume $\left(V_{i}\right)$ was 3.2 L , corresponding to time zero in the graphs (i.e., point $a$ in the four panels).
4. During inspiration, the subject shifted $P_{i P}$ from $-5 \mathrm{~cm} \mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O}$ to $-7.5 \mathrm{~cm} \mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O}$ with an exponential time course (time constant $=0.01$ second). This value of $\tau$ for $P_{i P}$ results in a $\tau$ for $V_{i}$ of -0.2 seconds, consistent with the theoretical requirement that $\tau_{\mathrm{ic}}=\mathrm{R} \times \mathrm{C}=1 \times 0.2=0.2$ second. Thus, $P_{i P}$ is the prime mover, the parameter that our hypothetical subject controlled. A second consequence is that because (a) $P_{i P}$ changes by $2.5 \mathrm{~cm} \mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O}$ and (b) the compliance is $0.2 \mathrm{~L} / \mathrm{cm} \mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O}$, the total change in lung volume $\left(\Delta V_{i}\right)$ was 0.5 L .
5. Expiration was the reverse of inspiration, with $P_{i P}$ shifting from -7.5 to $-5.0 \mathrm{~cm} \mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O}$ with a time constant, once again, of 0.01 seconds. Again, the $\tau$ for $V_{i}$ was -0.2 seconds. In the actual modeling, we divided the inspiration and expiration into multiple 10 -ms intervals. Each interval had two phases, one in which we allowed the change in $P_{i P}$ to change the lung volume-but with no airflow-and a second in which we allowed air to flow as dictated by the alveolar pressure ( PA ) and airway resistance.

First phase. At time zero (point $a$ in the four panels), we imagined that we plugged the trachea with a cork and then allowed the subject to begin the inspiratory effort for a duration
of 10 ms . $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{iP}}$ became slightly more negative according to the aforementioned exponential time course. At the end of the 10-ms period, we computed the new value of $V_{i}$, knowing the current value of $P_{i P}$ and the compliance and also assuming that the air molecules inside the lung obeyed the ideal gas law ( $\mathrm{PA} \cdot \mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{i}}=$ nRT). Keep in mind that because we plugged the trachea with a cork, the number of air molecules in the lung was fixed during the 10 -ms period of inspiratory effort. Thus, we could compute the new value of PA. From this new value of PA and the current value of $P_{i P}$, we could compute the $P_{i P}$ after 10 ms . Thus, at the end of the first phase, we have new values for $P_{i P}$, $V_{i}, P A$, and $P_{i P}$.

Second phase. Once we knew the new value of PA, we removed our hypothetical cork for 10 ms and allowed air to flow into the lung, governed by the equation $V_{i}=\Delta P / R$. Note that $\Delta P$ is the difference between Pb and PA . At the end of 10 ms , we replaced the cork (the flow of air is far from complete!). Knowing how many air molecules entered during the 10 -ms interval, and assuming that $P_{i P}$ has not changed, we can now recompute all the relevant parameters and start the first phase of the second cycle.

Thus, although we assumed the time course of the green $P_{i P}$-versus-time curve in the second gray panel of Figure 27-15, all of the other curves follow directly from the calculations and the assumed parameters. The time constant ( $\tau$ ) for $V_{i}$ that falls out of our computer simulation is -0.2 second. As we will see in $\mathbf{O}$ N27-10 the theoretical time constant for a change in lung volume is the product $R \times C$. In the model we have been discussing, $R \times C$ evaluates to a time constant of 0.2 seconds, which agrees well with our simulation. Note that the $\tau$ we assumed for the change in $P_{i P}$ was 0.01 second, which is considerably faster than the $\tau$ for the change in $V_{i}$ and thus is not rate limiting.

## N27-10 Calculating the Time Constant for a Change in Lung Volume

## Contributed by Emile Boulpaep and Walter Boron

It should not be very surprising that the $\tau$ for the time course of $V_{i}$ is 0.2 second. Just as for electrical circuits, where the time constant of a resistor-capacitor network is $R \times C$, where $R$ is resistance and $C$ is capacitance, the time constant for inflating a lung is the product of the airway resistance and the static compliance:

$$
\tau=R \times C
$$

(NE 27-10)
For healthy human lungs, we might assume that $R$ is $\sim 1 \mathrm{~cm}$ $\mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O} /(\mathrm{L} / \mathrm{s})$-on page 617 we pointed out that normal values for $R$ might range from 0.6 to $2.3 \mathrm{~cm} \mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O} /(\mathrm{L} / \mathrm{s})$. We also might assume that $C$ is $-0.2 \mathrm{~L} / \mathrm{cm} \mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O}$ (see Box 26-1). Thus, our predicted time constant is

$$
\tau=R \times C=\left(1 \frac{\mathrm{~cm} \mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O} \times \mathrm{s}}{\mathrm{~L}}\right) \times\left(0.2 \frac{\mathrm{~L}}{\mathrm{~cm} \mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O}}\right)=0.2 \mathrm{~s}
$$

If $R$ increased 5 -fold from 1 to $5 \mathrm{~cm} \mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O} /(\mathrm{L} / \mathrm{s})$, the time constant would also quintuple:

$$
\tau=R \times C=\left(5 \frac{\mathrm{~cm} \mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O} \times \mathrm{s}}{\mathrm{~L}}\right) \times\left(0.2 \frac{\mathrm{~L}}{\mathrm{~cm} \mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O}}\right)=1.0 \mathrm{~s}
$$

Note that the time constant that we have computed is a theoretical minimum. The actual $\tau$ can only be as small as $R \times C$ if the individual making an inspiratory or expiratory effort decides to change $P_{i P}$ (or is capable of changing $P_{i P}$ ) very rapidly compared to $R \times C$. If the person decides to inhale or exhale slowly, then of course the actual time course with which $V_{i}$ changes can be far larger than $R \times C$. As an extreme example, if an oboe player decides to exhale over a period of many tens of seconds, the time constant no longer depends on the mechanical properties of the lung, but on the activity of the cerebral cortex.

Figure 27-15 The respiratory cycle. $\mathrm{Pb}, \mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{IP}}, \mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{Tp}}$, and PA are all in centimeters of $\mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O}$. The colored points (labeled $a, b, c$, and $d$ ) in each of the central panels correspond to the illustrations on the left with the samecolored backgrounds. The two panels on the far right are taken from Figure 27-14.

![bo42465312c5](bo42465312c5.jpg)

C EFFECT OF RESPIRATORY FREQUENCY ON TIDAL VOLUME
![bo43465312c5](bo43465312c5.jpg)

Figure 27-16 Dynamic compliance. In $\mathbf{A}$ and $\mathbf{B}$, the colored points represent changes in volume at respiratory frequencies of 12,24 , and 48 breaths/min.
eventually achieve a $\Delta \mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{L}}$ of 500 mL . The problem is that we cannot wait that long. Therefore, if the frequency is 12 breaths/min and inspiration terminates after only 2.5 seconds (i.e., only 2.5 time constants), the $\Delta \mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{L}}$ would be only 459 mL (see Fig. 27-16B, green point on bottom curve). Thus, even at a relatively low frequency, the patient with increased $\mathrm{R}_{\mathrm{AW}}$ achieves a $\Delta \mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{L}}$ that is well below normal.

For a respiratory frequency of 24 breaths/min, when only 1.25 seconds is available for inspiration, the $\Delta \mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{L}}$ is only 357 mL (see Fig. 27-16B, blue point on bottom curve). At a respiratory frequency of 48 breaths/min, when only 0.625 second is available for inspiration, the $\Delta \mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{L}}$ is only 232 mL
(see Fig. 27-16B, red point on bottom curve). Thus, the $\Delta \mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{L}}$ for the subject with increased $\mathrm{R}_{\mathrm{AW}}$ falls rapidly as frequency increases.

We can represent the change in $\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{L}}$ during cyclic breathing by a parameter called dynamic compliance:

$$
\mathrm{C}_{\text {dynamic }}=\frac{\Delta \mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{L}}}{-\Delta \mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{IP}}}
$$

Note that $\mathrm{C}_{\text {dynamic }}$ is proportional to $\Delta \mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{L}}$ in Figure 27-16B. Under truly static conditions (i.e., frequency of 0 ), $-\Delta \mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{IP}}$ equals $\Delta \mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{TN}}$ and dynamic compliance is the same as static compliance ( C or $\mathrm{C}_{\text {static }}$ ) that we introduced in Equation 27-4. As frequency increases, $\mathrm{C}_{\text {dynamic }}$ falls below $\mathrm{C}_{\text {static }}$. The degree of divergence increases with resistance. For the normal lung in Figure 27-16B, $\Delta \mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{L}}$ and thus $\mathrm{C}_{\text {dynamic }}$ fall by only $\sim 5 \%$ as frequency rises from 0 to 48 breaths/min. However, for the lung with a 5 -fold increased airway resistance, $\mathrm{C}_{\text {dynamic }}$ falls by $>50 \%$ as frequency rises from 0 to 48 breaths/min.

The above pathological pattern is typical of asthma; $\mathrm{R}_{\mathrm{AW}}$ is elevated, but $\mathrm{C}_{\text {static }}$ is relatively normal. In emphysema, both $\mathrm{R}_{\mathrm{AW}}$ and $\mathrm{C}_{\text {static }}$ are elevated (see Fig. 27-5, upper curve). Thus, a plot of $\mathrm{C}_{\text {dynamic }}$ versus frequency would show that $\mathrm{C}_{\text {dynamic }}$ is initially greater than $\mathrm{C}_{\text {static }}$ at low respiratory frequencies, but it falls below $\mathrm{C}_{\text {static }}$ as frequency increases. What do these frequency-dependent decreases in $\mathrm{C}_{\text {dynamic }}$ mean for a patient? The greater the respiratory frequency, the less time is available for inspiration or expiration, and the smaller the $\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{T}}$ (see Fig. 27-16C). ( $\mathbf{N 2 7 - 1 1}$

This analysis greatly oversimplifies what happens in the lungs of real people. Although we have treated the lungs as if there were one value for $\mathrm{R}_{\mathrm{AW}}$ and one for $\mathrm{C}_{\text {static }}$, each conducting airway has its own airway resistance and each alveolar unit has its own static compliance, and these values vary with parameters such as $\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{L}}$, posture, and hormonal status. As a result, some alveolar units have greater time constants than others. Airway disease may make some of these time constants substantially higher. As respiratory frequency increases, alveoli with relatively high time constants will have less time to undergo volume changes. As a result, these "slow" airways-compared with the "faster" airways-will make progressively smaller contributions to the overall ventilation of the lungs. At sufficiently high frequencies, very slow airways may drop out of the picture entirely.

## Transmural pressure differences cause airways to dilate during inspiration and to compress during expiration

We have noted three factors that modulate airway caliber: (1) the ANS, (2) humoral substances, and (3) $\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{L}}$ (see Fig. 27-13). A fourth factor that modulates $\mathrm{R}_{\mathrm{AW}}$ is flow of air through the conducting airway itself. Airflow alters the pressure difference across the walls of an airway, and this change in transmural pressure $\left(\mathbf{P}_{\mathrm{TN}}\right)$ can cause the airway to dilate or to collapse. Figure 27-17A through C depicts the pressures along a single hypothetical airway, extending from the level of the alveolus to the lips, under three conditions: during inspiration (Fig. 27-17A), at rest (Fig. 27-17B), and during expiration (Fig. 27-17C). In all three cases, the lung is at the same volume, FRC; the only difference is whether air is

# N27-11 Implications of High-Frequency Breathing in a Patient with a High Time Constant 

## Contributed by Emile Boulpaep and Walter Boron

As discussed in the text, a patient with increased airway resistance will require longer than normal to inflate the lungs by the expected $\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{T}}$ during an inspiratory effort (i.e., $\tau$ is increased, as in Fig. 27-16.4) and will require longer than normal to deflate the lungs to the FRC during an expiratory effort. As a consequence, as such a patient breathes with ever-increasing respiratory frequencies, the effective $\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{T}}$ will fall (see Fig. 27-16C). The effect on the ventilation of alveoli is even more severe.

As we shall see in Chapter 31, even in healthy lungs, not all of the $\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{T}}$ reaches the alveoli-a fixed amount ( $\sim 150 \mathrm{~mL}$ per breath) is wasted ventilating the conducting airways (see pp. 675-676), which have no alveoli. Only the part of the $\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{T}}$ in excess of, say, 150 mL actually reaches the alveoli. Thus, as the $\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{T}}$ gradually falls at increasing respiratory frequencies, the first volume to fall is the air ventilating the alveoli. The fall in $\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{T}}$ in a patient with increased airway resistance (i.e., an increased $\tau \mathrm{l}$ can therefore cause alveolar ventilation to fall far more steeply than the total ventilation.

![bo44465312c5](bo44465312c5.jpg)

Figure 27-17 Dilation and collapse of airways with airflow. In all four panels, $\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{L}}$ is FRC. $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{TM}}$ is the transmural pressure across conducting airways. $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{AW}}$ and values in pale blue balloons represent pressures inside conducting airways (all in centimeters of $\mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O}$ ). Graphs at bottom show $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{AW}}$ profiles along airways (blue curve) from alveolus to mouth. $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{IP}}$ is constant throughout the thorax (green curve). The upward (i.e., positive) $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{TM}}$ arrows tend to expand the airways, whereas the downward (i.e., negative) $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{TM}}$ arrows tend to squeeze them.
flowing into the lung, not flowing at all, or flowing out of the lung. Because $\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{L}}$ is at $\mathrm{FRC}, \mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{TP}}$ (the $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{TM}}$ for the alveoli) is $5 \mathrm{~cm} \mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O}$ in all three cases.

Static Conditions First consider what happens under static conditions (see Fig. 27-17B). In the absence of airflow, the pressures inside all airways must be zero. Considering first the alveoli, $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{TP}}$ is $5 \mathrm{~cm} \mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O}$ and the PA is zero, and thus $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{IP}}$ is $-5 \mathrm{~cm} \mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O}$. We will ignore the effects of gravity on $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{IP}}$ and thus assume that $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{IP}}$ is uniform throughout the chest cavity. The $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{IP}}$ of $-5 \mathrm{~cm} \mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O}$ acts not only on alveoli but on all conducting airways within the thoracic cavity. For these, $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{TM}}$ at any point is the difference between the pressure inside the airway ( $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{AW}}$ ) and $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{IP}}$ (see Equation 27-1):

$$
\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{TM}}=\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{AW}}-\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{IP}}=0-\left(-5 \mathrm{~cm} \mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O}\right)=+5 \mathrm{~cm} \mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O}
$$

In other words, a transmural pressure of $+5 \mathrm{~cm} \mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O}$ acts on all thoracic airways (but not the trachea in the neck, for example), tending to expand them to the extent that their compliance permits.

Inspiration Now consider what happens during a vigorous inspiration (see Fig. 27-17A). We first exhale to a $\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{L}}$ below FRC and then vigorously inhale, so that the PA is $-15 \mathrm{~cm} \mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O}$ at the instant that $\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{L}}$ passes through FRC.

Because the lung is at $\mathrm{FRC}, \mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{TP}}$ is $+5 \mathrm{~cm} \mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O}$. The $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{IP}}$ needed to produce a PA of $-15 \mathrm{~cm} \mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O}$ is

$$
\begin{aligned}
\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{IP}} & =\left(-\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{TP}}\right)+\mathrm{PA} \\
& =-5 \mathrm{~cm} \mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O}+\left(-15 \mathrm{~cm} \mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O}\right) \\
& =-20 \mathrm{~cm} \mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O}
\end{aligned}
$$

This "inspiring" $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{IP}}$ of $-20 \mathrm{~cm} \mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O}$ is just enough to produce the desired airflow and also to maintain the alveoli at precisely the same volume that they had under static conditions. But how does this exceptionally negative $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{IP}}$ affect airways upstream from the alveoli? $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{AW}}$ gradually decays from $-15 \mathrm{~cm} \mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O}$ in the alveoli to zero at the lips. The farther we move from the alveoli, the less negative is $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{AW}}$, and thus the greater is $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{TM}}$.

As an illustration, consider a point about halfway up the airway's resistance profile, where $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{AW}}$ is $-8 \mathrm{~cm} \mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O}$ :

$$
\begin{aligned}
\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{TM}} & =\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{AW}}-\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{IP}} \\
& =-8 \mathrm{~cm} \mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O}-\left(-20 \mathrm{~cm} \mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O}\right) \\
& =+12 \mathrm{~cm} \mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O}
\end{aligned}
$$

Thus, the transmural pressure opposing the elastic recoil at this point has increased from $+5 \mathrm{~cm} \mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O}$ at rest (see Fig. 27-17B) to $+12 \mathrm{~cm} \mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O}$ during this vigorous inspiration (see

Fig. 27-17A). Because $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{TM}}$ has increased, the airway will dilate. The tendency to dilate increases as we move from the alveoli to larger airways. As shown in the graph in the lower part of Figure 27-17A, $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{AW}}$ (and thus $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{TM}}$, as indicated by the upward arrows) gradually increases. Note that the very positive $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{TM}}$ values that develop in the larger airways determine only the tendency to dilate. The extent to which an airway actually dilates also depends on its compliance. The amount of cartilage supporting the airways gradually increases from none for 11th-generation airways to a substantial amount for the mainstem bronchi. Because the increasing amount of cartilage in the larger airways decreases their compliance, they have an increasing ability to resist changes in caliber produced by a given change in $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{TM}}$.

Expiration As might be expected, conducting airways tend to collapse during expiration (see Fig. 27-17C). We first inhale to a $\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{L}}$ above FRC and then exhale vigorously, so that PA is $+15 \mathrm{~cm} \mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O}$ at the instant that $\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{L}}$ passes through FRC. Because the lung is at FRC, $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{TP}}$ is $+5 \mathrm{~cm} \mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O}$. The $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{IP}}$ needed to produce a PA of $+15 \mathrm{~cm} \mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O}$ is

$$
\begin{aligned}
\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{IP}} & =\left(-\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{TP}}\right)+\mathrm{PA} \\
& =-5 \mathrm{~cm} \mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O}+\left(+15 \mathrm{~cm} \mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O}\right) \\
& =+10 \mathrm{~cm} \mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O}
\end{aligned}
$$

This $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{IP}}$ of $+10 \mathrm{~cm} \mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O}$ is 5 less than the PA and thus maintains the alveoli at the same volume that prevailed under static conditions and during inspiration. What is the effect of this very positive $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{IP}}$ on the upstream airways? $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{AW}}$ must decrease gradually from $+15 \mathrm{~cm} \mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O}$ in the alveoli to zero at the lips. The farther we move from the alveolus, the lower the $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{AW}}$ and thus the lower the $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{TM}}$. At a point about halfway up the airway's resistance profile, where $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{AW}}$ is $+8 \mathrm{~cm} \mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O}$,

$$
\begin{aligned}
\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{TM}} & =\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{AW}}-\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{IP}} \\
& =+8 \mathrm{~cm} \mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O}-\left(+10 \mathrm{~cm} \mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O}\right) \\
& =-2 \mathrm{~cm} \mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O}
\end{aligned}
$$

Thus, at this point during a vigorous expiration, the transmural pressure opposing elastic recoil has fallen sharply from $+5 \mathrm{~cm} \mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O}$ at rest (which tends to mildly inflate the airway) to $-2 \mathrm{~cm} \mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O}$ during expiration (which actually tends to squeeze the airway). As we move from the alveoli to larger airways, $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{AW}}$ gradually decreases. That is, $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{TM}}$ gradually shifts from an ever-decreasing inflating force (positive values) to an ever-increasing squeezing force (negative values), as indicated by the change in the orientation of the arrows in the lower panel of Figure 27-17C. Fortunately, these larger airways-with the greatest collapsing tendencyhave the most cartilage and thus some resistance to the natural collapsing tendency that develops during expiration. In addition, mechanical tethering (see p. 620) helps all conducting airways surrounded by alveoli to resist collapse. Nevertheless, $\mathrm{R}_{\mathrm{AW}}$ is greater during expiration than it is during inspiration.

The problem of airway compression during expiration is exaggerated in patients with emphysema, a condition in which the alveolar walls break down. This process results in fewer and larger air spaces with fewer points of attachment and less mutual buttressing of air spaces. Although the
affected alveoli have an increased compliance and thus a larger diameter at the end of an inspiration, they are flimsy and exert less mechanical tethering on the conducting airways they surround. Thus, patients with emphysema have great difficulty exhaling because their conducting airways are less able to resist the tendency to collapse. However, these patients make their expirations easier in three ways. We could predict them all from our knowledge of dynamic respiratory mechanics:

1. They exhale slowly. A low $\dot{\mathrm{V}}$ during expiration translates to a less positive PA and thus a less positive $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{IP}}$, which minimizes the tendency to collapse.
2. They breathe at higher $\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{L}}$. A high $\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{L}}$ maximizes the mechanical tethering that opposes airway collapse during expiration and thus minimizes $\mathrm{R}_{\mathrm{AW}}$ (see Fig. 27-13A).
3. They exhale through pursed lips. This maneuverknown as puffing-creates an artificial high resistance at the lips. Because the greatest pressure drop occurs at the location of the greatest resistance, puffing causes a greater share of the $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{AW}}$ drop to occur across the lips than along collapsible, cartilage-free airways. Thus, puffing maintains relatively high $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{AW}}$ values farther along the tracheobronchial tree (see Fig. 27-17D) and reduces collapsing tendencies throughout. The greatest collapsing tendencies are reserved for the largest airways that have the most cartilage (see p. 597). Of course, the patient pays a price for puffing: $\dot{\mathrm{V}}$ and thus the ventilation of the alveoli are low.

## Because of airway collapse, expiratory flow rates become independent of effort at low lung volumes

Cartilage and mechanical tethering (see p. 620) oppose the tendency of conducting airways to collapse during expiration. Because tethering increases as $\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{L}}$ increases, we expect airways to better resist collapse when $\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{L}}$ is high. To see if this is true, we will examine how expiratory airflow varies with effort (i.e., PA) at different $\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{L}}$.

Imagine that we make a maximal inspiration and then hold our breath with glottis open (Fig. 27-18A). Thus, PA is zero. In addition, $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{TP}}$ is $+30 \mathrm{~cm} \mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O}$ to maintain TLC. From Equation 27-12, $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{IP}}=\left(-\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{TP}}\right)+\mathrm{PA}=-30 \mathrm{~cm} \mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O}$. Now, starting from TLC, we make a maximal expiratory effort. Figure 27-18B summarizes the pressures in the alveoli and thorax at the instant we begin exhaling but before $\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{L}}$ has had time to change. $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{TP}}$ is still $+30 \mathrm{~cm} \mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O}$, but PA is now +40 cm $\mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O}$ (to produce a maximal expiration) and $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{IP}}$ is therefore $+10 \mathrm{~cm} \mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O}$. As $\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{L}}$ decreases during the course of the expiration, we will monitor $\dot{\mathrm{V}}, \mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{L}}$, and PA .

The top curve in Figure 27-18C shows how $\dot{\mathrm{V}}$ changes as a function of $\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{L}}$ when, starting from TLC, we make a maximal expiratory effort. Notice that $\dot{V}$ rises to its maximal value at a $\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{L}}$ that is somewhat less than TLC and then gradually falls to zero as $\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{L}}$ approaches RV. The data in this top curve, obtained with maximal expiratory effort (i.e., at maximal PA), will help us determine how expiratory flow varies with effort. To get the rest of the necessary data, we repeat our experiment by again inhaling to TLC and again exhaling to RV. However, with each trial, we exhale with less effort (i.e., at smaller PA)-efforts labeled as high, medium, and low in Figure 27-18C.

![bo45465312c5](bo45465312c5.jpg)

C EXPIRATORY FLOW-VOLUME CURVE
![bo46465312c5](bo46465312c5.jpg)

B MAXIMAL FORCED EXPIRATION FROM TLC
![bo47465312c5](bo47465312c5.jpg)

D EXPIRATORY FLOWEFFORT CURVE
![bo48465312c5](bo48465312c5.jpg)

Figure 27-18 Dependence of airflow on effort. In $\mathbf{D}$, note that if airway resistance were fixed, increased effort (i.e., increased PA) would yield a proportionate increase in $\dot{V}_{L}$ as indicated by the red line. However, increased effort tends to narrow airways, raising resistance and tending to flatten the curves. As $\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{L}}$ decreases (e.g., 4 or 3 L ), airflow becomes independent of effort with increasingly low effort. $\mathrm{Ps}, \mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{IP}}, \mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{TP}}$, and PA are all in centimeters of $\mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O}$.

If we draw a vertical line upward from a $\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{L}}$ of 5 L in Figure 27-18C, we see that, at this very high $\dot{V}_{L}, \dot{V}$ gets larger and larger as the effort increases from low to medium to high to maximal. Because these efforts correspond to increasing PA values, we can plot these four $\dot{V}$ data points (all obtained at a $\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{L}}$ of 5 L ) versus PA in the top curve of Figure 27-18D. Because $\dot{V}$ increases continuously with the PA, flow is effort dependent at a $\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{L}}$ of 5 L . If the airways were made of steel, this plot of $\dot{V}$ versus PA would be a straight line. Because the actual plot bends downward at higher values of PA (i.e., greater efforts), $\mathrm{R}_{\mathrm{AN}}$ must have increased with effort (i.e., the airways collapsed somewhat).

Returning to Figure 27-18C, we see that an important characteristic of these data is that the $\dot{V}$-versus- $\dot{V}_{\mathrm{L}}$ curve for maximal expiratory effort defines an envelope that none of the other three curves could penetrate. Thus, at a $\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{L}}$ of 4 L , $\dot{V}$ is $\sim 7 \mathrm{~L} / \mathrm{s}$, regardless of whether the expiratory effort is high or maximal (i.e., the two points overlie one another on the graph). At a $\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{L}}$ of 3 L , the four curves have practically merged, so that $\dot{V}$ is $\sim 5 \mathrm{~L} / \mathrm{s}$ regardless of effort. Thus, at lung volumes that are $<3 \mathrm{~L}$, it does not matter how much effort we make; the expiratory flow can never exceed a certain value defined by the envelope.

Shifting back to Figure 27-18D, we see that for the lower two $\dot{V}$-versus-PA plots, $\dot{V}$ increases with PA-up to a point. Further increases in effort (i.e., PA) are to no avail because they produce a proportional increase in $\mathrm{R}_{\mathrm{AN}}$-expirationinduced airway collapse. Thus, the more positive values of $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{IP}}$ not only produce more positive values of PA but also increase $\mathrm{R}_{\mathrm{AN}}$, so that $\mathrm{PA} / \mathrm{R}_{\mathrm{AN}}$ and thus $\dot{V}$ remain constant.

At low lung volumes, flow becomes effort independent because the reduced mechanical tethering cannot oppose the tendency toward airway collapse that always exists during expiration. Moreover, at progressively lower $\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{L}}$, flow becomes effort independent earlier. In other words, particularly at low lung volumes, it simply does not pay to try any harder.

# ACID-BASE PHYSIOLOGY 

Walter F. Boron

Acid-base physiology is really the study of the proton, or hydrogen ion $\left(\mathrm{H}^{+}\right)$. Although they are present in exceedingly low concentrations in most intracellular and extracellular fluids, protons nevertheless have a major impact on biochemical reactions and on a variety of physiological processes that are critical for the homeostasis of the entire body and individual cells. Not surprisingly, the body has evolved sophisticated systems to maintain $\left[\mathrm{H}^{+}\right]$values within narrow and precise ranges in the blood plasma, intracellular fluid, and other compartments.

This chapter provides the introduction to acid-base physiology, including the chemistry of buffers, the $\mathrm{CO}_{2} / \mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$buffer system, the competition between the $\mathrm{CO}_{2} / \mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$buffer system and other buffers, and the regulation of intracellular pH . In other chapters, we discuss how blood pH - and, by extension, the pH of extracellular fluidis under the dual control of the respiratory system, which regulates plasma $\left[\mathrm{CO}_{2}\right]$ (see Chapter 31), and the kidneys, which regulate plasma $\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]$(see Chapter 39). In addition, we discuss the control of cerebrospinal fluid pH in Chapter 32.

## pH AND BUFFERS

## pH values vary enormously among different intracellular and extracellular compartments

According to Brønsted's definition, an acid $\bigcirc$ N28-1 is any chemical substance (e.g., $\mathrm{CH}_{3} \mathrm{COOH}, \mathrm{NH}_{4}^{+}$) that can donate an $\mathrm{H}^{+}$. A base is any chemical substance (e.g., $\mathrm{CH}_{3} \mathrm{COO}^{-}$, $\mathrm{NH}_{3}$ ) that can accept an $\mathrm{H}^{+}$. The term alkali can be used interchangeably with base.
$\left[\mathrm{H}^{+}\right]$varies over a large range in biological solutions, from $>100 \mathrm{mM}$ in gastric secretions to $<10 \mathrm{nM}$ in pancreatic secretions. In 1909, the chemist Sørensen introduced the $\mathbf{p H}$ scale in an effort to simplify the notation in experiments in which he was examining the influence of $\left[\mathrm{H}^{+}\right]$ on enzymatic reactions. He based the pH scale on powers of 10: N28-2

$$
\mathrm{pH} \equiv-\log _{10}\left[\mathrm{H}^{+}\right]
$$

Thus, when $\left[\mathrm{H}^{+}\right]$is $10^{-7} \mathrm{M}$, the pH is 7.0 . The higher the $\left[\mathrm{H}^{+}\right]$, the lower the pH (Table 28-1). It is worth remembering that a 10 -fold change in $\left[\mathrm{H}^{+}\right]$corresponds to a pH
shift of 1 , whereas a 2 -fold change in $\left[\mathrm{H}^{+}\right]$corresponds to a pH shift of $\sim 0.3$.

Even small changes in pH can have substantial physiological consequences because most biologically important molecules contain chemical groups that can either donate an $\mathrm{H}^{+}$ (e.g., $\mathrm{R}-\mathrm{COOH} \rightarrow \mathrm{R}-\mathrm{COO}^{-}+\mathrm{H}^{+}$) and thereby act as a weak acid, or accept an $\mathrm{H}^{+}$(e.g., $\mathrm{R}-\mathrm{NH}_{2}+\mathrm{H}^{+} \rightarrow \mathrm{R}-\mathrm{NH}_{3}^{+}$) and thus behave as a weak base. To the extent that these groups donate or accept protons, a pH shift causes a change in net electrical charge (or valence) that can, in turn, alter biological activity either directly (e.g., by altering the affinity for a charged ligand) or indirectly (e.g., by altering molecular conformation).
pH -sensitive molecules include a variety of enzymes, receptors and their ligands, ion channels, transporters, and structural proteins. For most proteins, pH sensitivity is modest. The activity of the $\mathrm{Na}-\mathrm{K}$ pump (see pp. 115-117), for example, falls by about half when the pH shifts by $\sim 1 \mathrm{pH}$ unit from the optimum pH , which is near the resting pH of the typical cell. However, the activity of phosphofructokinase, a key glycolytic enzyme (see p. 1176), falls by $\sim 90 \%$ when pH falls by only 0.1 . The overall impact of pH changes on cellular processes can be impressive. For example, cell proliferation in response to mitogenic activation is maximal at the normal, resting intracellular pH but may fall as much as $85 \%$ when intracellular pH falls by only 0.4 .

Table 28-2 lists the pH values in several body fluids. Because the pH of neutral water at $37^{\circ} \mathrm{C}$ is 6.81 , most major body compartments are alkaline.

## Buffers minimize the size of the pH changes produced by adding acid or alkali to a solution

A buffer is any substance that reversibly consumes or releases $\mathrm{H}^{+}$. In this way, buffers help to stabilize pH . Buffers do not prevent pH changes, they only help to minimize them.

Consider a hypothetical buffer B for which the protonated form $\mathrm{HB}^{(n+1)}$, with a valence of $n+1$, is in equilibrium with its deprotonated form $\mathrm{B}^{(n)}$, which has the valence of $n$ :

$$
\mathrm{HB}^{(n+1)} \rightleftarrows \mathrm{B}^{(n)}+\mathrm{H}^{+}
$$

Here, $\mathrm{HB}^{(n+1)}$ is a weak acid because it does not fully dissociate; $\mathrm{B}^{(n)}$ is its conjugate weak base. Conversely, $\mathrm{B}^{(n)}$ is a weak base and $\mathrm{HB}^{(n+1)}$ is its conjugate weak acid. The total buffer

## N28-1 Hydrogen lons in Aqueous Solutions

## Contributed by Emile Boulpaep and Walter Boron

Hydrogen ions do not truly exist as "free" protons in aqueous solutions. Instead, a shell of water molecules surrounds a proton, forming an extended complex sometimes denoted as $\mathrm{H}_{3} \mathrm{O}^{+}$(hydronium ion) or $\mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O}_{2}{ }^{+}$. Nevertheless, for practical purposes, we will treat the proton as if it were free. Also-as we do elsewhere in this book-we shall refer to concentrations of $\mathrm{H}^{+}$, bicarbonate $\left(\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right)$, and other ions. Bear in mind, however, that it is more precise to work with ion activities (i.e., the effective concentrations of ions in realistic, nonideal solutions).

## N28-2 Origin of the $p$ of pH

## Contributed by Walter Boron

Although the conventional wisdom is that the $p$ of pH stands for the power of 10, Norby's analysis of Sørensen's original papers reveals a far more accidental explanation.

Sørensen used $p$ and $q$ to represent two solutions in an electrometric experiment. He arbitrarily assigned the standard $q$ solution an $\left[\mathrm{H}^{+}\right]$of $1 N$ (normal). That is, his standard solution had an $\left[\mathrm{H}^{+}\right]$of $1 N$, which is $\mathrm{C}_{0}=10^{-9}$. His unknown, therefore, had an $\left[\mathrm{H}^{+}\right]$of $\mathrm{C}_{0}=10^{-9}$. Using this approach, Sørensen proposed the nomenclature $p_{H}^{+}$... and nowadays, we use simply pH .

## REFERENCES

Norby JG: The origin and the meaning of the little p in pH . Trends Biochem Sci 25:36-37, 2000.
Sørensen SPL: Enzymstudien. II. Mitteilung. Über die Messung und die Bedeutung der Wasserstoffionenkoncentration bei enzymatischen Prozessen. Biochem Z 21:131-304, 22:352-356, 1909.
Sørensen SPL: Études enzymatiques. II. Sur la mesure et l'importance de la concentration des ions hydrogène dans les réactions enzymatiques. C R Lab Carlsberg 8:1-168, 1909.

We thank Christian Aalkjaer of the University of Aarhus in Denmark for bringing Norby's paper to our attention.

concentration, $[\mathrm{TB}]$, is the sum of the concentrations of the protonated and unprotonated forms:

$$
[\mathrm{TB}]=\left[\mathrm{HB}^{(\mathrm{n}+1)}\right]+\left[\mathrm{B}^{(\mathrm{n})}\right]
$$

The valence of the acidic (i.e., protonated) form can be positive, zero, or negative:

$$
\begin{aligned}
& \text { Weak acid } \\
& \frac{\text { (HB }{ }^{(n+1)}}{\mathrm{NH}_{4}^{+}} \rightleftarrows \mathrm{NH}_{3} \quad+\mathrm{H}^{+} \\
& \mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{CO}_{3} \rightleftarrows \mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-} \quad+\mathrm{H}^{+} \\
& \mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{PO}_{4}^{-} \rightleftarrows \mathrm{HPO}_{4}^{2-} \quad+\mathrm{H}^{+}
\end{aligned}
$$

In these examples, $\mathrm{NH}_{4}^{+}$(ammonium), $\mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{CO}_{3}$ (carbonic acid), and $\mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{PO}_{4}^{-}$("monobasic" inorganic phosphate) are all weak acids, whereas $\mathrm{NH}_{3}$ (ammonia), $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$(bicarbonate), and $\mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{PO}_{4}^{2-}$ ("dibasic" inorganic phosphate) are the respective conjugate weak bases. Each buffer reaction is governed by a dissociation constant, $K$

$$
K=\frac{\left[\mathrm{B}^{(\mathrm{n})}\right]\left[\mathrm{H}^{+}\right]}{\left[\mathrm{HB}^{(\mathrm{n}+1)}\right]}
$$

If we add to a physiological solution a small amount of HCl - which is a strong acid because it fully dissociates-the buffers in the solution consume almost all added $\mathrm{H}^{+}$:

$$
\mathrm{H}^{+}+\mathrm{Cl}^{-}+\mathrm{B}^{(\mathrm{n})} \rightarrow \mathrm{HB}^{(\mathrm{n}+1)}+\mathrm{Cl}^{-}
$$

For each $\mathrm{H}^{+}$buffered, one $\mathrm{B}^{(\mathrm{n})}$ is consumed. The tiny amount of $\mathrm{H}^{+}$that is not buffered remains free in solution and is responsible for a decrease in pH .

If we instead titrate this same solution with a strong base such as $\mathrm{NaOH}, \mathrm{H}^{+}$derived from $\mathrm{HB}^{(\mathrm{n}+1)}$ neutralizes almost all the added $\mathrm{OH}^{-}$:

$$
\mathrm{Na}^{+}+\mathrm{OH}^{-}+\mathrm{HB}^{(\mathrm{n}+1)} \rightarrow \mathrm{Na}^{+}+\mathrm{B}^{(\mathrm{n})}+\mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O}
$$

For each $\mathrm{OH}^{-}$buffered, one $\mathrm{B}^{(\mathrm{n})}$ is formed. The tiny amount of added $\mathrm{OH}^{-}$that is not neutralized by the buffer equilibrates with $\mathrm{H}^{+}$and $\mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O}$ and is responsible for an increase in pH . (5) N28-3

A useful measure of the strength of a buffer is its buffering power $(\beta)$, which is the number of moles of strong base (e.g., NaOH ) that one must add to a liter of solution to increase pH by 1 pH unit. This value is equivalent to the amount of strong acid (e.g., HCl ) that one must add to decrease the pH by 1 pH unit. Thus, buffering power is

$$
\beta \equiv \frac{\frac{\text { moles/liter }}{\Delta[\text { Strong base }]}}{\Delta \mathrm{pH}}=\frac{\frac{\text { moles/liter }}{\Delta[\text { Strong acid }]}}{\Delta \mathrm{pH}}
$$

In the absence of $\mathrm{CO}_{2} / \mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$, the buffering power of whole blood (which contains erythrocytes, leukocytes, and platelets) is $\sim 25 \mathrm{mM} / \mathrm{pH}$ unit. This value is known as the non- $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$buffering power ( $\beta_{\text {non-HCO }}$ ). In other words, we would have to add 25 mmol of NaOH to a liter of whole blood to increase the pH by 1 unit, assuming that $\beta$ is constant over this wide pH range. For blood plasma, which lacks the cellular elements of whole blood, $\mathrm{B}_{\text {non-HCO }}$ is only $\sim 5 \mathrm{mM} / \mathrm{pH}$ unit, which means that only about one fifth as much strong base would be needed to produce the same pH increase.

## According to the Henderson-Hasselbalch equation, pH depends on the ratio $\left[\mathrm{CO}_{2}\right] /\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]$

The most important physiological buffer pair is $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ and $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$. The impressive strength of this buffer pair is due to the volatility of $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$, which allows the lungs to maintain stable $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ concentrations in the blood plasma despite ongoing metabolic and buffer reactions that produce or consume $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$. Imagine that a beaker contains an aqueous solution of $145 \mathrm{mM} \mathrm{NaCl}(\mathrm{pH}=6.81)$, but no buffers. We now expose this solution to an atmosphere containing $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ (Fig. 28-1). The concentration of dissolved $\mathrm{CO}_{2}\left(\left[\mathrm{CO}_{2}\right]_{\text {dis }}\right)$ is governed by Henry's law (see Box 26-2):

$$
\left[\mathrm{CO}_{2}\right]_{\text {dis }}=s \cdot \mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}
$$

At the temperature $\left(37^{\circ} \mathrm{C}\right)$ and ionic strength of mammalian blood plasma, the solubility coefficient, $s$, is $\sim 0.03 \mathrm{mM} / \mathrm{mm} \mathrm{Hg}$. Because the alveolar air with which arterial blood equilibrates has a $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ of $\sim 40 \mathrm{~mm} \mathrm{Hg}$, or torr, $\left[\mathrm{CO}_{2}\right]_{\text {dis }}$ in arterial blood is

$$
\left[\mathrm{CO}_{2}\right]_{\mathrm{a}}=(0.03 \mathrm{mM} / \mathrm{mm} \mathrm{Hg}) \cdot 40 \mathrm{mmHg}=1.2 \mathrm{mM}
$$

# N28-3 Buffering of $\mathrm{H}^{+}$ 

## Contributed by Walter Boron

For each $\mathrm{H}^{+}$buffered in Equation 28-6, one $\mathrm{B}^{\mathrm{III}}$ is consumed. For each $\mathrm{OH}^{-}$buffered in Equation 28-7, one $\mathrm{B}^{\mathrm{III}}$ is formed. Because almost all of the added $\mathrm{H}^{+}$or $\mathrm{OH}^{-}$is buffered, the change in the concentration of the unprotonated form of the buffer (i.e., $\Delta\left(B^{\mathrm{III}}\right)$ ) is a good index of the amount of strong acid or base added per liter of solution.

![bo49465312c5](bo49465312c5.jpg)

Figure 28-1 Interaction of $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ with water.

So far, the entry of $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ from the atmosphere into the aqueous solution has had no effect on pH . The reason is that we have neither generated nor consumed $\mathrm{H}^{+}$. $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ itself is neither an acid nor a base. If we were considering dissolved $\mathrm{N}_{2}$ or $\mathrm{O}_{2}$, our analysis would end here because these gases do not further interact with simple aqueous solutions. The aqueous chemistry of $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$, however, is more complicated, because $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ reacts with the solvent (i.e., $\mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O}$ ) to form carbonic acid:

$$
\mathrm{CO}_{2}+\mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O} \xrightarrow{\text { slow }} \mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{CO}_{3}
$$

This $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ hydration reaction is very slow. (3) N28-4 In fact, it is far too slow to meet certain physiological needs. The enzyme carbonic anhydrase, (3) N18-3 present in erythrocytes and elsewhere, catalyzes a reaction that effectively bypasses this slow hydration reaction. Carbonic acid is a weak acid that rapidly dissociates into $\mathrm{H}^{+}$and $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$:

$$
\mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{CO}_{3} \xrightarrow{\text { fast }} \mathrm{H}^{+}+\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}
$$

This dissociation reaction is the first point at which pH falls. Note that the formation of $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$(the conjugate weak base of $\mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{CO}_{3}$ ) necessarily accompanies the formation of $\mathrm{H}^{+}$ in a stoichiometry of $1: 1$. The observation that pH decreases, even though the above reaction produces the weak base $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$, is sometimes confusing. A safe way to reason through such an apparent paradox is to focus always on the fate of the proton: if the reaction forms $\mathrm{H}^{+}, \mathrm{pH}$ falls. Thus, even though the dissociation of $\mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{CO}_{3}$ leads to generation of a weak base, pH falls because $\mathrm{H}^{+}$forms along with the weak base.

Unlike the hydration of $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$, the dissociation of $\mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{CO}_{3}$ is extremely fast. Thus, in the absence of carbonic anhydrase, the slow $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ hydration reaction limits the speed at which increased $\left[\mathrm{CO}_{2}\right]_{\text {dis }}$ leads to the production of $\mathrm{H}^{+}$. $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$can accept a proton to form its conjugate weak acid (i.e., $\mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{CO}_{3}$ ) or release a second proton to form its conjugate weak base
(i.e., $\mathrm{CO}_{3}^{2-}$ ). Because this latter reaction generally is of only minor physiological significance for buffering in mammals, we will not discuss it further.

We may treat the hydration and dissociation reactions that occur when we expose water to $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ as if only one reaction were involved:

$$
\begin{aligned}
& \mathrm{CO}_{2}+\mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O} \rightleftarrows \mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{CO}_{3} \rightleftarrows \mathrm{H}^{+}+\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-} \\
& \mathrm{CO}_{2}+\mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O} \rightleftarrows \mathrm{H}^{+}+\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}
\end{aligned}
$$

Moreover, we can define a dissociation constant for this pseudoequilibrium:

$$
K=\frac{\left[\mathrm{H}^{+}\right]\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]}{\left[\mathrm{CO}_{2}\right]}
$$

In logarithmic form, this equation becomes (3) N28-5

$$
\mathrm{pH}=\mathrm{p} K+\log \frac{\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]}{\left[\mathrm{CO}_{2}\right]}
$$

Finally, we may express $\left[\mathrm{CO}_{2}\right]$ in terms of $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$, recalling from Henry's law that $\left[\mathrm{CO}_{2}\right]=s \cdot \mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ :

$$
\mathrm{pH}=\mathrm{p} K+\log \frac{\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]}{s \cdot \mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}}
$$

This is the Henderson-Hasselbalch equation, a logarithmic restatement of the $\mathrm{CO}_{2} / \mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$equilibrium in Equation 28-14. Its central message is that pH depends not on $\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]$or $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ per se, but on their ratio. Human arterial blood has a $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ of $\sim 40 \mathrm{~mm} \mathrm{Hg}$ and an $\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]$of $\sim 24 \mathrm{mM}$. If we assume that the pK governing the $\mathrm{CO}_{2} / \mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$equilibrium is 6.1 at $37^{\circ} \mathrm{C}$, then
$\mathrm{pH}=6.1+\log \frac{24 \mathrm{mM}}{(0.03 \mathrm{mM} / \mathrm{mmHg}) \times 40 \mathrm{mmHg}}=7.40$ (28-17)
Thus, the Henderson-Hasselbalch equation correctly predicts the normal pH of arterial blood.

## $\mathrm{CO}_{2} / \mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$has a far higher buffering power in an open than in a closed system

The buffering power of a buffer pair such as $\mathrm{CO}_{2} / \mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$ depends on three factors:

1. Total concentration of the buffer pair, [TB]. Other things being equal, $\beta$ is proportional to [TB].
2. The pH of the solution. The precise dependence on pH will become clear below.
3. Whether the system is open or closed. That is, can one member of the buffer pair equilibrate between the "system" (the solution in which the buffer is dissolved) and the "environment" (everything else)?

If neither member of the buffer pair can enter or leave the system, then $\mathrm{HB}^{(\mathrm{n}+1)}$ can become $\mathrm{B}^{(\mathrm{k})}$, and vice versa, but [TB] is fixed. This is a closed system. An example of a closedsystem buffer is inorganic phosphate in a beaker of water, or a titratable group on a protein in blood plasma. In a closed system, the buffering power of a buffer pair is (3) N28-6

# N28-4 Out-of-Equilibrium $\mathrm{CO}_{2} / \mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$Solutions 

## Contributed by Walter Boron

As described on page 629, adding $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ to an aqueous solution initiates a series of two reactions that, in the end, produce $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$ and protons:

$$
\mathrm{CO}_{2}+\mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O} \xrightarrow{\text { Siver }} \mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{CO}_{2} \xrightarrow{\text { Foc }} \mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}+\mathrm{H}^{+}
$$

In the absence of carbonic anhydrase (CA), the first reactionthe hydration of $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ to form carbonic acid-is extremely slow. The second is extremely fast. At equilibrium, the overall rate of the two forward reactions must be the same as the overall rate of the reverse reactions:

$$
\mathrm{CO}_{2}+\mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O} \leftarrow \xrightarrow{\text { Siver }} \mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{CO}_{2} \leftarrow \xrightarrow{\text { Foc }} \mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}+\mathrm{H}^{+}
$$

The first of these reverse reactions is extremely rapid, whereas the second-the dehydration of $\mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{CO}_{2}$ to form $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ and water-is extremely slow in the absence of CA. As noted in the text, we can treat the system as if only one reaction were involved:

$$
\mathrm{CO}_{2}+\mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O} \rightleftarrows \mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}+\mathrm{H}^{+}
$$

Moreover, we can describe this pseudoreaction by a single equilibrium constant. As shown by Equation 28-15, we can describe the equilibrium condition in logarithmic form by the following equation:

$$
\mathrm{pH}=\mathrm{p} K+\log _{10}\left(\frac{\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]}{\left[\mathrm{CO}_{2}^{-}\right]}\right)
$$

In other words, considering only equilibrium conditions, it is impossible to change pH or $\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]$or $\left[\mathrm{CO}_{2}\right]$ one at a time. If we change one of the three parameters (e.g., pH ), we must change at least one of the other two (i.e., $\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]$or $\left[\mathrm{CO}_{2}\right]$ ). Unfortunately, there are many cases in which the addition of $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ and $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$ markedly enhances some process. For example, at identical values of intracellular pH , the activation of quiescent cells by mitogens is far more robust in the presence than in the absence of the physiological $\mathrm{CO}_{2} / \mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$buffer. Which buffer component is critical in this case, $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ or $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$? There are also many cases in which a stress such as respiratory acidosis or metabolic acidosis triggers a particular response. For example, respiratory acidosis stimulates $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$reabsorption by the kidney-a metabolic compensation to a respiratory acidosis, as discussed beginning on pages 641-642 in the text. Again, we can ask which altered parameter signals the kidney to increase its reabsorption of $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$ , the rise in $\left[\mathrm{CO}_{2}\right]$ or the fall in pH ?

In the 1990s, the laboratory of Walter Boron realized that it could exploit the slow equilibrium $\mathrm{CO}_{2}+\mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O} \rightleftharpoons \mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{CO}_{2}$ to create $\mathrm{CO}_{2} / \mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$solutions that are temporarily out of equilibrium. eFigure 28-1A illustrates how one can make an out-of-equilibrium (OOE) "pure" $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ solution that contains a physiological level of $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$, has a physiological pH , but contains virtually no $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$. The approach is to use a dual syringe pump to rapidly mix the contents of two syringes, each flowing at the same rate. One syringe contains a double dose of $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ (e.g., $10 \% \mathrm{CO}_{2}$ ) at a pH that is so low (e.g., pH 5.40 ) that, given a pK of $\sim 6.1$, very little $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$is present. The other syringe contains a well-buffered, relatively alkaline solution that contains no $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ or $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$. The pH of this second solution is chosen so that at the instant of mixing at the T connection, the solution has a pH of 7.40 . Of course, the $\left[\mathrm{CO}_{2}\right]$
after mixing is $5 \%$ (which corresponds to a $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ of $\sim 37 \mathrm{~mm} \mathrm{Hg}$ ), and the $\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]$is virtually zero. The solutions flow so rapidly that they reach the cell of interest before any significant re-equilibration of the $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ and $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$. Moreover, a suction device continuously removes the solution. As a result, the cells are continuously exposed to a freshly generated OOE solution.
eFigure 28-1B illustrates how one could make the opposite solution-a "pure" $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$solution that has a physiological $\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]$and pH but virtually no $\left[\mathrm{CO}_{2}\right]$. The OOE approach can be used to make solutions with virtually any combination of $\left[\mathrm{CO}_{2}\right]$, $\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]$, and pH -at least for moderate pH values. At extremely alkaline pH values, the reaction $\mathrm{CO}_{2}+\mathrm{OH}^{-} \rightarrow \mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$generates $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$so fast that it effectively short-circuits the OOE approach. Conversely, at extremely acid pH values, the rapid reaction $\mathrm{H}^{+}+$ $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-} \rightleftharpoons \mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{CO}_{2}$ creates relatively high levels of $\mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{CO}_{2}$ so that even the uncatalyzed reaction $\mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{CO}_{2} \rightarrow \mathrm{CO}_{2}+\mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O}$ is high enough to short-circuit the OOE approach. Nevertheless, at almost any pH of interest to physiologists, OOE technology allows one to change $\left[\mathrm{CO}_{2}\right],\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]$, and pH one at a time.

Work on renal proximal tubules with OOE solutions has shown that proximal tubules have the ability to sense rapid shifts in the $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ concentration of the basolateral (i.e., blood-side) solution that surrounds the outside of the tubule. This work suggests that the tubule has a sensor for $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ that is independent of any changes in pH or $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$. The tubule uses this $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$-sensing mechanism in its response to respiratory acidosis (see pp. 637-638). This metabolic compensation involves a rapid increase in the rate at which it transports $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$from the tubule lumen to the blood, and thus a partial correction of the acidosis.

Work on neurons cultured from the hippocampus suggests that certain neurons can detect rapid decreases in the extracellular $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$concentration $\left(\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-} \mathrm{L}\right)$, independent of any changes in $\mathrm{pH}_{\mathrm{c}}$ or $\left[\mathrm{CO}_{2}\right]_{\mathrm{c}}$. The cell may use this detection system to stabilize intracellular pH ( pH ) during extracellular metabolic acidosis, which would otherwise lower pH .
![bo50465312c5](bo50465312c5.jpg)

B
Figure 28-1 Generation of out-of-equilibrium solutions. (Adapted from Zhao J, Hogan EM, Beverseet MO, et al: Out-of-equilibrium $\mathrm{CO}_{2} / \mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$solutions and their use in characterizing a novel $\mathrm{K} / \mathrm{HCO}_{3}$ cotransporter. Nature 374:636-639, 1995.)

# N28-4 Out-of-Equilibrium $\mathrm{CO}_{2} / \mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$Solutions—cont'd

Ganz MB, Perfetto MC, Boron WF: Effects of mitogens and other agents on mesangial cell proliferation, pH and $\mathrm{Ca}^{2+}$. Am J Physiol 259:F269-F278, 1990.
