Zhao J, Zhou Y, Boron WF: Effect of isolated removal of either basolateral $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$or basolateral $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ on $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$reabsorption by rabbit S2 proximal tubule. Am J Physiol Renal Physiol 285:F359-F369, 2003.

## N28-5 Derivation of the Henderson-Hasselbalch Equation

## Contributed by Emile Boulpaep and Walter Boron

As shown in Equation 28-13,

$$
\begin{aligned}
& \mathrm{CO}_{2}+\mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O} \rightleftarrows \mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{CO}_{3} \rightleftarrows \mathrm{H}^{+}+\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-} \\
& \mathrm{CO}_{2}+\mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O} \rightleftarrows \mathrm{H}^{+}+\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}
\end{aligned}
$$

We can define a dissociation constant for this pseudoequilibrium:

$$
K^{\prime}=\frac{\left[\mathrm{H}^{+}\right]\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]}{\left[\mathrm{CO}_{2}\right]\left[\mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O}\right]}
$$

(NE 28-5)
Factoring out $\left[\mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O}\right]$, we can define an apparent equilibrium constant:

$$
\begin{aligned}
K^{\prime}\left[H_{2} O\right] & =K=\frac{\left[H^{+}\right]\left[H C O_{2}^{-}\right]}{\left[C O_{2}\right]} \\
K & =\frac{\left[H^{+}\right]\left[H C O_{2}^{-}\right]}{\left[C O_{2}\right]}
\end{aligned}
$$

(NE 28-6)

The equation immediately above is Equation 28-14 in the text. $K$ at $37^{\circ} \mathrm{C}$ is $\sim 10^{-6.1} \mathrm{M}$ or $10^{-3.1} \mathrm{mM}$. Taking the log (to the base 10) of each side of this equation, and remembering that $\log (a \times$ $b)=\log (a)+\log (b)$, we have

$$
\log K=\log \left[H^{+}\right]+\log \frac{\left[H C O_{2}^{-}\right]}{\left[C O_{2}\right]}
$$

(NE 28-7)
Remembering that $\log \left[\mathrm{H}^{+}\right]=-\mathrm{pH}$ and $\log K=-\mathrm{pK}$, we may insert these expressions into Equation NE 28-7 and rearrange to obtain

$$
\mathrm{pH}=\mathrm{p} K+\log \frac{\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]}{\left[\mathrm{CO}_{2}\right]}
$$

(NE 28-8)
This expression is the same as Equation 28-15 in the text. Finally, we may express $\left[\mathrm{CO}_{2}\right]$ in terms of $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$, recalling from Henry's law that $\left[\mathrm{CO}_{2}\right]=s \times \mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ :

$$
\mathrm{pH}=\mathrm{p} K+\log \frac{\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]}{s \times \mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}}
$$

This is the Henderson-Hasselbalch equation, a logarithmic restatement of the $\mathrm{CO}_{2} / \mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$equilibrium in Equation NE 28-5 above.

# N28-6 Derivation of Expressions for Buffering Power in Closed and Open Systems 

## Contributed by Emile Boulpaep and Walter Boron

How does buffering power, in either a closed or open system, depend on pH ?

## Closed System

We start with a restatement of Equation 28-8, in which we define buffering power as the amount of strong base that we need to add (per liter of solution) in order to increase the pH by 1 pH unit. In differential form, this definition becomes the following:

$$
\begin{aligned}
p H & =p K+\log \frac{\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]}{s \times P_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}}
\end{aligned}
$$

(NE 28-10)
Second, because the change in the concentration of the unprotonated form of the buffer, $\left[\mathrm{B}^{(\mathrm{o})}\right]$, is very nearly the same as the amount of strong base added (see Equation 28-7), Equation NE 28-10 becomes

$$
\beta=\frac{d[\text { Strong Base }]}{d \mathrm{pH}}=\frac{d\left[\mathrm{~B}^{(\mathrm{o})}\right]}{d \mathrm{pH}}
$$

(NE 28-11)
The third step is to combine Equation 28-3, reproduced below,

$$
K=\frac{\left[\mathrm{B}^{(\mathrm{o})}\right]\left[\mathrm{H}^{+}\right]}{\left[\mathrm{HB}^{(\mathrm{o}+1)}\right]}
$$

(NE 28-12)
and Equation 28-5, reproduced below,

$$
[\mathrm{TB}]=\left[\mathrm{HB}^{(\mathrm{o}+1)}\right]+\left[\mathrm{B}^{(\mathrm{o})}\right]
$$

(NE 28-13)
to obtain an expression that describes how $\left[\mathrm{B}^{(\mathrm{o})}\right]$ depends on the concentration of total buffer, [TB], and $\left[\mathrm{H}^{+}\right]$:

$$
\left[\mathrm{B}^{(\mathrm{o})}\right]=[\mathrm{TB}] \frac{K}{\left[\mathrm{H}^{+}\right]+K}
$$

(NE 28-14)
Finally, we obtain the closed-system buffering power by taking the derivative of $\left[\mathrm{B}^{(\mathrm{o})}\right]$ in Equation NE 28-14 with respect
to pH . If we hold [TB] constant while taking this derivative (i.e., if we assume that the buffer can neither enter nor leave the system), we obtain the following expression for $\beta_{\text {closed }}$ :

$$
\beta_{\text {closed }}=\left(\frac{d\left[\mathrm{~B}^{(\mathrm{o})}\right]}{d \mathrm{pH}}\right)_{\mathrm{TB}}=2.3[\mathrm{TB}] \frac{\left[\mathrm{H}^{+}\right] K}{\left[\left[\mathrm{H}^{+}\right]+K\right]^{2}}
$$

(NE 28-15)

## Open System

How does $\beta_{\text {open }}$ depend on $P_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ and pH ? The first two steps of this derivation are the same as for the open system, except that we recognize that, for the $\mathrm{CO}_{2} / \mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$buffer pair, $\left[\mathrm{B}^{(\mathrm{o})}\right]$ is $\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]$:

$$
\beta=\frac{d[\text { Strong Base Added }]}{d \mathrm{pH}}=\frac{d\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]}{d \mathrm{pH}}
$$

(NE 28-16)
For the open system, the third step is to rearrange the Henderson-Hasselbalch equation (see Equation 28-16, reproduced below),

$$
\mathrm{pH}=\mathrm{p} K+\log \frac{\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]}{s \cdot \mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}}
$$

(NE 28-17)
and rearrange it to solve for $\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]$:

$$
\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]=s \times \mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}} \times 10^{(\mathrm{pH}-\mathrm{p} K)}
$$

(NE 28-18)
If we take the derivative of $\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]$in the above equation with respect to pH , holding $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ constant, the result is $\beta_{\text {open }}$ :

$$
\beta_{\text {open }}=2.3 \times s \times \mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}} \times 10^{(\mathrm{pH}-\mathrm{p} K)}
$$

(NE 28-19)
Because Equation NE 28-18 tells us that everything after "2.3" in the above equation is, in fact, " $\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]$," we obtain the final expression for $\beta_{\text {open }}$ :

$$
\beta_{\text {open }}=2.3 \times\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]
$$

(NE 28-20)
This is Equation 28-20 in the text.

![bo51465312c5](bo51465312c5.jpg)

B MULTIPLE BUFFERS
![bo52465312c5](bo52465312c5.jpg)

Figure 28-2 Buffering power in a closed system. In $\mathbf{B}$, the solution contains nine buffers, each at a concentration of 12.6 mM and with pK values evenly spaced 0.5 pH unit apart.

$$
\beta_{\text {closed }}=2.3\left[\mathrm{TB}\right] \frac{\left[\mathrm{H}^{+}\right] \cdot K}{\left(\left[\mathrm{H}^{+}+K\right]\right)^{2}}
$$

Two aspects of Equation 28-18 are of interest. First, at a given $\left[\mathrm{H}^{+}\right], \beta_{\text {closed }}$ is proportional to [TB]. Second, at a given [TB], $\beta_{\text {closed }}$ has a bell-shaped dependence on pH (green curve in Fig. 28-2A). $\beta_{\text {closed }}$ is maximal when $\left[\mathrm{H}^{+}\right]=K$ (i.e., when $\mathrm{pH}=\mathrm{p} K$ ). Most non- $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$buffers in biological fluids behave as if they are in a closed system. Although many fluids are actually mixtures of several non- $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$ buffers, the total $\beta_{\text {non-HCO3 }}$ in such a mixture is the sum of their $\beta_{\text {closed }}$ values, each described by Equation 28-18. The red curve in Figure 28-2B shows how total $\beta_{\text {non-HCO3 }}$ varies with pH for a solution containing a mixture of nine buffers (including the one described by the green curve), each present at a [TB] of 12.6 mM , with pK values evenly spaced at intervals of 0.5 pH unit. In this example, total $\beta_{\text {non-HCO3 }}$ is remarkably stable over a broad pH range and has a peak value that is the same as that of whole blood: $25 \mathrm{mM} / \mathrm{pH}$ unit. Indeed, whole blood is a complex mixture of many non- $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$buffers. The most important of these are titratable groups on hemoglobin and, to a far lesser extent, other proteins. Even less important than the "other proteins" are small molecules such as inorganic phosphate. The [TB] values for these many buffers are not identical, and the $\mathrm{p} K$ values are not evenly spaced. Nevertheless, the buffering power of whole blood is nearly constant near the physiological pH .

The other physiologically important condition under which a buffer can function is in an open system. Here, one buffer species (e.g., $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ ) equilibrates between the system and the environment. A laboratory example is a solution containing $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ and $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$in which dissolved $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ equilibrates with gaseous $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ in the atmosphere (see Fig. 28-1). A physiological example is blood plasma, in which dissolved $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ equilibrates with gaseous $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ in the alveoli. In either case, $\left[\mathrm{CO}_{2}\right]_{\text {dis }}$ is fixed during buffering reactions. However, the total $\mathrm{CO}_{2}-\left[\mathrm{CO}_{2}\right]+\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]$-can vary widely. Because total $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ can rise to very high values, $\mathrm{CO}_{2} / \mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$in an open system can be an extremely powerful buffer. Consider, for example, a liter of a solution having a pH of 7.4 , a $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ of $40 \mathrm{~mm} \mathrm{Hg}\left(1.2 \mathrm{mM} \mathrm{CO}_{2}\right)$, and an $\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]$of 24 mM -
but no other buffers (Fig. 28-3, stage 1). What happens when we add 10 mmol of HCl ? Available $\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]$neutralizes almost all of the added $\mathrm{H}^{+}$, forming nearly $10 \mathrm{mmol} \mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{CO}_{3}$ and then nearly $10 \mathrm{mmol} \mathrm{CO}_{2}$ plus nearly $10 \mathrm{mmol} \mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O}$ (see Fig. 28-3, stage 2A). The $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ that forms does not accumulate, but evolves to the atmosphere so that $\left[\mathrm{CO}_{2}\right]_{\text {dis }}$ is constant. What is the final pH ? If $\left[\mathrm{CO}_{2}\right]_{\text {dis }}$ remains at 1.2 mM in our open system, and if $\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]$decreases by almost exactly 10 mM (i.e., the amount of added $\mathrm{H}^{+}$), from 24 to 14 mM , the Henderson-Hasselbalch equation predicts a fall in pH from 7.40 to 7.17 , corresponding to an increase in free $\left[\mathrm{H}^{+}\right]$of

$$
\begin{aligned}
\Delta\left[\mathrm{H}^{+}\right] & =\left(10^{-7.17}-10^{-7.40}\right) \mathrm{M} \\
& =0.000,068 \mathrm{mM}-0.000,040 \mathrm{mM} \\
& =0.000,028 \mathrm{mM}
\end{aligned}
$$

Even though we have added 10 millimoles of HCl to 1 L , $\left[\mathrm{H}^{+}\right]$increased by only 28 nanomolar (see Fig. 28-3, stage 3A). Therefore, the open-system buffer has neutralized $9.999,972 \mathrm{mmol}$ of the added $10 \mathrm{mmol} \mathrm{H}^{+}$. The buffering provided by $\mathrm{CO}_{2} / \mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$in an open system $\left(\beta_{\text {open }}\right)$ is so powerful because only depletion of $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$limits neutralization of $\mathrm{H}^{+}$. The buildup of $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ is not a limiting factor because the atmosphere is an infinite sink for newly produced $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$.

The opposite acid-base disturbance occurs when we add a strong base such as NaOH . Here, the buffering reactions are just the reverse of those in the previous example. If we add 10 mmol of NaOH to 1 L of solution, almost all added $\mathrm{OH}^{-}$combines with $\mathrm{H}^{+}$derived from $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ that enters from the atmosphere (see Fig. 28-3, stage 2B). One $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$ion forms for each $\mathrm{OH}^{-}$neutralized. Buffering power in this open system is far higher than that in a closed system because $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ availability does not limit neutralization of added $\mathrm{OH}^{-}$. Only the buildup of $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$limits neutralization of more $\mathrm{OH}^{-}$. In this example, even though we added 10 millimoles NaOH , free $\left[\mathrm{H}^{+}\right]$decreased by only 12 nanomolars (see Fig. 28-3, stage 3B) as pH rose from 7.40 to 7.55 .

Whether $\mathrm{CO}_{2} / \mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$neutralizes an acid or a base, the open-system buffering power is $\bigcirc$ N28-6

$$
\beta_{\text {open }}=2.3 \cdot\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]
$$

![bo53465312c5](bo53465312c5.jpg)

Figure 28-3 Buffering of strong acids and bases by $\mathrm{CO}_{2} / \mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$in an open system.

Notice that $\beta_{\text {open }}$ does not have a maximum. Because $\beta_{\text {open }}$ is proportional to $\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right], \beta_{\text {open }}$ rises exponentially with pH when $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ is fixed (Fig. 28-4, blue curve). In normal arterial blood (i.e., $\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]=24 \mathrm{mM}$ ), $\beta_{\text {open }}$ is $\sim 55 \mathrm{mM} / \mathrm{pH}$ unit. As we have already noted, the buffering power of all non- $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$buffers ( $\beta_{\text {open }-\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}}$) in whole blood is $\sim 25 \mathrm{mM} /$ pH unit. Thus, in whole blood, $\beta_{\text {open }}$ represents more than two thirds of the total buffering power. The relative contribution of $\beta_{\text {open }}$ is far more striking in interstitial fluid, which lacks the cellular elements of blood and also has a lower protein concentration.
$\mathrm{CO}_{2} / \mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$does not necessarily behave as an opensystem buffer. In the previous example, we could have added NaOH to a $\mathrm{CO}_{2} / \mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$solution in a capped syringe. In such a closed system, not only does accumulation of $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$ limit neutralization of $\mathrm{OH}^{-}$, but the availability of $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ is limiting as well. Indeed, for fluid having the composition of normal arterial blood, the closed-system $\mathrm{CO}_{2} / \mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$buffering power is only $2.6 \mathrm{mM} / \mathrm{pH}$ unit (see Fig. 28-4, black curve), $<5 \%$ of the $\beta_{\text {open }}$ value of $55 \mathrm{mM} / \mathrm{pH}$ unit. You might
![bo54465312c5](bo54465312c5.jpg)

Figure 28-4 Buffering power of the $\mathrm{CO}_{2} / \mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$system. At pH 7.4 on the blue curve, the solution has the same composition as does arterial blood plasma: a $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ of 40 mm Hg and an $\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]$of 24 mM . If the system is "open" (i.e., $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ equilibrates with atmosphere), $\beta_{\text {open }}$ is $\sim 55 \mathrm{mM} / \mathrm{pH}$ unit at pH 7.40 and rises exponentially with pH (see Equation 28-20). If the system is closed, $\beta_{\text {closed }}$ is only $\sim 2.6 \mathrm{mM} / \mathrm{pH}$ unit at pH 7.4 and is maximal at the pK of the buffer (see Equation 28-18).

think that the only reason the closed-system buffering power was so low in this example is that the pH of 7.4 was 1.3 pH units above the pK . However, even if pH were equal to the pK of 6.1 , the closed-system $\mathrm{CO}_{2} / \mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$buffering power in our example would be only $\sim 14 \mathrm{mM} / \mathrm{pH}$ unit, about one quarter of the open-system value at pH 7.4 . A physiological example in which the $\mathrm{CO}_{2} / \mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$system is "poorly open" is ischemia, wherein a lack of blood flow minimizes the equilibration of tissue $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ with blood $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$. Thus, ischemic tissues are especially susceptible to large pH shifts.

## ACID-BASE CHEMISTRY WHEN $\mathrm{CO}_{2} / \mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$ IS THE ONLY BUFFER

In this section, we consider buffering by $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ and $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$ when these are the only buffers present in the solution. We defer to the following section the more complex example in which both $\mathrm{CO}_{2} / \mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$and non- $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$buffers are present in the same solution.

## In the absence of other buffers, doubling $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ causes pH to fall by 0.3 but causes almost no change in $\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]$

Figure 28-5 represents 1 L of a solution with the same $\mathrm{CO}_{2} / \mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$composition as arterial blood plasma but no buffers other than $\mathrm{CO}_{2} / \mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$. What are the consequences of increasing $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ in the gas phase? The resulting increase in $\left[\mathrm{CO}_{2}\right]_{\text {dis }}$ causes the $\mathrm{CO}_{2} / \mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$equilibrium to shift toward formation of $\mathrm{H}^{+}$and $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$. This disturbance is an example of a $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ titration, because we initiated it by altering $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$. More specifically, it is a respiratory acidosis-"acidosis" because pH falls, and "respiratory" because pulmonary problems (Table 28-3) are the most common causes of an increase in the $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ of arterial blood (see p. 680).

In the absence of non- $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$buffers, how far pH falls during respiratory acidosis depends on the initial pH and $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$, as well as on the final $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$. For example, doubling $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ from 40 mm Hg (see Fig. 28-5, stage 1) to 80 mm Hg causes $\left[\mathrm{CO}_{2}\right]_{\text {dis }}$ to double to 2.4 mM (see Fig. 28-5, stage 2A). At this point, the 1-L system is far out of equilibrium and can
![bo55465312c5](bo55465312c5.jpg)

Figure 28-5 Doubling of $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ or $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$concentrations.

TABLE 28-3 The Four Major Acid-Base Disorders

| DISORDER | PROXIMATE CAUSE(S) | CLINICAL CAUSES | CHANGES IN ARTERIAL ACI-D-BASE PARAMETERS |
| :--: | :--: | :--: | :--: |
| Respiratory <br> acidosis | $\uparrow \mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ | $\downarrow$ Alveolar ventilation (e.g., drug overdose) <br> $\downarrow$ Lung-diffusing capacity (e.g., pulmonary edema) Ventilation-perfusion mismatch | $\mathrm{pH}: \downarrow$ <br> $\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right] ; \uparrow$ <br> $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}} ; \uparrow$ |
| Respiratory <br> alkalosis | $\downarrow \mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ | $\uparrow$ Alveolar ventilation caused by: <br> Hypoxia (e.g., acclimatization to high altitude) Anxiety Aspirin intoxication | $\mathrm{pH}: \uparrow$ <br> $\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right] \downarrow$ <br> $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}} ; \downarrow$ |
| Metabolic <br> acidosis | Addition of acids other than $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ or $\mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{CO}_{3}$ <br> Removal of alkali (fixed $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ ) | $\downarrow$ Urinary secretion of $\mathrm{H}^{+}$(e.g., renal failure) <br> Ketoacidosis (e.g., diabetes mellitus) <br> Lactic acidosis (e.g., shock) <br> $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$loss (e.g., severe diarrhea) | $\mathrm{pH}: \downarrow$ <br> $\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right] ; \downarrow$ <br> $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$. No change |
| Metabolic <br> alkalosis | Addition of alkali <br> Removal of acids other than $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ or $\mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{CO}_{3}$ (fixed $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ ) | $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$load (e.g., $\mathrm{NaHCO}_{3}$ therapy) <br> Loss of $\mathrm{H}^{+}$(e.g., severe vomiting) | $\mathrm{pH}: \uparrow$ <br> $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-} ; \uparrow$ <br> $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$. No change |

return to equilibrium only if some $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ ( X mmol ) combines with $\mathrm{X}_{2} \mathrm{O}$ to form $\mathrm{XH}^{+}$and $\mathrm{X} \mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$. Thus, according to Equation 28-14, the following must be true at equilibrium:

Solving this equation for X yields an extremely small value, nearly 40 nmol , or $0.000,040 \mathrm{mmol} . \mathrm{X}$ represents the flux of $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ that passes through the reaction sequence $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ $+\mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O} \rightarrow \mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{CO}_{3} \rightarrow \mathrm{H}^{+}+\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$to re-establish the equilibrium. $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ from the atmosphere replenishes the $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ consumed in this reaction, so that $\left[\mathrm{CO}_{2}\right]_{\text {dis }}$ remains at 2.4 mM after the new equilibrium is achieved (see Fig. 28-5, stage 3A). The reason that the flux X is so small is that, with no other buffers present, every $\mathrm{H}^{+}$formed remains free in solution. Thus, only a minuscule amount of $\mathrm{H}^{+}$need be formed before $\left[\mathrm{H}^{+}\right]$nearly doubles from 40 to nearly 80 nM . However, $\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]$undergoes only a tiny fractional increase, from 24 to $24.000,040 \mathrm{mM}$. The doubling of the denominator in Equation 28-21 is matched by a doubling of the numerator, nearly all of which is due to the near-doubling of $\left[\mathrm{H}^{+}\right]$. The final pH in this example of respiratory acidosis is

$$
\mathrm{pH}=-\log (80 \mathrm{nM})=7.10
$$

Another way of arriving at the same answer is to insert the final values for $\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]$and $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ into the HendersonHasselbalch equation:
$\mathrm{pH}=6.1+\log \frac{24.000,040 \mathrm{mM}}{(0.03 \mathrm{nM} / \mathrm{mmHg}) \cdot(80 \mathrm{mmHg})}=7.10$
(28-23)
The opposite acid-base disturbance, in which $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ falls, is respiratory alkalosis. In a solution containing no buffers other than $\mathrm{CO}_{2} / \mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$reducing $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ by half, from 40 to 20 mm Hg , would cause all of the aforementioned reactions to shift in the opposite direction, so that pH would rise by
0.3 , from 7.4 to 7.7 . We could produce respiratory alkalosis in a beaker by lowering the $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ in the gas phase. In humans, hyperventilation (see Table 28-3) lowers alveolar and thus arterial $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ (see p. 680).

Thus, in the absence of non- $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$buffers, doubling $\left[\mathrm{CO}_{2}\right]$ causes pH to fall by 0.3 , whereas halving $\left[\mathrm{CO}_{2}\right]$ causes pH to rise by 0.3 (see Table 28-1). Remember, the log of 2 is 0.3 .

## In the absence of other buffers, doubling $\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]$ causes pH to rise by 0.3

What would happen if we doubled $\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]$, rather than $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ ? If we start with 1 L of solution that has the ionic composition of arterial blood (see Fig. 28-5, stage 1), adding 24 mmol of $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$(e.g., $\mathrm{NaHCO}_{3}$ ) drives the $\mathrm{CO}_{2} / \mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$ equilibrium toward $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ (see Fig. 28-5, stage 2B). The new equilibrium is achieved when X mmol of $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$combines with $\mathrm{XH}^{+}$to produce $\mathrm{XCO}_{2}$ and $\mathrm{XH}_{2} \mathrm{O}$. Because the system is open to $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$, the generation of X mmol $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ causes no change in $\left[\mathrm{CO}_{2}\right]_{\text {dis }}$; the newly formed $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ simply evolves into the atmosphere. Thus, according to Equation 28-14, the following must be true at equilibrium:

Solving this equation yields an X of nearly $0.000,020 \mathrm{mM}$. Thus, in the absence of other buffers, an initial doubling of $\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]$from 24 to 48 mM causes $\left[\mathrm{H}^{+}\right]$to fall by nearly half, from 40 to 20 nM . Although $\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]$also decreases by 20 nM , its fractional change from 48 to $47.999,980 \mathrm{mM}$ is insignificant. The final pH is:

$$
\mathrm{pH}=-\log (20 \mathrm{nM})=7.70
$$

The Henderson-Hasselbalch equation yields the same result:

$$
\mathrm{pH}=6.1+\log \frac{47.999,980 \mathrm{mM}}{(0.03 \mathrm{mM} / \mathrm{mmHg}) \cdot(40 \mathrm{mmHg})}=7.70
$$

Thus, in the absence of non- $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$buffers, doubling $\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]$causes pH to increase by $\sim 0.3$. Rather than adding $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$, we could produce an identical effect by adding the same amount of strong base (e.g., NaOH ), as we did in Figure 28-3, stages 2B and 3B, or by removing the same amount of strong acid (e.g., HCl ). Although the removal of HCl from a solution in a beaker may seem artificial, the removal of gastric HCl from the body occurs during vomiting (see Table 28-3). O28-7 We also could add a weak base (e.g., $\mathrm{NH}_{3}$ ) or remove any weak acid (e.g., lactic acid) other than $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ or $\mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{CO}_{3}$. However, to produce the same alkali load, we would have to add/remove more of a weak base/acid than for a strong base/acid. All of these maneuvers, carried out at a fixed $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$, are examples of metabolic alkalosis-"alkalosis" because the pH increases, and "metabolic" because derangements in metabolism are common clinical causes.

The opposite acid-base disturbance-in which we remove $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$or another alkali, or add an acid other than $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ or $\mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{CO}_{3}$-is metabolic acidosis. If we start with a solution having the ionic composition of arterial blood, removing half of the initial $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$or removing 12 mM NaOH or adding 12 mM HCl would cause all of the aforementioned reactions to shift in the opposite direction, so that pH would fall by 0.3 , from 7.4 to 7.1 . Common causes of metabolic acidosis in humans are renal failure and metabolic problems (see Table 28-3).

We saw in Equation 28-26 that the pH of a $\mathrm{CO}_{2} / \mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$ solution does not depend on $\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]$or $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ per se, but on their ratio. Because it is the kidney that controls $\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]$in the blood plasma (see Chapter 39), and because it is the lung that controls $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ (see Chapter 31), the pH of blood plasma is under the dual control of both organ systems, a concept embodied by a whimsical variant of the HendersonHasselbalch equation:

$$
\mathrm{pH}=\text { Constant }+\frac{\text { Kidney }}{\text { Lungs }}
$$

## ACID-BASE CHEMISTRY IN THE PRESENCE OF $\mathrm{CO}_{2} / \mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$AND NON- $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$ BUFFERS-THE DÄVENPORT DIAGRAM

So far, our discussion has focused on simple systems in which the only pH buffers are either (1) one or more non- $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$buffers or (2) $\mathrm{CO}_{2} / \mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$. Under these circumstances, it is relatively easy to predict the effects of acid-base disturbances. Real biological systems, however, are mixtures of $\mathrm{CO}_{2} / \mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$and many non- $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$buffers. Thus, to understand the effects of respiratory and metabolic acid-base disturbances in a biological system, we must consider multiple competing equilibria-one for $\mathrm{CO}_{2} / \mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$(described by Equation 28-16) and one for each of the non- $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$buffers (each described by its version of Equation 28-5). Obtaining a precise solution to such clinically relevant problems is impossible. One approach is to use a computer to make
increasingly more precise approximations of the correct answer. Another, more intuitive approach is to use a graphical method to estimate the final pH . The Davenport diagram is the best such tool.

## The Davenport diagram is a graphical tool for interpreting acid-base disturbances in blood

What happens to the pH of blood-a complex mixture that includes many non- $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$buffers-when $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ doubles? We simplify matters by lumping together the actions of all non- $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$buffers, so that $\mathrm{H}^{+}+\mathrm{B}^{(n)} \rightleftharpoons \mathrm{HB}^{(n+1)}$ represents the reactions of all non- $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$buffers. When we raise $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$, almost all of the newly formed $\mathrm{H}^{+}$reacts with $\mathrm{B}^{(n)}$ to form $\mathrm{HB}^{(n+1)}$ so that the free $\left[\mathrm{H}^{+}\right]$rises only slightly.
![bo56465312c5](bo56465312c5.jpg)

In this simplified approach, the final pH depends on two competing buffer reactions-one involving $\mathrm{CO}_{2} / \mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$and the other involving $\mathrm{HB}^{(n+1)} / \mathrm{B}^{(n)}$. Computing the final pH requires solving two simultaneous equations, one for each buffer reaction.

The $\mathrm{CO}_{2} / \mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$Buffer The first of the two equations that we must solve simultaneously is a rearrangement of the Henderson-Hasselbalch equation (see Equation 28-16):

$$
\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]=s \cdot \mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}} \cdot 10^{\left(\mathrm{pH}-\mathrm{p} K_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}\right)}
$$

For a $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ of 40 mm Hg , the equation requires that $\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]$be 24 mM when pH is 7.40 -as in normal arterial blood plasma. If pH decreases by 0.3 at this same $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$, Equation 28-29 states that $\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]$must fall by half to 12 mM . Conversely, if pH increases by $0.3,\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]$must double to 48 mM . The blue column of Table 28-4 lists the $\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]$values that Equation 28-29 predicts for various pH

TABLE 28-4 Relationship between $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$and pH at Three Fixed Levels of $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$

|  | $\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right](\mathrm{mM})$ |  |  |
| :-- | :--: | :--: | :--: |
| pH | $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}=20 \mathrm{~mm} \mathrm{Hg}$ | $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}=40 \mathrm{~mm} \mathrm{Hg}$ | $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}=60 \mathrm{~mm} \mathrm{Hg}$ |
| 7.1 | 6 mM | 12 mM | 24 mM |
| 7.2 | 8 | 15 | 30 |
| 7.3 | 10 | 19 | 38 |
| 7.4 | 12 | $\mathbf{2 4}$ | 48 |
| 7.5 | 15 | 30 | 60 |
| 7.6 | 19 | 38 | 76 |
| 7.7 | 24 | 48 | 96 |

# N28-7 Metabolic Alkalosis Caused by Vomiting 

## Contributed by Walter Boron

Because the stomach secretes HCl into the lumen of the stomach, vomiting necessarily results in a net loss of HCl , N28-9 and this by itself causes some degree of metabolic alkalosis (see p. 635). In addition, the loss of $\mathrm{Cl}^{-}$leads to volume contraction, that is, a decrease in effective circulating volume (see pp. 554-555). The body's multipronged response to this volume contraction (see p. 838) includes a stimulation of the renin-angiotensin-aldosterone axis, the end point of which is increased retention of NaCl and the osmotically obligated $\mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O}$. However, at the level of the renal proximal tubule, the increased levels of systemic [angiotensin II] lead not only to an increase in NaCl reabsorption, but also to an increase in $\mathrm{NaHCO}_{3}$ reabsorption. Moreover, at the level of the distal nephron, the increased levels of aldosterone lead not only to an increase in NaCl reabsorption, but also to an increase in $\mathrm{NaHCO}_{3}$ reabsorption. The result is that the hormonal stimuli that represent the appropriate response to volume depletion lead, as a side effect, to a metabolic alkalosis. The proper treatment of this contraction alkalosis is not to infuse the patient with $\mathrm{NaHCO}_{3}$, but rather to
replenish the lost volume by delivering "normal saline" intravenously. With effective circulating volume returned to normal, the stimulus to the renin-angiotensin-aldosterone axis is removed, and the acid-base disturbance resolves.
It is interesting to note that although volume depletion causes the kidney not only to retain NaCl but also to retain $\mathrm{NaHCO}_{3}$ inappropriately, the converse is not true. That is, respiratory or metabolic acidosis cause the kidney to retain $\mathrm{NaHCO}_{3}$ but to decrease the reabsorption of NaCl , so as to maintain a constant effective circulating volume.

What this all teaches us is that the defense of effective circulating volume trumps everything (including acid-base homeostasis). However, the body's mechanisms for regulating acid-base balance are far more sophisticated than those for regulating effective circulating volume. Thus, the response to acidosis or alkalosis is precise and appropriate-and does not involve inappropriate changes in effective circulating volume that would otherwise lead to hyper- or hypotension!

## N28-9 Equivalency of Adding $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$, Adding $\mathrm{OH}^{-}$, and Removing $\mathrm{H}^{+}$

## Contributed by Walter Boron

Imagine that we start with 1 L of an aqueous solution at $37^{\circ} \mathrm{C}$. This solution mimics blood plasma, $5 \% \mathrm{CO}_{2} / 24 \mathrm{mM} \mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$at pH 7.4 , and the system is "open" for $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ (i.e., $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ can freely enter and leave the aqueous solution so as to keep $\left[\mathrm{CO}_{2}\right]$ constant). Moreover, this solution contains a non- $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$buffer $\left(\mathrm{HB}^{2(+) \rightleftharpoons} \mathrm{H}^{+}\rightleftharpoons \mathrm{B}^{50}$ ) with a buffering power of $25 \mathrm{mM} / \mathrm{pH}$ unit.

## Add $\mathrm{NaHCO}_{3}$

If we were to add 1 mmol of $\mathrm{NaHCO}_{3}$ to this 1 L of solution, then $\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]$would instantly increase by 1 mM . Of course, over time, subsequent reactions cause $\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]$to gradually fall as the added $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$equilibrates with the pre-existing $\mathrm{CO}_{2} / \mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$buffer according to the overall reactions:

$$
\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}+\mathrm{H}^{+} \rightarrow \mathrm{CO}_{2}+\mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O}
$$

(NE 28-25)
Furthermore, because the consumption of $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$goes hand in hand with the consumption of $\mathrm{H}^{+}$, the pH will gradually rise, which in turn upsets the equilibrium of non- $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$buffers:

$$
\mathrm{HB}^{(n+1)} \rightarrow \mathrm{H}^{+}+\mathrm{B}^{59}
$$

(NE 28-26)
Thus, the rise in pH causes the $\mathrm{HB}^{(n+)} / \mathrm{B}^{59}$ equilibrium to shift to the right, thereby increasing $\left[\mathrm{B}^{59}\right]$. As a result, the final $\Delta\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]$ would be $<1 \mathrm{mM}$. However, the changes in $\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]$and $\left[\mathrm{B}^{59}\right]$ would sum to very nearly 1 mM (i.e., the amount of $\mathrm{NaHCO}_{3}$ originally added):

$$
\Delta\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]+\Delta\left[\mathrm{B}^{59}\right] \cong 1 \mathrm{mM}
$$

(NE 28-27)
The reason why we use "approximately equal" in the above equation is that a tiny amount of the added $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$causes the pH to rise (i.e., it is not reflected in the $\Delta\left[\mathrm{B}^{59}\right]$ ).

## Add NaOH

Imagine that instead of adding $\mathrm{NaHCO}_{3}$, we add 1 mmol of NaOH to our 1 L of solution. We can imagine that virtually all of the added $\mathrm{OH}^{-}$would react with the $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ to form an equivalent amount of $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$:

$$
\mathrm{OH}^{-}+\mathrm{CO}_{2} \rightarrow \mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}
$$

(NE 28-28)
(Note: this reaction is equivalent to $\mathrm{CO}_{2}+\mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O} \rightarrow \mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}+\mathrm{H}^{+}$.) Thus, $\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]$would rise by very nearly 1 mM . The reason we say "very nearly" is that a tiny fraction of the added $\mathrm{OH}^{-}$would equilibrate with $\mathrm{H}^{+}$and $\mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O}$, causing pH to rise:

$$
\mathrm{OH}^{-}+\mathrm{H}^{+} \rightarrow \mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O}
$$

(NE 28-29)
This consumption of $\mathrm{OH}^{-}$would cause pH to rise, which in turn would cause the $\mathrm{HB}^{(n+)} / \mathrm{B}^{59}$ equilibrium to shift to the right:

$$
\mathrm{HB}^{(n+1)} \rightleftharpoons \mathrm{H}^{+}+\mathrm{B}^{59}
$$

(NE 28-30)
As a result $\left[\mathrm{B}^{59}\right]$ would rise by a tiny amount. At the same time, however, the reaction $\mathrm{CO}_{2}+\mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O} \rightleftharpoons \mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}+\mathrm{H}^{+}$is out of equilibrium because the $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$that we formed in Equation NE 28-28 yields an $\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]$far too high for the prevailing $\left[\mathrm{H}^{+}\right]$. Thus, Equation NE 28-28 would partially reverse, releasing more $\mathrm{OH}^{-}$to react with $\mathrm{H}^{+}$, and leading to the formation of still more $\mathrm{B}^{59}$. This reversal of Equation NE 28-28 (the secondary fall in $\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]$) would continue until eventually the reactions in both Equation NE 28-28 and Equation NE 28-30 are in equilibrium. This process is illustrated by the panels on the right side of Figure 28-7. As a result, the final $\Delta\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]$would be less than the -1 mM that we predicted in Equation NE 28-28. However, the changes in $\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]$

# N28-9 Equivalency of Adding $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$, Adding $\mathrm{OH}^{-}$, and Removing $\mathrm{H}^{+}$-cont'd 

and $\left[\mathrm{B}^{\text {int }}\right]$ would sum to very nearly 1 mM (i.e., the amount of NaOH added):

$$
\Delta\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]+\Delta\left[\mathrm{B}^{\mathrm{NH}}\right] \cong 1 \mathrm{mM}
$$

(NE 28-31)
In fact, the values of $\Delta\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]$and $\Delta\left[\mathrm{B}^{\text {int }}\right]$ in Equation NE 28-31 (i.e., the example in which we added $1 \mathrm{mmol} \mathrm{NaOH}$ ) are precisely the same as in Equation NE 28-27 (i.e., the example in which we added $1 \mathrm{mmol} \mathrm{NaHCO}_{3}$ ).

Two provisos here. First, the reaction in Equation NE 28-28 would be very slow in the absence of added carbonic anhydrase. N18-3 Nevertheless, even in the absence of carbonic anhydrase, this reaction would eventually come to equilibrium, and we could imagine that $\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]$would eventually rise by very nearly 1 mM . Second, in this thought experiment, we are "imagining" that the added $\mathrm{OH}^{-}$initially reacts only with $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ to form $\sim 1 \mathrm{mM} \mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$, and that some of this $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$then dissociates to yield some $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ and $\mathrm{OH}^{-}$. In fact, a sizeable amount of the $\mathrm{OH}^{-}$that we originally added will also react instantly with $\mathrm{H}^{+}$(according to Equation NE 28-29), in turn leading to the formation of $\mathrm{B}^{\text {int }}$ in Equation NE 28-30.

## Remove HCl

Finally, imagine that we magically remove 1 mmol of HCl from a liter of our $\mathrm{CO}_{2} / \mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$solution. In real life, we could remove this $\mathrm{H}^{+}$either by consuming it in a chemical (or biochemical) reaction or by transporting it out of some biological compartment (e.g., the cytosol). We could imagine that the HCl that we remove causes the $\mathrm{CO}_{2} / \mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$equilibrium to shift, thereby causing $\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]$to rise by very nearly 1 mM :

$$
\mathrm{CO}_{2}+\mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O} \rightarrow \mathrm{H}^{+}+\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}
$$

(NE 28-32)
The consumed $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ would come from the atmosphere. A small amount of the $\mathrm{H}^{+}$that we removed would lower pH :

$$
\mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O} \rightarrow \mathrm{OH}^{-}+\mathrm{H}^{+}
$$

(NE 28-33)
However, the reaction in Equation NE 28-32 would be badly out of equilibrium because the rise in $\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]$would be out of proportion to the fall in $\left[\mathrm{H}^{+}\right]$. Thus, the reaction in Equation NE 28-32 would begin to reverse, consuming some of the $\sim 1 \mathrm{mM} \mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$that we initially formed. Moreover, the $\mathrm{HB}^{\text {int } / / / \mathrm{B}^{\text {int }}$ reaction would also be out of equilibrium because $\left[\mathrm{H}^{+}\right]$has fallen without any change in $\left[\mathrm{B}^{\text {int }}\right]$ or $\left[\mathrm{HB}^{\text {int } / \mathrm{I}}\right]$. As a result, the following will occur:

$$
\mathrm{HB}^{\text {int }+1} \rightarrow \mathrm{H}^{+}+\mathrm{B}^{\text {int }}
$$

(NE 28-34)
In fact, the reaction in Equation NE 28-32 would continue to reverse, releasing some $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$as well as an equal amount of $\mathrm{H}^{+}$ for consumption in Equation NE 28-34. Eventually, the reactions in Equations NE 28-32 through NE 28-34 would be in equilibrium. At this point,

$$
\Delta\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]+\Delta\left[\mathrm{B}^{\mathrm{NH}}\right] \cong 1 \mathrm{mM}
$$

(NE 28-35)
In fact, the values of $\Delta\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]$and $\Delta\left[\mathrm{B}^{\text {int }}\right]$ in Equation NE 28-35 (i.e., this example in which we removed 1 mmol HCl ) are precisely the same as in Equation NE 28-27 (i.e., the example in which we added 1 mmol NaHCO ) and in Equation NE 28-31 (i.e., the example in which we added 1 mmol NaOH ).

## Summary

Thus, regardless of whether we add 1 mmol of $\mathrm{NaHCO}_{3}$, add 1 mmol of NaOH , or remove 1 mmol of $\mathrm{HCl} \ldots$ in the end, the result is the same. The $\mathrm{CO}_{2} / \mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$and the $\mathrm{HB}^{\text {int } / / / \mathrm{B}^{\text {int }}}$ buffer systems will both wind up being in equilibrium, and in each example, $\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]$will increase by the same amount, $\left[\mathrm{B}^{\text {int }}\right]$ will increase by the same amount, and pH will increase by the same amount.

Plotting these $\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]$values against pH yields the blue curve labeled " $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}=40 \mathrm{~mm} \mathrm{Hg}$ " in Figure 28-6A. This curve, which is known as a $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ isobar or isopleth (from the Greek isos [equal] + plethein [to be full]) represents all possible combinations of $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$) and pH at a $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ of 40 mm Hg . Table 28-4 also summarizes $\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]$values prevailing at $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ values of 20 and 80 mm Hg . Note that at a $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ of 20 (orange column), representing respiratory alkalosis, $\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]$values are half those for a $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ of 40 at the same pH . At a $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ of 80 (green column), representing respiratory acidosis, $\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]$values are twice those at a $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ of 40 . Each of the isopleths in Figure 28-6A rises exponentially with pH . The slope of each isopleth also rises
exponentially with pH and represents $\beta_{\text {open }}$ for $\mathrm{CO}_{2} / \mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$. At a particular pH , an isopleth representing a higher $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ (i.e., a higher $\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]$) has a steeper slope, as Equation 28-20 predicts.

Non- $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$Buffers The second of the two equations that we must solve simultaneously describes the lumped reaction of all non- $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$buffers. To understand the origin of this equation, we begin with the green curve (surrounded by black curves) in Figure 28-6B, the titration curve of a single non- $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$buffer with a pK of 7 and total buffer concentration of 12.6 mM (as for the green curve in Fig. 28-2B). At a pH of $10,\left[\mathrm{HB}^{(p+1)}\right]$ for this single buffer is extremely low

because almost all of the " B " is in the form $\mathrm{B}^{(\mathrm{n})}$. As we lower pH by successively adding small amounts of $\mathrm{HCl},\left[\mathrm{HB}^{(\mathrm{n}+1)}\right]$ gradually rises-most steeply when pH equals pK . Indeed, at any pH , the slope of this curve is the negative of $\beta$ for this single buffer. The black curves in Figure 28-6B are the titration curves for eight other buffers, each present at a $[\mathrm{TB}]$ of 12.6 mM , with pK values evenly spaced at intervals of 0.5 pH unit on either side of 7 . The red curve in Figure 28-6B is the sum of the titration curves for all nine buffers. ( $\mathrm{N} 28-8$ Its slope-the negative of the total buffering power of all nine buffers-is remarkably constant over a broad pH range. (The red curve in Figure 28-2B shows how $\beta$, the slope of the red curve here in Figure 28-6B, varies with pH .) The red curve in Figure 28-6B represents the second of the two equations that we must solve simultaneously.

Solving the Problem Figure 28-6C is a Davenport diagram, a combination of the three $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ isopleths in Figure 28-6A and the linear part of the red non- $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$titration curve in Figure 28-6B. The red non- $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$titration line (representing $\beta_{\text {non-HCO }_{3}^{-}}$for whole blood, $25 \mathrm{mM} / \mathrm{pH}$ unit) intersects with the $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ isopleth for a $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ of 40 at the point labeled "Start," which represents the initial conditions for arterial blood. At this intersection, both $\mathrm{CO}_{2} / \mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$and non- $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$buffers are simultaneously in equilibrium.

We are now in a position to answer the question raised at the beginning of this section: What will be the final pH when we increase the $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ of whole blood from 40 to 80 mm Hg ? The final equilibrium conditions for this case of respiratory acidosis must be described by a point that lies simultaneously on the red non- $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$titration line and the green isopleth for 80 mm Hg . Obtaining the answer using the Davenport diagram requires a three-step process:

Step 1: Identify the point at the intersection of the initial $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ isopleth and the initial non- $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$titration line (see Fig. 28-6C, "Start").
Step 2: Identify the isopleth describing the final $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ ( 80 mm Hg in this case).
Step 3: Follow the non- $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$titration line to its intersection with the final $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ isopleth. In Figure 28-6C, this intersection occurs at point A , which corresponds to a pH of 7.19 and an $\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]$of 29.25 mM .

As discussed above, in the absence of non- $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$buffers, this same doubling of $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ causes a larger pH decrease, from 7.4 to 7.1 .

By following three similar steps, we can use the Davenport diagram to predict the final pH and $\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]$under conditions of a respiratory alkalosis. For example, what would be the effect of decreasing $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ by half, from 40 to 20 mm Hg ?

In Figure 28-6C follow the red non- $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$titration line from "Start" to its intersection with the orange isopleth for a $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ of 20 mm Hg (point B ), which corresponds to a pH of 7.60 and an $\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]$of 19 mM . If the solution had not contained non- $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$buffers, halving the $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ would have caused a larger pH increase, from 7.4 to 7.7 .

## The amount of $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$formed or consumed during "respiratory" acid-base disturbances increases with $\beta_{\text {non-HCO }_{3}^{-}}$

Although it is reasonable to focus on $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ during respiratory acid-base disturbances, it would be wrong to assume that $\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]$is constant. For example, in Figure 28-6C, where $\beta_{\text {non- } \mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}}$is $25 \mathrm{mM} / \mathrm{pH}$ unit, increasing $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ from 40 to 80 mm Hg causes $\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]$to increase from 24 to 29.25 mM . Thus, in each liter of solution, $5.25 \mathrm{mmol} \mathrm{CO}_{2}$ combines with $5.25 \mathrm{mmol} \mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O}$ to form $5.25 \mathrm{mmol} \mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$(which we see as the $\Delta\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]$value between "Start" and point A ) and $5.25 \mathrm{mmol} \mathrm{H}^{+}$. Nearly all of this $\mathrm{H}^{+}$disappears as nearly 5.25 mmol of the deprotonated non- $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$buffers $\left(\mathrm{B}^{(\mathrm{n})}\right)$ consumes $\mathrm{H}^{+}$to form nearly 5.25 mmol of their conjugate weak acids $\left(\mathrm{HB}^{(\mathrm{n}+1)}\right)$. Thus, the flux through the reaction sequence in Equation 28-28 is nearly 5.25 mmol for each liter of solution.

$$
\begin{aligned}
& \mathrm{CO}_{2}+\mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O}-\frac{5.25 \mathrm{mM}}{\mathrm{HB}^{(\mathrm{n}+1)}} \rightarrow \mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}+\mathrm{H}^{+} \\
& \text { Thus, the non- } \mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-} \text {buffers drive the conversion of } \mathrm{CO}_{2} \text { to } \mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-} \text {. These buffers minimize the increase in free } \left[\mathrm{H}^{+}\right] \text {that a given flux of } \mathrm{CO}_{2} \text { can produce. Thus, with a } \\
& \text { high } \beta_{\text {non-HCO } 3} \text {, a large amount of } \mathrm{CO}_{2} \text { must "flux" through } \\
& \text { Equation 28-30 before the free concentrations of } \mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-} \text {and } \mathrm{H}^{+} \text {rise sufficiently to satisfy the } \mathrm{CO}_{2} / \mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-} \text {equilibrium } \\
& \text { (see Equation 28-14). }
\end{aligned}
$$

$\beta_{\text {non-HCO }_{3}^{-}}$varies with the hemoglobin content of blood. Thus, patients with anemia have a low $\beta_{\text {non- } \mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}}$, whereas patients with polycythemia have a high $\beta_{\text {non-HCO }_{3}^{-}}$.

Figure 28-6D illustrates the importance of $\beta_{\text {non-HCO }_{3}^{-}}$. If $\beta_{\text {non-HCO }_{3}^{-}}$is zero, the non- $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$titration line is horizontal, and doubling $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ from 40 to 80 mm Hg does not change $\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]$significantly (see Fig. 28-6D, point $\mathrm{A}_{1}$ ); this verifies our earlier conclusion (see Fig. 28-5, stage 3A). Simultaneously, pH falls by 0.3 , which corresponds to a near-doubling of $\left[\mathrm{H}^{+}\right]$. Thus, doubling $\left[\mathrm{CO}_{2}\right]$ leads to a doubling of the product $\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]\left[\mathrm{H}^{+}\right]$, so that the ratio $\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]\left[\mathrm{H}^{+}\right] /\left[\mathrm{CO}_{2}\right]$-the equilibrium constant, $K$-is unchanged (Table 28-5, top row). Figure 28-6D also shows what happens if $\beta_{\text {non-HCO }_{3}^{-}}$

# N28-8 Davenport Diagram-Constructing the Non-HCO3 ${ }_{3}^{-}$Buffer Line 

## Contributed by Emile Boulpaep and Walter Boron

The non- $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$buffer line (the red line in Fig. 28-6B, $C$ ) describes the buffering power of all buffers other than $\mathrm{CO}_{2} / \mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\left(\beta_{\text {non-HCO3 }}\right)$. In whole blood-here we refer to the line as the blood buffer line-hundreds of buffer groups may contribute to the overall non- $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$buffering power. Each of these buffer groups has its own pK and concentration, and thus its own bell-shaped curvelike the nine small curves in Figure 28-28-describing the pH dependence of its buffering power. Each of these buffer groups also has its own titration curve, as shown in Fig. 28-6B, and the sum of these titration curves (the red curve in this figure) is the combined titration curve of these buffers (i.e., $\beta_{\text {non-HCO3 }}$ ). Notice that this red curve is nearly a straight line over the physiological pH range. In other words, in the physiological range, the slope of the red line is nearly constant, which is another way of stating that the non- $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$buffering power is nearly constant over this pH range-as illustrated in Figure 28-28.

We already know from Equation 28-8 (reproduced below as Equation NE 28-21) that we can define buffering power in terms of the amount of strong acid added to the solution:

$$
\beta_{\text {Non-HCO3 }} \equiv-\frac{\frac{\text { moles } / \mathrm{L}}{\Delta[\text { Strong Acid }]}}{\Delta \mathrm{pH}}
$$

From the perspective of the non- $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$buffers that are at work during respiratory acidosis, $\Delta$ [strong acid] is the $\mathrm{H}^{+}$ formed from $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ and $\mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O}$ as a result of the increase in $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ (see arrow from "Start" to point A in Fig. 28-6C). Because respiratory acidosis produces $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$and $\mathrm{H}^{+}$in a $1: 1$ ratio, the amount of $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$formed during respiratory acidosis-that is $\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]_{\text {total }}-\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]_{\text {total }}=\Delta\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]$-is the same as the amount of $\mathrm{H}^{+}$added ( $\Delta$ [strong acid]). Thus, we can replace $\Delta$ [strong acid] in Equation NE 28-21 with $\Delta\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]$and therefore somewhat paradoxically express the non- $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$buffering power in terms of $\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]$:

$$
\beta_{\text {Non-HCO3 }} \equiv-\frac{\Delta[\text { Strong Acid }]}{\Delta \mathrm{pH}} \cong-\frac{\Delta\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]}{\Delta \mathrm{pH}}=\frac{\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]_{\text {total }}-\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]}{\mathrm{pH}-\mathrm{pH}_{\text {init }}}
$$

( $\mathrm{HES}_{28-22}$ )
$\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]_{\text {tot }}$ and $\mathrm{pH}_{\text {init }}$ represent initial values (before increasing $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$-that is, the point labeled "Start" in Fig. 28-6C), and $\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]$ and pH represent final values (after increasing $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$-that is, point A in Fig. 28-6C). The equation describing the non- $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$buffer line is simply a rearrangement of Equation NE 28-22:

$$
\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]=\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]_{\text {init }}-\beta_{\text {Non-HCO3 }}\left(\mathrm{pH}-\mathrm{pH}_{\text {init }}\right) \quad(\text { NE 28-23) }
$$

This equation describes a line whose slope is negative $\beta_{\text {non-HCO3 }}$. For whole blood, $\beta_{\text {non-HCO3 }}$ is $25 \mathrm{mM} / \mathrm{pH}$ unit, so that the slope of the line is $\sim 25 \mathrm{mM} / \mathrm{pH}$ unit in Figure 28-6C. Note that because we assumed that the buffering power of the non- $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$ buffers is constant over the pH range of interest, and because this buffering power is represented by the slope of the non- $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$ buffer curve in Figure 28-6C, the function describing this buffering power must be a straight line (i.e., a function with a constant slope).

We are now in a position to understand why, in Figure 28-6C, the non- $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$buffer line (see Equation NE 28-23) shares the same $y$-axis as the $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ isopleth (e.g., the blue curve in Fig. 28-6C), which is described by Equation 28-29, reproduced here:

$$
\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]=s \times P_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}} \times 10^{\left(\mathrm{pH}-\mathrm{pK}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}\right)} \quad(\text { NE 28-24) }
$$

In both the case of the line describing the non- $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$buffering power (see Equation NE 28-23) and the exponential curve describing the open-system $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ buffering power (see Equation NE 28-24), the dependent variable is $\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]$.

The non- $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$buffer line passes through the point describing the initial conditions $\left(\mathrm{pH}_{\text {init }} \cdot \mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]_{\text {tot }}$ ). In Figure 28-6C, $\mathrm{pH}_{\text {init }}$ is 7.40 and $\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]_{\text {tot }}$ is 24 mM , as indicated by the point labeled "Start." Because these initial conditions for the non- $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$buffers can change, we will see below in the text that the non- $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$buffer line can translate up and down on the Davenport diagram in metabolic alkalosis and metabolic acidosis. However, as long as $\beta_{\text {non-HCO3 }}$ is constant, the slope of the line is always the same.

has the normal value of $25 \mathrm{mM} / \mathrm{pH}$ unit. Here, point A is the same as point A in Figure 28-6C, and it shows that $\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]$increases by 5.25 mM , or $17 \%$ (see Table 28-5, middle row). Finally, if $\beta_{\text {non-HCO }}$ were $\infty$, the non- $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$ titration line would be vertical, and doubling $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ would not change pH at all (point $\mathrm{A}_{2}$ in Fig. 28-6D). In this case, $\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]$would double, so that the doubling of the product $\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]\left[\mathrm{H}^{+}\right]$would match the doubling of $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ (see Table 28-5, bottom row).

## Adding or removing an acid or base-at a constant $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$-produces a "metabolic" acid-base disturbance

Above, we examined the effect of adding HCl in the absence of other buffers (see Fig. 28-3). Predicting the pH change for such an acid-base disturbance was straightforward because the titration of $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$to $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ consumed all buffered $\mathrm{H}^{+}$. The situation is more complex when the solution also contains non- $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$buffers (Fig. 28-7, stage 1). When we add 10 mmol of HCl to 1 L of solution, the open-system $\mathrm{CO}_{2} / \mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$buffer pair neutralizes most of the added $\mathrm{H}^{+}$, non- $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$buffers handle some, and a minute amount of added $\mathrm{H}^{+}$remains free and lowers pH (see Fig. 28-7, stage 2A). Because the system is open, the $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ formed during buffering of the added $\mathrm{H}^{+}$escapes to the atmosphere. If this buffering reaction were occurring in the blood, the newly formed $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ would escape first into the alveolar air, and then into the atmosphere.

How much of the added $\mathrm{H}^{+}(\Delta[$ strong acid $]=10 \mathrm{mM})$ follows each of the three pathways in this example of metabolic acidosis? Answering this question requires dealing with two competing equilibria, the $\mathrm{CO}_{2} / \mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$and the non- $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$buffering reactions. As for respiratory acid-base disturbances, we cannot precisely solve the equations governing metabolic acid-base disturbances. However, we can use the Davenport diagram on the left of Figure 28-7 to obtain a graphical estimate of the final pH and $\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]$ through a four-step process:

Step 1: Identify the point describing the initial conditions (see Fig. 28-7, "Start" in graph on left).
Step 2: Following the black arrow labeled "2," move downward (in the direction of decreased $\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]$) by 10 mM the concentration of added $\mathrm{H}^{+}$-to the point labeled with an asterisk. In this maneuver, we assume that the reaction $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}+\mathrm{H}^{+} \rightarrow \mathrm{CO}_{2}+\mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O}$ has initially consumed all of the added $\mathrm{H}^{+}$. Of course, if this were true, the $\mathrm{CO}_{2} / \mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$reaction would be far out of equilibrium and the pH would not have changed at all. Also, the non- $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$buffers would not have had a chance to participate in the buffering of $\mathrm{H}^{+}$.
Step 3: Through the asterisk, draw a line (see Fig. 28-7, black line in left graph) that is parallel to the non- $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$titration line.
Step 4: Following the black arrow labeled " 4 ," move to the intersection of the new black line and the original $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ isopleth. This intersection occurs at point C , which corresponds to a pH of 7.26 and an $\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]$of 17.4 mM . This maneuver tracks the reaction $\mathrm{CO}_{2}+\mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O} \rightarrow \mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}+\mathrm{H}^{+}$. Non- $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$buffers consume nearly all of this $\mathrm{H}^{+}$in the reaction $\mathrm{H}^{+}+\mathrm{B}^{(0)} \rightarrow \mathrm{HB}^{(\mathrm{n}+1)}$. As a result, $\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right],\left[\mathrm{H}^{+}\right]$, and $\left[\mathrm{HB}^{(\mathrm{n}+1)}\right]$ all rise and the system equilibrates.

As a shortcut, we could bypass the two black arrows and simply follow the red arrow along the $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ isopleth from "Start" to point C.

We now can return to the question of how much of the added $\mathrm{H}^{+}$follows each of the three pathways in Figure 28-7, stage 2A. Because $\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]$decreased by $24-17.4=6.6 \mathrm{mM}$, the amount of $\mathrm{H}^{+}$buffered by $\mathrm{CO}_{2} / \mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$must have been 6.6 mmol in each liter. Almost all of the remaining $\mathrm{H}^{+}$ that we added, nearly 3.4 mmol , must have been buffered by non- $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$buffers. A tiny amount of the added $\mathrm{H}^{+}$, $\sim 0.000,015 \mathrm{mmol}$, must have remained unbuffered and was responsible for decreasing pH from 7.40 to 7.26 (see Fig. 28-7, stage 3A).

Figure 28-7 also shows what would happen if we added 10 mmol of a strong base such as NaOH to our 1-L solution. The open-system $\mathrm{CO}_{2} / \mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$buffer pair neutralizes most of the added $\mathrm{OH}^{-}$, non- $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$buffers handle some, and a minute amount of added $\mathrm{OH}^{-}$remains unbuffered, thus raising pH (see Fig. 28-7, stage 2B). The Davenport diagram on the right of Figure 28-7 shows how much of the added $\mathrm{OH}^{-}$follows each of the three pathways in this example of metabolic alkalosis. The approach is similar to the one we used above for metabolic acidosis, except that in this case, we generate a new black line that is displaced 10 mM above the non- $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$titration line. We follow the $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ isopleth to its intersection with this black line at point D , which corresponds to a final pH of 7.51 and an $\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]$of 31.1 mM . Thus, $\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]$rose by $31.1-24.0=7.1 \mathrm{mM}$. This is the amount of added $\mathrm{OH}^{-}$that $\mathrm{CO}_{2} / \mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$buffered. Non- $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$buffers must have buffered almost all of the remaining $\mathrm{OH}^{-}$that we added, $\sim 2.9 \mathrm{mmol}$ in each liter. The unbuffered $\mathrm{OH}^{-}$, which was responsible for the pH increase, must have been in the nanomolar range (see Fig. 28-7, stage 3B, and Box 28-1).

During metabolic disturbances, $\mathrm{CO}_{2} / \mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$makes a greater contribution to total buffering when pH and $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ are high and when $\beta_{\text {non-HCO }}$ is low
In Figure 28-7, $\mathrm{CO}_{2} / \mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$neutralized 7.1 mmol of the 10 mmol of $\mathrm{OH}^{-}$added to $1 \mathrm{~L}(\Delta \mathrm{pH}=+0.11)$ but only

## BOX 28-1 Strong Ion Difference

Some clinicians assess acid-base disorders in terms of a virtual parameter termed strong-ion difference (SID). Unlike weak acids and bases, the so-called strong cations and strong anions are fully dissociated at physiological pH . In blood,

$$
\mathrm{SID}=\left[\mathrm{Na}^{+}\right]+\left[\mathrm{K}^{+}\right]+\left[\mathrm{Ca}^{2+}\right]+\left[\mathrm{Mg}^{2+}\right]-\left[\mathrm{Cl}^{-}\right]
$$

Three major limitations to the SID approach are that (1) proteins and physiological processes generally depend on pH , not SID; (2) cells and the body closely regulate pH but have no known mechanism for directly sensing or regulating SID; and (3) SID neither is uniquely related to pH nor has a causal role in changing pH . Because the SID approach provides no new mechanistic insight, we focus on the classical pH /buffer approach.

![bo61465312c5](bo61465312c5.jpg)

Figure 28-7 Metabolic acidosis and alkalosis in the presence of non- $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$buffers. The red arrows in the Davenport diagrams represent the transition to metabolic acidosis (point C) and metabolic alkalosis (point D). This example differs from Figure 28-3, in which the solution contained no buffers other than $\mathrm{CO}_{2} / \mathrm{HCO}_{3}$.

TABLE 28-6 Buffering Produced by $\mathrm{CO}_{2} / \mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$and $\mathrm{Non}_{-} \mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$Buffers*

| ADDITION | $\Delta \mathrm{pH}$ | $\Delta\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]$ | $\beta_{\text {open }}=\frac{\Delta\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]}{\Delta \mathrm{pH}}$ | $\Delta\left[\mathrm{B}^{\text {int }}\right]$ | $\beta_{\text {non-HCO3 }}=\frac{\Delta\left[\mathrm{B}^{\text {int }}\right]}{\Delta \mathrm{pH}}$ | $\beta_{\text {total }}$ |
| :--: | :--: | :--: | :--: | :--: | :--: | :--: |
| $+10 \mathrm{mmol} \mathrm{H}^{+}$ | $-0.14$ | $-6.6 \mathrm{mM}$ | $47 \mathrm{mM} / \mathrm{pH}$ unit | $-3.4 \mathrm{mM}$ | $25 \mathrm{mM} / \mathrm{pH}$ unit | $72 \mathrm{mM} / \mathrm{pH}$ |
| $+10 \mathrm{mmol} \mathrm{OH}^{-}$ | $+0.11$ | $-7.1 \mathrm{mM}$ | $65 \mathrm{mM} / \mathrm{pH}$ unit | $-2.9 \mathrm{mM}$ | $25 \mathrm{mM} / \mathrm{pH}$ unit | $89 \mathrm{mM} / \mathrm{pH}$ |

*The additions are made to 1 L of solution. Initial $\mathrm{pH}=7.40, \mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}=40 \mathrm{~mm} \mathrm{Hg},\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]_{\mathrm{o}}=24 \mathrm{mM} . \beta_{\text {non-HCO3 }}$ assumed to be $25 \mathrm{mM} / \mathrm{pH}$ unit.
6.6 mmol of the added 10 mmol of $\mathrm{H}^{+}(\Delta \mathrm{pH}=-0.14)$. The reason for this difference is that the buffering power of $\mathrm{CO}_{2} / \mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$in an open system increases exponentially with pH (see Fig. 28-4, blue curve). In the Davenport diagram, this pH dependence of $\beta_{\text {open }}$ appears as the slope of the $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ isopleth, which increases exponentially with pH . Thus, adding alkali will always cause a smaller pH change than adding an equivalent amount of acid. In our example of metabolic alkalosis (see right side of Fig. 28-7), the mean $\beta_{\text {open }}$ is $(7.1 \mathrm{mM}) /(0.11 \mathrm{pH}$ unit $)=65 \mathrm{mM} / \mathrm{pH}$ unit in the pH range 7.40 to 7.51 (Table 28-6). On the other hand, in our example of metabolic acidosis (see left side of Fig. 28-7), the mean $\beta_{\text {open }}$ is $(6.6 \mathrm{mM}) /(0.14 \mathrm{pH}$ unit $)=47 \mathrm{mM} / \mathrm{pH}$ unit in the pH range 7.26 to 7.40 , substantially less than in the more alkaline range. Because $\beta_{\text {non-HCO3 }}$ is $25 \mathrm{mM} / \mathrm{pH}$ unit over the entire pH range, $\beta_{\text {total }}$ is greater in the alkaline pH range.

We have already seen that $\beta_{\text {open }}$ is proportional to $\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]$(see Equation 28-20) and that-at a fixed $\mathrm{pH}-$ $\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]$is proportional to $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ (see Equation 28-29). Thus, other things being equal, the contribution of $\beta_{\text {open }}$ to total buffering increases with $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$. Patients with a high $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ due to respiratory failure will, therefore, have a higher buffering power than normal individuals at the same pH .

Because $\mathrm{CO}_{2} / \mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$and non- $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$buffers compete for added $\mathrm{OH}^{-}$or $\mathrm{H}^{+}$, the contribution of $\mathrm{CO}_{2} / \mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$to total buffering also depends on $\beta_{\text {non-HCO3 }}$. Figure 28-8 illustrates the effects of adding 10 mmol NaOH to our standard $1-\mathrm{L}$ $\mathrm{CO}_{2} / \mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$solution at $\beta_{\text {non-HCO3 }}$ values of $0,25 \mathrm{mM} / \mathrm{pH}$ unit, and $\infty$. We saw in Figure 28-3, stage 3B, that adding 10 mmol of NaOH to a $1-\mathrm{L}$ solution containing $\mathrm{CO}_{2} / \mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$ but no other buffers causes $\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]$to increase from 24 to 34 mM , and pH to increase from 7.40 to 7.55 . To use a Davenport diagram to solve the same problem (see Fig. 28-8A), we generate a non- $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$titration line with slope of zero (because $\beta_{\text {non-HCO3 }}=0$ ) through the "Start" point. We also draw a black line with the same slope, but displaced 10 mM higher. Following the blue $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}=40$ isopleth from "Start" to the point where the isopleth intersects with the black line at point $\mathrm{D}_{1}$, we see that-as expected-the final pH is 7.55 and the final $\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]$is 34 mM . Thus, $\mathrm{CO}_{2} / \mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$ must buffer virtually all 10 mmol of $\mathrm{OH}^{-}$added to 1 L . Because the total buffering power is simply $\beta_{\text {open }}$, the pH increase must be rather large, 0.15 .

When $\beta_{\text {non-HCO3 }}$ is $25 \mathrm{mM} / \mathrm{pH}$ unit, as in the Davenport diagram in the upper right of Figure 28-7 (replotted in Fig. 28-8B), $\mathrm{CO}_{2} / \mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$can buffer only 7.1 mmol because the non- $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$buffers neutralize 2.9 mmol . With the increased total buffering power, the pH increase is only 0.11 .

Finally, when $\beta_{\text {non-HCO3 }}$ is $\infty$, the black line lies right on top of the vertical non- $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$titration line (see Fig. 28-8C). Thus, neither $\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]$nor pH changes at all. However,
