$\left[\mathrm{HB}^{(\mathrm{n}+1)}\right]$ increases by 10 mM because the infinitely powerful non- $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$buffers do all of the buffering.

This set of three examples-for $\beta_{\text {non- } \mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}}$values of 0,25 , and $\infty$-is comparable to the set that we discussed above for respiratory acidosis in Figure 28-6D.

## A metabolic change can compensate for a respiratory disturbance

Thus far, we have considered what happens during primary respiratory and metabolic acid-base disturbances. When challenged by such acid or alkaline loads in the blood plasma, the body compensates by altering $\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]$or $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$, returning pH toward its initial value and minimizing the magnitude of the overall pH change.

In Figure 28-9A we revisit an example, originally introduced in Figure 28-6C, in which we produced a primary respiratory acidosis by increasing $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ from 40 to 80 mm Hg (red arrow in Fig. 28-9A between "Start" and point A at pH 7.19). If the high $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ persists, the only way we can restore pH toward its initial value of 7.40 is to add an alkali (e.g., $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$or $\mathrm{OH}^{-}$) or remove an acid (e.g., $\mathrm{H}^{+}$), all of which are equivalent. ( $\mathrm{N28-9}$ Adding 10 mmol of $\mathrm{OH}^{-}$to 1 L , for example, superimposes a metabolic alkalosis on the primary respiratory acidosis-a metabolic compensation to respiratory acidosis.

The Davenport diagram predicts the consequences of adding 10 mmol of $\mathrm{OH}^{-}$to 1 L . We start by generating a black line that is parallel to the red non- $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$titration line, but displaced upward by 10 mM . Point $\mathrm{A}_{1}$, the intersection of the black line and the $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ isopleth for 80 mm Hg , represents a final pH of 7.29 , still lower than the normal 7.40 , but much higher than the 7.19 prevailing before compensation.

If we add an additional $14 \mathrm{mmol} \mathrm{OH}^{-}$, for a total addition of $24 \mathrm{mmol} \mathrm{OH}^{-}$to $1 \mathrm{~L}, \mathrm{pH}$ returns to exactly its initial value of 7.40 (point $\mathrm{A}_{2}$ in Fig. 28-9A). In other words, we can perfectly compensate for doubling $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ from 40 ("Start") to 80 mm Hg (point A) by adding an amount of $\mathrm{OH}^{-}$equivalent to the amount of $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$that was present (i.e., 24 mM ) at "Start." Perfect compensation of respiratory acidosis is an example of isohydric hypercapnia (i.e., same pH at a higher $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ ):

$$
\begin{aligned}
\mathrm{pH} & =6.1+\log \frac{\frac{\text { Doubled }\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]}{48 \mathrm{mM}}}{(0.03 \mathrm{mM} / \mathrm{mmHg}) \times 80 \mathrm{mmHg}} \\
& =7.4
\end{aligned}
$$

The kidneys are responsible for the metabolic compensation to a primary respiratory acidosis. They acutely sense high $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ and may also chronically sense low blood pH . The response is to increase both the secretion of acid into the urine and the transport of $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$into the blood (see pp. 832-833), thereby raising plasma pH - a compensatory metabolic alkalosis. The renal compensation to a substantial respiratory acidosis is not perfect, so that pH remains below the normal value of 7.40 .

The kidneys can also perform a metabolic compensation to a respiratory alkalosis. In Figure 28-9B we revisit a second example, originally introduced in Figure 28-6C, in which we produced a primary respiratory alkalosis by decreasing $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ from 40 to 20 mm Hg (red arrow in Fig. 28-9B between "Start" and point B at pH 7.60 ). We can compensate for most of this respiratory alkalosis by adding 10 mmol of $\mathrm{H}^{+}$to each liter of solution or by removing $10 \mathrm{mmol} \mathrm{NaHCO}_{3}$ or NaOH , all of which produce the same effect. We generate a black line that is parallel to the red non- $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$titration line but is displaced downward by 10 mM . Point $\mathrm{B}_{1}(\mathrm{pH} 7.44$ ), at the intersection of the black line and the $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ isopleth for 20 mm Hg , represents a partial compensation.

![bo66465312c5](bo66465312c5.jpg)

Figure 28-10 Respiratory compensation to primary metabolic acid-base disturbances.

If we add an additional $2 \mathrm{mmol} \mathrm{H}^{+}$, for a total addition of 12 mmol of $\mathrm{H}^{+}$to each liter, pH returns to exactly its initial value of 7.40 (point $\mathrm{B}_{2}$ in Fig. 28-9B). Why is this amount so much less than the 24 mmol of $\mathrm{OH}^{-}$that we added to each liter to compensate perfectly for a doubling of $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ ? When we halve $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$, we need only add an amount of $\mathrm{H}^{+}$equivalent to half the amount of $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$that was present $(24 / 2=12 \mathrm{mM})$ at "Start."

In response to a primary respiratory alkalosis, the kidneys secrete less acid into the urine and transport less $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$into the blood (see pp. 833-834), thereby lowering plasma $\mathrm{pH}-\mathrm{a}$ compensatory metabolic acidosis.

## A respiratory change can compensate for a metabolic disturbance

Just as metabolic changes can compensate for respiratory disturbances, respiratory changes can compensate for metabolic ones. In Figure 28-10A we return to an example originally introduced on the left side of Figure 28-7, in which we produced a primary metabolic acidosis by adding 10 mmol of HCl to 1 L of arterial blood (red arrow in Fig. 28-10A between "Start" and point C at pH 7.26). Other than removing the $\mathrm{H}^{+}$(or adding $\mathrm{OH}^{-}$to neutralize the $\mathrm{H}^{+}$), the only way we can restore pH toward the initial value of 7.40 is to lower $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$. That is, we can superimpose a respiratory alkalosis on the primary metabolic acidosis-a respiratory compensation to a metabolic acidosis. For example, we can compensate for most of the metabolic acidosis by reducing $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ from 40 to 30 mm Hg . Starting at C , follow the black line from the $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ isopleth for 40 mm Hg to the new isopleth for 30 mm Hg at point $\mathrm{C}_{1}(\mathrm{pH} 7.34$ ). This maneuver represents a partial compensation.

If we reduce $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ by an additional 6.6 mm Hg to 23.4 mm Hg , we produce a perfect compensation, which we represent in Figure 28-10A by following the black line from C until we reach pH 7.40 at point $\mathrm{C}_{2}$, which is on the $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ isopleth for 23.4 mm Hg . Perfect compensation requires that we reduce $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ by a fraction (i.e., $[40-23.4] / 40 \cong 0.42$ )
that is identical to the ratio of added HCl to the original $\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]$(i.e., $10 / 24 \cong 0.42$ ).

Not surprisingly, the respiratory system is responsible for the respiratory compensation to a primary metabolic acidosis. The low plasma pH or $\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]$stimulates the peripheral chemoreceptors (see p. 710) and, if sufficiently long-standing, also the central chemoreceptors (see pp. 713715). The result is an increase in alveolar ventilation, which lowers $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ (see pp. 679-680) and thus provides the respiratory compensation.

The body achieves a respiratory compensation to a primary metabolic alkalosis in just the opposite manner. In Figure 28-10B, we revisit an example originally presented on the right side of Figure 28-7, in which we produce a primary metabolic alkalosis by adding 10 mmol of NaOH to 1 L (red arrow in Fig. 28-10B between "Start" and point D at pH 7.51). If we now increase $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ from 40 to 50 mm Hg , we partially compensate for the metabolic alkalosis by moving from D to $\mathrm{D}_{1}$ ( pH 7.44 ). If we raise $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ by an additional 6.7 mm Hg to $\sim 56.7 \mathrm{~mm} \mathrm{Hg}$, the compensation is perfect, and pH returns to $7.40\left(\mathrm{D}_{2}\right)$.

In response to a primary metabolic alkalosis, the respiratory system decreases alveolar ventilation and thereby raises $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$. The compensatory respiratory acidosis is the least "perfect" of the four types of compensation we have discussed. The reason is that one can decrease alveolar ventilation-and thus oxygenation-only so far before compromising one's very existence.

In a real person, each of the four primary acid-base disturbances-points A, B, C, and D in Figures 28-9 and 28-10-occurs in the extracellular fluid. In each case, the body's first and almost instantaneous response is to use extracellular buffers to neutralize part of the acid or alkaline load. In addition, cells rapidly take up some of the acid or alkaline load and thus participate in the buffering, as discussed below. Furthermore, renal tubule cells may respond to a metabolic acidosis of extrarenal origin (e.g., diabetic ketoacidosis) by increasing acid secretion into the urine, or to a metabolic alkalosis (e.g., $\mathrm{NaHCO}_{3}$ therapy) by

![bo67465312c5](bo67465312c5.jpg)

Figure 28-11 Acid-base states represented by position on a Davenport diagram.
decreasing acid secretion-examples of metabolic compensation to a metabolic disturbance. Points C and D in Figure 28-10 include the effects of the actual acid or alkaline load, extracellular and intracellular buffering, and any renal (i.e., "metabolic") response.

## Position on a Davenport diagram defines the nature of an acid-base disturbance

The only tools needed for characterizing acid-base status (i.e., $\mathrm{pH},\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]$, and $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ ) are a Davenport diagram and electrodes for measuring pH and $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$. From these last two parameters, we can compute $\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]$using the Henderson-Hasselbalch equation. The acid-base status of blood plasma-or of any aqueous solution-must fall into one of five major categories:

1. Normal. For arterial blood, pH is $7.40,\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]$is 24 mM , and $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ is 40 mm Hg . This point was labeled "Start" in the previous figures and is the central point in Figure 28-11A through $D$.
2. One of the four primary (or uncompensated) acid-base disturbances. The coordinates for pH and $\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]$fall either (a) on the non- $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$titration line, but off the $40-\mathrm{mm} \mathrm{Hg}$ isopleth for uncompensated respiratory acidosis (point A, Fig. 28-11A) or alkalosis (point B); or (b) on the $40-\mathrm{mm} \mathrm{Hg}$ isopleth, but off the non- $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$
titration line for an uncompensated metabolic acidosis (point C) or alkalosis (point D).
3. A partially compensated disturbance. The coordinates for pH and $\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]$fall in any of the four colored regions of Figure 28-11B. For example, point $\mathrm{A}_{1}$ in the blue region represents a partially compensated respiratory acidosis. In a subject for whom a respiratory acidosis has existed for longer than a few hours, increased renal $\mathrm{H}^{+}$excretion has probably already begun producing a metabolic compensation. Depending on the extent of the original acidosis and the degree of compensation, point $\mathrm{A}_{1}$ could lie anywhere in the blue region. We can reach similar conclusions for the other three disturbances/ compensations.
4. A perfectly compensated disturbance. The point falls on the vertical line through pH 7.4 . As indicated by point $\mathrm{A}_{2} \mathrm{D}_{2}$ in Figure 28-11C, a perfect compensation following respiratory acidosis is indistinguishable from that following metabolic alkalosis. In either case, the final outcome is isohydric hypercapnia. Similarly, point $\mathrm{B}_{2} \mathrm{C}_{2}$ represents a perfect compensation following either metabolic acidosis or respiratory alkalosis (isohydric hypocapnia).
5. A compound respiratory/metabolic disturbance. The point falls in one of the two colored regions in Figure 28-11D. For example, points $\mathrm{A}_{3}$ and $\mathrm{C}_{3}$ represent a respiratory acidosis complicated by metabolic acidosis, and

![bo68465312c5](bo68465312c5.jpg)

![bo69465312c5](bo69465312c5.jpg)

Figure 28-12 Recovery of a cell from intracellular acid and alkali loads. CA II, carbonic anhydrase II.
vice versa. The second disturbance causes the pH to move even further away from "normal," as might occur in an individual with both respiratory and renal failure (see Table 28-3). Of course, it is impossible to determine if one of the two compounding disturbances developed before the other.

## pH REGULATION OF INTRACELLULAR FLUID

Clinicians focus on the acid-base status of blood plasma, the fluid compartment whose acid-base status is easiest to assess. However, the number of biochemical reactions and other processes that occur outside a cell pales in comparison to the number of those that occur inside. It stands to reason that the most important biological fluid for pH regulation is the cytosol. It is possible to monitor intracellular $\mathrm{pH}\left(\mathrm{pH}_{\mathrm{i}}\right)$ continuously in isolated cells and tissues using pH -sensitive dyes or microelectrodes. Moreover, using magnetic resonance techniques, one can monitor pH , in living humans (e.g., in skeletal muscle). The pH of cells both influences, and is influenced by, extracellular pH .

## Ion transporters at the plasma membrane closely regulate the pH inside of cells

Figure 28-12A shows a hypothetical cell with three acid-base transporters: an electroneutral $\mathrm{Na} / \mathrm{HCO}_{3}$ cotransporter (NBCn1; see p. 122) a Na-H exchanger (NHE1; pp. 124125), and a $\mathrm{Cl}-\mathrm{HCO}_{3}$ or anion exchanger (AE2; see p. 124). We will use NBCn1 and NHE1 as prototypic acid extruders, transporters that tend to raise intracellular $\mathrm{pH}\left(\mathrm{pH}_{\mathrm{i}}\right)$. Both use the energy of the $\mathrm{Na}^{+}$gradient to either import $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$or export $\mathrm{H}^{+}$from the cytosol. We will take the $\mathrm{Cl}-\mathrm{HCO}_{3}$

exchanger AE2 as the prototypic acid loader, a transporter that tends to lower $\mathrm{pH}_{\mathrm{o}}$. AE2, driven by the steep out-to-in $\mathrm{Cl}^{-}$gradient, moves $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$out of the cell. The intracellular acid loading produced by $\mathrm{Cl}-\mathrm{HCO}_{3}$ exchange is a chronic acid load because it tends to acidify the cell as long as the transporter is active. In our example, the chronic acid extrusion via NBCn1 and NHE1 balances the chronic acid loading via AE2, producing a steady state. Several other acid extruders and acid loaders may contribute to pH , regulation (see p. 127), and each cell type has a characteristic complement of such transporters.

Imagine that we use a micropipette to inject HCl into the cell in Figure 28-12A. This injection of an acute acid load-a one-time-only introduction of a fixed quantity of acidcauses an immediate fall in $\mathrm{pH}_{\mathrm{o}}$ (see Fig. 28-12B, black curve), an example of intracellular metabolic acidosis. If we add HCl to a beaker, the pH remains low indefinitely. However, when we acutely acid-load a cell, $\mathrm{pH}_{\mathrm{i}}$ spontaneously recovers to the initial value. This $\mathrm{pH}_{\mathrm{i}}$ recovery cannot be due to passive $\mathrm{H}^{+}$efflux out of the cell, inasmuch as the electrochemical gradient for $\mathrm{H}^{+}$(see p. 127) usually favors $\mathrm{H}^{+}$influx. Hence, the $\mathrm{pH}_{\mathrm{i}}$ recovery reflects the active transport of acid from the cell-a metabolic compensation to a metabolic acid load. 0 N28-10

During the recovery of $\mathrm{pH}_{\mathrm{i}}$ from the acid load in Figure 28-12B, NBCn1 and NHE1not only must extrude the quantity of $\mathrm{H}^{+}$previously injected into the cell (the acute acid load), they also must counteract whatever acid loading AE2 continues to impose on the cell (the chronic acid load).

Acid-extrusion rates tend to be greatest at low $\mathrm{pH}_{\mathrm{i}}$ values and fall off as pH , rises (see Fig. 28-12C, black curve). As discussed below, low pH , also inhibits AE2 and thus acid loading. The response to low $\mathrm{pH}_{\mathrm{i}}$ therefore involves two feedback loops operating in push-pull fashion, stimulating acid extrusion and inhibiting acid loading.

In addition to $\mathrm{pH}_{\mathrm{o}}$, hormones, growth factors, oncogenes, cell volume, and extracellular $\mathrm{pH}\left(\mathrm{pH}_{\mathrm{o}}\right)$ all can modulate these transporters. The $\mathrm{pH}_{\mathrm{o}}$ effect is especially important. In general, a low $\mathrm{pH}_{\mathrm{o}}$ slows the rate of $\mathrm{pH}_{\mathrm{i}}$ recovery from acute acid loads and reduces the final steady-state $\mathrm{pH}_{\mathrm{i}}$ (see Fig. 28-12B, red curve), whereas a high $\mathrm{pH}_{\mathrm{o}}$ does the opposite (blue curve). The underlying cause of these $\mathrm{pH}_{\mathrm{o}}$ effects is a shift of the pH , dependence of acid extrusion (see Fig. 28-12C, red and blue curves) and, as we shall see, changes in acid loading.

Cells also spontaneously recover from acute alkaline loads. If we inject a cell with potassium hydroxide ( KOH ) (see Fig. 28-12D), pH , rapidly increases but then slowly recovers to its initial value (see Fig. 28-12E, black curve), which reflects stimulation of acid loading and inhibition of acid extrusion. The injection of $\mathrm{OH}^{-}$represents a metabolic alkalosis, whereas increased $\mathrm{Cl}-\mathrm{HCO}_{3}$ exchange represents a metabolic compensation. (○) N28-10 The $\mathrm{Cl}-\mathrm{HCO}_{3}$ exchanger is most active at high pH , values (see Fig. 28-12F). As discussed previously, increases in pH , generally inhibit acid extruders. Thus, the response to alkaline loads-like the response to acid loads-includes dual feedback loops operating in push-pull fashion, stimulating acid loading and inhibiting acid extrusion. During the $\mathrm{pH}_{\mathrm{i}}$ recovery from an acute alkaline load, $\mathrm{Cl}-\mathrm{HCO}_{3}$ exchange not only must neutralize the alkali previously injected into the cell (the acute
alkali load), but also must counteract the alkalinizing effect of acid extruders such as the $\mathrm{Na} / \mathrm{HCO}_{3}$ cotransporter.

Like acid extruders, $\mathrm{Cl}-\mathrm{HCO}_{3}$ exchangers are under the control of hormones and growth factors with a $\mathrm{pH}_{\mathrm{o}}$ sensitivity that is opposite to that of the acid extruders (i.e., low $\mathrm{pH}_{\mathrm{o}}$ enhances $\mathrm{Cl}-\mathrm{HCO}_{3}$ exchange). In general, an extracellular metabolic acidosis, with its simultaneous fall in both $\mathrm{pH}_{\mathrm{o}}$ and $\left[\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right]$(see Table 28-3), stimulates acid loading via $\mathrm{Cl}-\mathrm{HCO}_{3}$ exchange (see Fig. 28-12F, red curve). During the recovery from acute alkali loads, metabolic acidosis therefore causes pH , to fall more rapidly and to reach a lower steady-state value (see Fig. 28-12E, red curve).

## Indirect interactions between $\mathrm{K}^{+}$and $\mathrm{H}^{+}$make it appear as if cells have a K-H exchanger

Clinicians have long known that extracellular acidosis leads to a release of $\mathrm{K}^{+}$from cells, and thus hyperkalemia (see p. 795). Conversely, hyperkalemia leads to the exit of $\mathrm{H}^{+}$ from cells, resulting in extracellular acidosis. These observations, which one can duplicate in single, isolated cells, led to the suggestion that cells generally have K-H exchangers. It is true that specialized cells in the stomach (see pp. 865-866) and kidney (see pp. 827-828) do have an ATP-driven pump that extrudes $\mathrm{H}^{+}$in exchange for $\mathrm{K}^{+}$. Moreover, the $\mathrm{K} / \mathrm{HCO}_{3}$ cotransporter described in certain cells could mimic a K-H exchanger. Nevertheless, the effects that long ago led to the $\mathrm{K}-\mathrm{H}$-exchange hypothesis probably reflect indirect interactions of $\mathrm{H}^{+}$and $\mathrm{K}^{+}$.

One example of apparent K-H exchange is that hyperkalemia causes an intracellular alkalosis. Not only is this effect not due to a $1: 1$ exchange of $\mathrm{K}^{+}$for $\mathrm{H}^{+}$, it is not even due to the increase in $\left[\mathrm{K}^{+}\right]_{\mathrm{o}}$ per se. Instead, the high $\left[\mathrm{K}^{+}\right]_{\mathrm{o}}$ depolarizes the cell, and this positive shift in membrane voltage can promote events such as the net uptake of $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$via the electrogenic $\mathrm{Na} / \mathrm{HCO}_{3}$ cotransporter (see p. 122) and thus a rise in $\mathrm{pH}_{\mathrm{i}}$-a depolarization-induced alkalinization. Depolarization also can indirectly stimulate other transporters that alkalinize the cell. We discuss the converse example of acidemia causing hyperkalemia on page 795: Extracellular acidosis lowers pH , and inhibits transporters responsible for $\mathrm{K}^{+}$uptake, which leads to net $\mathrm{K}^{+}$release from cells. Although we have no evidence that a K-H exchanger exists in humans, imagining that it exists is sometimes a helpful tool for quickly predicting interactions of $\mathrm{H}^{+}$and $\mathrm{K}^{+}$in a clinical setting.

## Changes in intracellular pH are often a sign of changes in extracellular $\mathrm{pH}_{\text {, }}$ and vice versa

By definition, acid extrusion (e.g., $\mathrm{Na} / \mathrm{HCO}_{3}$ cotransport) equals acid loading (e.g., $\mathrm{Cl}-\mathrm{HCO}_{3}$ exchange) in the steady state. Disturbing this balance shifts $\mathrm{pH}_{\mathrm{o}}$. For example, extracellular metabolic acidosis inhibits acid extrusion and stimulates acid loading, thus lowering steady-state $\mathrm{pH}_{\mathrm{o}}$. This intracellular acidosis is not instantaneous, but develops over a minute or two. Conversely, extracellular metabolic alkalosis leads to a slow increase in steady-state $\mathrm{pH}_{\mathrm{i}}$. Generally, a change in $\mathrm{pH}_{\mathrm{o}}$ shifts $\mathrm{pH}_{\mathrm{i}}$ in the same direction, but $\Delta \mathrm{pH}_{\mathrm{i}}$ is usually only $20 \%$ to $60 \%$ of $\Delta \mathrm{pH}_{\mathrm{o}}$. In other words, an extracellular metabolic acidosis causes a net transfer of acid from the extracellular to the intracellular space, and an

# N28-10 Acid Extruders and Acid Loaders in a Cell 

## Contributed by Emile Boulpaep and Walter Boron

Of the acid extruders that can produce pH , recovery, the most widely distributed is the NHE1 isoform of the $\mathrm{Na}-\mathrm{H}$ exchanger. However, mechanisms that mediate $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$uptake often coexist with the $\mathrm{Na}-\mathrm{H}$ exchanger in the same cell and are sometimes far more powerful than the $\mathrm{Na}-\mathrm{H}$ exchanger. These $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$transporters include the $\mathrm{Na}^{+}$-driven $\mathrm{Cl}-\mathrm{HCO}_{3}$ exchanger (or NDCBE, illustrated in Fig. 5-14, no. 17) and the $\mathrm{Na} / \mathrm{HCO}_{3}$ cotransporters with $\mathrm{Na}^{+}: \mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$stoichiometries of 1:2 (NBCe1 and NBCe2, the electrogenic $\mathrm{Na} / \mathrm{HCO}_{3}$ cotransporters) and 1:1 (NBCn1, the electroneutral $\mathrm{Na} / \mathrm{HCO}_{3}$ cotransporter). Note that the electrogenic $\mathrm{Na} / \mathrm{HCO}_{3}$ cotransporter that operates with an $\mathrm{Na}^{+}: \mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$stoichiometry of 1:3 (see Fig. 5-14, no. 19) mediates net $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$efflux-which reflects the ionic and electrical gradients that govern its thermodynamic properties-and functions as an acid loader. However, the $\mathrm{Na} /$ $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}$ cotransporters with $\mathrm{Na}^{+}: \mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$stoichiometries of 1:2 and 1:1 both mediate the net uptake of $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$and function as acid extruders.

Other transporters that extrude acid from cells include the V-type H pump and the H-K exchange pump.

Other transport processes that acid-load cells include the passive influx of $\mathrm{H}^{+}$as well as the efflux of $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$either through channels (e.g., gamma-aminobutyric acid or glycineactivated $\mathrm{Cl}^{-}$channels) or through the $\mathrm{K} / \mathrm{HCO}_{3}$ cotransporter.

For a more detailed discussion of these transporters, see page 127 .

![bo74465312c5](bo74465312c5.jpg)

Figure 28-13 Response of cell to extracellular respiratory acidosis.
extracellular metabolic alkalosis has the opposite effect. Thus, cells participate in buffering extracellular acid and alkali loads.

Extracellular respiratory acidosis generally affects $\mathrm{pH}_{\mathrm{i}}$ in three phases. First, the increase in extracellular $\left[\mathrm{CO}_{2}\right]_{\text {dis }}$ creates an inwardly directed gradient for $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$. This dissolved gas rapidly enters the cell (Fig. 28-13A) and produces $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$ and $\mathrm{H}^{+}$. This intracellular respiratory acidosis manifests itself as a rapid fall in $\mathrm{pH}_{\mathrm{i}}$ (see Fig. 28-13B, phase A in lower panel). Carbonic anhydrase (1) 978-3 greatly accelerates the formation of $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$and $\mathrm{H}^{+}$from $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$, so that $\mathrm{pH}_{\mathrm{i}}$ can complete its decline in just a few seconds.

The cell recovers from this acid load, but only feebly (see Fig. 28-13B, phase B in lower panel) because the decrease in $\mathrm{pH}_{\mathrm{o}}$ inhibits acid extrusion and stimulates acid loading. Over a period of minutes, pH , may recover only partially if at all.

Finally, the extracellular respiratory acidosis stimulates the kidneys to upregulate acid-base transporters and thus stimulate urinary acid secretion (see p. 832). The net effect, over a period of hours or days, is an extracellular metabolic compensation that causes $\mathrm{pH}_{\mathrm{o}}$ to increase gradually (see Fig. 28-13B, phase C in upper panel). As $\mathrm{pH}_{\mathrm{o}}$ rises, it gradually relieves the inhibition of NBCn1 and NHE1 and other acid extruders and cancels the stimulation of $\mathrm{Cl}-\mathrm{HCO}_{3}$ exchange and other acid loaders. Thus, intracellular pH recovers in parallel with extracellular pH , albeit by only $20 \%$ to $60 \%$ as
much as $\mathrm{pH}_{\mathrm{o}}$ (see Fig. 28-13B, phase C in lower panel). The pH , recovery represents an intracellular metabolic compensation to the intracellular respiratory acidosis.

Not all cell types have the same resting $\mathrm{pH}_{\mathrm{i}}$. Moreover, different cell types often have very different complements of acid-base transporters and very different ways of regulating these transporters. Nevertheless, the example in Figure 28-13 illustrates that the fate of $\mathrm{pH}_{\mathrm{i}}$ is closely intertwined with that of interstitial fluid, and thus blood plasma. During respiratory acid-base disturbances, the lungs generate the insult, and virtually every other cell in the body must defend itself against it. In the case of metabolic acidbase disturbances, however, some cells may generate the insult, whereas the others attempt to defend themselves against it. From a teleological perspective, one can imagine that the primary reason that the body regulates the pH of the blood plasma and extracellular fluids is to allow the cells to properly regulate their $\mathrm{pH}_{\mathrm{i}}$. The primary reason why the clinical assessment of blood acid-base parameters can be useful is that these parameters tend to parallel cellular acidbase status.

# TRANSPORT OF OXYGEN AND CARBON DIOXIDE IN THE BLOOD 

Walter F. Boron

## CARRIAGE OF $\mathrm{O}_{2}$

## The amount of $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ dissolved in blood is far too small to meet the metabolic demands of the body

Blood carries oxygen in two forms. More than $98 \%$ of the $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ normally binds to hemoglobin within the erythrocytes, also known as red blood cells or RBCs (see pp. 434-435). A tiny fraction physically dissolves in the aqueous phases of both blood plasma and the cytoplasm of blood cells (predominantly RBCs). What is the significance of the $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ that is bound to hemoglobin?

Imagine that we expose a liter of blood plasma, initially free of $\mathrm{O}_{2}$, to an atmosphere having the same $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ as alveolar air -100 mm Hg . Oxygen will move from the atmosphere to the plasma until an equilibrium is established, at which time the concentration of dissolved $\mathrm{O}_{2}\left(\left[\mathrm{O}_{2}\right]_{\text {dis }}\right)$ in the blood obeys Henry's law (see p. 593):

$$
\left[\mathrm{O}_{2}\right]_{\text {dis }}=k_{\mathrm{O}_{2}} \cdot \mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}
$$

If we express $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ in units of mm Hg at $37^{\circ} \mathrm{C}$ and $\left[\mathrm{O}_{2}\right]_{\text {dis }}$ in units of ( mL of $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ gas)/( dL of blood), then the solubility $\mathrm{k}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ is $\sim 0.003 \mathrm{~mL} \mathrm{O}_{2} /(\mathrm{dL}$ of blood $\cdot \mathrm{mm} \mathrm{Hg})$. For arterial blood,

$$
\begin{aligned}
{\left[\mathrm{O}_{2}\right]_{\text {dis }} } & =\frac{0.003 \mathrm{~mL} \mathrm{O}_{2}}{100 \mathrm{~mL} \text { blood } \cdot \mathrm{mmHg}} \cdot 100 \mathrm{mmHg} \\
& =0.3 \mathrm{~mL} \mathrm{O}_{2} / 100 \mathrm{~mL} \text { blood }
\end{aligned}
$$

Is such an $\mathrm{O}_{2}$-carrying capacity adequate for supplying $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ to the systemic tissues? If these tissues could extract all the $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ dissolved in arterial blood so that no $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ remained in venous blood, and if cardiac output (see p. 414) were $5000 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{min}$, then-according to the Fick principle (see p. 423)-the delivery of dissolved $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ to the tissues would be

$$
\begin{aligned}
& \overbrace{\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}}^{\mathrm{O}_{2}}=\overbrace{\frac{5000 \mathrm{~mL} \text { blood }}{m \mathrm{~m}}}^{\text {Blood flow }} \cdot \overbrace{\frac{0.3 \mathrm{~mL} \mathrm{O}_{2}}{100 \mathrm{~mL} \text { blood }}}^{\text {O2 }} \\
& =15 \mathrm{~mL} \mathrm{O}_{2} / \mathrm{min}
\end{aligned}
$$

However, the average $70-\mathrm{kg}$ human at rest consumes $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ at the rate of $\sim 250 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{min}$. Dissolved $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ could supply the body's metabolic demands only if cardiac output increased by a factor of $250 / 15$, or nearly 17 -fold! Thus, the body cannot rely on dissolved $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ as a mechanism for $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ carriage.

## Hemoglobin consists of two $\alpha$ and two $\beta$ subunits, each of which has an iron-containing "heme" and a polypeptide "globin"

Normal adult hemoglobin (Hb) is a tetramer having a molecular weight of $\sim 68 \mathrm{kDa}$, each monomer consisting of a heme and a globin (Fig. 29-1A). The heme is a porphyrin compound coordinated to a single iron atom. The globin is a polypeptide, either an $\alpha$ chain ( 141 amino acids) or a $\beta$ chain ( 146 amino acids). The homology between the $\alpha$ and $\beta$ chains is sufficient that they have similar conformations, a series of seven helices enveloping a single heme. Thus, the complete Hb molecule has the stoichiometry $[\alpha(\text { heme })]_{2}[\beta(\text { heme })]_{2}$ and can bind as many as four $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ molecules, one for each iron atom. The erythroblasts (see pp. 431-433) that synthesize Hb closely coordinate the production of $\alpha$ chains, $\beta$ chains, and heme.

Heme is a general term for a metal ion chelated to a porphyrin ring. In the case of Hb , the metal is iron in the $\mathrm{Fe}^{2+}$ or ferrous state (see Fig. 29-1B). The porphyrin consists of four linked pyrrole rings that, through their nitrogen atoms, coordinate a single, centrally located $\mathrm{Fe}^{2+}$. Because the ironporphyrin complex is rich in conjugated double bonds, it absorbs photons of relatively low energy (i.e., light in the visible range). The interaction among $\mathrm{O}_{2} \mathrm{Fe}^{2+}$, and porphyrin causes the complex to have a red color when fully saturated with $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ (e.g., arterial blood) and a purple color when devoid of $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ (e.g., venous blood).

Hb can bind $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ only when the iron is in the $\mathrm{Fe}^{2+}$ state. The $\mathrm{Fe}^{2+}$ in Hb can become oxidized to ferric iron $\left(\mathrm{Fe}^{3+}\right)$, either spontaneously or under the influence of compounds such as nitrites or sulfonamides. The result of such an oxidation is methemoglobin (metHb), which is incapable of binding $\mathrm{O}_{2}$. Inside the RBC , a heme-containing enzyme methemoglobin reductase uses the reduced form of nicotinamide adenine dinucleotide (NADH) to reduce metHb back to Hb , so that only about $1.5 \%$ of total Hb is in the metHb state. In the rare case in which a genetic defect results in a deficiency of this enzyme, N29-1 metHb may

# N29-1 Methemoglobinemia 

## Contributed by Walter Boron

An increase in the amount of Hb with its iron in the oxidized or $\mathrm{Fe}^{3+}$ (i.e., ferric) state is known as methemoglobinemia. As discussed on pages 647-648, the problem that arises is that, with $\mathrm{Fe}^{3+}$ in the porphyrin ring of $\mathrm{Hb}, \mathrm{O}_{2}$ cannot bind, and there is a consequent reduction in the $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ carrying capacity of the blood. The $\mathrm{Fe}^{2+}$ (i.e., ferrous) iron of Hb spontaneously oxidizes to $\mathrm{Fe}^{3+}$, and a family of methemoglobin reductase enzymes normally returns the Hb to the ferrous state.

Methemoglobinemia can arise in three ways:

1. Mutant $M$ forms of Hb. Normally the globin moiety of Hb cradles the porphyrin ring in such a way as to limit the accessibility of $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ to the $\mathrm{Fe}^{3+}$. In M forms of Hb (at least eight of which have been identified), point mutations in the $\alpha$ or $\beta$ globin chains allow the $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ to approach the $\mathrm{Fe}^{3+}$ more closely and to oxidize—rather than to bind to-the $\mathrm{Fe}^{3+}$.
This action shifts the balance between the normally slow rate of oxidation and reductase activity toward the formation of $\mathrm{Fe}^{3+}$.
2. Genetic deficiency (autosomal recessive) of one or both of the splice variants of methemoglobin reductase enzymes. The enzyme is reduced nicotinamide adenine dinucleotide (NADH)-cytochrome b5 reductase (cytb5: E.C.1.6.2.2). Two splice variants, differing in the N -terminal region, are present: (a) a soluble form with 275 amino acids, and (b) a membrane-bound form with 300 amino acids. The membrane-bound form is present mainly in the endoplasmic reticulum and mitochondrial outer membrane, where it is
important for a variety of reactions (i.e., synthesis of fatty acids and cholesterol, P-450-mediated drug metabolism). A deficiency in the soluble form of RBCs-usually caused by missense mutations that reduce enzyme stability-causes type I methemoglobinemia, which is usually not severe. A deficiency in the membrane-bound form causes type II methemoglobinemia, which can cause severe mental retardation and neurological problems. The type I disease can be treated with the reducing agents ascorbic acid and methylene blue, either singly or together.
3. Toxin-induced oxidation of the $\mathrm{Fe}^{3+}$ in Hb . Oxidation of the $\mathrm{Fe}^{3+}$ of Hb to $\mathrm{Fe}^{3+}$ can occur by three routes: (a) direct oxidation, promoted under hypoxic conditions; (b) indirect oxidation in the presence of bound $\mathrm{O}_{2}$, a mechanism that is important in the methemoglobinemia produced by nitrite; and (c) drug-induced oxidation, in which drug metabolites of compounds (e.g., aminobenzenes and nitrobenzenes) promote the oxidation.

## REFERENCES

Percy MJ, McFerran NV, Lappin TRJ: Disorders of oxidized haemoglobin. Blood Rev 19:61-68, 2005.
Prchal JT, Borgese N, Moore MR, et al: Congenital methemoglobinemia due to methemoglobin reductase deficiency in two unrelated American Black families. Am J Med 89:516-522, 1990.

![bo75465312c5](bo75465312c5.jpg)

Figure 29-1 Structure of hemoglobin.
represent $25 \%$ or more of the total Hb . Such a deficiency results in a decreased $\mathrm{O}_{2}$-carrying capacity, leading to tissue hypoxia.

The environment provided by the globin portion of Hb is crucial for the $\mathrm{O}_{2}$-heme interaction. To be useful, this interaction must be fully reversible under physiological conditions, allowing repetitive capture and release of $\mathrm{O}_{2}$. The interaction of $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ with free $\mathrm{Fe}^{2+}$ normally produces $\mathrm{Fe}^{3+}$, the simplest example of which is rust. Even with isolated heme, $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ irreversibly oxidizes $\mathrm{Fe}^{2+}$ to $\mathrm{Fe}^{3+}$. However, when heme is part of Hb , interactions with $\sim 20$ amino acids cradle the heme in the globin, so that $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ loosely and reversibly binds to $\mathrm{Fe}^{2+}$. The crucial residue is a histidine that bonds to the $\mathrm{Fe}^{2+}$ and donates negative charge that stabilizes the $\mathrm{Fe}^{2+}-\mathrm{O}_{2}$ complex. This histidine is also crucial for transmitting, to the rest of the Hb tetramer, the information that an $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ molecule is or is not bound to the $\mathrm{Fe}^{2+}$. When all four hemes are devoid of $\mathrm{O}_{2}$, each of the four histidines pulls its $\mathrm{Fe}^{2+}$ above the plane of its porphyrin ring by $\sim 0.06 \mathrm{~nm}$ (the blue conformation in Fig. 29-1C), distorting the porphyrin ring. Thus, the $\mathrm{Fe}^{2+}$-histidine bond is under strain in deoxyhemoglobin, a strain that it transmits to the rest of the $\alpha$ or $\beta$ subunit, and thence to the rest of the Hb molecule. The various components of the Hb tetramer are so tightly
interlinked, as if by a snugly fitting system of levers and joints, that no one subunit can leave this tensed (T) state unless they all leave it together. Because the shape of the heme in the T state sterically inhibits the approach of $\mathrm{O}_{2}$, empty Hb has a very low affinity for $\mathrm{O}_{2}$.

When one $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ binds to one of the $\mathrm{Fe}^{2+}$ atoms, the $\mathrm{Fe}^{2+}$ tends to move down into the plane of the porphyrin ring. If the $\mathrm{Fe}^{2+}$ actually could move, it would flatten the ring and relieve the strain on the $\mathrm{Fe}^{2+}$-histidine bond. When enough $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ molecules bind, enough energy builds up and all four subunits of the Hb simultaneously snap into the relaxed (R) state, whether or not they are bound to $\mathrm{O}_{2}$. In this R state, with its flattened heme, the Hb molecule has an $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ affinity that is $\sim 150$-fold greater than that in the T state. Thus, when $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ is zero, all Hb molecules are in the T state and have a low $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ affinity. When $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ is very high, all Hb molecules are in the R state and have a high $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ affinity. At intermediate $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ values, an equilibrium exists between Hb molecules in the T and R states.

Myoglobin (Mb) is another heme-containing, $\mathrm{O}_{2}$-binding protein that is specific for muscle (see p. 249). The globin portion of Mb arose in a gene duplication event from a primordial globin. Additional duplications along the non-Mb branch of the globin family led first to the $\alpha$ and $\beta$ chains of

# BOX 29-1 Forms of Hemoglobin 

The normal adult form of hemoglobin $\left(\alpha_{1} \beta_{1}\right)$, known as HbA, is only one of several normal forms that are present in prenatal or postnatal life. Some of these other hemoglobins contain naturally occurring $\alpha$-like chains (e.g., $\alpha$ and $\zeta$ ) or $\beta$-like chains (e.g., $\beta, \gamma, \delta$, and $\varepsilon$ ), whereas others reflect posttranslational modifications. Three genes for $\boldsymbol{\alpha}$-like chains (all encoding 141 amino acids) cluster on chromosome 16: At the $5^{\prime}$ end of the cluster is one gene for a $\zeta$ chain, followed by two for the $\alpha$ chain. Pseudogenes for $\zeta$ and $\alpha$ are also present. Five genes for $\beta$-like chains (all encoding 146 amino acids) are clustered on chromosome 11: Starting at the $5^{\prime}$ end is one for $\varepsilon$, followed by two for $\gamma$ ( $\gamma_{2}$, encoding for glycine at position $136, \gamma_{4}$, for alanine), and one each for $\delta$ and $\beta$. A pseudogene for $\beta$ is also present. A locus control region regulates the expression of these $\beta$-like chains during development (see pp. 80-81).

The four prenatal hemoglobins (Table 29-1) consist of various combinations of two $\alpha$-like chains (e.g., $\alpha$ and $\zeta$ ) and two $\beta$-like chains (e.g., $\varepsilon$ and $\gamma$ ). Very early in life, when erythropoiesis occurs in the yolk sac, the hemoglobin products are the three embryonic hemoglobins. When erythropoiesis shifts to the liver and spleen at $\sim 10$ weeks' gestation, the hemoglobin product is fetal hemoglobin, or HbF ( $\alpha_{1} \gamma_{2}$ ). Erythrocytes containing HbF have a higher $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ affinity than those containing HbA , owing to special properties of $\gamma$ chains. The newborn's blood contains both HbA and HbF ; the latter gradually falls by 1 year of age to the minute levels that are characteristic of the adult (rarely $>1 \%$ to $2 \%$ of total hemoglobin). With severe stress to the erythroid system-such as marked hemolysis (see p. 429), bone marrow failure, or recovery from bone marrow transplantation-immature erythroid precursors may be forced to mature before they have differentiated sufficiently to produce HbA. In these conditions, circulating levels of HbF may increase considerably. In some hereditary cases, normal HbF persists in the adult, with no clinically significant consequences.

Even adult blood contains several normal minor-component hemoglobins (Table 29-2), which account for $5 \%$ to $10 \%$ of the total blood hemoglobin. In $\mathrm{HbA}_{1}$ ( $\sim 2.5 \%$ of total hemoglobin), $\delta$ chains replace the $\beta$ chains of HbA . Although the physiological significance of $\mathrm{HbA}_{2}$ is unknown, the $\delta$ chains reduce the sickling of sickle hemoglobin (see below). Three other minor-component hemoglobins are the result of the nonenzymatic glycosylation of $\mathrm{HbA}, \mathrm{HbA}_{1 \alpha}, \mathrm{HbA}_{1 \varepsilon}$, and $\mathrm{HbA}_{1 \gamma}$ form when intracellular glucose-8-phosphate reacts with the terminal amino groups of the $\beta$ chains of HbA . In poorly controlled diabetes mellitus, a disease characterized by decreased insulin or insulin sensitivity (see Box 51-5), blood glucose concentrations rise and, with them, intracellular concentrations of glucose-6-phosphate. As a result, glycosylated hemoglobins may represent $10 \%$ or more of the total hemoglobin. Because hemoglobin glycosylation is irreversible, and because the RBC has a mean lifetime of 120 days, levels of these glycosylated hemoglobins are clinically useful for assessing the long-term control of blood glucose levels in diabetics.

Numerous abnormal hemoglobins exist, most of which are caused by single-amino-acid substitutions on one of the polypeptide chains. One of the most clinically important is HbS , or sickle hemoglobin, in which a valine replaces the glutamate normally present at position 6 of the $\beta$ chain. Although oxygenated HbS has a normal solubility, deoxygenated HbS is only about half as soluble as deoxygenated HbA. As a result, in low- $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ environments, HbS can crystallize into long fibers, giving the cells a sickle-like appearance. The sickled erythrocytes may disrupt blood flow in small vessels, causing many of the acute symptoms of "sickle cell crisis," including pain, renal dysfunction, retinal bleeding, and aseptic necrosis of bone. In addition, sickle cells are prone to hemolysis (mean lifetime, <20 days), which leads to a chronic hemolytic anemia.

| Hb | $\alpha$-LIKE SUBUNIT | $\beta$-LIKE SUBUNIT | TIME OF EXPRESSION |
| :--: | :--: | :--: | :--: |
| Gower 1 | $\zeta$ | $\varepsilon$ | Embryonic |
| Gower 2 | $\alpha$ | $\varepsilon$ | Embryonic |
| Portland | $\zeta$ | $\gamma$ | Embryonic |
| HbF (fetal) | $\alpha$ | $\gamma$ | Fetal |
| $\mathrm{HbA}_{2}$ | $\alpha$ | $\delta$ | Postnatal |
| HbA (adult) | $\alpha$ | $\beta$ | Postnatal |

Hb , and then to other $\alpha$-like and $\beta$-like chains (Box 29-1). Mb functions as a monomer, homologous to either an $\alpha$ or a $\beta$ chain of Hb . Although capable of binding only a single $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ molecule, Mb has a much higher $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ affinity than Hb . In the capillaries, Hb can thus hand off $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ to an Mb inside a muscle cell; this Mb then transfers its $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ to the next Mb , and so on, which speeds diffusion of $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ through the muscle cell. Because of the low solubility of $\mathrm{O}_{2}$, this action is essential: There is insufficient dissolved $\mathrm{O}_{2}$, by itself, to establish an intracellular $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ gradient large enough to deliver adequate $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ to mitochondria.

## The $\mathrm{Hb}-\mathrm{O}_{2}$ dissociation curve has a sigmoidal shape because of cooperativity among the four subunits of the Hb molecule

Imagine that we expose whole blood (see pp. 429-431) to a gas phase with a $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ that we can set at any one of several values (Fig. 29-2). For example, we could incubate the blood with a $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ of 40 mm Hg , typical of mixed-venous blood, and centrifuge a sample to separate plasma from erythrocytes, as one would for determining hematocrit (see pp. 102 and 429). Next, we could individually determine the $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ content of the plasma (i.e., dissolved $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ ) and packed RBCs. If we know how much water is inside the RBCs, we can subtract the amount

The brown curve is the sum of the red and purple curves. The $y$-axis on the left, which pertains only to the red curve, gives the percentage of Hb saturation or $\mathrm{S}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$.

Repeating this exercise over a range of $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ values, we obtain the red curve in Figure 29-3. The right-hand y-axis gives the $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ bound to Hb in the units $\left(\mathrm{mL} \mathrm{O}_{2}\right) /(\mathrm{dL}$ blood). The left-hand $y$-axis gives the same data in terms of percent $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ saturation of $\mathrm{Hb}\left(\mathbf{S}_{\mathbf{O}_{2}}\right.$ or "Sat"). To compute $\mathrm{S}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$, we need to know the maximal amount of $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ that can bind to Hb at extremely high $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ values. Expressed in terms of grams of

Hb protein, this $\mathbf{O}_{2}$ capacity is $\sim 1.39 \mathrm{~mL} \mathrm{O}_{2} / \mathrm{g} \mathrm{Hb}$ —assuming that no metHb is present. In real life, the $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ capacity may be closer to $1.35 \mathrm{~mL} \mathrm{O}_{2} / \mathrm{g} \mathrm{Hb}$ because $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ cannot bind to Hb that either is in the $\mathrm{Fe}^{3+}$ state (e.g., MetHb) or, as discussed below, is bound to carbon monoxide. We can translate this $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ capacity to a value for the maximal amount of $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ that can bind to Hb in 100 mL of blood. If the Hb content is $15 \mathrm{~g} \mathrm{Hb} / \mathrm{dL}$ blood (i.e., normal for an adult male), then

$$
\begin{aligned}
& \text { Maximal } \mathrm{O}_{2} \text { bound to } \mathrm{Hb} \\
& \quad=\left(\mathrm{O}_{2} \text { capacity of } \mathrm{Hb}\right) \times(\mathrm{Hb} \text { content of blood }) \\
& \quad=\frac{1.35 \mathrm{~mL} \mathrm{O}_{2}}{\mathrm{~g} \mathrm{Hb}} \cdot \frac{15 \mathrm{~g} \mathrm{Hb}}{\mathrm{dL} \text { blood }} \\
& \quad=\sim 20.3\left(\mathrm{~mL} \mathrm{O}_{2}\right) / \mathrm{dL} \text { blood }
\end{aligned}
$$

The percent saturation of Hb is
\% Saturation of $\mathrm{Hb}=\frac{\mathrm{O}_{2}$ actually bound to Hb $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ capacity of Hb $\cdot 100$
Notice that the curve in Figure 29-3 is sigmoidal or S-shaped, owing to the cooperativity among the four $\mathrm{O}_{2^{-}}$ binding sites on the Hb molecule. At low $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ values, increases in $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ produce relatively small increases in $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ binding, which reflects the relatively low $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ affinity of Hb in the T state. At moderate $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ values, the amount of bound $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ increases more steeply with increases in $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$, which reflects the increased $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ affinity as more Hb molecules shift to the R state. The $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ at which the Hb is half saturated is known as the $\mathbf{P}_{98}$. Finally, the $\mathrm{Hb}-\mathrm{O}_{2}$ versus $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ curve flattens at high $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ values as the Hb saturates. The difference in Hb saturation at low versus high $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ values is the basis for an important clinical tool, the pulse oximeter (Box 29-2).

At the $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ prevailing in normal arterial blood $\left(\mathrm{Pa}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}\right)$ approximately 100 mm Hg -the Hb saturation $\left(\mathrm{Sa}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}\right)$ is $\sim 97.5 \%$ or $19.7 \mathrm{~mL} \mathrm{O}_{2} / \mathrm{dL}$ bound to Hb (Table 29-3). The dissolved $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ (purple curve in Fig. 29-3) would add an additional $0.3 \mathrm{~mL} \mathrm{O}_{2} / \mathrm{dL}$ for a total $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ content of $20.0 \mathrm{~mL} \mathrm{O}_{2} / \mathrm{dL}$ (point $a$ on the brown curve in Fig. 29-3). In mixed-venous blood, in which $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}\left(\mathrm{PV}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}\right)$ is $\sim 40 \mathrm{~mm} \mathrm{Hg}$, the Hb saturation $\left(\mathrm{SV}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}\right)$ is $\sim 75 \%$ or $15.2 \mathrm{~mL} \mathrm{O}_{2} / \mathrm{dL}$ bound to Hb (see Table 29-3). The dissolved $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ would add 0.1 mL $\mathrm{O}_{2} / \mathrm{dL}$ for a total of $15.3 \mathrm{~mL} \mathrm{O}_{2} / \mathrm{dL}$ (point $\bar{v}$ in Fig. 29-3). The difference in total $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ content between points $a$ and $\bar{v}$, the $\mathrm{a}-\overline{\mathrm{v}}$ difference, is the amount of $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ that the lungs add to the blood in the pulmonary capillaries, which is the same amount that all the tissues extract from the blood in the systemic capillaries:

$$
\begin{aligned}
& \frac{\mathrm{a}-\overline{\mathrm{v}}}{\text { difference for total } \mathrm{O}_{2} \text { content }} \quad \frac{\mathrm{O}_{2} \text { content of arterial blood }}{\frac{20.0 \mathrm{~mL} \mathrm{O}_{2}}{\mathrm{dL} \text { blood }}} \cdot \frac{\mathrm{O}_{2} \text { content of mixed-venous blood }}{\frac{15.3 \mathrm{~mL} \mathrm{O}_{2}}{\mathrm{dL} \text { blood }}} \quad \frac{\overline{29-6)}}{29-6} \\
& \quad=\frac{4.7 \mathrm{~mL} \mathrm{O}_{2}}{\mathrm{dL} \text { blood }}
\end{aligned}
$$

Of the total a- $\overline{\mathrm{v}}$ difference of $4.7 \mathrm{~mL} \mathrm{O}_{2} / \mathrm{dL}, \mathrm{Hb}$ provides $4.5 \mathrm{~mL} \mathrm{O}_{2} / \mathrm{dL}$ or nearly $96 \%$ of the $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ that the lungs add and

# BOX 29-2 Measuring the Oxygen Saturation of Hb Clinically: The Pulse Oximeter 

The different colors of venous and arterial blood reflect difference in light absorbance between oxygenated and deoxygenated Hb . Clinicians now routinely exploit these differences to obtain simple, noninvasive measurements of the arterial $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ saturation $\left(\mathrm{Sa}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}\right)$ of Hb in patients. The pulse oximeter has a probe that one attaches to the ear, finger, or any part of the body at which pulsating blood vessels are accessible externally. On one side of the pulsating vascular bed, the pulse oximeter shines red and infrared light; on the other side, it detects the light transmitted through the bed, and calculates absorbances. These total absorbances have two components: (1) a nonpulsatile component that arises from stationary tissues, including blood inside capillaries and veins; and (2) a pulsatile component that arises from blood inside arterioles and arteries. The difference between the total and nonpulsatile absorbance is thus the pulsatile component, which represents only arterial or oxygenated blood. Because oxygenated and deoxygenated Hb absorb red and infrared light differently, the pulse oximeter can calculate $\mathrm{Sa}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ from the ratio of pulsatile light absorbed at the two wavelengths. The pulse oximeter accomplishes this magic by using a sophisticated microprocessor and software to produce
results that strongly agree with those provided by blood-gas analysis of a sample of arterial blood.

The pulse oximeter measures $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ saturation in arterial blood. Because systemic capillaries and veins do not pulsate, they do not contribute to the measurement. Thus, a patient with peripheral cyanosis (e.g., purple fingertips caused by cold-induced vasoconstriction) may have a perfectly normal "central" (i.e., arterial) $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ saturation. It is worth noting that pulse oximetry cannot detect CO poisoning because the absorbance spectra of $\mathrm{Hb}-\mathrm{CO}$ and $\mathrm{Hb}-\mathrm{O}_{2}$ are similar.

Health professionals widely employ pulse oximetry in hospitalized patients, particularly those in intensive care units, where continuous monitoring of $\mathrm{Sa}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ is critical. These patients include those on mechanical ventilators and others, less severely ill, who have some degree of respiratory compromise. Pulse oximetry has also become popular as an outpatient tool for assessing the presence of hypoxemia during sleep and thus screening for sleep apnea (Box 32-5). Because of the insidious nature of hypoxia, pilots of light aircraft have begun to use pulse oximeters to detect developing hypoxia at high altitudes.
