the systemic tissues extract from blood (see Table 29-3). Is this a- $\overline{\mathrm{v}}$ difference in $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ content enough to satisfy the metabolic demands of the body (i.e., $\sim 250 \mathrm{~mL} \mathrm{O}_{2} / \mathrm{min}$ )? Using the Fick principle, as we did in Equation 29-3, we see that the combination of a cardiac output of $5 \mathrm{~L} / \mathrm{min}$ and an $\mathrm{a}-\overline{\mathrm{v}}$ difference of $4.7 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{dL}$ would be nearly adequate:
![bo78465312c5](bo78465312c5.jpg)

$$
=\sim 235 \mathrm{~mL} \mathrm{O}_{2} / \mathrm{min}
$$

By either increasing cardiac output by $\sim 6 \%$ or decreasing the $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ of mixed-venous blood, the body could meet a demand of $250 \mathrm{~mL} \mathrm{O}_{2} / \mathrm{min}$. We spend our lives moving endlessly from point $a$ in Figure 29-3 to point $\overline{\mathrm{v}}$ (as we deliver $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ to the tissues) and then back to point $a$ (as we take up more $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ from alveolar air).

Because the plot of $\left[\mathrm{O}_{2}\right]_{\text {dis }}$ versus $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ is linear (see Fig. 29-3, purple curve), the amount of $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ that can dissolve in blood plasma has no theoretical maximum. Thus, breathing $100 \% \mathrm{O}_{2}$ would raise arterial $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ by $\sim 6$-fold, so that $\sim 1.8 \mathrm{~mL}$ of $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ would be dissolved in each deciliter of arterial blood.

Although dissolved $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ would make a correspondingly greater contribution to overall $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ carriage under such unphysiological conditions, Hb would still carry the vast majority of $\mathrm{O}_{2}$. Hence, a decrease in the Hb content of the blood-known as anemia-can markedly reduce $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ carriage. The body can compensate for decreased Hb content in the same two ways that, in the above example, we increased the $\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ from 235 to $250 \mathrm{~mL} \mathrm{O}_{2} / \mathrm{min}$. First, it can increase cardiac output. Second, it can increase $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ extraction, thereby reducing mixed-venous $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ content. Anemia leads to pallor of the mucous membranes and skin, reflecting the decrease in the red Hb pigment. Impaired $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ delivery may cause lethargy and fatigue. The accompanying increase in cardiac output may manifest itself as palpitations and a systolic murmur. Shortness of breath may also be a part of the syndrome.

If decreased Hb levels are detrimental, then increasing Hb content should increase the maximal $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ content and thus provide a competitive advantage for athletes. Even in normal individuals, $[\mathrm{Hb}]$ in RBC cytoplasm is already extremely high (see p. 434). Hypoxia (e.g., adaptation to high altitude) leads to the increased production of erythropoietin (see pp. 431-433), 19-2 a hormone that somewhat increases the amount of Hb per erythrocyte, but especially increases their number. Indeed, a few instances have been highly publicized in the international press in which elite athletes have

# N29-2 Interactions of $\mathrm{H}^{+}$with Hemoglobin 

## Contributed by Emile Boulpaep and Walter Boron

Like most proteins, Hb has many titratable groups. However, the ones that are most important physiologically are the ones whose pK values are near the physiological pH . For a general discussion of buffers, consult the passage beginning on pages 628-633. Of particular interest is the passage dealing with buffers in a closed system (see pp. 630-633) and Figure 28-2B.

Several titratable groups in the Hb molecule contribute to the pH -Bohr effect. The most important single group is the histidine at residue 146 of the $\beta$ chains. When the Hb molecule is fully deoxygenated (tensed state), the protonated His-146 forms a salt bridge with the negatively charged aspartate group at position 94 of the same $\beta$ chain. This salt bridge stabilizes the protonated form of His-146, so that it has a high affinity for $\mathrm{H}^{+}$(i.e., a relatively high pK value of $\sim 8.0$ ).

When the Hb becomes fully oxygenated (relaxed state), the twisting of the Hb molecule disrupts the salt bridge and thus destabilizes the protonated form of His-146, so that it has a low affinity for $\mathrm{H}^{+}$and thus a relatively low $\mathrm{p} K$ value of $\sim 7.1$. This $\mathrm{p} K$ is roughly the same as the pH inside the RBC. According to Equation 28-5, the ratio $\left[\mathrm{R}-\mathrm{NH}_{4}^{+}\right] /\left[\mathrm{R}-\mathrm{NH}_{3}\right]$ must be $1: 1$ (so that in 50 out of every 100 Hb molecules, this group is protonated).

Now let us fully deoxygenate this Hb and return the pK of His-146 to where it was at the beginning of this example, $\sim 8.0$.

The pH inside the erythrocyte remains at 7.1. Equation 28-5 tells us that the ratio $\left[\mathrm{R}-\mathrm{NH}_{4}^{+}\right] /\left[\mathrm{R}-\mathrm{NH}_{3}\right]$ will be $8: 1$. That is, in 89 of every 100 Hb molecules, this His-146 group is protonated. Thus, for every 100 Hb molecules, $39 \mathrm{H}^{+}$ions have been taken up from the solution to titrate $39 \mathrm{R}-\mathrm{NH}_{3}$ groups to form 39 additional $\mathrm{R}-\mathrm{NH}_{4}^{+}$groups at His-146.

The above is an extreme example, because the Hb does not become fully deoxygenated in the systemic capillaries. It is also a simplified example, inasmuch as multiple residues in Hb contribute to the pH -Bohr effect. Nevertheless, the example illustrates how deoxygenated Hb is better able to buffer excess protons.

In the above paragraphs, we have been emphasizing one side of the physiological coin: deoxygenation makes Hb a weaker acid (and thus causes Hb to take up $\mathrm{H}^{+}$). However, as summarized by the reaction in Equation 29-9,

$$
\mathrm{Hb}\left(\mathrm{O}_{2}\right)_{4}+2 \mathrm{H}^{+} \rightleftarrows \mathrm{Hb}\left(\mathrm{H}^{+}\right)_{2}+4 \mathrm{O}_{2}
$$

( $\mathbf{N E} \mathbf{2 9 - 1}$ )
the converse is also true: the protonation of Hb lowers Hb's affinity for $\mathrm{O}_{2}$.

infused themselves with erythrocytes or injected themselves with recombinant erythropoietin. However, an excessive increase in hematocrit-polycythemia-has the adverse effect of increasing blood viscosity and thus vascular resistance (see pp. 437-439). The consequences include increased blood pressure in both the systemic and pulmonary circulations and a mismatch of ventilation to perfusion within the lung. Such a ventilation-perfusion mismatch leads to hypoxia (see p. 693), and thus desaturation of arterial Hb. Thus, the optimal hematocrit-presumably $\sim 45 \%$-is one that achieves a high maximal $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ content, but at a reasonable blood viscosity.

The purplish color of desaturated Hb produces the physical sign known as cyanosis, a purplish coloration of the skin and mucous membranes. Cyanosis results not from the absence of saturated or oxygenated Hb , but from the presence of desaturated Hb . Thus, an anemic patient with poorly saturated Hb might have too little unsaturated Hb for it to manifest as cyanosis. The physician's ability to detect cyanosis also depends on other factors, such as the subject's skin pigmentation and the lighting conditions for the physical examination.

## Increases in temperature, $\left[\mathrm{CO}_{2}\right]$, and $\left[\mathrm{H}^{+}\right]$, all of which are characteristic of metabolically active tissues, cause Hb to dump $\mathrm{O}_{2}$

Metabolically active tissues not only have a high demand for $\mathrm{O}_{2}$, they also are warm, produce large amounts of $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$, and are acidic. Indeed, high temperature, high $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$, and low pH of metabolically active tissues all decrease the $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ affinity of Hb by acting at nonheme sites to shift the equilibrium between the T and R states of Hb more toward the lowaffinity T state. The net effect is that metabolically active tissues can signal Hb in the systemic capillaries to release more $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ than usual, whereas less active tissues can signal Hb to release less. In the pulmonary capillaries-where temperature is lower than in active tissues, $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ is relatively low and pH is high-these same properties promote $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ uptake by Hb .

Temperature Increasing the temperature causes the $\mathrm{Hb}-\mathrm{O}_{2}$ dissociation curve to shift to the right, whereas decreasing the temperature has the opposite effect (Fig. 29-4). Comparing the three $\mathrm{Hb}-\mathrm{O}_{2}$ dissociation curves in Figure 29-4 at the $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ of mixed-venous blood ( 40 mm Hg ), we see that the amount of $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ bound to Hb becomes progressively less at higher temperatures. In other words, high temperature decreases the $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ affinity of Hb and leads to release of $\mathrm{O}_{2}$. One mechanism of this temperature effect may be small shifts in the $\mathrm{p} K$ values of various amino-acid side chains, which cause shifts in net charge and thus a conformational change.

The maximal temperature achieved in active muscle is $\sim 40^{\circ} \mathrm{C}$. Of course, very low temperatures can prevail in the skin of extremities exposed to extreme cold.

Acid In 1904, Christian Bohr, a physiologist and father of atomic physicist Niels Bohr, observed that respiratory acidosis (see p. 633) shifts the $\mathrm{Hb}-\mathrm{O}_{2}$ dissociation curve to the right (Fig. 29-5A). This decrease in $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ affinity has come to be known as the Bohr effect. A mild respiratory acidosis
![bo79465312c5](bo79465312c5.jpg)

Figure 29-4 Effect of temperature changes on the $\mathrm{Hb}-\mathrm{O}_{2}$ dissociation curve.
occurs physiologically as erythrocytes enter the systemic capillaries. There, the increase in extracellular $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ causes $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ to enter erythrocytes, which leads to a fall in intracellular pH (see p. 646). Other acidic metabolites may also lower extracellular and, therefore, intracellular pH . Thus, this intracellular respiratory acidosis has two componentsa decrease in pH and an increase in $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$. We now appreciate that both contribute to the rightward shift of the $\mathrm{Hb}-\mathrm{O}_{2}$ dissociation curve observed by Bohr.

The effect of acidosis per se on the $\mathrm{Hb}-\mathrm{O}_{2}$ dissociation curve (see Fig. 29-5B)-the $\mathbf{p H}$-Bohr effect-accounts for most of the overall Bohr effect. One can readily demonstrate the pH -Bohr effect in a solution of Hb by imposing a metabolic acidosis (e.g., decreasing pH at a fixed $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ ). It should not be surprising that Hb is sensitive to changes in pH because Hb is an outstanding $\mathrm{H}^{+}$buffer (see p. 630):

$$
\mathrm{Hb}+\mathrm{H}^{+} \rightleftharpoons \mathrm{Hb}-\mathrm{H}^{+}
$$

Although Hb has many titratable groups, the ones important here are those with $\mathrm{p} K$ values in the physiological pH range. (5) N29-2 As we acidify the solution, raising the ratio $\left[\mathrm{Hb}-\mathrm{H}^{+}\right] /[\mathrm{Hb}]$ for susceptible groups, we change the conformation of the Hb molecule, thus lowering its $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ affinity:

$$
\mathrm{Hb}\left(\mathrm{O}_{2}\right)_{4}+2 \mathrm{H}^{+} \rightleftharpoons \mathrm{Hb}\left(\mathrm{H}^{+}\right)_{2}+4 \mathrm{O}_{2}
$$

This is an extreme example in which we added enough $\mathrm{H}^{+}$ to cause Hb to dump all of its $\mathrm{O}_{2}$. Under more physiological conditions, the binding of $\sim 0.7$ mole of $\mathrm{H}^{+}$causes Hb to release 1 mole of $\mathrm{O}_{2}$. This property is important in the systemic tissues, where $\left[\mathrm{H}^{+}\right]$is high. The converse is also true: $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ binding causes a conformational change in the Hb molecule, which lowers the affinity of Hb for $\mathrm{H}^{+}$.

![bo80465312c5](bo80465312c5.jpg)

B EFFECT OF pH
![bo81465312c5](bo81465312c5.jpg)

Carbon Dioxide The isolated effect of hypercapnia per se on the $\mathrm{Hb}-\mathrm{O}_{2}$ dissociation curve (see Fig. 29-5C) represents a small portion of the overall Bohr effect. Demonstrating such a $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$-Bohr effect requires that we study the $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ affinity of Hb at a fixed pH , increasing $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ and $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$ proportionally-an example of isohydric hypercapnia (see p. 641). As $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ increases, $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ combines with unprotonated amino groups on $\mathrm{Hb}\left(\mathrm{Hb}-\mathrm{NH}_{2}\right)$ to form carbamino groups

Figure 29-5 Effect of acidosis and hypercapnia on the $\mathrm{Hb}-\mathrm{O}_{2}$ dissociation curve (Bohr effect).

C EFFECT OF $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$
![bo82465312c5](bo82465312c5.jpg)
![bo83465312c5](bo83465312c5.jpg)
(Hb-NH- $\mathrm{COO}^{-}$). (C) N29-3 Although Hb has other amino groups, only the four amino termini of the globin chains are susceptible to appreciable carbamino formation, the $\beta$ chains more so than the $\alpha$ chains. Because amino groups exist in a protonated form $\left(\mathrm{Hb}-\mathrm{NH}_{3}^{+}\right)$in equilibrium with an unprotonated form, the reaction of $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ with $\mathrm{Hb}-\mathrm{NH}_{2}$ tends to shift Hb away from the $\mathrm{Hb}-\mathrm{NH}_{3}^{+}$form and toward the $\mathrm{Hb}-\mathrm{NH}-\mathrm{COO}^{-}$form:

# N29-3 Interactions of $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ with Hemoglobin 

## Contributed by Emile Boulpaep and Walter Boron

As discussed on page $653, \mathrm{CO}_{2}$ can reversibly react with the four amino groups that constitute the four N termini of the globin chains-the two $\alpha$ chains and, especially, the two $\beta$ chains. As shown by Equation 29-10 in the text, the interaction of $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ with these amino termini causes the net charge on the residue to change from positive $(\mathrm{Hb}-\mathrm{NH})_{2}^{+}$to negative ( $\mathrm{Hb}-\mathrm{NH}-$ COO ). A consequence is the formation of salt bridgesamong several positively and negatively charged amino-acid residues-that tend to stabilize the deoxygenated (or tensed) form of Hb . Thus, in the systemic capillaries, the increased levels of $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ favor the deoxygenated form of Hb , which is tantamount to dumping $\mathrm{O}_{2}$. In other words, increased levels of $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ cause the $\mathrm{Hb}-\mathrm{O}_{2}$ dissociation curve to shift to the right (see Fig. 29-5C).

In the lungs, the converse takes place as the binding of $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ to Hb causes a twisting of the Hb molecule as it shifts from the tensed to the relaxed state. This twisting disrupts the aforementioned salt bridges and destabilizes carbamino. As a result, the Hb molecule dumps $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$, which exits the erythrocyte and diffuses into the alveolar air space.

![bo84465312c5](bo84465312c5.jpg)

The overall effect of carbamino formation is therefore a negative shift in the charge on one amino-acid side chain, causing a shift in the conformation of Hb and reducing its $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ affinity:

$$
\left(\mathrm{O}_{2}\right)_{4} \mathrm{Hb}-\mathrm{NH}_{3}^{+}+\mathrm{CO}_{2} \rightleftharpoons\left(\mathrm{O}_{2}\right)_{3} \mathrm{Hb}-\mathrm{NH}-\mathrm{COO}^{-}+\mathrm{O}_{2}+2 \mathrm{H}^{+}
$$

Thus, an increased $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ causes Hb to unload $\mathrm{O}_{2}$, which is important in the systemic tissues. Conversely, an increased $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ causes Hb to unload $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$, which is important in the lungs.

In conclusion, the $\mathrm{Hb}-\mathrm{O}_{2}$ dissociation curve shifts to the right under conditions prevailing in the capillaries of metabolically active systemic tissues-increased temperature (see Fig. 29-4), decreased pH (see Fig. 29-5B), and increased $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ (see Fig. 29-5C). These right shifts are synonymous with decreased $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ affinity. Thus, high metabolic rates promote the unloading of $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ from Hb . Clearly, blood cannot unload $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ unless the blood gets to the tissues. Indeed, in most systemic arterioles, local hypercapnia and acidosis also are powerful stimuli for vasodilation (see p. 556), enhancing $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ delivery to metabolically active tissues.

## 2,3-Diphosphoglycerate reduces the affinity of adult, but not of fetal, Hb

The affinity of Hb for $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ is very sensitive to the presence of the glycolytic metabolite 2,3-diphosphoglycerate (2,3DPG) (5) N29-4 and, to a lesser extent, organic phosphates such as ATP. The concentration of 2,3-DPG is about the same as that of Hb . Indeed, 2,3-DPG binds to Hb in a $1: 1$ stoichiometry, interacting with a central cavity formed by the two $\beta$ chains. At physiological $\mathrm{pH}, 2,3$-DPG has an average of $\sim 3.5$ negative charges, which interact with eight positively charged amino-acid residues in this central cavity. $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ binding, however, changes the shape of the central cavity, destabilizing DPG-bound Hb. As a result, deoxygenated Hb has a 100 -fold higher affinity for 2,3-DPG than does oxygenated Hb . Conversely, binding of 2,3-DPG to Hb destabilizes the interaction of Hb with $\mathrm{O}_{2}$, promoting the release of $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ :

$$
\mathrm{Hb}\left(\mathrm{O}_{2}\right)_{4}+2,3-\mathrm{DPG} \rightleftharpoons \mathrm{Hb}(2,3-\mathrm{DPG})+4 \mathrm{O}_{2}
$$

The result is a right shift in the $\mathrm{Hb}-\mathrm{O}_{2}$ dissociation curve (Fig. 29-6). This effect of 2,3-DPG on the $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ affinity of Hb is important both in hypoxia and in understanding the physiology of fetal Hb .

Decreasing the $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ of RBCs stimulates glycolysis, which leads to increased levels of 2,3-DPG. Indeed, chronic hypoxia, anemia, and acclimation to high altitude are all associated with an increase in 2,3-DPG levels and thus lower the $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ affinity of Hb . Reducing the affinity is a two-edged sword. At the relatively high $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ in alveoli, where the $\mathrm{Hb}-\mathrm{O}_{2}$ dissociation curve is fairly flat, this decrease in $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ affinity reduces $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ uptake-but only slightly. At the low $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ in systemic tissues, where the $\mathrm{Hb}-\mathrm{O}_{2}$ dissociation curve is steep, this
![bo85465312c5](bo85465312c5.jpg)

Figure 29-6 Effect of 2,3-DPG on $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ affinity of Hb . After lowlanders spent about 2 days at an altitude of $\sim 4500 \mathrm{~m}$, their cytosolic [2,3-DPG] increased by $\sim 50 \%$, shifting the $\mathrm{Hb}-\mathrm{O}_{2}$ dissociation curve to the right.
decrease in $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ affinity markedly increases the $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ release. The net effect is enhanced $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ unloading to metabolizing tissues, which is more important than $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ per se.

In Figure 29-7, the teal $\mathrm{Hb}-\mathrm{O}_{2}$ dissociation curve represents pure or "stripped" Hb (i.e., in the absence of $\mathrm{CO}_{2}, 2,3$ DPG, and other organic phosphates). The $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ affinity of pure Hb is quite high, as evidenced by the left shift of the curve. Adding only $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ (orange curve) or only 2,3-DPG (green curve) to the solution shifts the curve somewhat to the right, and adding both yields the brown curve, which is indistinguishable from the red curve for intact RBCs under physiological conditions.

The fetal hemoglobin (HbF) in fetal erythrocytes (see p. 649) has a higher $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ affinity than the Hb inside adult RBCs $(\mathrm{HbA})$. This difference is crucial for the fetus, whose blood must extract $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ from maternal blood in the placenta (see p. 1137). The difference in $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ affinities does not, however, reflect differences in $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ affinities of stripped $\mathrm{HbA}$ and HbF , which are nearly identical. The crucial difference is that the $\gamma$ chains of HbF bind 2,3-DPG less avidly than do the $\beta$ chains of HbA . With less 2,3-DPG bound, the dissociation curve of HbF is left-shifted, similar to the HbA curve labeled " $\mathrm{Hb}+\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ " in Figure 29-7.
$\mathrm{O}_{2}$ is not the only gas that can bind to the $\mathrm{Fe}^{3+}$ of Hb ; carbon monoxide (CO), nitric oxide (NO), and $\mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{~S}$ can also bind to Hb and snap it into the R state. (5) N29-5 In CO poisoning (see pp. 1224-1225), CO binds to Hb with an affinity that is $\sim 200$-fold greater than that of $\mathrm{O}_{2}$. Thus, the maximal $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ capacity falls to the extent that CO binds to Hb . However, the major reason that CO is toxic is that, as it snaps Hb into the R state, CO increases the $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ affinity of Hb and

# N29-4 2,3-DPG 

## Contributed by Emile Boulpaep and Walter Boron

2,3-Diphosphoglycerate (2,3-DPG), also known as 2,3bisphosphoglycerate (2,3-BPG), is an intermediate in one of the later reactions in glycolysis (Fig. 58-64). The overall reaction in question is 3-phosphoglycerate (3-PG) $\rightarrow$ 2-phosphoglycerate (2-PG), catalyzed by phosphoglycerate mutase (PGM):

$$
2-\mathrm{PG} \xrightarrow{\text { PGM }} 3-\mathrm{PG}
$$

The actual mechanism is interesting. A specific kinase initially activates the PGM by transferring a phosphate group from the 2 position of 2,3-DPG to a histidine residue of the enzyme, creating PGM-P and 3-PG:

In the actual mutase reaction, this activated enzyme transfers the phosphate group to 2-PG, creating 2,3-DPG:

$$
3-\mathrm{PG}+\mathrm{PGM}-\mathrm{P} \rightarrow 2,3-\mathrm{DPG}+\mathrm{PGM}
$$

Finally, the enzyme can accept a phosphate group from 2,3DPG, creating 3-PG:

$$
2,3-D P G+P G M \rightarrow 2-P G+P G M-P
$$

Thus, 2,3-DPG is a cofactor in the mutase reaction. It also is continuously formed and consumed.

# N29-5 Other Gases That Bind to Hemoglobin 

## Contributed by Arthur DuBois, Emile Boulpaep, and Walter Boron

The text discusses in some detail the interaction of $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ and $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ with Hb , briefly discusses the interaction of CO with Hb , and mentions the interactions of NO and $\mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{~S}$ with Hb .

## Carbon Monoxide

As noted in the text, the affinity of Hb for CO is $\sim 200$-fold greater than for $\mathrm{O}_{2}$. Thus, when a fully oxygenated Hb molecule$\mathrm{Hb}\left(\mathrm{O}_{2}\right)_{4}$-is exposed to even a low level of CO , a CO molecule displaces one of the $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ molecules from some of the Hb molecules, resulting in $\mathrm{Hb}\left(\mathrm{O}_{2}\right)_{2}(\mathrm{CO})$. If the only effect of CO poisoning were to render ineffective one of the four $\mathrm{O}_{2}$-binding sites, then the symptoms would be no worse than reducing the hematocrit by $25 \%$ (e.g., from $40 \%$ to $30 \%$ ) or perfusing the arteries with mixed-venous blood (which also has an $\mathrm{Hb}-\mathrm{O}_{2}$ saturation of $75 \%$ ). If fact, if a single CO molecule binds to $40 \%$ of the Hb molecules in the blood, the patient will experience symptoms, as outlined below. Why?

## Basis of CO Toxicity

CO is toxic for two reasons. First, it reduces the number of binding sites available for $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ carriage. Second, the binding of CO stabilizes the Hb molecule in its relaxed state, which shifts the $\mathrm{Hb}-\mathrm{O}_{2}$ dissociation curve far to the left and makes it more difficult for the Hb to release $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ in the systemic capillaries.

## Calculating the CO Saturation of Hb

To calculate the HbCO concentration, remember that when the Hb is exposed to CO , the [HbCO] will equal the $\left[\mathrm{HbO}_{2}\right]$ when the Hb is exposed to $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ at 210 times the [CO] in question. For example, Hb is $50 \%$ saturated with $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ (at equilibrium) when the $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ is 28 mm Hg . The $50 \% \mathrm{HbCO}$ concentration will be reached at equilibrium with $28 / 210 \mathrm{~mm} \mathrm{Hg}$ of $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ which would be only 0.133 mm Hg of CO . Because an atmosphere of pressure is $760 \mathrm{~mm} \mathrm{Hg}, 0.133 \mathrm{~mm} \mathrm{Hg}$ of CO is equivalent to $0.133 / 760$ or 0.000175 atmosphere of CO , or 175 ppm of CO . This level of CO would severely incapacitate or kill a person exposed to it for a long time. However, because it takes 4 hours to reach half the final equilibrated level of HbCO , a 4 -hour exposure to 175 ppm alveolar CO would lead to a HbCO level of $50 \% / 2$, or $25 \% \mathrm{HbCO}$. This level would give the person a headache and interfere with mental function. In 2 hours of exposure, the HbCO would be $1 / 4$ of $50 \%$ saturation, or $12.5 \% \mathrm{HbCO}$. This lower level would interfere with night vision and-because of some interference with $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ transport-raise the risk of angina in a person with limited coronary blood flow.

## Symptoms and Treatments

Symptoms of mild CO poisoning may begin when the CO saturation of Hb reaches $10 \%$; that is, when $40 \%$ of the Hb molecules bind one $\mathrm{CO}(25 \%$ of sites on one Hb molecule $\times 40 \%$ of Hb molecules $=10 \%$ saturation). These symptoms-which are not very specific-may include headache, nausea, and vomiting.

For mild CO poisoning, the treatment is simply to remove the causative agent, which leaves the body with a half-time of $\sim 4$ hours (i.e., CO dissociates very, very slowly from Hb -it has a low off-rate).

For more severe CO poisoning, the recommended treatment is to have the patient breathe $100 \% \mathrm{O}_{2}$, which increases the probability that $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ will displace CO from Hb (i.e., it increases the off-rate) and thereby speeds the washout of CO by about a factor of 4 (half-time $\cong 60$ minutes).

When the CO saturation of Hb reaches $20 \%$ to $25 \%$ (i.e., when, on average, $80 \%$ to $100 \%$ of Hb molecules each bind one CO molecule), the symptoms are severe and include confusion, chest pain, and unconsciousness. The treatment is hyperbaric $100 \% \mathrm{O}_{2}$-oxygen delivered at high pressure (OHP)—which has two positive effects. First, the hyperbaric $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ (i.e., increased $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ ) accelerates even further the displacement of CO from Hb . Breathing hyperbaric $100 \% \mathrm{O}_{2}$ at 2.5 atmospheres of pressure reduces the half-time of washout to 20 minutes. Second, the hyperbaric- $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ treatment can increase the concentration of dissolved $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ to such an extent that this dissolved $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ in the blood can deliver sufficient $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ to satisfy the body's metabolic demands (see Equations 29-1 through 29-3).

CO reaches a lethal level at an Hb saturation of $\sim 50 \%$.
Interestingly, hyperventilation is not an effective treatment for CO poisoning. One reason for this lack of effect may be that hyperventilation blows off $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ and reduces the arterial $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ (see pp. 679-680). According to the Bohr effect, low levels of $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ (e.g., caused by hyperventilation) would shift the $\mathrm{Hb}-\mathrm{O}_{2}$ dissociation curve to the left, exacerbating the effect of the CO poisoning. In fact, Yandell Henderson showed that use of $5 \% \mathrm{CO}_{2}$ in $95 \%$ $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ decreased plasma pH , which accelerated the displacement of CO from HbCO as well as increasing the tissue $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ through the Bohr effect (communicated by Arthur DuBois).

## Nitric Oxide

Hb also binds NO. This interaction may help ensure that the effects of NO are paracrine rather than endocrine-that is, that the effects of NO are restricted to the sites of release. The physiological target of NO is soluble guanylyl cyclase (see pp. 66-67), which binds NO through a heme group, just as does Hb !

![bo86465312c5](bo86465312c5.jpg)

Figure 29-7 Effect of adding $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ or 2,3-DPG to stripped Hb. The teal, orange, green, and brown curves are $\mathrm{Hb}-\mathrm{O}_{2}$ dissociation curves for pure $\mathrm{Hb}(2 \mathrm{mM})$ in artificial solutions. "Stripped Hb" is devoid of both $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ and 2,3-DPG. When $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ or 2,3-DPG was present, $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ was 40 mm Hg and $[2,3-\mathrm{DPG}]$ was 2.4 mM . The cytosol of erythrocytes in blood (red curve) had the same composition as the artificial solutions.
shifts the $\mathrm{Hb}-\mathrm{O}_{2}$ dissociation curve far to the left. Thus, when Hb reaches the systemic capillaries in CO poisoning, its tenacity for $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ is so high that the bright red blood cannot release enough $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ to the tissues.

## CARRIAGE OF $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$

## Blood carries "total $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ " mainly as $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$

The blood carries $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ and related compounds in five forms: (5) N29-6

1. Dissolved carbon dioxide. $\left[\mathrm{CO}_{2}\right]_{\text {dis }}$ follows Henry's law (see p. 593), and it is in the millimolar range in both blood plasma and blood cells. It makes up only $\sim 5 \%$ of the total $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ of arterial blood (gold portion of leftmost bar in Fig. 29-8).
2. Carbonic acid. $\mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{CO}_{3}$ can form either from $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ and $\mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O}$, or from $\mathrm{H}^{+}$and $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$(see p. 630). Because the equilibrium constant governing the reaction $\mathrm{CO}_{2}+\mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O}$ $\rightleftharpoons \mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{CO}_{3}$ is $\sim 0.0025,\left[\mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{CO}_{3}\right]$ is only $1 / 400$ th as large as $\left[\mathrm{CO}_{2}\right]$. Thus, $\mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{CO}_{3}$ is not quantitatively important for $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ carriage.
3. Bicarbonate. $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$can form in three ways. First, $\mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{CO}_{3}$ can dissociate into $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$and $\mathrm{H}^{+}$. Second, $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ can combine directly with $\mathrm{OH}^{-}$to form $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$, the reaction catalyzed by carbonic anhydrase. (5) N18-3 Third, $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$ forms when carbonate combines with $\mathrm{H}^{+}$. In arterial blood, $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$is $\sim 24 \mathrm{mM}$, so that $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$represents $\sim 90 \%$ of total $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ (purple portion of leftmost bar in Fig. 29-8).
4. Carbonate. $\mathrm{CO}_{3}^{2-}$ forms from the dissociation of bicarbonate: $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-} \rightarrow \mathrm{CO}_{3}^{2-}+\mathrm{H}^{+}$. Because the $\mathrm{p} K$ of this reaction is so high $\left(\sim 10.3\right),\left[\mathrm{CO}_{3}^{2-}\right]$ is only $\sim 1 / 1000$ th as high as $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$at $\mathrm{pH} 7.4$. Thus, like $\mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{CO}_{3}, \mathrm{CO}_{3}^{2-}$ is not quantitatively important for $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ carriage.
![bo87465312c5](bo87465312c5.jpg)

Figure 29-8 Constituents of "total $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ in blood." The left bar (a) represents arterial blood; the middle bar ( $\bar{v}$ ), mixed-venous blood; and the right bar, the incremental $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ that the blood picks up in the systemic capillaries.
5. Carbamino compounds. By far the most important carbamino compound is carbamino hemoglobin ( $\mathrm{Hb}-\mathrm{NH}-$ $\mathrm{COO}^{-}$), which forms rapidly and reversibly as $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ reacts with free amino groups on Hb (see p. 653). In arterial blood, carbamino compounds account for $\sim 5 \%$ of total $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ (blue) portion of leftmost bar in Fig. 29-8).

The reason we group together the previously listed five $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$-related compounds under the term total $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ is that the method Van Slyke (5) N29-7 introduced in the 1920s-which remains the basis for assaying blood $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$in modern clinical laboratories-cannot distinguish among the five.

## $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ transport depends critically on carbonic anhydrase, the $\mathrm{Cl}-\mathrm{HCO}_{3}$ exchanger, and Hb

The total $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ concentration of arterial blood is $\sim 26 \mathrm{mM}$, or $\sim 48 \mathrm{~mL}$ of $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ gas/dL (measured at STP). $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$constitutes $\sim 90 \%$ of this $48 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{dL}$, with $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ and carbamino compounds contributing $\sim 5 \%$ each (bar $a$ in Fig. 29-8). As blood courses through the systemic capillary beds, it picks up $\sim 4 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{dL}$ of $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$, so that the total $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ of mixed-venous blood is $\sim 52 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{dL}$ (bar $\bar{v}$ in Fig. 29-8). In what forms does blood carry this incremental $4 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{dL}$ of $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ to the lungs? About $10 \%$ of the incremental $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ moves as dissolved $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$, $\sim 69 \%$ as $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$, and $\sim 21 \%$ as carbamino compounds (rightmost two bars in Fig. 29-8). Therefore, dissolved $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ and carbamino $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ are far more important for carrying incremental $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ to the lungs than we might have surmised, given their contribution to total $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ in arterial blood.

Figure 29-9 summarizes the events that occur as incremental $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ enters systemic capillaries. As fast as biological oxidations in the mitochondria produce $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$, this gas diffuses out of cells, through the extracellular space, across the

## N29-7 Van Slyke's Manometric Method for Determining Total $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$

## Contributed by Emile Boulpaep and Walter Boron

In 1924, Van Slyke and Neill introduced a technique for determining what is today known as the "total $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ " of blood $\mathbf{0}$ N29-6 and other solutions. This technique quickly became the standard for determining total $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ in clinical chemistry laboratories. Moreover, the Van Slyke approach is the foundation even for modern, automated approaches for determining total $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$.

Although the original approach requires painstaking precision in the laboratory in order to obtain reliable results, the fundamental principle is a rather simple two-step process. First, one uses an acid (Van Slyke and Neill used 1 N lactic acid) to titrate virtually all of the carbamino Hb , carbonate $\left(\mathrm{CO}_{3}^{2-}\right)$, and bicarbonate $\left(\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}\right)$ to carbon dioxide $\left(\mathrm{CO}_{2}\right)$, which enters the gaseous phase. Second, one uses a manometer to measure the pressure $(P)$ of a known volume $(V)$ of this $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ gas. The ideal gas law tells us that

$$
P V=n R T
$$

$(\mathrm{NE} 29-2)$
Here, $n$ is the number of molecules, $R$ is the universal gas constant, and $T$ is the absolute temperature. Thus, with the proper corrections for nonideality, it is possible to compute the number of $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ molecules.

We will now examine the titration reactions in a little more detail. For the titration of carbamino Hb by the $\mathrm{H}^{+}$in lactic acid, two reactions occur in series:

$$
\begin{aligned}
& \mathrm{Hb}-\mathrm{NH}-\mathrm{COO}^{-}+\mathrm{H}^{+} \rightarrow \mathrm{Hb}-\mathrm{NH}_{2}+\mathrm{CO}_{2} \\
& \mathrm{Hb}-\mathrm{NH}_{2}+\mathrm{H}^{+} \rightarrow \mathrm{Hb}-\mathrm{NH}_{3}^{+}
\end{aligned}
$$

These reactions are the reverse of the ones shown in Equation 29-10.

The titration of $\mathrm{CO}_{3}^{2-}$ by the $\mathrm{H}^{+}$in lactic acid yields $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$:

$$
\mathrm{CO}_{3}^{2-}+\mathrm{H}^{+} \rightarrow \mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-} \quad(\mathrm{NE} 29-4)
$$

Finally, the titration of this newly formed $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$-as well as the pre-existing $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$(which is a far larger amount under physiological conditions)-by the $\mathrm{H}^{+}$in lactic acid yields carbonic acid $\left(\mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{CO}_{3}\right)$, which in turn yields $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ and $\mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O}$ :

$$
\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}+\mathrm{H}^{+} \rightarrow \mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{CO}_{3} \rightarrow \mathrm{CO}_{2}+\mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O} \quad(\mathrm{NE} 29-5)
$$

Because the pK governing the equilibrium $\mathrm{CO}_{2}+\mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O} \rightleftharpoons$ $\mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{CO}_{3}$ is $\sim 2.6,>99.7 \%$ of the newly formed $\mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{CO}_{3}$ goes on to form $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$.

In practice, the technique used to generate the $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ gas also extracts a variable amount of $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ from the solution. In other words, the $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ in the gas phase is mixed with an amount of $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ that is difficult to predict. Therefore, Van Slyke and Neill introduced an additional step to their analysis: they added 1 N NaOH to convert all total $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ to $\mathrm{CO}_{3}^{2-}$, thereby removing the $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ from the gas phase. This last step allowed them to obtain, with precision, the amount of $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ gas in the gas phase and thus to calculate the amount of total $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ that had been in their sample.

## REFERENCES

Simoni RD, Hill RL, Vaughan M: The determination of gases in blood and other solutions by vacuum extraction and manometric measurement. I. [Classics: A paper in a series reprinted to celebrate the centenary of the JBC in 2005.] J Biol Chem 277:e16, 2002.
Van Slyke DD, Neill JM: The determination of gases in blood and other solutions by vacuum extraction and manometric measurement. I. J Biol Chem 61:523-573, 1924. [Available online at the JBC website by accessing the commentary in the reference above.]

![bo88465312c5](bo88465312c5.jpg)

Figure 29-9 Carriage of $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ from systemic capillaries to the lungs.
capillary endothelium, and into the blood plasma. Some of the incremental $\mathrm{CO}_{2}(\sim 11 \%)$ remains in blood plasma throughout its journey to the lungs, but most ( $\sim 89 \%$ ), at least initially, enters the RBCs.

The $\sim 11 \%$ of the incremental $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ in plasma travels in three forms:

1. Dissolved $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$. About $6 \%$ of incremental $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ remains dissolved in blood plasma (assuming a hematocrit of $40 \%$ ).
2. Carbamino compounds. An insignificant amount forms carbamino compounds with plasma proteins.
3. Bicarbonate. About $5 \%$ of incremental $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ forms $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$ in the plasma and remains in the plasma: $\mathrm{CO}_{2}+\mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O} \rightarrow$ $\mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{CO}_{3} \rightarrow \mathrm{H}^{+}+\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$. The amount of $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$that follows this path depends critically on non- $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$ buffering power (see pp. 637-638), which is very low in plasma ( $\sim 5 \mathrm{mM} / \mathrm{pH}$ unit).

The remaining $\sim 89 \%$ of incremental $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ enters the RBCs, predominantly through two "gas channels," aquaporin 1 (AQP1) and the Rh complex. 0 N25-8 This $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ also has three fates:

1. Dissolved $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$. About $4 \%$ of incremental $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ remains dissolved inside the RBC.
2. Carbamino compounds. About $21 \%$ of incremental $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ forms carbamino compounds with Hb . Why does so much $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ travel as carbamino compounds inside the RBC, whereas so little does in the blood plasma? First, the Hb concentration inside RBCs ( $\sim 33 \mathrm{~g} / \mathrm{dL}$ ) is far higher than that of albumin and globulins in plasma ( $\sim 7 \mathrm{~g} / \mathrm{dL}$ ).

Second, Hb forms carbamino compounds far more easily than do major plasma proteins. Moreover, Hb forms carbamino compounds even more easily as it loses $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ in the systemic capillaries (reverse of $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$-Bohr effect). Finally, Hb is a far better buffer than the plasma proteins for the $\mathrm{H}^{+}$formed as a byproduct in carbamino formation, and becomes an even better buffer as it loses $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ in the systemic capillaries (reverse of the pH -Bohr effect).
3. Bicarbonate. About $64 \%$ of the incremental $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ forms $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$. Why does so much more $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ form $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$in the erythrocyte than in plasma? First, erythrocytes contain a high level of carbonic anhydrases (see p. 434), (1) N18-3 which greatly accelerates the conversion of $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ to $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$. In the absence of enzyme, hardly any $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$ would form inside erythrocytes during the brief time that the cells spend during their passage through a typical systemic capillary. Second, the $\mathrm{Cl}-\mathrm{HCO}_{3}$ exchanger AE1 (see pp. 124-125 and 434-435) transports some of the newly formed $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$out of the cell, promoting formation of more $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$. This uptake of $\mathrm{Cl}^{-}$in exchange for $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$ is known as the chloride or Hamburger shift. Third, the buffering of $\mathrm{H}^{+}$by Hb (see point 2 above) also pulls the reaction to the right.

The combined effects of the described intracellular and extracellular events is that $\sim 10 \%$ of incremental $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ formed in systemic tissues moves to the lungs as dissolved $\mathrm{CO}_{2}, 6 \%$ in plasma and $4 \%$ inside erythrocytes (gold portion of rightmost bar in Fig. 29-8). About $21 \%$ moves as carbamino compounds, almost exclusively inside erythrocytes as carbamino

# N29-8 Gas Channels 

## Contributed by Walter Boron

The traditional view had been that all gases cross all cell membranes by simply dissolving in the lipid phase of the membrane. The first evidence that the gas dogma was in need of refinement was the observation that apical membranes of gastric gland cells are impermeable to $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ and $\mathrm{NH}_{2}$ (Waisbren et al, 1994) and that apical membranes of colonic crypts are impermeable to $\mathrm{NH}_{3}$ (Singh et al, 1995). Low permeabilities to gases and $\mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O}$ may be a general property of membranes facing inhospitable environments (Cooper et al, 2002), including the mechanical stresses experienced by RBCs and capillary endothelial cells. Cells exposed to such hostile environments may have evolved specialized, robust membranes with unique lipid composition or protein or oligosaccharide coatings at the membrane surface.

The second observation that upset the gas dogma was the discovery that $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ passes through the water channel AQP1 (Cooper et al, 1998; Nakhoul et al, 1998). Peter Agre shared the 2003 Nobel Prize in Chemistry for discovering AQPs and their $\mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O}$ permeability. (5) N5-7 The physiological role of AQPs transcends water. For example, AQP7 and AQP9 transport glycerol. Might AQP1's $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ permeability be physiologically relevant? The first such evidence was the demonstration that an AQP plays a critical role in $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ uptake for photosynthesis by tobacco leaves (Uehlein et al, 2003). Endeward and colleagues (2008) demonstrated that approximately half of the $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ entering human RBCs passes through the AQP1 channel.

The AQPs form homotetramers, and each monomer has a pore for $\mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O}$. Molecular dynamics simulations suggest that $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ could pass single file with $\mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O}$ through each of the four aquapores as well as through the central pore between the four monomers (Wang et al, 2007). Preliminary inhibitor studies from the Boron laboratory confirm this prediction, indicating that $\sim 40 \%$ of the $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ that passes through AQP1 moves through the four aquapores, whereas the remaining $60 \%$ passes through the central pore. The molecular dynamics simulations suggest that virtually all of the $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ passes through the central pore.

A second family of gas channels is the Rh proteins. The bacterial Rh homolog AmtB forms a homotrimer, and each monomer appears to have a pore for $\mathrm{NH}_{2}$. The RhAG protein from the human Rh complex in RBCs is permeable both to $\mathrm{NH}_{2}$ and to $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ (Endeward et al, 2008). Preliminary work from the Boron laboratory indicates that $\mathrm{NH}_{2}$ moves exclusively through one of the three ammonia pores, whereas $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ moves mainly through the central pore of both AmtB and RhAG.

Interestingly, gas channels exhibit selectivity for gas in much the same way as ion channels exhibit selectivity for ions. For example, AQP4 (heavily expressed at the blood-brain barrier) and AQP5 (heavily expressed in alveolar type I pneumocytes) are virtually totally selective for $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ over $\mathrm{NH}_{2}$. AQP1 (heavily expressed in RBCs) is intermediate in its $\mathrm{CO}_{2} / \mathrm{NH}_{2}$ selectivity, AmtB is shifted more toward $\mathrm{NH}_{2}$ selectivity, and RhAG (heavily expressed in RBCs) is even more shifted toward selectivity for $\mathrm{NH}_{2}$ over $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$. This work is the first evidence for gas selectivity by a protein channel (Musa-Aziz et al, 2009).

Figure 29-10 $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ dissociation curves (Haldane effect).

Hb (blue portion of rightmost bar in Fig. 29-8). Finally, $\sim 69 \%$ of incremental $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ moves as $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}, 5 \%$ that forms in plasma and $64 \%$ that forms inside the RBC (purple portion of rightmost bar in Fig. 29-8). Because $\mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O}$ enters the cell during $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$formation, erythrocytes swell as they pass through systemic capillaries.

When mixed-venous blood (with a $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ of $\sim 46 \mathrm{~mm} \mathrm{Hg}$ ) reaches the pulmonary capillaries (surrounded by alveoli with a $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ of only $\sim 40 \mathrm{~mm} \mathrm{Hg}$ ), $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ moves from the erythrocytes and blood plasma into the alveolar air space. All of the reactions discussed above reverse. In the process, $\mathrm{Cl}^{-}$and $\mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O}$ leave the erythrocytes, and the cells shrink.

## The high $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ in the lungs causes the blood to dump $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$

The carriage of total $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ in the blood depends on the three blood-gas parameters- $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$, plasma pH , and $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$. The three plots in the main portion of Figure 29-10 are $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ dissociation curves. Each plot shows how changes in $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ affect the total- $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ content of blood. Although pH per se does not appear in this diagram, pH decreases as $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ increases along the x -axis (i.e., respiratory acidosis; see p. 833). The blue plot is the $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ dissociation curve when $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ is zero ( $\mathrm{S}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}} \cong 0 \% \mathrm{Hb}$ ). The next two plots are $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ dissociation curves for $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ values of $40 \mathrm{~mm} \mathrm{Hg}\left(\mathrm{S}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}} \cong 75 \% ;\right.$ purple curve) and 100 mm Hg ( $\mathrm{S}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}} \cong 97.5 \%$; red curve). The green line at the bottom of Figure 29-10 shows that the dissolved component of total $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ rises only slightly with increases in $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$.

Three features of the $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ dissociation curves in Figure 29-10 are noteworthy:

1. Near-linear relationship in the physiological range of $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ and $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ values (see Fig. 29-10, inset). In contrast, the $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ dissociation curve is highly nonlinear in its physiological range (i.e., 40 to 100 mm Hg ).
2. Up-shift of curve with decreasing $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$. At any $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$, total $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ content rises as $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ (or Hb saturation) falls-the Haldane effect. (5) N29-9 Thus, as blood enters systemic capillaries and releases $\mathrm{O}_{2}$, the $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$-carrying capacity rises so that blood picks up extra $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$. Conversely, as blood enters the pulmonary capillaries and binds $\mathrm{O}_{2}$, the $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$-carrying capacity falls so that blood dumps extra $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ (Table 29-4). The Haldane effect is the flip side of the coin from the pH -Bohr and $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$-Bohr effects. First, just as $\mathrm{H}^{+}$binding lowers the $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ affinity of Hb (see Equation 29-9), $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ binding destabilizes protonated hemoglobin $\left(\mathrm{Hb}-\mathrm{H}^{+}\right)$, promoting $\mathrm{H}^{+}$release. By mass action, this $\mathrm{H}^{+}$ reduces $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$-carrying capacity by favoring the formation of $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ from both carbamino Hb and $\mathrm{HCO}_{3}^{-}$(see Fig. 29-9). Second, just as carbamino formation lowers the $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ affinity of Hb (see Equation 29-11), $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ binding destabilizes carbamino $\mathrm{Hb}\left(\mathrm{Hb}-\mathrm{NH}-\mathrm{COO}^{-}\right)$, promoting $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ release.
3. Steepness. Because $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ dissociation curves (see Fig. 29-10) are much steeper than $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ dissociation curves (see Fig. 29-3), $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ must increase from 40 mm Hg in arterial blood to only 46 mm Hg in mixed-venous blood to increase the $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ content by the $\sim 4 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{dL}$ of $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ required to remove $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ as fast as the mitochondria produce it. In contrast, $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ must decrease from 100 to 40 mm Hg to dump enough $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ to meet metabolic demands.

In the inset to Figure 29-10, point $a$ on the red curve represents arterial blood, with a $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ of 40 mm Hg and a $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ of $100 \mathrm{~mm} \mathrm{Hg}\left(\mathrm{S}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}} \cong 97.5 \%\right)$. Point $\bar{v}$ on the purple curve represents mixed-venous blood, with a $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ of 46 mm Hg but a $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ of only $40 \mathrm{~mm} \mathrm{Hg}\left(\mathrm{S}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}} \cong 75 \%\right)$. The difference between the total $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ contents represented by the two points (i.e., 52 versus $48 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{dL}$ ) represents the $4 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{dL}$ of $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ the blood takes up as it passes through systemic capillaries. If it

# N29-9 John Scott Haldane 

## Contributed by Emile Boulpaep and Walter Boron

John Scott Haldane (1860-1936) was born in Edinburgh, where he received his medical degree in 1884. He began his academic career at Queen's College, Dundee, before moving to Oxford. Haldane is credited with several notable discoveries.

- He showed that high blood levels of $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ are a more powerful stimulus for breathing than low levels of $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ (1905).
- He developed a method for the staged decompression of deep-sea divers to avoid the bends on the return of the divers to the surface. He published the first diving decompression tables (1908).
- He—along with Christiansen and Douglas—showed that the $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ content of the blood decreases with increasing $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{Sc}}$, known as the Haldane effect (1914). This effect is responsible for about half of the $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ exchange in the blood.
For more information about Haldane, visit the following websites:

1. http://www.geo.ed.ac.uk/scotgaz/people/famousfirst1349 .html.
2. http://www.diegoweb.com/diving/cards/page2.html.

Thus, at a $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ of 46 mm Hg , the fall in $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ that occurs as blood flows through systemic capillaries allows the blood to pick up $\sim 50 \%$ more $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ (i.e., 4 versus $2.7 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{dL}$ ). ## The $\mathrm{O}_{2}-\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ diagram describes the interaction of $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ and $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ in the blood

We have seen that Hb plays a key role in transporting $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ from the lungs to peripheral tissues, transporting $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ in the
opposite direction, and buffering $\mathrm{H}^{+}$. These functions are all intimately interrelated: $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ and pH influence the $\mathrm{O}_{2}-\mathrm{Hb}$ dissociation curve (Bohr effects; see Fig. 29-5), and $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ influences the $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ dissociation curve (Haldane effect; see Fig. 29-10). A useful way of illustrating this mutual dependence is the $\mathrm{O}_{2}-\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ diagram, which we will revisit on page 691 to understand regional differences between alveolar $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ and $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$.

On a coordinate system with $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ on the $y$-axis and $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ on the x -axis, each blue curve in Figure 29-11 represents an isopleth of identical $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ content in whole blood (from the Greek isos [equal] + plethein [to be full]). For example, arterial blood (point $a$ ) lies on the isopleth for an $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ content of $20.0 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{dL}$, with coordinates of $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}=100 \mathrm{~mm} \mathrm{Hg}$ and $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}=40 \mathrm{~mm} \mathrm{Hg}$. Following this isopleth from a $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ of 40 mm Hg (point $a$ ) to, say, 46 mm Hg , we see that the blood could carry the same $20.0 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{dL}$ of $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ only if we increase

$\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ from 100 to nearly 105 mm Hg . Thus, as $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ increases, the $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ content of blood decreases (Bohr effect). If it were not for the Bohr effect, all the blue curves would be vertical lines. Mixed-venous blood (point $\vec{v}$ ) is on the $\mathrm{O}_{2}$-content isopleth for $15.3 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{dL}$, at a $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ of 40 mm Hg and a $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ of 46 mm Hg . If blood were equilibrated with inspired air (point $I$ ), it would have a $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ of 150 mm Hg and a $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ of zero.

Each red curve is an isopleth of identical $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ content. Arterial blood (point $a$ ) lies on the isopleth for $48 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{dL}$. Similarly, mixed-venous blood (point $\vec{v}$ ) lies on the isopleth for $52 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{dL}$. Following this $52-\mathrm{mL} / \mathrm{dL}$ isopleth from a $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ of 40 mm Hg (point $\vec{v}$ ) to, say, 100 mm Hg , we see that the blood could carry the same $52 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{dL}$ of $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ only if we
increase $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ from 46 to nearly 50 mm Hg . Thus, as the $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ increases, the $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ content of blood decreases (Haldane effect). If it were not for the Haldane effect, all the red curves would be horizontal lines. Blood equilibrated with inspired air (point $I$ ) would have a $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ content of zero.

In Figure 29-11, the green curve connecting the points $\vec{v}, a$, and $I$ represents all possible combinations of $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ and $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ in normal lungs.
