# GAS EXCHANGE IN THE LUNGS 

Walter F. Boron

The complex anatomy of the pulmonary tree, the mechanics of the respiratory system, and the sophisticated carriage mechanisms for $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ and $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ combine to serve two essential purposes: the ready diffusion of $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ from the alveoli to the pulmonary-capillary blood, and the movement of $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ in the opposite direction. In this chapter, we consider principles that govern these diffusive events and factors that, in certain diseases, can limit gas exchange.

## DIFFUSION OF GASES

## Gas flow across a barrier is proportional to diffusing capacity and concentration gradient (Fick's law)

Although, early on, physiologists debated whether the lung actively secretes $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ into the blood, we now know that the movements of both $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ and $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ across the alveolar blood-gas barrier occur by simple diffusion (see p. 108). Random motion alone causes a net movement of molecules from areas of high concentration to areas of low concentration. Although diffusion per se involves no expenditure of energy, the body must do work-in the form of ventilation and circulation-to create the concentration gradients down which $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ and $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ diffuse. Over short distances, diffusion can be highly effective.

Suppose that a barrier that is permeable to $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ separates two air-filled compartments (Fig. 30-1A). The partial pressures (see p. 593) of $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ on the two sides are $\mathrm{P}_{1}$ and $\mathrm{P}_{2}$. The probability that an $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ molecule on side 1 will collide with the barrier and move to the opposite side is proportional to $\mathrm{P}_{1}$ :

$$
\text { Flow }_{1 \rightarrow 2} \propto \mathrm{P}_{1}
$$

The unidirectional movement of $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ in the opposite direction, from side 2 to side 1 , is proportional to the partial pressure of $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ on side 2 :

$$
\text { Flow }_{2 \rightarrow 1} \propto \mathrm{P}_{2}
$$

The net movement of $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ from side 1 to side 2 is the difference between the two unidirectional flows:

$$
\text { Flow }_{\text {net }}=\left(\mathrm{P}_{1}-\mathrm{P}_{2}\right)
$$

Note that net flow is proportional to the difference in partial pressures, not the ratio. Thus, when $\mathrm{P}_{1}$ is 100 mm Hg (or torr) and $\mathrm{P}_{2}$ is 95 mm Hg (ratio of 1.05 ), the net flow is 5 -fold greater than when $\mathrm{P}_{1}$ is 2 mm Hg and $\mathrm{P}_{2}$ is 1 mm Hg (ratio of 2 ).

The term flow describes the number of $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ molecules moving across the entire area of the barrier per unit time (units: moles/s). If we normalize flow for the area of the barrier, the result is a flux (units: moles $/\left[\mathrm{cm}^{2} \cdot \mathrm{~s}\right]$ ). Respiratory physiologists usually measure the flow of a gas such as $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ as the volume of gas (measured at standard temperature and pressure/dry; see Box 26-3) moving per unit time. V refers to the volume and $\dot{\mathrm{V}}$ is its time derivative (volume of gas moving per unit time), or flow.

The proportionality constant in Equation 30-3 is the diffusing capacity for the lung, $\mathrm{D}_{\mathrm{L}}$ (units: $\mathrm{mL} /[\mathrm{min} \cdot \mathrm{mm} \mathrm{Hg}]$ ). Thus, the flow of gas becomes

$$
\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\text {net }}=\mathrm{D}_{\mathrm{L}} \cdot\left(\mathrm{P}_{1}-\mathrm{P}_{2}\right)
$$

This equation is a simplified version of Fick's law (see p. 108), which states that net flow is proportional to the concentration gradient, expressed here as the partial-pressure gradient.

Applying Fick's law to the diffusion of gas across the alveolar wall requires that we extend our model somewhat. Rather than a simple barrier separating two compartments filled with dry gas, a wet barrier covered with a film of water on one side will separate a volume filled with moist air from a volume of blood plasma at $37^{\circ} \mathrm{C}$ (see Fig. 30-1B). Now we can examine how the physical characteristics of the gas and the barrier contribute to $\mathrm{D}_{\mathrm{L}}$.

Two properties of the gas contribute to $\mathrm{D}_{\mathrm{L}}$-molecular weight (MW) and solubility in water. First, the mobility of the gas should decrease as its molecular weight increases. Indeed, Graham's law states that diffusion is inversely proportional to the square root of molecular weight. Second, Fick's law states that the flow of the gas across the wet barrier is proportional to the concentration gradient of the gas dissolved in water. According to Henry's law (see Box 26-2), these concentrations are proportional to the respective partial pressures, and the proportionality constant is the solubility of the gas (s). Therefore, poorly soluble gases (e.g., $\mathrm{N}_{2}, \mathrm{He}$ ) diffuse poorly across the alveolar wall.

![bo91465312c5](bo91465312c5.jpg)

Figure 30-1 Diffusion of a gas across a barrier.
![bo92465312c5](bo92465312c5.jpg)

Figure 30-2 Effect of barrier thickness.

Two properties of the barrier contribute to $\mathrm{D}_{\mathrm{L}}$-area and thickness. First, the net flow of $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ is proportional to the area (A) of the barrier, describing the odds that an $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ molecule will collide with the barrier. Second, the net flow is inversely proportional to the thickness (a) of the barrier, including the water layer. The thicker the barrier, the smaller the $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ partialpressure gradient $\left(\Delta \mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}} / \mathrm{a}\right)$ through the barrier (Fig. 30-2). An analogy is the slope of the trail that a skier takes from a mountain peak to the base. Whether the skier takes a steep "expert" trail or a shallow "beginner's" trail, the end points
of the journey are the same. However, the trip is much faster along the steeper trail!

Finally, a combined property of both the barrier and the gas also contributes to $\mathrm{D}_{\mathrm{L}}$, a proportionality constant $k$ that describes the interaction of the gas with the barrier.

Replacing $\mathrm{D}_{\mathrm{L}}$ in Equation 30-4 with an area, solubility, thickness, molecular weight, and the proportionality constant yields

$$
\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\text {net }}=\underbrace{\left[k \frac{\mathrm{~A} \cdot \mathrm{~s}}{\mathrm{a} \sqrt{\mathrm{MW}}}\right]}_{\mathrm{D}_{\mathrm{L}}}\left(\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{I}}-\mathrm{P}_{2}\right)
$$

Equations 30-4 and 30-5 are analogous to Ohm's law for electricity:

$$
\mathrm{I}=\left(\frac{1}{\mathrm{R}}\right) \cdot \Delta \mathrm{V}
$$

Electrical current (I) in Ohm's law corresponds to the net flow of gas ( $\mathrm{V}_{\text {net }}$ ); the reciprocal of resistance (i.e., conductance) corresponds to diffusing capacity $\left(\mathrm{D}_{\mathrm{L}}\right)$; and the voltage difference $(\Delta \mathrm{V})$ that drives electrical current corresponds to the pressure difference $\left(\mathrm{P}_{1}-\mathrm{P}_{2}\right.$, or $\left.\Delta \mathrm{P}\right)$.

## The total flux of a gas between alveolar air and blood is the summation of multiple diffusion events along each pulmonary capillary during the respiratory cycle

Equation 30-5 describes $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ diffusion between two compartments whose properties are uniform both spatially and temporally. Does this equation work for the lungs? If we assume that the alveolar air, blood-gas barrier, and pulmonarycapillary blood are uniform in space and time, then the net diffusion of $\mathrm{O}_{2}\left(\dot{\mathrm{~V}}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}\right)$ from alveolar air to pulmonary-capillary blood is

$$
\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}=\underbrace{k \frac{\mathrm{~A} \cdot \mathrm{~s}}{\mathrm{a} \sqrt{\mathrm{MW}}}}_{\mathrm{D}_{\mathrm{L}}}
$$

![bo93465312c5](bo93465312c5.jpg)

Figure 30-3 Complications of using Fick's law.
$\mathrm{DL}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ is the diffusing capacity for $\mathrm{O}_{2}, \mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ is the $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ partial pressure in the alveolar air, and $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ is the comparable parameter in pulmonary-capillary blood. Although Equation 30-7 may seem sophisticated enough, a closer examination reveals that $\mathrm{DL}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}, \mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$, and $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ are each even more complicated than they at first appear.
$\mathrm{DL}_{0_{2}}$ Among the five terms that make up $\mathrm{DL}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$, two vary both temporally (during the respiratory cycle) and spatially
![bo94465312c5](bo94465312c5.jpg)
(from one piece of alveolar wall to another). During inspiration, lung expansion causes the surface area (A) available for diffusion to increase and the thickness of the barrier (a) to decrease (Fig. 30-3A). Because of these temporal differences, $\mathrm{DL}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ should be maximal at the end of inspiration. However, even at one instant in time, barrier thickness and the surface area of alveolar wall differ among pieces of alveolar wall. These spatial differences exist both at rest and during the respiratory cycle. (T) N30-1

# N30-1 Spatial Differences in Alveolar Dimensions 

## Contributed by Emile Boulpaep and Walter Boron

The total area of the lungs is not distributed evenly among all alveoli. First, all else being equal, some alveoli are "naturally" larger than others. Thus, some have a greater area for diffusion than do others, and some have a thinner wall than do others.

Second, the position of an alveolus in the lung can affect its size. As we saw in Chapter 27, when a person is positioned vertically, the effects of gravity cause the intrapleural pressure to be more negative near the apex of the lung than near the base (see Fig. 27-2). Thus, other things being equal, alveoli near the apex of the lung tend to be more inflated, so that they have a greater area and smaller thickness compared to alveoli near the base of the lung.

Third, during inspiration, alveoli undergo an increase in volume that causes their surface area to increase and their wall thickness to decrease. However, these changes are not uniform among alveoli. Again, the differences can be purely anatomical: all other things being equal, some alveoli "naturally" have a greater static compliance (see p. 610) than others. Thus, during inspiration, their area will increase more, and their wall thickness will decrease more. However, other things being equal, the compliance of an alveolus also depends on its position in the lung. We will see in Chapter 31 that the relatively overinflated alveoli near the apex of the lung (in an upright individual) have a relatively low compliance. In other words, during inspiration these apical alveoli have a smaller volume increase (see Fig. 31-5D). Thus, their area for diffusion undergoes a relatively smaller increase, and their wall thickness undergoes a relatively smaller decrease, than alveoli near the base of the lung.

In summary, for all of the reasons we have discussed, the area and thickness parameters vary widely among alveoli at the end of a quiet inspiration, and the relation among these differences changes dynamically during a respiratory cycle.

$\mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{B}_{4}}$ Like area and thickness, alveolar $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ varies both temporally and spatially (see Fig. 30-3B). In any given alveolus, $\mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ is greatest during inspiration (when $\mathrm{O}_{2}$-rich air enters the lungs) and least just before the initiation of the next inspiration (after perfusion has maximally drained $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ from the alveoli), as discussed on page 676. These are temporal differences. We will see that when an individual is standing, $\mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ is greatest near the lung apex and least near the base (see pp. 681-682). Moreover, mechanical variations in the resistance of conducting airways (see pp. 681-682) and the compliance of alveoli (see p. 597 or pp. 608-610) cause ventilation-and thus $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ (see p. 610)-to vary among alveoli. These are spatial differences.
$\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{F}_{\mathrm{B}_{4}}}$ As discussed below, as the blood flows down the capillary, capillary $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ rises to match $\mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ (see Fig. 30-3C). Therefore, $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ diffusion is maximal at the beginning of the pulmonary capillary and gradually falls to zero farther along the capillary. Moreover, this profile varies during the respiratory cycle.

The complications that we have raised for $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ diffusion apply as well to $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ diffusion. Of these complications, by far the most serious is the change in $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{F}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}}$ with distance along the pulmonary capillary. How, then, can we use Fick's law to understand the diffusion of $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ and $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ ? Clearly, we cannot insert a single set of fixed values for $\mathrm{Di}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}, \mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$, and $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{F}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}}$ into Equation 30-7 and hope to describe the overall flow of $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ between all alveoli and their pulmonary capillaries throughout the entire respiratory cycle. However, Fick's law does describe gas flow between air and blood for a single piece of alveolar wall (and its apposed capillary wall) at a single time during the respiratory cycle. For $\mathrm{O}_{2}$,

$$
\begin{aligned}
& \dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}} \text { for one piece } \\
& \text { of alveolar wall, } \\
& \text { at one time }
\end{aligned}=\underbrace{k \frac{\mathrm{~A} \cdot \mathrm{~s}}{\mathrm{a} \sqrt{\mathrm{MW}}}}_{\mathrm{Di}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}} \quad\left(\mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}-\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{F}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}}\right)
$$

For one piece of alveolar wall and at one instant in time, A and a (and thus $\mathrm{Di}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ ) have well defined values, as do $\mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ and $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{F}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}}$. The total amount of $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ flowing from all alveoli to all pulmonary capillaries throughout the entire respiratory cycle is simply the sum of all individual diffusion events, added up over all pieces of alveolar wall (and their apposed pieces of capillary wall) and over all times in the respiratory cycle:

$$
\text { Overall } \dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}=\sum_{\substack{\text { wall } \\ \text { to }}}^{\text {All pieces }} \sum_{\text {to }}^{\text {All times }} \sum_{\text {clyde }}^{\left(\mathrm{DI}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}\left(\mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}-\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{F}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}}\right)\right.}
$$

Here, $\mathrm{Di}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}, \mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$, and $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{F}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}}$ are the "microscopic" values for one piece of alveolar wall, at one instant in time.

Even though the version of Fick's law in Equation 30-9 does indeed describe $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ diffusion from alveolar air to pulmonary-capillary blood-and a comparable equation would do the same for $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ diffusion in the opposite direction-it is not of much practical value for predicting $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ uptake. However, we can easily compute the uptake of $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ that has already taken place by use of the Fick principle (see p. 423). The rate of $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ uptake by the lungs is the difference between the rate at which $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ leaves the lungs via the
pulmonary veins and the rate at which $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ enters the lungs via the pulmonary arteries. The rate of $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ departure from the lungs is the product of blood flow (i.e., cardiac output, $\dot{\mathrm{Q}}$ ) and the $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ content of pulmonary venous blood, which is virtually the same as that of systemic arterial blood $\left(\mathrm{Ca}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}\right)$. Remember that "content" (see p. 650) is the sum of dissolved $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ and $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ bound to hemoglobin $(\mathrm{Hb})$. Similarly, the rate of $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ delivery to the lungs is the product of $\dot{\mathrm{Q}}$ and the $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ content of pulmonary arterial blood, which is the same as that of the mixed-venous blood $\left(\mathrm{CV}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}\right)$. Thus, the difference between the rates of $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ departure and $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ delivery is

$$
\begin{aligned}
& \text { For a cardiac output of } 5 \mathrm{~L} / \mathrm{min}, \text { a } \mathrm{Ca}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}} \text { of } 20 \mathrm{~mL} \mathrm{O}_{2} / \mathrm{dL} \\
& \text { blood, and a } \mathrm{C}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}} \text { of } 15 \mathrm{~mL} \mathrm{O}_{2} / \mathrm{dL} \text { blood, the rate of } \mathrm{O}_{2}
\end{aligned}
$$

$$
=250 \mathrm{~mL} \mathrm{O}_{2} / \mathrm{min}
$$

For a cardiac output of $5 \mathrm{~L} / \mathrm{min}$, a $\mathrm{Ca}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ of $20 \mathrm{~mL} \mathrm{O}_{2} / \mathrm{dL}$ blood, and a $\mathrm{CV}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ of $15 \mathrm{~mL} \mathrm{O}_{2} / \mathrm{dL}$ blood, the rate of $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ uptake by the pulmonary-capillary blood is

$$
\begin{aligned}
& \text { The flow of } \mathrm{O}_{2}, \mathrm{CO} \text {, and } \mathrm{CO}_{2} \text { between alveolar air and } \\
& \text { blood depends on the interaction of these gases } \\
& \text { with red blood cells }
\end{aligned}
$$

The flow of $\mathrm{O}_{2}, \mathrm{CO}$, and $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ between alveolar air and blood depends on the interaction of these gases with red blood cells

We have been treating $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ transport as if it involved only the diffusion of the gas across a homogeneous barrier. In fact, the barrier is a three-ply structure (5) N30-2 comprising an alveolar epithelial cell, a capillary endothelial cell, and the intervening interstitial space containing extracellular matrix. The barrier is remarkable not only for its impressive surface area ( 50 to $100 \mathrm{~m}^{2}$ ) and thinness ( $\sim 0.6 \mu \mathrm{~m}$ ) but also for its strength, which derives mainly from type IV collagen in the lamina densa of the basement membrane (often $<50 \mathrm{~nm}$ ) within the extracellular matrix.

One could imagine that, as $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ diffuses from the alveolar air to the Hb inside an erythrocyte (red blood cell, or RBC), the $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ must cross 12 discrete mini-barriers (Fig. 30-4). A mini-diffusing capacity ( $\mathrm{D}_{1}$ to $\mathrm{D}_{12}$ ) governs each of the 12 steps and contributes to a so-called membrane diffusing capacity $\left(\mathbf{D}_{\mathrm{m}}\right)$ because it primarily describes how $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ diffuses

# N30-2 Three-Ply Structure of the Alveolar Barrier 

See the following review:
Maina JN, West JB: Thin and strong! The bioengineering dilemma in the structural and functional design of the blood-gas barrier. Physiol Rev 85:811-844, 2005.

![bo95465312c5](bo95465312c5.jpg)

Figure 30-4 Transport of $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ from alveolar air to Hb . The 12 diffusion constants $\left(D_{1}\right.$ to $\left.D_{12}\right)$ govern 12 diffusive steps across a series of 12 barriers: (1) the interface between the alveolar air and water layer, (2) the water layer itself, (3-5) the two membranes and cytoplasm of the type I alveolar pneumocyte (i.e., epithelial cell), (6) the interstitial space containing the extracellular matrix, (7-9) the two membranes and cytoplasm of the capillary endothelial cell, (10) a thin layer of blood plasma ( $<0.2 \mu \mathrm{~m}$ in mammals), and $(11,12)$ the membrane and cytoplasm of the erythrocyte. $\theta \cdot \mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{c}}$ describes how fast $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ binds to Hb .
through various membranes. How do these mini-diffusing capacities contribute to $\mathrm{D}_{\mathrm{M}}$ ? Returning to our electrical model (see Equation 30-6), we recognize that D is analogous to the reciprocal of resistance. Therefore, we can represent the 12 diffusive steps by 12 resistors in series. Because the total resistance is the sum of the individual resistances, the reciprocal of $\mathrm{D}_{\mathrm{M}}$ is the sum of the reciprocals of the minidiffusing capacities:

$$
\frac{1}{\mathrm{D}_{\mathrm{M}}}=\frac{1}{\mathrm{D}_{1}}+\frac{1}{\mathrm{D}_{2}}+\frac{1}{\mathrm{D}_{3}}+\cdots+\frac{1}{\mathrm{D}_{10}}+\frac{1}{\mathrm{D}_{11}}+\frac{1}{\mathrm{D}_{12}}
$$

Of course, these parameters vary with location in the lung and position in the respiratory cycle.

For most of the $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ entering the blood, the final step is binding to Hb (see p. 647), which occurs at a finite rate:

Rate of $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ uptake by hemoglobin $=\left(\theta \cdot \mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{c}}\right) \mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$
$\theta$ is a rate constant that describes how many milliliters of $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ gas bind to the Hb in 1 mL of blood each minute, and for each millimeter of mercury ( mm Hg ) of partial pressure. $\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{c}}$ is the volume of blood in the pulmonary capillaries. The product $\theta \cdot \mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{c}}$ has the same dimensions as $\mathrm{D}_{\mathrm{M}}$ (units: $\mathrm{mL} /[\min \cdot \mathrm{mm} \mathrm{Hg}]$ ), and both contribute to the overall diffusing capacity:

$$
\frac{1}{\mathrm{D}_{\mathrm{L}}}=\frac{1}{\mathrm{D}_{\mathrm{M}}}+\frac{1}{\theta \cdot \mathrm{~V}_{\mathrm{c}}}
$$

Because $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ binds to Hb so rapidly, its "Hb" term $1 /\left(\theta \cdot \mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{c}}\right)$ is probably only $\sim 5 \%$ as large as its "membrane" term $1 / \mathrm{D}_{\mathrm{M}}$.

For carbon monoxide (CO), which binds to Hb even more tightly than does $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ (see pp. 654-655)-but far more slowly- $\theta \cdot \mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{c}}$ is quantitatively far more important. The overall uptake of CO , which pulmonary specialists use to
compute $\mathrm{D}_{\mathrm{L}}$ (see p. 670), depends about equally on the $\mathrm{D}_{\mathrm{M}}$ and $\theta \cdot \mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{c}}$ terms.

As far as the movement of $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ is concerned, one might expect the $\mathrm{D}_{\mathrm{L}}$ for $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ to be substantially higher than that for $\mathrm{O}_{2}$, inasmuch as the solubility of $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ in water is $\sim 23$-fold higher than that of $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ (see p. 593). However, measurements show that $\mathrm{Dt}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ is only 3 - to 5 -fold greater than $\mathrm{Dt}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$. The likely explanation is that the interaction of $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ with the RBC is more complicated than that of $\mathrm{O}_{2}$, involving interactions with Hb , carbonic anhydrase, and the $\mathrm{Cl}-\mathrm{HCO}_{3}$ exchanger (see pp. 655-657).

In summary, the movement of $\mathrm{O}_{2}, \mathrm{CO}$, and $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ between the alveolus and the pulmonary capillary involves not only diffusion but also interactions with Hb . Although these interactions have only a minor effect on the diffusing capacity for $\mathrm{O}_{2}$, they are extremely important for CO and $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$. Although we will generally refer to "diffusing capacity" as if it represented only the diffusion across a homogeneous barrier, one must keep in mind its more complex nature.

## DIFFUSION AND PERFUSION LIMITATIONS ON GAS TRANSPORT

## The diffusing capacity normally limits the uptake of CO from alveolar air to blood

Imagine that a subject breathes air containing $0.1 \% \mathrm{CO}$ for a brief period of time. Breathing somewhat higher levels of CO for many hours could be fatal (see pp. 1224-1225) because CO , which binds to Hb with an affinity that is 200 to 300 times higher than that of $\mathrm{O}_{2}$, prevents Hb from releasing $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ to the tissues (see pp. 655-657). If we assume that barometric pressure ( $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{B}}$ ) is 760 mm Hg and that $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O}}$ is 47 mm Hg at $37^{\circ} \mathrm{C}$, then we can compute the $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}}$ of the wet inspired air entering the alveoli (see Box 26-1):

![bo96465312c5](bo96465312c5.jpg)

Figure 30-5 Diffusion of CO. In $\mathbf{A}, \Delta \mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}}$ is the CO partial-pressure gradient from alveolar air to pulmonarycapillary blood. As the RBC enters the capillary, $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ occupies three of the four sites on Hb . In $\mathbf{B}, \mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{CO}}$ is alveolar $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}}$. In $\mathbf{C}$, the blood flow $\dot{\mathrm{O}}$ has a relative value of 1 . In $\mathbf{D}$, the diffusing capacity D , has a relative value of 1. Because $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}}$ is very low in $\mathbf{B}$ through $\mathbf{D}$, we are on the linear portion of the Hb-CO dissociation curve. Thus, the CO content at the end of the capillary is approximately proportional to the capillary $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}}$.

$$
\begin{aligned}
\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}} & =\stackrel{\text { Fraction of inspired }}{\mathrm{F}_{\mathrm{CO}}} \cdot\left(\mathrm{~Pa}-\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O}}\right) \\
& =0.1 \% \cdot(760-47) \mathrm{mmHg} \\
& =-0.7 \mathrm{mmHg}
\end{aligned}
$$

If the subject smokes cigarettes or lives in a polluted environment, CO will be present in the mixed-venous bloodand, therefore, the alveolar air-even before our test begins. If not, the initial $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}}$ of the mixed-venous blood entering the pulmonary capillaries will be $\sim 0 \mathrm{~mm} \mathrm{Hg}$. Thus, a small gradient ( $\sim 0.7 \mathrm{~mm} \mathrm{Hg}$ ) drives CO diffusion from alveolar air into blood plasma (Fig. 30-5A). As CO enters the blood plasma, it diffuses into the cytoplasm of RBCs, where Hb binds it avidly. The flow of CO from alveolus to RBC is so slow, and the affinity and capacity of Hb to bind CO is so great, that Hb binds almost all incoming CO. Because only a small fraction of the total CO in the pulmonary-capillary blood remains free in solution, the aqueous phase of the blood remains nearly a perfect sink for CO. That is, $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}}$ in the capillary $\left(\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{a}_{\mathrm{CO}}}\right)$, which is proportional to free $[\mathrm{CO}]$ in the capillary, rises only slightly above 0 mm Hg as the blood
courses down the capillary (see Fig. 30-5B). Thus, by the time the blood reaches the end of the capillary ( $\sim 0.75$ second later), $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{a}_{\mathrm{CO}}}$ is still far below alveolar $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}}\left(\mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{CO}}\right)$. In other words, CO fails to reach diffusion equilibrium (c) N30-3 between the alveolus and the blood.

There are two reasons that $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{a}_{\mathrm{CO}}}$ rises so slowly as blood flows down the pulmonary capillary:

1. The CO flux $\left(\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{CO}}\right)$ is low. According to Fick's law, $\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{CO}}=$ $\mathrm{Dt}_{\mathrm{CO}}\left(\mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{CO}}-\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{a}_{\mathrm{CO}}}\right)$. Because we chose to use an extremely low inspired $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}}$, the alveolar $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}}$ driving CO diffusion was likewise extremely low, causing $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{a}_{\mathrm{CO}}}$ to rise slowly. In addition, the physiological $\mathrm{Dt}_{\mathrm{CO}}$ is moderate.
2. Hb continuously traps incoming CO. Hb has a high affinity and high capacity for CO. Thus, for a low $\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{CO}}$, $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{a}_{\mathrm{CO}}}$-proportional to the free [CO]-rises very slowly.

We will now explore factors that influence how much CO the blood takes up as it flows down the capillary. The principles that we develop here apply equally well to $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ and $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$. First, we use the Fick principle (see Equation 30-10) to quantitate, after the fact, how much CO has entered the blood:

# N30-3 An Analogy for the Diffusion-Limited Uptake of CO 

## Contributed by Emile Boulpaep and Walter Boron

The following may be a helpful analogy for how the partial pressure of $\mathrm{CO}\left(\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}}\right)$ changes with time as blood flows down a pulmonary capillary. Keep in mind that, if the linear velocity of the blood is constant, time corresponds to distance down the capillary as shown on the x-axis in Figure 30-5B. Imagine a basement that is somewhat leaky to water. The leakiness of the wall is analogous to the diffusing capacity $\left(D_{L}\right)$ of the lung. After a severe rainstorm, the water outside the basement rises to a level that is 100 cm above the floor of the basement. This 100 cm of water is analogous to the alveolar $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}}$. The combination of the hydrostatic pressure gradient of 100 cm and the leakiness of the wall allow water to leak in at the rate of $100 \mathrm{~L} / \mathrm{min}$. Thus, the influx of water is analogous to the uptake of CO by the pulmonarycapillary blood $\left(\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{C} O}\right)$. Fortunately, the basement is equipped with a sump pump-activated by the presence of water-that removes water at $99 \mathrm{~L} / \mathrm{min}$. The sump pump is analogous to Hb , and the pumping of the water is analogous to the binding of CO to Hb . Thus, under the conditions of this example, the pump can remove all but $1 \mathrm{~L} / \mathrm{min}$ of the incoming water, so that water level (analogous to $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}}$ ) will slowly rise. By how much will the water level rise within 5 hours, a period that is analogous to the time that an RBC spends in a pulmonary capillary?

If the leakiness of the basement wall is low (low $\mathrm{D}_{\mathrm{L}}$ ), then the water level may rise by only a few centimeters over the 5 -hour period. Thus, even though a large amount of water may have
entered during the 5 hours, the basement water level never rose enough to equilibrate with the outside water level, which was 100 cm above the floor. The reason for this failure of the water to equilibrate is that the pump was able to remove almost all the water. If the leakiness of the basement wall is higher (higher $\mathrm{D}_{\mathrm{L}}$ ), then the water level after 5 hours may be 50 cm -but still less than the water level outside the wall.

It is important to recognize that the failure of the water to equilibrate across the wall in our examples was not a function of the water per se but of three parameters: (1) the relatively low leakiness of the basement wall (low $\mathrm{D}_{\mathrm{L}}$ ), (2) the modest $100-\mathrm{cm}$ pressure gradient driving water across the wall into the basement (low $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{A}_{\mathrm{CO}}}$ ), and (3) the high capacity of the pump for removing water (CO binding capacity of Hb ). Obviously if the wall were substantially leakier, if the pressure gradient were substantially higher, or if the pump were substantially slower, the water level in the basement could have risen to as much as 100 cm during the 5 -hour period-but never more. However, given low driving pressure and the high pump capacity, the total amount of water that entered the basement during the 5 -hour period was limited by the leakiness of the basement wall. If the leakiness had been twice as great, the amount of water entering the basement during the 5 -hour period also would have been twice as great. Thus, the entry of water was leakiness limited.

$$
\text { Overall } \dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{CO}}=\dot{\mathrm{Q}} \cdot\left(\mathrm{C}_{\mathrm{C}_{\mathrm{CO}}}^{\prime \prime}-\mathrm{C}_{\mathrm{C}_{\mathrm{CO}}}^{\prime}\right)
$$

Overall $\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{CO}}$ is the total flow of CO along the entire length of all capillaries throughout the lungs, $\dot{\mathrm{Q}}$ is cardiac output, $\mathrm{C}_{\mathrm{C}_{\mathrm{CO}}}$ is the CO content of blood at the end of the pulmonary capillary (dissolved and bound to Hb ), and $\mathrm{C}_{\mathrm{C}_{\mathrm{CO}}}$ is the CO content of mixed-venous blood at the beginning of the capillary. If we assume that $\mathrm{C}_{\mathrm{C}_{\mathrm{CO}}}$ is zero, then Equation 30-17 simplifies to

$$
\text { Overall } \dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{CO}}=\dot{\mathrm{Q}} \cdot \mathrm{C}_{\mathrm{C}_{\mathrm{CO}}}^{\prime}
$$

Of course, this overall $\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{CO}}$, computed from the Fick principle, must be the same as the sum of the individual diffusion events, computed from Fick's law (analogous to Equation 30-12):

$$
\begin{aligned}
& \text { Fick principle } \\
& \text { All pieces of alveolar wall wall times in respiratory } \\
& \text { of } \mathrm{Q} \cdot \mathrm{C}_{\mathrm{C}_{\mathrm{CO}}}^{\prime}=\sum \sum \sum\left(\mathrm{DL}_{\mathrm{CO}} \cdot\left(\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{A}_{\mathrm{CO}}}-\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{C}_{\mathrm{CO}}}\right)\right)
\end{aligned}
$$

How does CO uptake depend on $\mathrm{DL}_{\mathrm{CO}}$ and $\dot{\mathrm{Q}}$ ? For basal conditions, we assume that $\mathrm{DL}_{\mathrm{CO}}$ and $\dot{\mathrm{Q}}$ each have relative values of 1 and that the curve labeled " $\mathrm{D}_{\mathrm{L}}=1$ " in Figure 30-5C describes the trajectory of $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{C}_{\mathrm{CO}}}$. As a result, $\mathrm{C}_{\mathrm{C}_{\mathrm{CO}}}$ also has a relative value of 1 . According to the Fick principle, the total amount of CO moving into the blood along the capillary is

$$
\begin{aligned}
\text { Overall } \dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{CO}} & =\dot{\mathrm{Q}} \cdot \mathrm{C}_{\mathrm{C}_{\mathrm{CO}}}^{\prime} \\
& =1 \cdot 1 \\
& =1
\end{aligned}
$$

What would happen if we kept $\dot{\mathrm{Q}}$ constant, but doubled $\mathrm{DL}_{\mathrm{CO}}$ ? Fick's law (see Equation 30-19) predicts that the flow of CO into the blood for each diffusion event along the capillary would double. Thus, along the entire capillary, $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{C}_{\mathrm{CO}}}$ would rise twice as steeply (see Fig. 30-5C, curve labeled " $\mathrm{D}_{\mathrm{L}}$ $=2$ ") as before. As a result, $\bigcirc \mathrm{N} 30-4 \mathrm{C}_{\mathrm{C}_{\mathrm{CO}}}$ and thus $\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{CO}}$ would also double.

Reducing $\mathrm{DL}_{\mathrm{CO}}$ by half would have the opposite effect: $\mathrm{C}_{\mathrm{C}_{\mathrm{CO}}}$ and thus $\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{CO}}$ would also halve (see Fig. 30-5C, bottom curve). Therefore, CO uptake is proportional to $\mathrm{DL}_{\mathrm{CO}}$ over a wide range of $\mathrm{DL}_{\mathrm{CO}}$ values (Table 30-1, upper half). Of course, if it were possible to make $\mathrm{DL}_{\mathrm{CO}}$ extremely high, then capillary $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}}$ would rise so fast that CO would equilibrate with the Hb before the end of the capillary, and capillary $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}}$ would reach alveolar $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}}$ (see Fig. 30-5C, top curve). However, for realistic values of $\mathrm{DL}_{\mathrm{CO}}$-as well as low alveolar $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}}$ levels and normal Hb concentrations- CO would fail to reach equilibrium by the end of the capillary.

How would alteration of blood flow affect $\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{CO}}$ ? If $\dot{\mathrm{Q}}$ were halved and the dimensions of the capillary remained constant, then the contact time of the blood with the alveolar capillary would double. Thus, at any distance down the capillary, twice as much cumulative time would be available for CO diffusion. The trajectory of capillary $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}}$ versus distance would be twice as steep (see Fig. 30-5D, curve labeled

TABLE 30-1 Alveolar Transport of CO

| :--: | :--: | :--: | :--: | :--: | :--: | :--: |
| Vary $\mathrm{D}_{\mathrm{L}}(\dot{\mathrm{Q}}$ constant) | 2 | 1 |  | 2 |  | 2 |
|  | 1 | 1 |  | 1 |  | 1 |
|  | $1 / 2$ | 1 |  | $1 / 2$ |  | $1 / 2$ |
| Vary $\dot{\mathrm{Q}}\left(\mathrm{D}_{\mathrm{L}}\right.$ constant) | 1 | $1 / 2$ |  | 2 |  | 1 |
|  | 1 | 1 |  | 1 |  | 1 |
|  | 1 | 2 |  | $1 / 2$ |  | 1 |

The values in the table are all relative to "control" values of unity. $\mathrm{C}_{\mathrm{C}_{\mathrm{CO}}}$ CO content of the blood at the end of the capillary; $\mathrm{D}_{\mathrm{L}}$, diffusing capacity; $\mathrm{D}_{\mathrm{L}_{\mathrm{CO}}}$, diffusing capacity of the lungs for CO ; $\dot{\mathrm{Q}}$, cardiac output; $\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{CO}}$, overall rate of CO uptake by the pulmonary-capillary blood.
" $\dot{\mathrm{Q}}=0.5$ ") as in the basal state (curve labeled " $\dot{\mathrm{Q}}=1$ "), and $\mathrm{C}_{\mathrm{C}_{\mathrm{CO}}}$ would also be twice as great. However, because we achieved this increase in $\mathrm{C}_{\mathrm{C}_{\mathrm{CO}}}$ by cutting $\dot{\mathrm{Q}}$ in half, the product $\dot{\mathrm{Q}} \cdot \mathrm{C}_{\mathrm{C}_{\mathrm{CO}}}=\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{CO}}$ would be the same as that in the basal state (see Table 30-1, lower half).

Doubling of blood flow would cause capillary $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}}$ to rise only half as steeply (see Fig. 30-5D, bottom curve) as in the basal state, but still would have no effect on $\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{CO}}$. Thus, for the range of $\mathrm{D}_{\mathrm{L}}$ and $\dot{\mathrm{Q}}$ values in this example, CO uptake is unaffected by changes in blood flow. Of course, if we were to reduce $\dot{\mathrm{Q}}$ to extremely low values, then capillary $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}}$ would reach alveolar $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}}$ by the end of the capillary (see Fig. 30-5D, top curve).

In our example, we have assumed that the $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{C}_{\mathrm{CO}}}$ profile along the capillary is linear and that changes in $\dot{\mathrm{Q}}$ do not affect capillary dimensions. (5) N30-5 In fact, these assumptions are not entirely valid. Nevertheless, the uptake of CO is more or less proportional to the $\mathrm{D}_{\mathrm{L}}$ for CO , and rather insensitive to perfusion. Therefore, we say that the uptake of CO is diffusion limited because it is the diffusing capacity that predominantly limits CO transport. We can judge whether the transport of a gas is predominantly diffusion limited by comparing the partial pressure of the gas at the end of the pulmonary capillary with the alveolar partial pressure. If the gas does not reach diffusion equilibrium (i.e., if the end-capillary partial pressure fails to reach the alveolar partial pressure), then transport is predominantly diffusion limited. However, if the gas does reach diffusion equilibrium, then its transport is perfusion limited, as discussed next.

## Perfusion normally limits the uptake of $\mathrm{N}_{2} \mathrm{O}$ from alveolar air to blood

Unlike CO, nitrous oxide ("laughing gas," $\mathrm{N}_{2} \mathrm{O}$ ) does not bind to Hb . Therefore, when a subject inhales $\mathrm{N}_{2} \mathrm{O}$, the gas enters the blood plasma and the RBC cytoplasm but has nowhere else to go (Fig. 30-6A). Consequently, as blood courses down the pulmonary capillary, the concentration of free $\mathrm{N}_{2} \mathrm{O}$-and thus capillary $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{N}_{2} \mathrm{O}}\left(\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{C}_{\mathrm{N}_{2} \mathrm{O}}}\right)$-rises very rapidly (see Fig. 30-6B). By the time the blood is $\sim 10 \%$ of the way along the capillary, $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{C}_{\mathrm{N}_{2} \mathrm{O}}}$ has reached alveolar $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{N}_{2} \mathrm{O}}\left(\mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{N}_{2} \mathrm{O}}\right)$, and $\mathrm{N}_{2} \mathrm{O}$ is thus in diffusion equilibrium between alveolus and blood. The reason $\mathrm{N}_{2} \mathrm{O}$ reaches diffusion equilibriumwhereas CO does not-is not that its $\mathrm{DL}_{\mathrm{N}_{2} \mathrm{O}}$ is particularly high, or that we chose a high inspired $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{N}_{2} \mathrm{O}}$. The key difference is that Hb does not bind to $\mathrm{N}_{2} \mathrm{O}$.

## N30-4 Assumptions Underlying Figure 30-5C

## Contributed by Emile Boulpaep and Walter Boron

In our analysis-in which we are halving or doubling $\mathrm{D}_{\mathrm{L}}$-we are assuming that $D_{L}$ is strictly a reflection of Fick's law. That is, we are assuming that $D_{L}$ equals $D_{M}$, and we are ignoring the buffering of CO by Hb .

In our example in which we have doubled $D_{L}$ (curve labeled " $D_{L}=2$ " in Fig. 30-5C), we showed that the partial pressure of $\mathrm{CO}\left(\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}}\right)$ at the end of the pulmonary capillary has doubled. The CO content of the blood at the end of the pulmonary capillary $\left(\mathrm{C}_{\mathrm{C}}\right)_{\mathrm{co}}$ also very nearly doubles. $\mathrm{C}_{\mathrm{C}} \mathrm{C}_{\mathrm{co}}$ has two components, the CO freely dissolved in blood and the CO bound to Hb . The latter is by far the more important. At the low capillary $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}}$ values in our example, the relationship between capillary $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}}$ and the CO saturation of Hb is approximately linear. According to Henry's law, the relationship between $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}}$ and dissolved CO is also linear. Thus, the total CO content of blood at the end of the capillary is approximately proportional to $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}}$ at the end of the capillary in our example. Thus, when $D_{L}$ doubles, both the $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}}$ and the total CO content of the blood also double at the end of the pulmonary capillary.

## N30-5 Assumptions Underlying Figure 30-5D

## Contributed by Emile Boulpaep and Walter Boron

In analyzing Figure 30-5D, we have assumed that the linear velocity of the pulmonary-capillary blood is proportional to the flow $(\dot{\mathrm{Q}})$. Thus, when the flow doubles, we are assuming that the contact time of the blood with the alveolar capillary would fall by half.

This assumption requires that the dimensions of the vessel be fixed (i.e., that neither the diameter nor the length of the capillary increase) and that the increase in flow not recruit new blood vessels. In fact, as we shall see in Chapter 31, neither of these assumptions is correct. First, if we start from a low perfusion pressure, increasing the perfusion pressure causes the recruitment of pulmonary vessels that previously were not conducting blood. Second, pulmonary vessels are highly compliant, so that increases in perfusion pressure cause the vessels to dilate. For both reasons (i.e., recruitment and dilation), pulmonary vascular resistance falls as the perfusion pressure increases. Thus, if the flow doubles, the contact time of the blood with the alveolar capillary will fall, but by less than half.

![bo97465312c5](bo97465312c5.jpg)

Figure 30-6 Diffusion of $\mathrm{N}_{2} \mathrm{O}$. In $\mathbf{B}, \mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{N}_{2} \mathrm{O}}$ is alveolar $\mathrm{R}_{\mathrm{N}_{2} \mathrm{O}}$ and $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{N}_{2} \mathrm{OO}}$ is capillary $\mathrm{R}_{\mathrm{N}_{2} \mathrm{O}}$. In $\mathbf{C}$, the blood flow $\dot{\mathrm{Q}}$ has a relative value of 1 . In $\mathbf{D}$, the diffusing capacity $D_{L}$ has a relative value of 1 . The $\mathrm{N}_{2} \mathrm{O}$ content at the end of the capillary is proportional to $\mathrm{R}_{\mathrm{N}_{2} \mathrm{O}}$.

How does $\mathrm{N}_{2} \mathrm{O}$ uptake by the lungs depend on $\mathrm{DL}_{\mathrm{N}_{2} \mathrm{O}}$ and $\dot{\mathrm{Q}}$ ? If we assume that the $\mathrm{N}_{2} \mathrm{O}$ content of the mixedvenous blood entering the pulmonary capillary $\left(\mathrm{CV}_{\mathrm{N}_{2} \mathrm{O}}\right)$ is zero, then

$$
\text { Overall } \dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{N}_{2} \mathrm{O}}=\dot{\mathrm{Q}} \cdot \mathrm{Cc}_{\mathrm{N}_{2} \mathrm{O}}^{\prime}
$$

$\mathrm{Cc}_{\mathrm{N}_{2} \mathrm{O}}^{\prime}$ is the $\mathrm{N}_{2} \mathrm{O}$ content of the blood at the end of the pulmonary capillary and represents entirely $\mathrm{N}_{2} \mathrm{O}$ physically dissolved in blood, which according to Henry's law (see Box $26-2$ ) is proportional to $\mathrm{Pc}_{\mathrm{N}_{2} \mathrm{O}}$. The overall $\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{N}_{2} \mathrm{O}}$ computed from the Fick principle in Equation 30-21 is the sum of individual diffusion events along the capillary:

![bo98465312c5](bo98465312c5.jpg)

Because $\mathrm{N}_{2} \mathrm{O}$ reached diffusion equilibrium at $\sim 10 \%$ of the way down the capillary (i.e., $\mathrm{Pc}_{\mathrm{N}_{2} \mathrm{O}}=\mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{N}_{2} \mathrm{O}}$ ), the
individual diffusion terms in Equation 30-22 equate to zero for the distal $90 \%$ of the capillary?

We can approach the uptake of $\mathrm{N}_{2} \mathrm{O}$ in the same way we did the uptake of CO. We begin, under basal conditions, with relative values of 1 for $\mathrm{DL}_{\mathrm{N}_{2} \mathrm{O}}, \dot{\mathrm{Q}}$, and end-capillary $\mathrm{N}_{2} \mathrm{O}$ content $\left(\mathrm{Cc}_{\mathrm{N}_{2} \mathrm{O}}^{\prime}\right)$. Thus, the initial $\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{N}_{2} \mathrm{O}}$ is $\dot{\mathrm{Q}} \times \mathrm{Cc}_{\mathrm{N}_{2} \mathrm{O}}^{\prime}=1$.

Figure 30-6C shows that doubling of $\mathrm{DL}_{\mathrm{N}_{2} \mathrm{O}}$ doubles the flow of $\mathrm{N}_{2} \mathrm{O}$ into the blood for each diffusion event, causing $\mathrm{Pc}_{\mathrm{N}_{2} \mathrm{O}}$ to rise twice as steeply as before along the capillary. However, even though this doubling of $\mathrm{DL}_{\mathrm{N}_{2} \mathrm{O}}$ causes $\mathrm{N}_{2} \mathrm{O}$ to come into diffusion equilibrium twice as fast as before, it has no effect either on the $\mathrm{N}_{2} \mathrm{O}$ content of end-capillary blood or on $\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{N}_{2} \mathrm{O}}$, both of which remain at 1 . Cutting $\mathrm{DL}_{\mathrm{N}_{2} \mathrm{O}}$ in half also would have no effect on $\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{N}_{2} \mathrm{O}}$. Thus, $\mathrm{N}_{2} \mathrm{O}$ uptake is insensitive to changes in $\mathrm{DL}_{\mathrm{N}_{2} \mathrm{O}}$ at least over the range of values that we examined (Table 30-2, upper half). In other words, the uptake of $\mathrm{N}_{2} \mathrm{O}$ is not diffusion limited.

What would be the effect of reducing $\dot{\mathrm{Q}}$ by half while holding $\mathrm{DL}_{\mathrm{N}_{2} \mathrm{O}}$ constant? If we assume that capillary dimensions remain constant, then halving of $\dot{\mathrm{Q}}$ would double the contact time of blood with the alveolus and make the $\mathrm{Pc}_{\mathrm{N}_{2} \mathrm{O}}$ trajectory along the capillary twice as steep as before (see

TABLE 30-2 Alveolar Transport of $\mathrm{N}_{2} \mathrm{O}$

|  | $\mathrm{D}_{\mathrm{L}_{\mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O}}}$ | $\dot{\mathrm{Q}}$ | $\times$ | $\mathrm{C}_{\mathrm{O}_{2} \mathrm{O}_{2}}$ | $=$ | $\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{O}_{2} \mathrm{O}}$ |
| :--: | :--: | :--: | :--: | :--: | :--: | :--: |
| Vary $\mathrm{D}_{\mathrm{L}}$ (Q constant) | 2 | 1 |  | 1 |  | 1 |
|  | 1 | 1 |  | 1 |  | 1 |
|  | $1 / 2$ | 1 |  | 1 |  | 1 |

|  | 1 | 2 |  | 1 |  | 2 |

Fig. 30-6D). Nevertheless, $\mathrm{C}_{\mathrm{O}_{2} \mathrm{O}_{2}}$ remains unchanged at 1 . However, because we reduced Q by half, $\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{N}_{2} \mathrm{O}}$ also falls by half. Conversely, doubling of Q causes $\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{N}_{2} \mathrm{O}}$ to double. Thus, $\mathrm{N}_{2} \mathrm{O}$ uptake is more or less proportional to blood flow (i.e., perfusion) over a wide range of Q values (see Table 30-2, lower half). For this reason, we say that $\mathrm{N}_{2} \mathrm{O}$ transport is predominantly perfusion limited. The transport of a gas is predominantly perfusion limited if the gas in the capillary comes into equilibrium with the gas in the alveolar air by the end of the capillary.

## In principle, CO transport could become perfusion limited and $\mathrm{N}_{2} \mathrm{O}$ transport could become diffusion limited under special conditions

Although normally CO transport is diffusion limited and $\mathrm{N}_{2} \mathrm{O}$ transport is perfusion limited, changes in certain parameters could, at least in theory, make CO uptake perfusion limited, or make $\mathrm{N}_{2} \mathrm{O}$ uptake diffusion limited. To illustrate, we introduce an analogy (Fig. 30-7): workers at a railroad siding trying to load boxes (transport gas at a rate $\dot{\mathrm{V}}$ ) onto the cars of a passing train. Each worker (the diffusive event) has a limited rate for putting boxes on the train, and the total rate is the sum for all the workers $\left(D_{L}\right)$. Each railway car (the RBC) has a limited capacity for holding boxes, and the total capacity is the sum for all cars (Hb concentration). Finally, because the train is moving (the perfusion rate, $\dot{\mathrm{Q}}$ ), a limited time is available to load each railway car.

First imagine that the speed of the train perfectly matches the number of workers (see Fig. 30-7A). Thus, all workers are always fully occupied, and the railway cars depart the siding fully loaded; the last box is put on each car just as the car leaves the siding. Any decrease in worker number, any increase in the carrying capacity of each car, or any increase in train speed causes the railway cars to leave the siding at least partially empty. Thus, if we fix train speed at "normal," decreasing the number of workers below "normal" (see Fig. 30-7B) would cause a proportional decrease in the shipping rate-the number of boxes the train carries away per hour. In other words, when the number of workers is between zero and normal, the shipping rate is worker (diffusion) limited.

Now return to the original "perfect-match" condition in which both worker number and train speed are normal. Increasing worker number at constant train speed has no effect on either the number of boxes loaded onto each railway car (which remain filled to capacity) or the shipping rate (see Fig. 30-7C). In this higher range of worker number, the shipping rate is no longer limited by the workers, but by the speed of the train or by the carrying capacity of the railway
cars. Thus, we could say that the shipping rate is speed (perfusion) limited, although it would be equally true to say that it is carrying-capacity $(\mathrm{Hb})$ limited.

Let us return again to the original condition in which worker number perfectly matches train speed. Increasing train speed while fixing worker number at the normal value causes railway cars to leave the siding partially empty (see Fig. 30-7D). However, shipping rate is unaffected because the normal number of boxes is simply distributed over a greater number of cars. Under these conditions, shipping rate is again worker (diffusion) limited because increasing the number of workers would proportionally increase shipping rate. In fact, whenever you see cars leaving the siding only partially filled, you can conclude that shipping rate is worker (diffusion) limited, regardless of whether this situation arose because of a decrease in worker number, an increase in train speed, or an increase in car carrying-capacity.

Finally, let us again return to the perfectly matched initial condition and now decrease train speed while holding worker number fixed at the normal value. As velocity decreases below normal, shipping rate decreases proportionally (see Fig. 30-7E), even though the railway cars leave the siding fully loaded. Thus, shipping rate is speed (perfusion) limited. It would be equally true to characterize the system as being limited by carrying capacity $(\mathrm{Hb})$. In fact, whenever you see cars leaving the siding fully filled, you can conclude that shipping rate is speed (perfusion) limited, regardless of whether this situation arose because of an increase in worker number, a decrease in train speed, or a decrease in car carrying capacity.

The reader is now in a position to consider factors that could render CO transport predominantly perfusion limited or $\mathrm{N}_{2} \mathrm{O}$ transport diffusion limited. Several changes to the system would cause CO transport to be no longer limited by $\mathrm{DL}_{\mathrm{CO}}$. 3 N30-6 Conversely, several other changes would cause the transport of $\mathrm{N}_{2} \mathrm{O}$ to be no longer limited by $\dot{\mathrm{Q}}$.

## The uptake of CO provides an estimate of $\mathrm{D}_{\mathrm{L}}$

Because the pulmonary diffusing capacity plays such an important role in determining the partial-pressure profile of a gas along the pulmonary capillary, being able to measure $\mathrm{D}_{\mathrm{L}}$ would be valuable. Moreover, an approach that is easily applicable to patients could be useful both as a diagnostic tool and as a means to follow the progression of diseases affecting $\mathrm{D}_{\mathrm{L}}$.

We have already observed (see Equation 30-9) that we can use Fick's law to compute the overall uptake of a gas if we summate many individual diffusion events for all pieces of alveolar wall and all times in the respiratory cycle:

$$
\text { Overall } \dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\text {gas }}=\sum_{\substack{\text { wall }}}^{\substack{\text { All pieces } \\ \text { of alveolar } \\ \text { of alveolar }}} \frac{\text { All times }}{\text { in respiratory }}$
If we could ignore spatial and temporal nonuniformities, then we could eliminate the two troublesome $\Sigma$ symbols and compute an overall $\mathrm{DL}_{\text {gas }}$ from the overall $\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\text {gas }}$. We could accommodate modest spatial and temporal variations in $\mathrm{PA}_{\text {gas }}$ by computing an average alveolar partial pressure

# N30-6 Making CO Uptake Perfusion Limited or Making $\mathrm{N}_{2} \mathrm{O}$ Uptake Diffusion Limited 

## Contributed by Emile Boulpaep and Walter Boron

After analyzing our railway car analogy, we are now in a position to consider factors that could-at least in principle-render the transport of CO predominantly perfusion limited, or that couldat least in principle-render the transport of $\mathrm{N}_{2} \mathrm{O}$ predominantly diffusion limited. Let us first consider CO uptake.

At least six changes to the system (eTable 30-1) could cause pulmonary capillary $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}}\left(\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{rcO}}\right)$ to reach alveolar $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}}\left(\mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{CO}}\right)$ by the end of the capillary and thus cause CO transport no longer to be limited by $\mathrm{DL}_{\mathrm{CO}}$.

1. Increase $\mathbf{D L}_{\mathbf{C O}}$ (i.e., increase the number of workers in the analogy of Fig. 30-7). This change would increase the flow of CO into the capillary for each diffusion event and thus increase both the rate at which the Hb would load up with CO and the rate at which free CO (proportional to $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}}$ ) could accumulate in the capillary. Another way of increasing the flow of CO into the capillary would be to increase $\mathbf{P}_{\mathbf{C O}}$, which would increase the driving force for CO diffusion. Keep in mind that it is the product of $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}}$ and $\mathrm{DL}_{\mathrm{CO}}$ that-according to Fick's law-determines diffusion.
2. Decrease (or totally eliminate) Hb (i.e., decrease the carrying capacity of each railway car). In the extreme, eliminating Hb would force all incoming CO to remain free in solution, so that free [CO] (i.e., $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{rcO}}$ ) would rapidly rise along the pulmonary capillary.
3. Increase the CO content of mixed-venous blood (partially preload railway cars with dummy boxes before the cars enter the dock area). By partially loading the Hb with CO before the blood even came into contact with the alveoli, we would reduce the amount of additional CO that could bind to Hb before the $\mathrm{Hb}-\mathrm{CO}$ complex was in equilibrium with free CO. With fewer binding sites available for the CO diffusing in from the alveoli, the level of free CO would rise more rapidly. In the extreme, $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{rcO}}$ would reach $\mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{CO}}$ by the end of the capillary.
4. Decrease $\dot{\mathbf{Q}}$ (decrease train speed). By increasing the contact time of capillary blood with the alveoli, we would allow each bolus of capillary blood to undergo more diffusion events as it moves a certain distance along the length of the capillary. Thus, for a given distance traveled along the capillary, the blood would take up more CO, more

CO would bind to Hb , and more CO also would remain free-so that $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{rcO}}$ would more closely approach $\mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{CO}}$.
5. Lengthen the capillary (increase the length of the loading dock, with a proportional increase in the number of workers). The effect of this maneuver is the same as for point 4 above in that it increases the contact time of the blood with the pulmonary capillary.
6. Distend the capillary while keeping $\dot{\mathbf{Q}}$ constant (decrease train speed). In this case, we would decrease the linear velocity of the blood, increasing the contact time with the capillary. The effect, however, would not be quite as dramatic as that in point 4 because the increase in diameter would decrease the surface-to-volume ratio so that each bolus of blood would be able to avail itself of a smaller surface area for diffusion. Keep in mind that linear velocity will decrease with the square of the radius, whereas surface area will increase only linearly with area.
At least four changes to the system would cause the transport of $\mathrm{N}_{2} \mathrm{O}$, normally perfusion limited, to become diffusion limited (eTable 30-2). In other words, capillary $\mathrm{R}_{\mathrm{N} 2}$ would fail to reach alveolar $\mathrm{R}_{\mathrm{N} 2}$ by the end of the pulmonary capillary.

1. The first is to greatly decrease $\mathbf{D L}_{\mathbf{N}_{2} \mathrm{O}}$ (i.e., decrease the number of workers). The result will be to slow diffusion. In the extreme, we could decrease the flow of $\mathrm{N}_{2} \mathrm{O}$ so much that the $\mathrm{N}_{2} \mathrm{O}$ fails to achieve equilibrium by the end of the capillary. Another way of decreasing the flow of $\mathrm{N}_{2} \mathrm{O}$ into the capillary would be to decrease $\mathbf{P}_{\mathbf{N}_{2} \mathrm{O}}$, which would decrease the driving force for $\mathrm{N}_{2} \mathrm{O}$ diffusion. Keep in mind that it is the product of $\mathrm{R}_{\mathrm{N} 2}$ and $\mathbf{D L}_{\mathbf{N}_{2} \mathrm{O}}$ that-according to Fick's law-determines diffusion.
2. Increase $\mathbf{Q}$ (increase train speed). An increase in $\dot{\mathrm{Q}}$ increases the linear velocity of the blood and thus decreases the time available for $\mathrm{N}_{2} \mathrm{O}$ diffusion into a bolus of blood. If the increase in $\dot{\mathrm{Q}}$ is sufficiently large, then the amount of $\mathrm{N}_{2} \mathrm{O}$ diffusing into the lumen will be so small that capillary $\mathrm{R}_{\mathrm{N} 2}$ will never reach alveolar $\mathrm{R}_{\mathrm{N} 2}$. That is, the contact time will be insufficient for $\mathrm{N}_{2} \mathrm{O}$ to achieve equilibrium.
3. Shorten the capillary (decrease the length of the loading dock). Like the increase in $\dot{\mathrm{Q}}$, the decrease in capillary

## eTABLE 30-1 Changes that Could Theoretically Make the Transport of a Diffusion-Limited Gas (CO) Become Perfusion Limited

| $\uparrow \mathrm{D}_{\mathrm{L}}$ or $\uparrow \mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{CO}}$ ( $\uparrow$ number of workers) | If CO could enter rapidly enough, it could load the Hb with CO and cause free [CO] to rise rapidly. |
| $\downarrow[\mathrm{Hb}]$ ( $\downarrow$ capacity of each car) | Because carrying capacity for CO is reduced, a given rate of CO transport causes the concentration of free CO to rise rapidly. |
| $\uparrow \mathrm{Ce}_{\mathrm{CO}}$ (partially preload cars with dummy boxes) | If the Hb were sufficiently preloaded with CO, not much additional CO would need to enter to fully load the Hb . |
| $\downarrow \dot{\mathrm{Q}}$ ( $\downarrow$ train speed) | If the contact time of blood with alveolar air were sufficiently long, enough CO could enter to load the Hb , causing the concentration of free CO to rise rapidly. |
| $\uparrow$ Capillary length (lengthen loading dock) | The contact time of blood with alveolar air would be increased, so that Hb would eventually become loaded, as described above. |
| $\uparrow$ Capillary diameter at fixed $\dot{\mathrm{Q}}$ ( $\downarrow$ train speed) | As above, contact time would increase. |

length will decrease the contact time for a bolus of blood with the pulmonary capillary.
4. Decrease capillary diameter while keeping $\dot{Q}$ constant (increase train speed). This maneuver would increase the linear velocity of the blood, decreasing the contact time of a bolus of blood with the capillary. The effect might be even more dramatic than that in point 2 because the decrease in diameter would increase the surface-to-volume ratio so that
each bolus of blood would be able to avail itself of a greater surface area for diffusion.
In case you are wondering why the $\mathrm{N}_{2} \mathrm{O}$ analysis has two terms less than the CO analysis, one term is missing because $\mathrm{N}_{2} \mathrm{O}$ does not interact with Hb (point 2 for CO ), and a second term is missing because we cannot further decrease the mixedvenous $\mathrm{N}_{2} \mathrm{O}$ content below zero (point 3 for CO ).

Figure 30-7 Railway car analogy. Workers represent the diffusion of $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ across the alveolar wall. Railway cars represent the capacity of the blood to carry $\mathrm{O}_{2}$. The speed of the train represents blood flow.

$\left(\overline{\mathrm{P}}_{\mathrm{A}_{\text {gas }}}\right.$ ). Furthermore, if the partial-pressure profile of the gas along the capillary were linear, we could use an average capillary partial pressure $\left(\overline{\mathrm{P}}_{\mathrm{C}_{\text {gas }}}\right)$ as well. If we could identify a gas for which these assumptions are reasonable, we could simplify Equation 30-23 to an expression that is similar to the version of Fick's law with which we started this chapter (see Equation 30-7):

$$
\text { Overall } \dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{gas}}=\mathrm{DL}_{\mathrm{gas}} \cdot\left(\overline{\mathrm{P}}_{\mathrm{A}_{\mathrm{gas}}}-\overline{\mathrm{P}}_{\mathrm{C}_{\mathrm{gas}}}\right)
$$

Which gas could we use to estimate $\mathrm{D}_{\mathrm{L}}$ ? We certainly do not want to use $\mathrm{N}_{2} \mathrm{O}$, whose uptake is perfusion limited. After all, $\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{N}_{2} \mathrm{O}}$ is more or less proportional to changes in $\dot{\mathrm{Q}}$, but virtually insensitive to changes in $\mathrm{D}_{\mathrm{L}}$ (see Table 30-2). Viewed differently, the driving pressure between alveolus and capillary $\left(\mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{N}_{2} \mathrm{O}}-\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CN}_{2} \mathrm{O}}\right)$ is high at the beginning of the capillary but soon falls to zero (see Fig. 30-6B). Thus, it would be very difficult to pick a reasonable value for the average capillary $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{N}_{2} \mathrm{O}}$ to insert into Equation 30-24. However, CO is an excellent choice because its uptake is diffusion limited, so that changes in the parameter of interest (i.e., $\mathrm{D}_{\mathrm{L}}$ ) have nearly a proportionate effect on $\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{CO}}$. Viewed differently, the driving pressure between alveolus and capillary $\left(\mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{CO}}-\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CN}_{2}}\right)$ is nearly ideal because it falls more or less linearly as blood courses down the pulmonary capillary (see Fig. 30-5B). Thus, we might solve Equation 30-24 for $\mathrm{DL}_{\mathrm{CO}}$ :

$$
\mathrm{DL}_{\mathrm{CO}}=\frac{\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{CO}}}{\left(\overline{\mathrm{P}}_{\mathrm{A}_{\mathrm{CO}}}-\overline{\mathrm{P}}_{\mathrm{C}_{\mathrm{CO}}}\right)}
$$

Note that $\mathrm{DL}_{\mathrm{CO}}$ and $\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{CO}}$ are average values that reflect properties of all alveoli throughout both lungs at all times in the respiratory cycle. $\overline{\mathrm{P}}_{\mathrm{A}_{\mathrm{CO}}}$ reflects minor changes during the respiratory cycle, as well as more substantial variations in $\mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{CO}}$ from alveolus to alveolus due to local differences in ventilation and perfusion (see pp. 689-690). Finally, $\overline{\mathrm{P}}_{\mathrm{C}_{\mathrm{CO}}}$ reflects not only the small increase in $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{C}_{\mathrm{CO}}}$ as blood flows down the capillary, but also any CO that may be present in the mixed-venous blood that enters the pulmonary capillary. For nonsmokers who live in a nonpolluted environment, $\mathrm{PV}_{\mathrm{CO}}$ is nearly zero, and thus we often can ignore $\overline{\mathrm{P}}_{\mathrm{C}_{\mathrm{CO}}}$.

We will discuss two general methods for estimating $\mathrm{DL}_{\mathrm{CO}}$, the steady-state technique and the single-breath test. These tests, both using CO, are useful for estimation of pulmonary diffusing capacity in a clinical setting, even among very ill patients in the intensive care unit.

In the steady-state technique (Fig. 30-8), the subject breathes a low-CO air mixture (e.g., $0.1 \%$ to $0.2 \%$ ) for approximately a dozen breaths to allow $\mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{CO}}$ to stabilize. Calculating $\mathrm{DL}_{\mathrm{CO}}$ requires at least two measurements: $\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{CO}}$ and $\overline{\mathrm{P}}_{\mathrm{A}_{\mathrm{CO}}}$. We compute the rate of CO uptake from the difference between the amounts of CO in inspired air versus expired air over time. We can directly measure $\overline{\mathrm{P}}_{\mathrm{A}_{\mathrm{CO}}}$ in an alveolar-air sample (see pp. 676-677). $\mathrm{D}_{\mathrm{L}}$ is then $\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{CO}} / \overline{\mathrm{P}}_{\mathrm{A}_{\mathrm{CO}}}$. If the subject happens to be a smoker or to live in a polluted environment, an accurate measurement of $\mathrm{D}_{\mathrm{L}}$ requires a venous blood sample to estimate the $\mathrm{PV}_{\mathrm{CO}}$. In this case, we calculate $\mathrm{D}_{\mathrm{L}}$ from the more complete expression in Equation 30-25.

In the single-breath technique (Fig. 30-9), the subject makes a maximal expiratory effort to residual volume (see
![bo104465312c5](bo104465312c5.jpg)

Figure 30-8 Steady-state method for estimating $\mathrm{DL}_{\mathrm{CO}}$.
p. 602) and then makes a maximal inspiration of air containing CO and holds the breath for 10 seconds. The inhaled air is a mixture of dilute CO (e.g., $0.3 \%$ ) plus a gas such as helium, which has a low water solubility and thus a negligible transport across the blood-gas barrier (see Equation 30-5). We can use helium to compute the extent to which the inhaled $\mathrm{CO} / \mathrm{He}$ mixture becomes diluted as it first enters the alveoli, and also to calculate the alveolar volume into which the $\mathrm{CO} / \mathrm{He}$ mixture distributes (see p. 602). This information allows us to calculate two crucial parameters at the beginning of the breath-holding period: (1) $\mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{CO}}$ and (2) the amount of CO in the alveoli. During the 10 seconds of breath holding, some of the inhaled CO diffuses into the blood. The greater the $\mathrm{DL}_{\mathrm{CO}}$, the greater the diffusion of CO , and the more $\mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{CO}}$ falls. As the subject exhales, we obtain a sample of alveolar air and use it to determine two crucial parameters at the end of the breath-holding period: (1) $\mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{CO}}$ and (2) the amount of CO in the alveoli. The initial and final $\mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{CO}}$ values allow us to compute $\overline{\mathrm{P}}_{\mathrm{A}_{\mathrm{CO}}}$. The initial and final alveolar CO amounts allow us to compute $\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{CO}}$ during the 10 -second breath-holding period.

Bear in mind that the value of $\mathrm{DL}_{\mathrm{CO}}$ determined by use of either of these methods is an average pulmonary diffusing capacity. As discussed in Chapter 31, pulmonary disease can cause ventilation to become nonuniform, making it difficult to obtain alveolar-air samples that are representative of the entire lung. N30-7 A normal value for $\mathrm{DL}_{\mathrm{CO}}$ is $\sim 25 \mathrm{~mL}$ CO taken up per minute for each millimeter of mercury of partial pressure driving CO diffusion and for each milliliter of blood having a normal Hb content. This value of $\mathrm{DL}_{\mathrm{CO}}$

# N30-7 Effect of Nonuniformity of Ventilation on Alveolar-Air Samples 

## Contributed by Emile Boulpaep and Walter Boron

In Chapter 31, we will introduce the single-breath $\mathrm{N}_{2}$-washout technique and Fowler method for measuring anatomical dead space (see pp. 676-677). In this approach, the subject inhales one breath of $100 \% \mathrm{O}_{2}$, and we assume that the inspired $100 \%$ $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ distributes evenly throughout all the alveoli of the lungs. If ventilation is indeed uniform, the inspired $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ dilutes alveolar $\mathrm{N}_{2}$ uniformly in all regions of the lung. Thus, when the subject exhales, the air emerging from the alveolar air spaces should have a uniform $\left[\mathrm{N}_{2}\right]$, and the plateau of the single-breath $\mathrm{N}_{2}$ washout should be flat, as shown by the part of the red curve in Figure 31-2C labeled "Pure alveolar air."

However, if the alveoli are unevenly ventilated, the inhaled $100 \% \mathrm{O}_{2}$ will not distribute uniformly throughout the lungs and therefore will not uniformly dilute the pre-existing alveolar $\mathrm{N}_{2}$. Regions of the lung that are relatively hypoventilated will receive
relatively less $100 \% \mathrm{O}_{2}$ during the single inspiration, so that they will be relatively poor in $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ but rich in $\mathrm{N}_{2}$. Conversely, hyperventilated regions will receive relatively more of the inhaled $100 \%$ $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ and hence will be $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ rich and $\mathrm{N}_{2}$ poor. During the expiration, we no longer observe a plateau for $\left[\mathrm{N}_{2}\right]$. Why? After exhalation of the anatomical dead space, the first alveolar air out of the lungs is dominated by the $\mathrm{O}_{2}$-rich $/ \mathrm{N}_{2}$-poor gas coming from the relatively hyperventilated airways-which inflate and deflate relatively quickly. As the expiration continues, the alveolar air gradually becomes dominated more and more by the $\mathrm{O}_{2}$-poor $/ \mathrm{N}_{2}$ rich gas from the hypoventilated airways, which inflate and deflate relatively slowly. Because of this shift from hyperventilated to hypoventilated regions, the $\left[\mathrm{N}_{2}\right]$ gradually creeps upward-that is, there is no clear plateau-in subjects with a sufficiently high nonuniformity of ventilation.

![bo105465312c5](bo105465312c5.jpg)

Figure 30-9 Single-breath method for estimating $\mathrm{DL}_{\mathrm{CO}}$.

$\mathrm{DL}_{\mathrm{CO}}$, diffusion capacity of $\mathrm{CO} ; \mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$, partial pressure of $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ in alveolar air; $\mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ partial pressure of $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ in alveolar air; $\dot{\mathrm{O}}$, cardiac output.
depends not only on $\mathrm{D}_{\mathrm{M}}$ (i.e., the "membrane" or truly diffusive component of $\mathrm{D}_{\mathrm{L}}$ ) in Equation 30-15 but also on $\theta \cdot \mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{c}}$. For CO transport, $\mathrm{D}_{\mathrm{M}}$ and $\theta \cdot \mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{c}}$ are each $\sim 50 \mathrm{~mL}$ $\mathrm{CO} /(\min \cdot \mathrm{mL}$ blood $):$

$$
\frac{1}{\mathrm{D}_{\mathrm{L}}}=\frac{1}{50}+\frac{1}{50}=\frac{1}{25}
$$

Thus, $1 /\left(\theta \cdot \mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{c}}\right)$ makes a major contribution to the final $\mathrm{DL}_{\mathrm{CO}}$. Because $\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{c}}$ is proportional to the Hb content of the blood, and because Hb content is decreased in anemia, a subject can have a reduced $\mathrm{DL}_{\mathrm{CO}}$ even though the diffusion pathways in the lung (i.e., $\mathrm{D}_{\mathrm{M}}$ ) are perfectly normal. Recall
that $1 /\left(\theta \cdot \mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{c}}\right)$ makes an insignificant contribution to the $\mathrm{D}_{\mathrm{L}}$ for $\mathrm{O}_{2}$. Nevertheless, it is the $\mathrm{D}_{\mathrm{L}}$ for CO - and not that for $\mathrm{O}_{2}$-that one uses for a clinical index of diffusing capacity. Table 30-3 summarizes several factors that can affect the calculated $\mathrm{DL}_{\mathrm{CO}}$.

## For both $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ and $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$, transport is normally perfusion limited

Uptake of $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ Blood enters the pulmonary capillaries (Fig. 30-10A) with the $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ of mixed-venous blood, typically 40 mm Hg . Capillary $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ reaches the alveolar $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ of $\sim 100 \mathrm{~mm} \mathrm{Hg}$ about one third of the way along the capillary

# N30-10 Effect of $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ on the Diffusing Capacity 

## Contributed by Emile Boulpaep and Walter Boron

An increase in arterial $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ causes an increase in pulmonarycapillary blood $\left(\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{c}}\right)$ and thus an increase in the $\left(\theta \cdot \mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{c}}\right)$ term of $\mathrm{D}_{\mathrm{L}}$ :

$$
\frac{1}{\mathrm{D}_{\mathrm{L}}} \cong \frac{1}{\mathrm{D}_{\mathrm{M}}}+\frac{1}{\theta \cdot \mathrm{~V}_{\mathrm{c}}}
$$

The preceding equation is a repetition of Equation 30-15. We will see in Chapter 31 that a high $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ causes pulmonary vasoconstriction (see p. 687). Evidently, the predominant effect of increasing $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ (e.g., caused by breathing $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ ) is to constrict pulmonary venules more than arterioles, thereby distending the pulmonary capillaries and thus increasing $\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{c}}$. Note that according to this scenario, $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ has no effect on $\mathrm{D}_{\mathrm{M}}$ (i.e., it has no direct effect on diffusion per se).
