Schnermann J, Chou C-L, Ma T, et al: Defective proximal tubular fluid reabsorption in transgenic aquaporin-1 null mice. Proc Natl Acad Sci U S A 95:9660-9664, 1998.

![bo106465312c5](bo106465312c5.jpg)

Figure 30-10 Diffusion of $\mathrm{O}_{2}$. In $\mathbf{A}$, as the RBC enters the capillary, $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ occupies three of the four sites on Hb . In $\mathbf{B}$ through $\mathbf{D}, \mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ is alveolar $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$. In $\mathbf{B}$ and $\mathbf{D}_{L} \dot{\mathrm{Q}}$ is constant.
(see Fig. 30-10B, black curve). This $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ profile along the pulmonary capillary is intermediate between that of CO in Figure 30-5B (where CO fails to reach diffusion equilibrium) and $\mathrm{N}_{2} \mathrm{O}$ in Figure 30-6B (where $\mathrm{N}_{2} \mathrm{O}$ reaches diffusion equilibrium $\sim 10 \%$ of the way along the capillary). The transport of $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ is similar to that of CO in that both molecules bind to Hb . Why, then, does $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ reach diffusion equilibrium, whereas CO does not?

The uptake of $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ differs from that of CO in three important respects. First, Hb that enters the pulmonary capillary is already heavily preloaded with $\mathrm{O}_{2}$. Because Hb in the mixed-venous blood is $\sim 75 \%$ saturated with $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ (see p. 650) -versus $\sim 0 \%$ for CO-the available $\mathrm{O}_{2}$-binding capacity of Hb is relatively low. Second, because the alveolar $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ is rather high (i.e., $\sim 100 \mathrm{~mm} \mathrm{Hg}$ )-versus $<1 \mathrm{~mm} \mathrm{Hg}$ for CO-the alveolar-blood $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ gradient is large (i.e., $\sim 60 \mathrm{~mm} \mathrm{Hg}$ ) and the initial rate of $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ diffusion from the alveolus into pulmonary-capillary blood is immense. Third, $\mathrm{D}_{\mathrm{L}}$ for $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ is higher than that for CO , owing to a greater $\theta \cdot \mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{c}}$. As a result of these three factors, Hb in pulmonary-capillary blood rapidly approaches its equilibrium carrying capacity for $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ along the first third of the capillary. Because capillary $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ reaches alveolar $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{D}_{2}}, \mathrm{O}_{2}$ transport is perfusion limited, as is the case for $\mathrm{N}_{2} \mathrm{O}$. Because $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ normally reaches diffusion equilibrium so soon along the capillary, the lung has a tremendous $\mathbf{D}_{\mathrm{L}}$ reserve for $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ uptake. (T) N30-8 Even if we reduce $\mathrm{DL}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ by half, $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ still reaches diffusion equilibrium about two thirds of the way along the capillary (see Fig. 30-10B, blue curve). If we could double $\mathrm{DL}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}, \mathrm{O}_{2}$ would reach diffusion equilibrium much earlier than usual (see Fig. 30-10B, red curve). However, neither change in $\mathrm{DL}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ would affect $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ uptake, which is not diffusion limited.

The $\mathrm{D}_{\mathrm{L}}$ reserve for $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ uptake is extremely important during exercise, when cardiac output can increase by up to a factor of 5 (see p. 410), a change that substantially decreases the contact time of the blood with the pulmonary capillaries. The contact time appears not to decrease by more than a factor of $\sim 3$, probably because the slightly increased pressure recruits and distends the pulmonary vessels (see p. 687). As a result, even with vigorous exercise, $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ reaches virtual equilibrium with the alveolar air by the end of the capillary (see Fig. 30-10C, green curve)-except in some elite athletes. (T) N30-9 Thus, the increase in $\dot{\mathrm{Q}}$ during exercise leads to a corresponding increase in $\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$, which carries obvious survival benefits. In patients with pulmonary disease, thickening of the alveolar blood-gas barrier can reduce $\mathrm{DL}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ sufficiently that equilibration of $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ fails to occur by capillary's end during exercise (see Fig. 30-10C, brown curve). In this case, $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ transport becomes diffusion limited.

Like exercise, high altitude stretches out the $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ profile along the capillary (see Fig. 30-10D, red curve). The reasons are two, both of which stem from the low barometric pressure at altitude, which leads to a proportional decrease in ambient $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ (see p. 1230) and thus to a fall in alveolar $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$. First, the low $\mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ causes the alveolar-capillary $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ gradient at the beginning of the capillary to fall, reducing the absolute $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ transport rate. It is true that continued $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ extraction by the systemic tissues causes $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ to fall as well in the mixedvenous blood that enters the pulmonary capillaries. However, at altitude, the difference driving $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ uptake, $\mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}-\mathrm{PV}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$, is

## N30-8 Capillary Reserve in Renal Glomerulus

## Contributed by Emile Boulpaep and Walter Boron

The $D_{L}$ reserve for $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ along the pulmonary capillaries is reminiscent of the filtration reserve that exists along the capillaries of the glomerulus in the kidney (see pp. 745-746). In both cases, the system is able to accommodate a substantial increase in blood flow before saturating.

## N30-9 Exercise-Induced Arterial Hypoxemia in Females

## Contributed by Emile Boulpaep and Walter Boron

In elite male athletes-and thoroughbred race horses, which are bred to have an impressive cardiac output-maximal aerobic exercise may lead to arterial hypoxemia-a fall in the $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ of arterial blood. The reason for this hypoxemia is believed to be a cardiac output that outstrips the lung's diffusion reserve, leading to a failure to reach diffusion equilibrium in the pulmonary capillaries (i.e., diffusion-limited $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ transport). Note that in most men-those with normal levels of maximal cardiac output-maximum exercise does not lead to arterial hypoxemia. Instead, the exercise-induced increase in ventilation maintains a near-normal arterial $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ and usually a lower-than-normal arterial $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$.

In women, however, it is common for exercise to lead to arterial hypoxemia. This effect does not correlate with level of physical fitness but appears to be constitutional, possibly reflecting an exercise-induced increase in ventilation-perfusion mismatch.

## REFERENCE

Harms CA, McClaran SR, Nickele GA, et al: Exercise-induced arterial hypoxaemia in healthy young women. J Physiol 507:619-628, 1998.

![bo107465312c5](bo107465312c5.jpg)

B $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ PROFILE
![bo108465312c5](bo108465312c5.jpg)

Figure 30-11 Diffusion of $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$. In $\mathbf{B}, \mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ is alveolar $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$.
still smaller than normal. Second, because at altitude $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ is lower, we now operate on a steeper part of the $\mathrm{Hb}-\mathrm{O}_{2}$ dissociation curve (see Fig. 29-3). Thus, a given increment in the $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ content of the pulmonary-capillary blood occurs with a smaller increase in $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$.

The combination of exercise and high altitude can cause $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ transport to become diffusion limited even in healthy individuals (see Fig. 30-10D, green curve). If the subject also has a pathological condition that lowers $\mathrm{DL}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$, then transport may become diffusion limited even more readily at altitude. Obviously, any combination of exercise, high altitude, and reduced $\mathrm{DL}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ compounds the problems for $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ transport.

Escape of $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ Mixed-venous blood entering the pulmonary capillary has a $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ of $\sim 46 \mathrm{~mm} \mathrm{Hg}$, whereas the alveolar $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ is $\sim 40 \mathrm{~mm} \mathrm{Hg}$ (Fig. 30-11A). Thus, $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ diffuses in the opposite direction of $\mathrm{O}_{2}$-from blood to alveolus-and $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ falls along the pulmonary capillary (see Fig. 30-11B, black curve), eventually reaching diffusion equilibrium. Compared with $\mathrm{O}_{2}$, one factor that tends to speed $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ equilibration is that the $\mathrm{D}_{\mathrm{L}}$ for $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ is 3 -fold to 5 -fold greater than that for $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ (see p. 664). However, two factors tend to slow the equilibration of $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$. First, the initial $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ gradient across the blood-gas barrier is only $\sim 6 \mathrm{~mm} \mathrm{Hg}$ at the beginning of the capillary, $\sim 10 \%$ as large as the initial $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ gradient. Second, in their physiological ranges, the $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$-dissociation curve (see Fig. 29-10) is far steeper than the $\mathrm{Hb}-\mathrm{O}_{2}$ dissociation curve (see Fig. 29-3). Thus, a decrement in the $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ content of the pulmonary-capillary blood causes a relatively small decrease in $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$. Thus, a smaller gradient and a steeper dissociation curve counteract the larger $\mathrm{D}_{\mathrm{L}}$. Many authors believe that, as indicated by the black curve in Figure 30-11B, capillary $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ reaches alveolar $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ about one third of the way along the pulmonary capillary (as is the case for $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ ). Others have suggested that $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ equilibration is slower, occurring just before the end of the pulmonary capillary. In either case, $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ excretion is perfusion limited. The decrease in $\mathrm{D}_{\mathrm{L}}$ that occurs in
certain lung diseases (see next section) or heavy exercise (see Fig. 30-11B, red and blue curves) may cause the transport of $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ to become diffusion limited.

## Pathological changes that reduce $\mathrm{D}_{\mathrm{L}}$ do not necessarily produce hypoxia

The measured pulmonary diffusing capacity for CO falls in disease states accompanied by a thickening of the alveolar blood-gas barrier, a reduction in the surface area (i.e., capillaries) available for diffusion, or a decrease in the amount of Hb in the pulmonary capillaries. Examples of pathological processes accompanied by a decrease in $\mathrm{D}_{\mathrm{L}}$ include the following:

Diffuse interstitial pulmonary fibrosis, a fibrotic process causing a thickening of the interstitium, thickening of alveolar walls, and destruction of capillaries-sometimes with marked decreases in $\mathrm{D}_{\mathrm{L}}$. In some cases, the cause is unknown (i.e., idiopathic); in others it is secondary to such disorders as sarcoidosis, scleroderma, and exposure to occupational agents (e.g., asbestos).
Chronic obstructive pulmonary disease (COPD), which not only increases the resistance of conducting airways (see p. 597) but can lead to a destruction of pulmonary capillaries and thus a reduction in both (1) surface area available for diffusion, and (2) total pulmonary-capillary Hb content (see p. 619).
Loss of functional lung tissue, as caused by surgical removal of lung tissue. $\mathrm{D}_{\mathrm{L}}$ falls because of a decrease in both (1) surface area, and (2) total pulmonary-capillary Hb content.
Anemia, in which the fall in total Hb content decreases the $\theta \cdot \mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{s}}$ component of $\mathrm{DL}_{\mathrm{CO}}$.

Although pulmonary diseases can cause both a decrease in $\mathrm{D}_{\mathrm{L}}$ and hypoxemia (i.e., a decrease in arterial $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ ), it is not necessarily true that the decrease in $\mathrm{D}_{\mathrm{L}}$ is the sole or even

the major cause of the hypoxemia. The same diseases that lower $D_{L}$ also upset the distribution of ventilation and perfusion throughout the lung. As discussed in Chapter 31, mismatching of ventilation to perfusion among various regions of the lungs can be a powerful influence leading to hypoxemia. Furthermore, because the lung has a sizeable $\mathrm{D}_{\mathrm{L}}$ reserve for $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ (and perhaps for $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ as well), $\mathrm{D}_{\mathrm{L}}$ would have to decrease to about one third of its normal value for $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ transport to become diffusion limited. Thus, in a disease causing
both a decrease in $D_{L}$ and disturbances in the distribution of ventilation and perfusion, it is difficult to determine the extent to which an accompanying reduction of $D_{L}$ is responsible for the resulting hypoxemia.

# VENTILATION AND PERFUSION OF THE LUNGS 

Walter F. Boron

Although diffusion is at the very heart of the gas exchange, as we discussed in Chapter 30, two other parameters are also extremely important. Ventilation and perfusion-both of which require energy-are critical because they set up the partial-pressure gradients along which $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ and $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ diffuse. Ventilation is the convective movement of air that exchanges gases between the atmosphere and the alveoli. In Chapter 27 we discussed the mechanics of ventilation. In the first part of the present chapter we consider the importance of ventilation for determining alveolar $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ and $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$, and also see that ventilation varies from one group of alveoli to the next. Perfusion is the convective movement of blood that carries the dissolved gases to and from the lung. In Chapters 17 through 25 we discussed the cardiovascular system. In the second part of the present chapter, we examine the special properties of the pulmonary circulation and see that, like ventilation, perfusion varies in different regions of the lung. Finally, in the third part of this chapter, we see that the ratio of ventilation to perfusionand the distribution of ventilation-perfusion ratios among alveolar units-is critically important for gas exchange and thus for the composition of the arterial blood gases: $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$, $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$, and pH .

## VENTILATION

About 30\% of total ventilation in a respiratory cycle is wasted ventilating anatomical dead space (i.e., conducting airways)

Total ventilation $\left(\dot{\mathbf{V}}_{\mathrm{T}}\right)$ is the volume of air moved out of the lungs per unit of time:

$$
\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{T}}=\frac{\mathrm{V}}{\mathrm{t}}
$$

Here V is the volume of air exiting the lungs during a series of breaths. Note that we are using $\dot{V}$ differently than in Chapter 27, where $\dot{V}$ represented flow through an airway at a particular instant in time. A practical definition is that $\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{T}}$ is the product of tidal volume (TV or $\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{T}}$ ) and the respiratory frequency (f). Thus, for someone with a tidal volume of 0.5 L , breathing 12 breaths/min,

$$
\begin{aligned}
\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{T}} & =\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{T}} \cdot \mathrm{f} \\
& =(0.5 \mathrm{~L}) \cdot(12 / \mathrm{min}) \\
& =6 \mathrm{~L} / \mathrm{min}
\end{aligned}
$$

Because total ventilation usually is reported in $\mathrm{L} / \mathrm{min}$, it is sometimes called minute ventilation.

Before an inspiration, the conducting airways are filled with "stale" air having the same composition as alveolar air (Fig. 31-1, step 1); we will see why shortly. During inspiration, $\sim 500 \mathrm{~mL}$ of "fresh" atmospheric air (high $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}} / \mathrm{low} \mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ ) enters the body (step 2). However, only the first 350 mL reaches the alveoli; the final 150 mL remains in the conducting airways (i.e., nose, pharynx, larynx, trachea, and other airways without alveoli)-that is, the anatomical dead space. These figures are typical for a $70-\mathrm{kg}$ person; $\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{T}}$ and $\mathrm{V}_{0}$ are roughly proportional to body size. During inspiration, $\sim 500 \mathrm{~mL}$ of air also enters the alveoli. However, the first 150 mL is stale air previously in the conducting airways; only the final 350 mL is fresh air. By the end of inspiration, the 500 mL of air that entered the alveoli ( 150 mL of stale air plus 350 mL of fresh air) has mixed by diffusion with the pre-existing alveolar air (see Fig. 31-1, step 3). During expiration (step 4), the first 150 mL of air emerging from the body is the fresh air left in the conducting airways from the previous inspiration. As the expiration continues, 350 mL of stale alveolar air sequentially moves into the conducting airways and then exits the body-for a total of 500 mL of air leaving the body. Simultaneously, 500 mL of air leaves the alveoli. The first 350 mL is the same 350 mL that exited the body. The final 150 mL of stale air to exit the alveoli remains in the conducting airways, as we are ready to begin the next inspiration.

Thus, with each $500-\mathrm{mL}$ inspiration, only the initial 350 mL of fresh air entering the body reaches the alveoli. With each $500-\mathrm{mL}$ expiration, only the final 350 mL of air exiting the body comes from the alveoli. One $150-\mathrm{mL}$ bolus of fresh air shuttles back and forth between the atmosphere and conducting airways. Another $150-\mathrm{mL}$ bolus of stale air shuttles back and forth between the conducting airways and alveoli. Dead-space ventilation $\left(\dot{\mathbf{V}}_{\mathrm{D}}\right)$ is the volume of the stale air so shuttled per minute. Alveolar ventilation $\left(\dot{\mathbf{V}}_{\mathrm{A}}\right)$ is the volume of fresh air per minute that actually reaches the

![bo109465312c5](bo109465312c5.jpg)

Figure 31-1 Ventilation of dead space and alveolar space during a respiratory cycle.
alveoli, or the volume of stale alveolar air that reaches the atmosphere. (1) N31-1 Thus, total ventilation-a reflection of the work invested in breathing-is the sum of the wasted dead-space ventilation and the useful alveolar ventilation. In our example,

$$
\begin{aligned}
& \text { liters breaths liters } \\
& \text { breath minute minute } \\
& \mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{D}}=\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{D}} \cdot \mathrm{f}=0.150 \cdot 12=1.8 \\
& \frac{\mathrm{~V}_{\mathrm{A}}=\left(\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{T}}-\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{D}}\right) \cdot \mathrm{f}=0.350 \cdot 12=4.2}{\mathrm{~V}_{\mathrm{T}}=\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{T}} \cdot \mathrm{f}=0.500 \cdot 12=6.0}
\end{aligned}
$$

so that the dead-space ventilation is $30 \%$ of the total ventilation.

The inset of Figure 31-1 illustrates how inspiration and expiration lead to small fluctuations in the alveolar partial pressures for $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ and $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$, noted above in Figure 30-3B. Throughout the respiratory cycle, blood flowing through the pulmonary capillaries continuously draws $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ out of the alveolar air and adds $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$. Just before an inspiration, alveolar $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ has fallen to its lowest point, and alveolar $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ has risen to its highest. During inspiration, a new bolus of inspired fresh air mixes with pre-existing alveolar air, which causes alveolar $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ to rise and alveolar $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ to fall. During expiration and until the next inspiration, alveolar $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ and $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ gradually drift to the values that we saw at the start of the respiratory cycle. Assuming that functional residual capacity (FRC) is 3 L and that each breath adds 350 mL of fresh air, one can calculate that alveolar $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ oscillates with an amplitude of $\sim 5 \mathrm{~mm} \mathrm{Hg}$, whereas alveolar $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ oscillates with an amplitude of $\sim 4 \mathrm{~mm} \mathrm{Hg}$. (1) N31-2

## The Fowler single-breath $\mathrm{N}_{2}$-washout technique estimates anatomical dead space

In 1948, Ward Fowler introduced an approach for estimating the anatomical dead space, based on the washout of $\mathrm{N}_{2}$ from the lungs. The key concept is that $\mathrm{N}_{2}$ is physiologically inert. After the subject has been breathing room air, the alveolar air is $\sim 75 \% \mathrm{~N}_{2}$. After a quiet expiration, when lung volume is FRC (Fig. 31-2A, step 1), the subject takes a single normal-sized breath ( $\sim 500 \mathrm{~mL}$ ). The inspired air is $100 \% \mathrm{O}_{2}$, although, in principle we could use any nontoxic gas mixture lacking $\mathrm{N}_{2}$. The first portion of inspired $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ enters the alveolar spaces (step 2), where it rapidly mixes by diffusion and dilutes the $\mathrm{N}_{2}$ and other gases remaining after the previous breaths of room air (step 3). The last portion of the inspired $\mathrm{O}_{2}(\sim 150 \mathrm{~mL})$ remains in the conducting airways, which have a $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{N}_{2}}$ of zero.

The subject now exhales $\sim 500 \mathrm{~mL}$ of air (see Fig. 31-2A, step 4). If no mixing occurred between the $\mathrm{N}_{2}$-free air in the most distal conducting airways and the $\mathrm{N}_{2}$-containing air in the most proximal alveolar spaces, then the first $\sim 150 \mathrm{~mL}$ of air emerging from the body would have an $\left[\mathrm{N}_{2}\right]$ of zero (see Fig. 31-2B, red lines). After this would come a sharp transition to a much higher $\left[\mathrm{N}_{2}\right]$ for the final $\sim 350 \mathrm{~mL}$ of expired air. Thus, the expired volume with an $\left[\mathrm{N}_{2}\right]$ of zero would represent air from the conducting airways (anatomical dead space), whereas the remainder would represent air from the alveoli.

In reality, some mixing occurs between the air in the conducting airways and alveoli, so that the transition is S-shaped (see Fig. 31-2C, red curve). A vertical line drawn through the S-shaped curve (1) N31-3 so that area $a$ is the same as area $b$ marks the idealized transition between air

## N31-1 Inhaled Versus Exhaled Volume

## Contributed by Emile Boulpaep and Walter Boron

The rate of $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ consumption (e.g., $250 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{min}$ ) is generally greater than the rate of $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ production (e.g., $200 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{min}$ ) in individuals consuming a typical Western-pattern diet. That is, the respiratory quotient (RQ) is generally less than unity (see p. 681). As a result, every minute we inhale slightly more air than we exhale (e.g., $250 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{min}-200 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{min}=50 \mathrm{~mL} /$ min ). Because of this difference, in reporting ventilatory volumes, respiratory physiologists have standardized on the volume of exhaled air $\left(\mathrm{V}_{2}\right)$, which as we have noted is slightly smaller than the volume of inhaled air (V.).

## N31-3 Shape of the Single-Breath $\mathrm{N}_{2}$-Washout Curve

## Contributed by Emile Boulpaep and Walter Boron

Exhaling vigorously causes turbulence (see p. 617) in the larger airways, further blurring the boundary between dead space and alveolar air. The greater the mixing, the more spread out is the S-shaped transition in Figure 31-2C.

Breath holding not only blurs the boundary (because more time is available for diffusional mixing of $\mathrm{N}_{2}$ in the alveolar spaces with the $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ in the dead space) but also moves the boundary to the left. In fact, if you were to hold your breath infinitely long, all the $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ in your conducting airways would be contaminated with $\mathrm{N}_{2}$. As a result, the $\mathrm{N}_{2}$ profile in Figure 31-2B or $C$ would be a low, stable value from the very first milliliter of exhaled air. That is, there would be no gray area, and it would appear-according to the Fowler technique-as if you did not have any dead space.

Inhaling a large tidal volume of $100 \% \mathrm{O}_{2}$ increases the apparent anatomical dead space as measured by the Fowler technique. The reason is that the conducting airways have a finite compliance (see p. 610)—lower than the compliance of alveoli, but still greater than zero. Thus, with a large inhalation (and thus a rather negative intrapleural pressure), the conducting airways dilate somewhat, and this dilation is reflected in a larger-than-normal value for anatomical dead space.

## N31-2 Oscillations in $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ and $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ During Breathing

## Contributed by Emile Boulpaep and Walter Boron

We will assume that the volume of alveolar air after a quiet expiration is 3000 mL (the FRC), and that this air has a $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ of 98.4 mm Hg-the nadir of $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ during our hypothetical respiratory cycle. The subsequent inspiration delivers to the alveoli 350 mL of fresh air (at 500 mL tidal volume less 150 mL of anatomical dead space) that has a $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ of 149 mm Hg . Thus, after the 350 mL of fresh air mixes with the 3000 mL of preexisting alveolar air, the alveolar $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ will be

$$
\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}=\frac{(3000 \times 98.4)+(350 \times 149)}{(3000+350)}=103.6 \mathrm{~mm} \mathrm{Hg} \quad(\text { NE 31-1) }
$$

This value is the zenith of $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ during our hypothetical respiratory cycle. During the ensuing several seconds, the alveolar $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ drifts back down to 98.4 mm Hg as $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ diffuses from the alveolar air into the pulmonary-capillary blood. Thus, during a respiratory cycle, alveolar $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ oscillates from a low of 98.4 mm Hg to a high of 103.6 mm Hg , with a mean $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ of 101 mm Hg . In other words, alveolar $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ oscillates around a mean of 101 mm Hg , with the peak and nadir deviating from the mean by $\sim 2.6 \mathrm{~mm} \mathrm{Hg}$-an amplitude of $\sim 5 \mathrm{~mm} \mathrm{Hg}$.

In the case of $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$, the analysis is similar. After a quiet expiration, the 3000 mL of alveolar air has a $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ of 42.2 mm Hg -the zenith of $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ during our hypothetical respiratory cycle. The subsequent inspiration delivers to the alveoli 350 mL of fresh air, which has a $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ of $\sim 0 \mathrm{~mm} \mathrm{Hg}$. Thus, after the 350 mL of fresh air mixes with the 3000 mL of preexisting alveolar air, the alveolar $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ will be

$$
\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2} \mathrm{O}_{2}}=\frac{(3000 \times 42.2)+(350 \times 0)}{(3000+350)}=37.8 \mathrm{~mm} \mathrm{Hg} \quad(\text { NE 31-2) }
$$

This value is the nadir of $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ during our hypothetical respiratory cycle. During the ensuing several seconds, the alveolar $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ drifts back up to 42.2 mm Hg as $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ diffuses from the pulmonary-capillary blood to the alveolar air. Thus, during a respiratory cycle, alveolar $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ oscillates from a high of 42.2 mm Hg to a low of 37.8 mm Hg , with a mean $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ of 40 mm Hg . In other words, alveolar $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ oscillates around a mean of 40 mm Hg , with the peak and nadir deviating from the mean by $\sim 2.2 \mathrm{~mm} \mathrm{Hg}$-an amplitude of $\sim 4 \mathrm{~mm} \mathrm{Hg}$.

![bo110465312c5](bo110465312c5.jpg)

B $\left[\mathrm{N}_{2}\right]$ PROFILE OF EXPIRED AIR WITH NO MIXING
![bo111465312c5](bo111465312c5.jpg)

C MEASURED $\left[\mathrm{N}_{2}\right]$ PROFILE
![bo112465312c5](bo112465312c5.jpg)

Figure 31-2 Fowler technique for measuring anatomical dead space.
from conducing and alveolar airways-as in Figure 31-2B. The expired lung volume at the point of this vertical line is thus the anatomical dead space. In Figure 31-2C, the part of the S-shaped curve with an expired $\left[\mathrm{N}_{2}\right]$ of zero represents pure dead-space air, the part where $\left[\mathrm{N}_{2}\right]$ gradually rises represents a mixture of dead-space and alveolar air, and the part where $\left[\mathrm{N}_{2}\right]$ is high and flat represents pure alveolar air. This plateau is important, because it is during this plateau that one obtains an alveolar gas sample. (5) N31-4

## The Bohr expired- $\left[\mathrm{CO}_{2}\right]$ approach estimates physiological dead space

In principle, we could compute the dead space using any gas whose expiration profile looks like that of $\mathrm{N}_{2}$. Nitrogen is useful because we can easily create an artificial situation in which the subject makes a single inhalation of $\mathrm{N}_{2}$-free air (e.g., a single breath of $100 \% \mathrm{O}_{2}$ ). Another possibility is $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$. Its profile during expiration is similar to that of $\mathrm{N}_{2}$. Moreover, we do not need to use any special tricks to get it to work because room air has practically no $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$. Yet plenty of $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ is in the alveoli, where it evolves from the incoming mixedvenous blood. After a quiet expiration (Fig. 31-3A, step 1), the $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ of the alveolar air is virtually the same as the $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ of the arterial blood (see p. 673), $\sim 40 \mathrm{~mm} \mathrm{Hg}$. The subject now inhales a normal tidal volume ( $\sim 500 \mathrm{~mL}$ ) of room air, although any $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$-free gas mixture would do. The first portion enters the alveoli (step 2), where it rapidly dilutes the $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ and other gases remaining after the previous breath (step 3). The rest $(\sim 150 \mathrm{~mL})$ remains in the conducting
airways, which now have a $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ of $\sim 0$. When the subject now expires (step 4), the first air that exits the body is the $\mathrm{CO}_{2^{-}}$ free gas that had filled the conducting airways, followed by the $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$-containing alveolar air. Thus, the idealized profile of expired $\left[\mathrm{CO}_{2}\right]$ (see Fig. 31-3B, red lines) is similar to the idealized $\left[\mathrm{N}_{2}\right]$ profile (see Fig. 31-2B). In particular, the volume of expired air at the vertical line in Figure 31-3B is the estimated anatomical dead space.

One could use a $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ probe to record the expired $\left[\mathrm{CO}_{2}\right]$ profile during a single-breath $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ washout, rather than the $\left[\mathrm{N}_{2}\right]$ profile that Fowler used to measure anatomical dead space. However, because $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ probes did not exist in his day, Christian Bohr (5) N31-5 used a single-breath $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ washout but analyzed the average $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ in the mixed-expired air (i.e., averaged over the dead space plus expired alveolar air).

The principle of the Bohr approach is that the amount of $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ present in the volume of mixed-expired air $\left(\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{E}}\right)$ is the sum of the $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ contributed by the volume of air from the dead space $\left(\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{D}}\right)$ plus the $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ contributed by the volume of air coming from the alveoli $\left(\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{E}}-\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{D}}\right)$. As summarized in Figure 31-3B:

1. The amount of $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ coming from the dead space is the product of $\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{D}}$ and $\left[\mathrm{CO}_{2}\right]$ in this dead-space air. Because $\left[\mathrm{CO}_{2}\right]_{\mathrm{D}}$ is zero, the area beneath $\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{D}}$ is also zero.
2. The amount of $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ coming from alveolar air is the product of $\left(\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{E}}-\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{D}}\right)$ and alveolar $\left[\mathrm{CO}_{2}\right]$ and is represented by the rose area in Figure 31-3B.
3. The total amount of $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ in the mixed-expired air is the product of $\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{E}}$ and the average $\left[\mathrm{CO}_{2}\right]$ in this air and is represented by the hatched area in Figure 31-3B.

## N31-4 Obtaining a Sample of Alveolar Air

## Contributed by Emile Boulpaep and Walter Boron

How do we obtain the alveolar air sample? As noted in our discussion of the Fowler single-breath $\mathrm{N}_{2}$-washout technique on pages 676-677, we ask the subject to make a prolonged expiratory effort. We discard the first several hundred milliliters of expired air-which contains pure dead-space air, a mixture of dead-space and alveolar air, and some pure alveolar air-to be certain that we do not contaminate our sample with air from the conducting airways. We then collect the end-tidal sample of air-pure alveolar air-and assay it for $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$.

## N31-21 August Krogh

For more information about Schack August Steenberg Krogh (1874-1949) and the work that led to his Nobel Prize, visit http://www.nobelprize.org/nobel_prizes/medicine/laureates/ 1920/ (accessed January 2015).

## N31-5 Christian Bohr

## Contributed by Walter Boron

Christian Bohr (1855-1911), a native of Copenhagen, was an eminent physiologist and also the father of the physicist Niels Bohr. The major contributions of Christian Bohr were the first description of dead space (specifically the physiological dead space) and the description of the Bohr effect. Christian Bohr was a proponent of the hypothesis that the lung actively secretes $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ into the pulmonary-capillary blood. His trainee, future Nobelist August Krogh. (5) N31-21 later disproved this hypothesis.

![bo113465312c5](bo113465312c5.jpg)

B $\left[\mathrm{CO}_{2}\right]$ PROFILE OF EXPIRED AIR
![bo114465312c5](bo114465312c5.jpg)

C EFFECT OF ALVEOLAR DEAD-SPACE VENTILATION ON EXPIRED- $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ PROFILE
![bo115465312c5](bo115465312c5.jpg)

Figure 31-3 Bohr method for measuring physiological dead space.

Because the rose and hatched areas in Figure 31-3B must be equal, and because the alveolar and expired $\left[\mathrm{CO}_{2}\right]$ values are proportional to their respective $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ values, it is possible to show that (5) R21-6

$$
\frac{\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{D}}}{\mathrm{~V}_{\mathrm{E}}}=\frac{\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{A}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}}-\mathrm{PE}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}}{\mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}}
$$

This is the Bohr equation. Typically, $\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{D}} / \mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{E}}$ ranges between 0.20 and 0.35 . For a $\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{D}}$ of 150 mL and a $\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{E}}$ of $500 \mathrm{~mL}, \mathrm{~V}_{\mathrm{D}} / \mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{E}}$ would be 0.30 . For example, if the alveolar $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ is 40 mm Hg and the mixed-expired $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ is 28 mm Hg , then

$$
\frac{\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{D}}}{\mathrm{~V}_{\mathrm{E}}}=\frac{40-28}{40}=30 \%
$$

Equation 31-4 makes good intuitive sense. In an imaginary case in which we reduced $\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{D}}$ to zero, the expired air would be entirely from the alveoli, so that

$$
\frac{\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{D}}}{\mathrm{~V}_{\mathrm{E}}}=\frac{40-40}{40}=0 \%
$$

On the other hand, if we reduced the tidal volume to a value at or below the dead-space volume, then all of the expired air would be dead-space air. In this case, $\mathrm{PE}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ would be zero, and

$$
\frac{\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{D}}}{\mathrm{~V}_{\mathrm{E}}}=\frac{40-0}{40}=100 \%
$$

Two examples of this principle are of practical importance. During panting, the respiratory frequency is very high, but the tidal volume is only slightly greater than the anatomical dead space. Thus, most of the total ventilation is wasted as dead-space ventilation. If we reduced tidal volume below $\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{D}}$, then in principle there would be no alveolar ventilation at all! During snorkeling, a swimmer breathes through a tube that increases $\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{D}}$. If the snorkeling tube had a volume of 350 mL and the dead space within the body of the swimmer were 150 mL , then a tidal volume of 500 mL would in principle produce no alveolar ventilation. Consequently, the swimmer would suffocate, even though total ventilation was normal! Thus, the fractional dead space $\left(\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{D}} / \mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{E}}\right)$ depends critically on tidal volume.

It is important to recognize that although the Fowler and Bohr methods yield about the same estimate for $\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{D}}$ in

# N31-6 The Bohr Equation 

## Contributed by Emile Boulpaep and Walter Boron

The principle of the Bohr approach is that the $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ concentration of the expired air is the $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ concentration of the alveolar air-as diluted by the $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ in the dead space, which contains no $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$. Imagine that we have a volume of expired air $\left(\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{E}}\right)$. The total amount of $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ present in this expired air is the sum of the $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ contributed by the volume of air from the dead space $\left(V_{D}\right)$ and the $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ contributed by the volume of air coming from the alveoli $\left(V_{E}-V_{D}\right):$

$$
\left[\text { Amount of } \mathrm{CO}_{2}\right] \text { in expired air }=\left[\begin{array}{l}
\text { Amount of } \mathrm{CO}_{2} \\
\text { contributed by } \\
\text { conducting airways }
\end{array}\right]+\left[\begin{array}{l}
\text { Amount of } \mathrm{CO}_{2} \\
\text { contributed by } \\
\text { alveolar air }
\end{array}\right]
$$

(NE 31-3)
Knowing that the amount of the gas is simply the product of the volume and the concentration of the gas in that volume, we can write

$$
\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{E}} \times\left[\mathrm{CO}_{2}\right]_{\mathrm{E}}=\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{D}} \times\left[\mathrm{CO}_{2}\right]_{\mathrm{D}}+\left(\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{E}}-\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{D}}\right) \times\left[\mathrm{CO}_{2}\right]_{\mathrm{A}}
$$

Note that the volume of air coming from the alveoli is not the total volume of alveolar air, which might be a couple of liters, but rather that part of the expired volume that came from the alveoli. For example, if the expired volume were 500 mL and the dead space were 150 mL , the air coming from the alveoli $\left(V_{E}-V_{D}\right)$ would be 350 mL .

Because $\left[\mathrm{CO}_{2}\right]_{\mathrm{D}}$ is virtually zero, we can drop the "conducting airway" term from Equation NE 31-4. In other words, the hatched area beneath the dashed line in Figure 31-3B is equal to the red area under the solid red line. The simplified version of Equation NE 31-4 is thus

$$
\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{E}} \times\left[\mathrm{CO}_{2}\right]_{\mathrm{E}}=\left(\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{E}}-\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{D}}\right) \times\left[\mathrm{CO}_{2}\right]_{\mathrm{A}}
$$

Furthermore, because the $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ concentration is proportional to the $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ partial pressure, and because the proportionality constant is the same for the expired air and the alveolar air,

$$
\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{E}} \times \mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}=\left(\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{E}}-\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{D}}\right) \times \mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}
$$

We can rearrange this equation and solve for the ratio $\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{D}} / \mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{E}}$, which is the fraction of the expired volume that came from the dead space:

$$
\frac{V_{D}}{V_{E}}=\frac{P_{V_{C O_{2}}}-P_{V_{C O_{2}}}}{P_{V_{C O_{2}}}}
$$

(NE 31-7)
This is the Bohr equation. Typically, $\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{D}} / \mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{E}}$ ranges between 0.20 and 0.35 . For a $\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{D}}$ of 150 mL and a $\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{E}}$ of $500 \mathrm{~mL}, \mathrm{~V}_{\mathrm{D}} / \mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{E}}$ would be 0.30 . Because the partial pressure of $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ in the deadspace air is virtually zero, $\mathrm{PE}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ must be less than $\mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$. For example, imagine that the alveolar $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ were 40 mm Hg and the average $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ in the expired air were 28 mm Hg :

$$
\frac{V_{D}}{V_{E}}=\frac{40-28}{40}=30 \%
$$

Equation NE 31-7 makes good intuitive sense. In an extreme hypothetical case in which we reduced $\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{D}}$ to zero, the expired air would be entirely from the alveoli, so that

$$
\frac{V_{D}}{V_{E}}=\frac{40-40}{40}=0 \%
$$

On the other hand, if the tidal volume and the dead space were both 150 mL , then all of the expired air would be deadspace air. Thus, the $\mathrm{PE}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ would have to be zero, and

$$
\frac{V_{D}}{V_{E}}=\frac{40-0}{40}=100 \%
$$

If the tidal volume were increased from 500 mL to 600 mL and $\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{D}}$ remained at 150 mL , the ratio $\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{D}} / \mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{E}}$ would fall from 0.30 to 0.25 . With a greater $\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{E}}$, the alveolar air would make a greater contribution to the mixed expired air, so that the mean expired $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ would be 30 mm Hg (i.e., closer to $\mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ ) instead of only 28 mm Hg . Thus, if you wish to compute $\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{D}}$ using the Bohr approach, it is best to use an expired volume that is larger than $\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{D}}$, but not too much larger.

healthy individuals, the two techniques actually measure somewhat different things. The Fowler approach measures anatomical dead space-the volume of the conducting airways from the mouth/nose up to the point where $\mathrm{N}_{2}$ in the alveolar gas rapidly dilutes inspired $100 \% \mathrm{O}_{2}$. The Bohr approach, on the other hand, measures the physiological dead space-the volume of airways not receiving $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ from the pulmonary circulation, and, therefore, not engaging in gas exchange. In a healthy person, the anatomical and physiological dead spaces are identical-the volume of the conducting airways. However, if some alveoli are ventilated but not perfused by pulmonary-capillary blood, these unperfused alveoli, like conducting airways, do not contain $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$. The air in such unperfused alveoli, known as alveolar dead space, contributes to the physiological dead space:

$$
\frac{\text { Physiological }}{\text { dead space }}=\frac{\text { Anatomical }}{\text { dead space }}+\frac{\text { Alveolar }}{\text { Fowler method }}
$$

The Fowler and Bohr methods could yield very different results in a patient with a pulmonary embolism, a condition in which a mass such as a blood clot wedges into and obstructs part or all of the pulmonary circulation. Alveoli downstream from the embolus are ventilated but not perfused; that is, they are alveolar dead space (see Fig. 31-3C). Thus, the Bohr method-but not the Fowler method-could detect an increase in the physiological dead space caused by a pulmonary embolism.

## Alveolar ventilation is the ratio of $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ production rate to $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ mole fraction in alveolar air

One way of computing alveolar ventilation is to subtract the dead space from the tidal volume and multiply the difference by the respiratory frequency (see Equation 31-3). We can also calculate $\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{A}}$ from alveolar $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$. The body produces $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ via oxidative metabolism at a rate of $\sim 200 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{min}$. In the steady state, this rate of $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ production $\left(\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}\right)$ must equal the rate at which the $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ enters the alveoli, and the rate at which we exhale the $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$. Of course, this $200 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{min}$ of exhaled $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ is part of the -4200 mL of total alveolar air that we exhale each minute. Therefore, the exhaled 200 mL of $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ is $\sim 5 \%$ of the exhaled 4200 mL of alveolar air:

$$
\begin{aligned}
& \text { Volume of } \mathrm{CO}_{2} \\
& \text { leaving alveoli } \\
& \frac{\text { in } 1 \mathrm{~min}}{\text { Volume of air }}=\binom{\text { Volume }}{\text { fraction }}=\binom{\text { Volume }}{\text { of } \mathrm{CO}_{2} \text { in }}}{\text { alveolar air }}=\left(\mathrm{CO}_{2} \text { mole fraction }\right)_{\mathrm{A}} \\
& \text { in } 1 \mathrm{~min} \\
& \quad \frac{\dot{\mathrm{~V}}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}}{\dot{\mathrm{~V}}_{\mathrm{A}}}=\frac{200 \mathrm{~mL}}{4200 \mathrm{~mL}}=5 \%
\end{aligned}
$$

Rearranging the foregoing equation and solving for $\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{A}}$ yields

$$
\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{A}}=\frac{\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}}{\left(\mathrm{CO}_{2} \text { mole fraction }\right)_{\mathrm{A}}}=k \frac{\dot{\mathrm{~V}}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}}{\mathrm{~Pa}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}}
$$

The equation above the brace is true only when we measure all parameters under the same conditions. This obvious point would hardly be worth noting if respiratory physiologists had not managed, by historical accident, to measure the two volume terms under different conditions:

1. Body temperature and pressure, saturated with water vapor (BTPS; see p. 594) for $\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{A}}$.
2. Standard temperature and pressure/dry (STPD; see p. 594) for $\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$.

Thus, in Equation 31-10 we introduce a constant $k$ that not only indicates that alveolar $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}\left(\mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}\right.$; measured at $37^{\circ} \mathrm{C}$ ) is proportional to the mole fraction of $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ in alveolar air, but also accounts for the different conditions for measuring the parameters. (5) N31-7
![bo116465312c5](bo116465312c5.jpg)
$(L / \min , B T P S) \quad(\mathrm{mmHg} \cdot \mathrm{L} / \mathrm{mL}) \quad(\mathrm{mm} \mathrm{Hg})$

This is the alveolar ventilation equation, which we can use to compute $\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{A}}$. We determine $\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ by collecting a known volume of expired air over a fixed time period and analyzing its $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ content. For a $70-\mathrm{kg}$ human, $\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ is $\sim 200 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{min}$. We can determine $\mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ by sampling the expired air at the end of an expiration-an alveolar gas sample (see pp. 676-677). (5) N31-4 This end-tidal $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ (see Fig. 31-2C) is $\sim 40 \mathrm{~mm} \mathrm{Hg}$. In practice, clinicians generally measure arterial $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}\left(\mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}\right)$, and assume that alveolar and arterial $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ are identical (see p. 673). Inserting these values into the alveolar ventilation equation, we have

$$
\begin{aligned}
\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{A}} & =0.863 \cdot \frac{\dot{\mathrm{~V}}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}}{\mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}}=0.863 \cdot \frac{200 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{min}}{40 \mathrm{mmHg}} \\
& =4.315 \mathrm{~L} / \mathrm{min}
\end{aligned}
$$

For the sake of simplicity-and consistency with our example in Equation 31-3-we round this figure to $4.2 \mathrm{~L} / \mathrm{min}$.

Although it is usually safe to regard $\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ as being constant for a person at rest, a clinical example in which $\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ can increase markedly is malignant hyperthermia (see Box $9-2$ ), which is associated with increased oxidative metabolism. One hallmark of this clinical catastrophe is that the increase in $\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ leads to an increase in $\mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ even though $\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{A}}$ is normal.

## Alveolar and arterial $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ are inversely proportional to alveolar ventilation

Viewing Equation 31-11 from a different perspective illustrates one of the most important concepts in respiratory physiology: Other things being equal, alveolar $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ is inversely proportional to alveolar ventilation. This conclusion makes intuitive sense because the greater the $\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{A}}$, the more the fresh, inspired air dilutes the alveolar $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$. Rearranging Equation 31-11 yields

# N31-7 The Conversion Factor 0.863 

## Contributed by Emile Boulpaep and Walter Boron

Unfortunately for the student of respiratory physiology, the way customs have evolved for measuring respiratory volumes and partial pressures is a case study in mixed conventions. In a rational world, all parameters would be measured under a consistent set of conditions. In the real world of respiratory science, however, the parameters you (and clinicians) will need to compute alveolar ventilation, alveolar $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$, and alveolar $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ are measured under at least three very different sets of conditions. As a result, the student is faced with "correction factors" such as 0.863 . Our advice to the student, when working numerical problems, is to insert the laboratory data directly into the correct equation-which you ought to intuitively understand-and use the proper correction factor. You will get the right answer. Before explaining the origin of 0.863 in Equation 31-11, we will examine the system of units in which clinical laboratories report each of the three terms in that equation.

First, $\dot{V}_{A}$, the alveolar ventilation, is reported in the same units as $\dot{V}_{C}$ (the rate at which air is expired from the lungs). If you collect all the warm, moist air that a subject exhales over a period of 1 minute, the laboratory will report that volume BTPS (body temperature and pressure, saturated with water vapor), precisely the same volume that the sample of expired air would have occupied in the warm, moist confines of the lung (see Box 26-3).

Second, $\dot{V}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$, the rate at which the body produces $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$, is measured at STPD (standard temperature and pressure/dry). Thus, if you collect a sample of warm, moist air expired over a period of 1 minute and give it to a clinical laboratory for analysis, the laboratory will report the $\dot{V}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ value as the volume that the dry $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ in the sample would occupy at $0^{\circ} \mathrm{C}$. The rationale is that this is the way chemists treat gases.

Third, $\mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$, the partial pressure of $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ in the alveoli, is reported in BTPD (body temperature and pressure/dry). If you obtain a sample of alveolar air and send it to a clinical laboratory, it will take the warm, moist air you send it and keep it at $37^{\circ} \mathrm{C}$ but remove the $\mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O}$. Of course, if the sample is in a rigid container and if P is is 760 mm Hg , this removal of water vapor will lower the total pressure of the sample by 47 mm Hg (i.e., the vapor pressure of water at $37^{\circ} \mathrm{C}$ ) to 713 mm Hg . To keep the pressure constant, the laboratory will allow the sample volume to decrease to a volume that is $713 / 760$ of the original volume. This, however, means that the mole fraction of $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ increases by the fraction $760 / 713$. Thus, the $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ in the warm, moist alveolar air is actually lower than the reported BTPD; the BTPS value of $\mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ is the reported value multiplied by $(713 / 760)$ or -0.983 . The real tragedy of the convention for reporting $\mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ at BTPD is that the same laboratory will report arterial $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ as BTPS.

In deriving the factor 0.863 , we begin with a rearrangement of Equation 31-9, using a consistent set of units (i.e., BTPS):

$$
\dot{V}_{A}(\text { BTPS })=\frac{\dot{V}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}(\mathrm{BTPS})}{\left(\% \mathrm{CO}_{2} \text { BTPS }\right)_{A}}
$$

(NE 31-11)
Because $\dot{V}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ is actually reported in STPD, our first task is to convert $\dot{V}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ (STPD) to $\dot{V}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ (BTPS). Because standard temperature is $0^{\circ} \mathrm{C}$ and body temperature is $37^{\circ} \mathrm{C}$, we must multiply $\dot{V}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ (STPD) by the ratio of temperatures in degrees Kelvin, the factor
$[(273+37) / 273]$. Furthermore, as noted in the previous paragraph, when we add water vapor to a dry sample of a gas and then expand the volume to keep the total pressure fixed at P 0 , the partial pressure of that gas will decrease to $(713 / 760)$ of its dry partial pressure. Thus,

$$
\dot{V}_{A}(\text { BTPS })=\frac{\dot{V}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}(\mathrm{STPD})\left(\frac{310}{273}\right)\left(\frac{713}{760}\right)}{\left(\% \mathrm{CO}_{2} \text { BTPS }\right)_{A}} \quad(\text { NE 31-12) }
$$

Our final task is to replace $\left(\% \mathrm{CO}_{2} \mathrm{BTPS}_{A}\right.$ with $\mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ (BTPD). Because $\mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}(\mathrm{BTPS})=\left(\% \mathrm{CO}_{2} \mathrm{BTPS}_{A} \times \mathrm{P}_{B}\right.$,

$$
\left(\% \mathrm{CO}_{2} \mathrm{BTPS}\right)=\frac{\mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}(\mathrm{BTPD})}{\mathrm{P}_{B}} \quad \text { (NE 31-13) }
$$

Because $\mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ is reported in BTPD, we must multiply it by the factor $(713 / 760)$. Thus,

$$
(\% \mathrm{CO}_{2} \mathrm{BTPS})=\frac{\mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}(\mathrm{BTPD})\left(\frac{713}{760}\right)}{760}
$$

(NE 31-14)
Substituting the expression for $\left(\% \mathrm{CO}_{2} \mathrm{BTPS}\right)_{A}$ in Equation NE 31-14 into Equation NE 31-12, we have

$$
\begin{aligned}
& \dot{V}_{A}(\text { BTPS })=\frac{\dot{V}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}(\mathrm{STPD})\left(\frac{310}{273}\right)\left(\frac{713}{760}\right)}{\left(\frac{\mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}(\mathrm{BTPD})}{\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{B}}}\right)(\text { (NE 31-15) }} \\
& =\frac{\dot{V}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}(\mathrm{STPD})\left(\frac{310}{273}\right)(760)}{\mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}(\mathrm{BTPD})} \\
& =863 \times \frac{\dot{V}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}(\mathrm{STPD})}{\mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}(\mathrm{BTPD})}
\end{aligned}
$$

In the above equation, both $\dot{V}_{A}$ and $\dot{V}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ are reported in $\mathrm{mL} /$ min . Because it is customary to report $\dot{V}_{A}$ in $\mathrm{L} / \mathrm{min}$, the factor 863 becomes 0.863 :

$$
\begin{gathered}
\mathrm{L} / \mathrm{min} \quad \mathrm{mL} / \mathrm{min} \\
\dot{\mathrm{~V}}_{A}(\mathrm{BTPS})=0.863 \times \frac{\dot{\mathrm{~V}}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}(\mathrm{STPD})}{\mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}(\mathrm{BTPD})} \quad \text { (NE 31-16) }
\end{gathered}
$$

The above is the alveolar ventilation equation.
For example, if $\dot{V}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ is $200 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{min}$ and $\mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ is 40 mm Hg , then

$$
\dot{V}_{A}(\text { BTPS })=0.863 \times \frac{200 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{min}}{40 \mathrm{~mm} \mathrm{Hg}}=4.315 \mathrm{~L} / \mathrm{min}
$$

(NE 31-17)

![bo117465312c5](bo117465312c5.jpg)

Figure 31-4 Dependence of alveolar $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ and $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ on alveolar ventilation. As alveolar ventilation increases, alveolar $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ and $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ approach their values in inspired air. At extremely low or high $\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{A}}$, where $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ consumption and $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ production do not remain constant, these idealized curves are no longer valid.

$$
\mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}=0.863 \cdot \frac{\dot{\mathrm{~V}}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}}{\dot{\mathrm{~V}}_{\mathrm{A}}}
$$

In other words, if $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ production is fixed, then doubling $\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{A}}$ causes $\mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ to fall to half of its initial value. Conversely, halving $\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{A}}$ causes $\mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ to double. Because, arterial $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ is virtually the same as alveolar $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ (see p. 673), changes in $\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{A}}$ affect both $\mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ and $\mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$.

The blue curve in Figure 31-4 helps illustrate the principle. Imagine that your tissues are producing $200 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{min}$ of $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$. In a steady state, your lungs must blow off 200 mL of $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ each minute. Also, imagine that your lungs are exhaling $4200 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{min}$ of alveolar air. Because the 200 mL of expired $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ must dissolve in the 4200 mL of exhaled alveolar air (center red point in Fig. 31-4), Equation 31-13 tells us that your $\mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ (and thus $\mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ ) must be $\sim 40 \mathrm{~mm} \mathrm{Hg}$.

What would happen if the excitement of reading about respiratory physiology caused your alveolar ventilation to double, to $8400 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{min}$ ? This is an example of hyperventilation. You could double $\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{A}}$ either by doubling respiratory frequency or by doubling the difference between tidal volume and dead space, or a combination of the two (see Equation 31-3). Immediately on doubling $\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{A}}$, you would be blowing off at twice the previous rate not only alveolar air (i.e., $8400 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{min}$ ) but also $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ (i.e., $400 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{min}$ ). Because your body would continue to produce only $200 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{min}$ of $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$-assuming that doubling $\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{A}}$ does not increase $\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}-$ you would initially blow off $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ faster than you made it, causing $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ levels throughout your body to fall. However, the falling $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ of mixed-venous blood would cause alveolar $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ to fall as well, and thus the rate at which you blow off $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ would gradually fall. Eventually, you would reach a new steady state in which the rate at which you blow off $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$
would exactly match the rate at which you produce $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ (i.e., $200 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{min})$.

When you reach a new steady state, the $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ values in your mixed-venous blood, arterial blood, and alveolar air would be stable. But what would be the $\mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ ? Because each minute you now are blowing off 8400 mL of alveolar air (i.e., twice normal) but still only 200 mL of $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ (right red point in Fig. 31-4), your $\mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ must be half normal, or $\sim 20 \mathrm{~mm} \mathrm{Hg}$. Not only does the hyperventilation cause alveolar $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ to fall by half, it also causes arterial $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ to fall by half. Thus, hyperventilation leads to respiratory alkalosis (see p. 634). This respiratory alkalosis causes the arterioles in the brain to constrict (see p. 559), reducing blood flow to the brain, which causes dizziness.

What would happen if, instead of doubling alveolar ventilation, you halved it from 4200 to $2100 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{min}$ ? This is an example of hypoventilation. At the instant you began hypoventilating, the volume of $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ expired per unit time would fall by half, to $100 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{min}$, even though $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ production by the tissues would remain at $200 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{min}$. Thus, $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ would build up throughout the body, causing $\mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ to rise. To what value would $\mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ have to increase before you would reach a new steady state? Because each minute you must exhale 200 mL of $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ but this can be diluted in only 2100 mL , or half the usual amount of alveolar air (left red point in Fig. 31-4), the alveolar $\left[\mathrm{CO}_{2}\right]$ must double from -40 to 80 mm Hg . Of course, this doubling of alveolar $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ is paralleled by a doubling of arterial $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$, leading to a respiratory acidosis (see p. 633).

Therefore, the steady-state alveolar $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ is inversely proportional to alveolar ventilation. The higher the $\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{A}}$, the lower the $\mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$. If $\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{A}}$ were infinitely high, then $\mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ would theoretically fall to zero, the $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ of inspired air.

## Alveolar and arterial $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ rise with increased alveolar ventilation

As illustrated by the red curve in Fig. 31-4, increases in alveolar ventilation cause alveolar $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ to rise and-at an infinite $\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{A}}$-approach the inspired $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ of $\sim 149 \mathrm{~mm} \mathrm{Hg}$.

Although alveolar $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ obviously depends critically on $\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{A}}$, it is also influenced to a lesser extent by alveolar gases other than $\mathrm{O}_{2}$-namely, $\mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O}, \mathrm{N}_{2}$, and $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$. The partial pressure of $\mathbf{H}_{2} \mathbf{O}$ at $37^{\circ} \mathrm{C}$ is 47 mm Hg , and $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O}}$ will not change unless body temperature changes. The partial pressures of all of the other gases "fit" into what remains of barometric pressure (PB) after $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O}}$ has claimed its mandatory 47 mm Hg . We can think of $\mathrm{N}_{2}$ as a "spectator molecule," because it is not metabolized; $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{N}_{2}}$ is whatever it has to be to keep the total pressure of the dry alveolar air at $760-47=713 \mathrm{~mm} \mathrm{Hg}$. That leaves us to deal with $\mathbf{C O}_{2}$.

Because inspired air contains virtually no $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$, we can think of the alveolar $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ as coming exclusively from metabolism. However, $\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ depends not only on how fast the tissues burn $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ but also on the kind of fuel they burn. If the fuel is carbohydrate, then the tissues produce one molecule of $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ for each $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ consumed (see p. 1188). This ratio is termed the respiratory quotient (RQ):

$$
\mathrm{RQ}=\frac{\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}}{\dot{\mathrm{~V}}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}}
$$

In this example the RQ is 1 , which is a good place to start when considering how alveolar $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ affects alveolar $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$. If we consider only the dry part of the inspired air that enters the alveoli (see Table 26-1), then $\mathrm{Pt}_{\mathrm{N}_{2}}$ is $713 \times 0.78=557, \mathrm{Pt}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ is $713 \times 0.2095=149 \mathrm{~mm} \mathrm{Hg}$, and $\mathrm{Pt}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ is $\sim 0$. As pulmonarycapillary blood takes up incoming $\mathrm{O}_{2}$, it replaces the $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ with an equal number of $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ molecules in the steady state $(\mathrm{RQ}=1)$. Because the exchange of $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ for $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ is precisely 1 for 1 , alveolar $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ is what is left of the inspired $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ after metabolism replaces some alveolar $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ with $\mathrm{CO}_{2}\left(\mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}=\right.$ 40 mm Hg ):

$$
\begin{aligned}
\mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}} & =\mathrm{Pt}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}-\mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}} \\
& =149-40 \\
& =109 \mathrm{~mm} \mathrm{Hg}
\end{aligned}
$$

A typical fat-containing Western-pattern diet produces an RQ of $\sim 0.8$ (see p. 1188), so that 8 molecules of $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ replace 10 molecules of $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ in the alveolar air. This 8 -for-10 replacement has two consequences. First, the volume of alveolar air falls slightly during gas exchange. Because the non- $\mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O}$ pressure remains at 713 mm Hg , this volume contraction concentrates the $\mathrm{N}_{2}$ and dilutes the $\mathrm{O}_{2}$. Second, the volume of expired alveolar air is slightly less than the volume of inspired air.

The alveolar gas equation (5) N31-8 describes how alveolar $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ depends on RQ:

$$
\mathbf{R Q}=\mathbf{l}: \mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}=\mathrm{Pt}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}-\mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}
$$

General case: $\mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}=\mathrm{Pt}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}-\mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}} \cdot\left(\mathrm{Ft}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}+\frac{1-\mathrm{Ft}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}}{\mathrm{RQ}}\right)$

FI $_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ is the fraction of inspired $d r y$ air that is $\mathrm{O}_{2}$, which is 0.21 for room air (see Table 26-1). Note that when RQ is 1 , the term in parentheses becomes unity, and Equation 31-17 reduces to Equation 31-16. The term in parentheses also becomes unity, regardless of RQ, if $\mathrm{Ft}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ is $100 \%$ (i.e., the subject breathes pure $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ )-in this case, no $\mathrm{N}_{2}$ is present to dilute the $\mathrm{O}_{2}$.

A simplified version of Equation 31-17 is nearly as accurate:

$$
\mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}=\mathrm{Pt}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}-\frac{\mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}}{\mathrm{RQ}}
$$

The concepts developed in the last two sections allow us to compute both alveolar $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ and alveolar $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$. The approach is first to use Equation 31-13 to calculate $\mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ from $\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ and $\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{A}}$, and then use Equation 31-17 to calculate $\mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ from $\mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ and RQ. Imagine that we first found that $\mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ is 40 mm Hg and that we know that RQ is 0.8 . What is $\mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ ?

$$
\begin{aligned}
\mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}} & =\mathrm{Pt}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}-\mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}} \cdot\left(\mathrm{Ft}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}+\frac{1-\mathrm{Ft}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}}{\mathrm{RQ}}\right) \\
\mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}} & =149-40 \cdot \underbrace{0.21+\frac{1-0.21}{0.8}}_{1.2} \\
& =149-48 \\
& =101 \mathrm{mmHg}
\end{aligned}
$$

By default, the partial pressure of $\mathrm{N}_{2}$ and other gases (e.g., argon) is $\mathrm{Pb}-\mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}-\mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ or $713-40-101=572 \mathrm{~mm} \mathrm{Hg}$. For simplicity, we round down this $\mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ to 100 mm Hg in our examples.

## Because of the action of gravity on the lung, regional ventilation in an upright subject is normally greater at the base than the apex

Until now, we have assumed that all alveoli are ventilated to the same extent. We could test this hypothesis by using an imaging technique for assessing the uniformity of ventilation. Imagine that a subject who is standing up breathes air containing ${ }^{133} \mathrm{Xe}$. Because Xe has very low water solubility, it (like He and $\mathrm{N}_{2}$ ) has a very low diffusing capacity (see pp. 661-663) and-over a short period-remains almost entirely within the alveoli.

Imaging the ${ }^{133} \mathrm{Xe}$ radioactivity immediately after a single breath of ${ }^{133} \mathrm{Xe}$ provides an index of absolute regional ventilation (Fig. 31-5A). However, $\left[{ }^{133} \mathrm{Xe}\right]$ might be low in a particular region either because the alveoli truly receive little ventilation or because the region has relatively little tissue. Therefore, we normalize the absolute data to the maximal regional alveolar volume. The subject continues to breathe the ${ }^{133} \mathrm{Xe}$ until $\left[{ }^{133} \mathrm{Xe}\right]$ values stabilize throughout the lungs. When the subject now makes a maximal inspiratory effort ( $\mathrm{V}_{1}=$ total lung capacity [TLC]), the level of radioactivity detected over any region reflects that region's maximal volume. Dividing the single-breath image by the steady-state image at TLC yields a ratio that describes regional ventilation per unit volume.

# N31-8 The Alveolar Gas Equation 

## Contributed by Emile Boulpaep and Walter Boron

The alveolar gas equation allows one to compute the ideal alveolar $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}\left(\mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}\right)$ from one's knowledge of the alveolar $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}\left(\mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}\right)$ as well as two other parameters, the mole fraction of $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ in the dry inspired air $\left(\mathrm{F}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}\right)$ and the $\mathrm{RQ}$. We begin by deriving an expression for $R Q$, which, as described on page 1188, is defined as the ratio of the rate of metabolic $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ production $\left(\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}\right)$ to the rate of $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ consumption $\left(\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}\right)$ :

$$
\mathrm{RQ}=\frac{\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}}{\dot{\mathrm{~V}}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}}
$$

For $R Q$ values less than unity, the body consumes more $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ than it produces $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$, so that the inspired alveolar ventilation $\left(\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{A}}\right)$ must be greater than the expired alveolar ventilation $\left(\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{A}_{E}}\right)$. Because the metabolically produced $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ must all appear in the expired alveolar gas, $\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ is the fraction of the expired alveolar ventilation that is $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ gas $\left(\mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}\right)$ :

$$
\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}=\mathrm{F}_{\mathrm{A}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}} \times \dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{A}_{E}}
$$

(NE 31-19)
Similarly, the consumed $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ must all enter the body via the lungs; $\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ is the difference between (1) the amount of $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ entering the alveoli during inspirations over a certain period of time, and (2) the amount of $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ exiting the alveoli during expirations over that same time interval:

$$
\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}=\underbrace{\left(\mathrm{F}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}} \times \dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{A}_{1}}\right)}_{\begin{array}{c}
\text { Rate of } \mathrm{O}_{2} \\
\text { entering the } \\
\text { alveoli }
\end{array}}-\underbrace{\left(\mathrm{F}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}} \times \dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{A}_{2}}\right)}_{\begin{array}{c}
\text { Rate of } \mathrm{O}_{2} \\
\text { leaving the } \\
\text { alveoli }
\end{array}}
$$

(NE 31-20)

Here, $\mathrm{F}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ is the mole fraction of $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ in the inspired air, and $\mathrm{FA}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ is the mole fraction of $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ in the expired alveolar air. Substituting the expressions for $\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ (see Equation NE 31-19) and $\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ (see Equation NE 31-20) into the definition of RQ (see Equation NE 31-18), we have

$$
\mathrm{RQ}=\frac{\mathrm{F}_{\mathrm{A}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}} \times \dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{A}_{E}}}{\left(\mathrm{~F}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}} \times \dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{A}_{1}}\right)-\left(\mathrm{F}_{\mathrm{A}_{O 2}} \times \dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{A}_{E}}\right)}=\frac{\mathrm{F}_{\mathrm{A}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}}}{\mathrm{~F}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}} \times \underbrace{\left(\frac{\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{A}_{1}}}{\dot{\mathrm{~V}}_{\mathrm{A}_{E}}}\right)-\mathrm{F}_{\mathrm{A}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}}}
$$

(NE 31-21)

We now need an expression for the ratio $\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{A}_{1}} \dot{\mathrm{~V}}_{\mathrm{A}_{E}}$. In the derivation of this expression, the fundamental assumption is that the $N_{2}$ gas in the lungs is not metabolized. Thus, in the steady state, the amount of $\mathrm{N}_{2}$ entering the alveoli with inspirations over a certain period of time is the same as the amount of $\mathrm{N}_{2}$ leaving the alveoli with the expirations over the same period. In other words,

$$
\underset{\substack{\text { Amount of } \mathrm{N}_{2} \\ \text { O}_{2} \\ \text { atient of } \mathrm{N}_{2}}}{\stackrel{\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{A}_{1}} \times \mathrm{F}_{\mathrm{N}_{2}}}{\text { Amount of } \mathrm{N}_{2}}}
$$

Here, $\mathrm{F}_{\mathrm{N}_{2}}$ is the mole fraction of $\mathrm{N}_{2}$ in this inspired gas, and $\mathrm{FA}_{\mathrm{N}_{2}}$ is the mole fraction of $\mathrm{N}_{2}$ in the expired alveolar air. For the rest of this derivation, we will consider only dry gases; the laboratory reports all partial pressure ( P ) and mole fraction ( F ) values in terms of the "dry" gas (see Box 26-1). Thus, considering only the dry inspired air, the mole fractions of $\mathrm{N}_{2}, \mathrm{O}_{2}$, and $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ must sum to 1 :

$$
\begin{gathered}
1=\mathrm{F}_{\mathrm{N}_{2}}+\mathrm{F}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}+\mathrm{F}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}} \\
\mathrm{~F}_{\mathrm{N}_{2}}=1-\mathrm{F}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}-\mathrm{F}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}
\end{gathered}
$$

Because the inspired air contains virtually no $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ (i.e., $\mathrm{F}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}} \cong 0$ ),

$$
\mathrm{F}_{\mathrm{N}_{2}}=1-\mathrm{F}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}
$$

(NE 31-24)
Similarly, considering only the dry expired air, the mole fractions of $\mathrm{N}_{2}, \mathrm{O}_{2}$, and $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ must likewise sum to 1 :

$$
\begin{gathered}
1=\mathrm{F}_{\mathrm{A}_{N_{2}}}+\mathrm{F}_{\mathrm{A}_{O_{2}}}+\mathrm{F}_{\mathrm{A}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}} \\
\mathrm{~F}_{\mathrm{A}_{N_{2}}}=1-\mathrm{F}_{\mathrm{A}_{O_{2}}}-\mathrm{F}_{\mathrm{A}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}}
\end{gathered}
$$

If we now substitute the expressions for $\mathrm{F}_{\mathrm{N}_{2}}$ (see Equation NE 31-24) and $\mathrm{FA}_{\mathrm{N}_{2}}$ (see Equation NE 31-25) into Equation NE $31-22$, we have

$$
\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{A}_{1}} \times\left(1-\mathrm{F}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}\right)=\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{A}_{E}} \times\left(1-\mathrm{F}_{\mathrm{A}_{O_{2}}}-\mathrm{F}_{\mathrm{A}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}}\right)
$$

(NE 31-26)
Solving for the ratio of inspired to expired alveolar ventilation yields

$$
\frac{\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{A}_{1}}}{\dot{\mathrm{~V}}_{\mathrm{A}_{E}}}=\frac{1-\mathrm{F}_{\mathrm{A}_{O_{2}}}-\mathrm{F}_{\mathrm{A}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}}}{1-\mathrm{F}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}}
$$

(NE 31-27)
We now have the ratio $\mathrm{IV}_{\mathrm{A}_{1}} \mathrm{IV}_{\mathrm{A}_{2}}$ that we needed for Equation NE 31-21. Substituting Equation NE 31-27 into Equation NE $31-21$, we get

$$
\mathrm{RQ}=\frac{\mathrm{F}_{\mathrm{A}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}}}{\mathrm{~F}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}} \times\left(\frac{1-\mathrm{F}_{\mathrm{A}_{O_{2}}}-\mathrm{F}_{\mathrm{A}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}}}{1-\mathrm{F}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}}\right)-\mathrm{F}_{\mathrm{A}_{O_{2}}}}
$$

(NE 31-28)
If we now solve Equation NE 31-28 for $\mathrm{FA}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$, we have

$$
\mathrm{F}_{\mathrm{A}_{O_{2}}}=\mathrm{F}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}-\mathrm{F}_{\mathrm{A}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}}\left[\mathrm{~F}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}+\frac{1-\mathrm{F}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}}{\mathrm{RQ}}\right]
$$

(NE 31-29)
Realizing that the "dry" partial pressure ( P ) is the product of mole fraction (the above F terms) and ( $\mathrm{Pb}-47$ ), we can multiply Equation NE 31-12 through by $(\mathrm{Pb}-47)$ to arrive at our final equation, which expresses quantities in terms of partial pressures:

$$
\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{A}_{O_{2}}}=\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}-\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{A}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}}\left[\mathrm{~F}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}+\frac{1-\mathrm{F}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}}{\mathrm{RQ}}\right]
$$

(NE 31-30)
Remember, all the partial-pressure and mole-fraction values in the above equation refer to dry gases. Thus, $\mathrm{Pb}_{\mathrm{b}_{2}}$ is $\mathrm{Fb}_{\mathrm{b}_{2}} \times$ $(\mathrm{Pb}-47)$.

On page 681, we provide a simplified version of Equation NE 31-30:

$$
\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{A}_{O_{2}}}=\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}-\frac{\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{A}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}}}{\mathrm{RQ}}
$$

(NE 31-31)
Why is this equation a reasonable approximation? Examination of Equations NE 31-30 and NE 31-31 shows that they are identical

# N31-8 The Alveolar Gas Equation-cont'd 

except that the term in brackets in Equation NE 31-30 is replaced by $1 / R Q$ in Equation NE 31-31. For Equation NE 31-30, we can rearrange the term in brackets as follows:

$$
\begin{aligned}
& {\left[\mathrm{F}_{1 / 2}+\frac{1-\mathrm{F}_{1 / 2}}{R Q}\right]=\left[\mathrm{F}_{1 / 2} \frac{R Q}{R Q}+\frac{1-\mathrm{F}_{1 / 2}}{R Q}\right]} \\
& =\left[\frac{\mathrm{F}_{1 / 2}-R Q+1-\mathrm{F}_{1 / 2}}{R Q}\right] \quad \text { (NE 31-32) } \\
& =\left[\frac{\mathrm{F}_{1 / 2}(R Q-1 / 2+1}{R Q}\right] \quad \text { (NE 31-33) }
\end{aligned}
$$

Under physiological conditions-when $F_{1 / 2}$ is 0.21 and $R Q$ is 0.8 -the above expression evaluates to $0.958 / R Q$, which is quite close to $1 / R Q \ldots$ which is to say that Equation NE 31-31 is a reasonable approximation of Equation 31-30 under these conditions.

When RQ is unity, the term in brackets in Equation 31-30 evaluates to unity, and Equation NE 31-30 simplifies to Equation 31-16 in the text:

$$
\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{A}_{1 / 2}}=\mathrm{P}_{1 / 2}-\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{A}_{1 / 2}}
$$

(NE 31-33)

![bo118465312c5](bo118465312c5.jpg)

Figure 31-5 Distribution of ventilation. (Data from West JB: Respiratory Physiology—The Essentials, 4th ed. Baltimore, Williams \& Wilkins, 1990.)

This sort of analysis shows that alveolar ventilation in a standing person gradually falls from the base to the apex of the lung (see Fig. 31-5B). Why? The answers are posture and gravity. In Chapter 27 we saw that, because of the lung's weight, intrapleural pressure $\left(\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{IP}}\right)$ is more negative at the apex than at the base when the subject is upright (see Fig. 31-5C). The practical consequences of this $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{IP}}$ gradient become clear when we examine a static pressure-volume diagram not for the lungs as a whole (see Fig. 27-5), but rather for a small piece of lung (see Fig. 31-5D). We assume that the intrinsic mechanical properties of the airways are the same, regardless of whether the tissue is at the base or at the apex. At the base, where $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{IP}}$ might be only $-2.5 \mathrm{~cm} \mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O}$ at FRC, the alveoli are relatively underinflated compared to tissues at the apex, where $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{IP}}$ might be $-10 \mathrm{~cm} \mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O}$ and the alveoli are relatively overinflated. However, because the base of the lung is underinflated at FRC, it is on a steeper part of the pressure-volume curve (i.e., it has a greater static compliance) than the overinflated apex. Thus, during an inspiration, the same $\Delta \mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{IP}}$ (e.g., $2.5 \mathrm{~cm} \mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O}$ ) produces a larger $\Delta \mathrm{V}_{1}$ near the base than near the apex. Keep in mind that it is the
change in volume per unit time, not the initial volume, that defines ventilation.

The relationship between ventilation and basal versus apical location in the lung would be reversed if the subject hung by the knees from a trapeze. A person reclining on the right side would ventilate the dependent lung tissue on the right side better than the elevated lung tissue on the left. Of course, the right-to-left $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{IP}}$ gradient in the reclining subject would be smaller than the apex-to-base $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{IP}}$ gradient in the standing subject, reflecting the smaller distance (i.e., smaller hydrostatic pressure difference). Subjects under microgravity conditions (see p. 1233), such as astronauts aboard the International Space Station, experience no $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{IP}}$ gradients, and thus no gravity-dependent regional differences in ventilation.

## Restrictive and obstructive pulmonary diseases can exacerbate the nonuniformity of ventilation

Even in microgravity, where we would expect no regional differences in ventilation, ventilation would still be nonuniform

A REDUCE COMPLJANCE OF ONE LUNG TO HALF NORMAL
![bo119465312c5](bo119465312c5.jpg)

Figure 31-6 Pathological nonuniformity of ventilation.
at the microscopic or local level because of seemingly random differences in local static compliance (C) and airway resistance (R). In fact, such local differences in the ventilation of alveolar units are probably more impressive than gravitydependent regional differences.
