# N31-18 Analysis of $\dot{V} / \dot{\mathrm{O}}$ Patterns in Figure 31-13 

## Contributed by Emile Boulpaep and Walter Boron

The following is a discussion of Figure 31-13 that is more complete than the material presented in the subsection "Normal Lungs" (see p. 693).

Figure 31-13 is a highly simplified example illustrating the principles underlying how an individual with a normal $\dot{V}_{A} / \dot{Q}$ in each lung handles $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ (Fig. 31-13A) and $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ (Fig. 31-13B) as blood flows through the lungs, the systemic circulation, and then back to the lungs. We make the following assumptions:

- Total $\dot{V}_{A}\left(4.2 \mathrm{~L} / \mathrm{min}\right)$ and $\dot{Q}\left(5 \mathrm{~L} / \mathrm{min}\right)$ are normal.
- $\dot{V}_{A}$ and $\dot{Q}$ are evenly divided between the two lungs. Thus, the overall $\dot{V}_{A} / \dot{Q}$ ratio is $\left(4.2 \mathrm{~L} / \mathrm{min}\right) /\left(5 \mathrm{~L} / \mathrm{min}\right)$ or 0.84 . Of course, the $\dot{V}_{A} / \dot{Q}$ in each of the two lungs is also 0.84 .
- $\dot{V}_{A}$ and $\dot{Q}$ are uniformly distributed within each lung. In other words, we are ignoring the usual apex-to-base inequalities of both $\dot{V}_{A}$ and $\dot{Q}$ (see Fig. 31-10). In addition, we assume that the lungs are free of pathological causes of either alveolar dead-space ventilation or shunt.
- The system has no physiological right-to-left shunts.
- The $\mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ in each lung will be 40 mm Hg ; and the $\mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ 100 mm Hg .
- Total-body $\dot{V}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ will be $200 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{min}$ and total-body $\dot{V}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ will be $250 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{min}$.
- The entire system is in a steady state.

Predicting precisely how $\dot{V}_{A} / \dot{Q}$ abnormalities affect the $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ and $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ partial pressures in the three key compartments (alveoli, arterial blood, and mixed-venous blood) is extremely complicated, in part because changes in $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ and pH affect $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ carriage (the Bohr effect; see p. 652) and changes in $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ affect $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ carriage (the Haldane effect; see p. 657). Moreover, in reality, $\dot{V}_{A}, \dot{Q}$, and the $\dot{V}_{A} / \dot{Q}$ ratio are not uniform throughout each lung. However, although the examples in the text are greatly simplified, they illustrate the principles of how $\dot{V}_{A} / \dot{Q}$ mismatches lead to abnormalities in the arterial blood gases. We begin here with a discussion of the normal condition. We will consider alveolar dead-space ventilation in (5) N31-19 and shunt in (5) N31-20.

We will now follow the blood during one circuit through the circulation to see the relationship among the composition of the arterial blood, the $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ and $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ transport rates in the lung, and the composition of the mixed-venous blood.

## $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ Handling

First consider the fate of $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ in the following six steps (see Fig. 31-13A):
Step 1: Each lung must first receive and then exhale half of the total $200 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{min}$ of $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ that body metabolism produces. In other words, 100 mL of $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ enters each lung each minute, and because they are in a steady state, each lung exhales precisely this same amount each minute.
Step 2: In each lung, a $\dot{V}_{A}$ of $2.1 \mathrm{~L} / \mathrm{min}$ dilutes 100 mL of exhaled $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ (i.e., the exhaled 100 mL of $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ is contained within the 2100 mL of exhaled air). This combination of $\dot{V}_{A}$ and $\dot{V}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ corresponds to a $\mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ of -40 mm Hg .
Step 3: Because $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ transport is perfusion limited (see pp. 671-673), the blood that emerges from each lung has the same $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ as the alveolar air, 40 mm Hg . According to the $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ dissociation curve, we see that this $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ of 40 mm Hg corresponds to an arterial $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ content of $48 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{dL}$ (see lower panel of Fig. 31-13A, point a).
Step 4: As the blood flows through the systemic capillaries, it picks up the $200 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{min}$ of $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ produced by metabolism. Given a cardiac output of $5 \mathrm{~L} / \mathrm{min}$, we can use the Fick
principle (see p. 423) to compute how much the $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ content must rise in the capillaries for each 100 mL of blood:
$\Delta \mathrm{C}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}=\frac{\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}}{\dot{\mathrm{O}}}=\frac{200 \mathrm{~mL} \mathrm{CO}_{2} / \mathrm{min}}{5 \mathrm{~L} \text { blood } / \mathrm{min}}=40 \mathrm{~mL} \mathrm{CO}_{2} / \mathrm{L}=4 \mathrm{~mL} \mathrm{CO}_{2} / \mathrm{dL}$
(NE 31-42)
Thus, the mixed-venous $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ content must be $48+4=$ $52 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{dL}$.
Step 5: According to the $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ dissociation curve, when the $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ content of the blood is $52 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{dL}$, the mixed-venous $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ must be 46 mm Hg (lower panel of Fig. 31-13A, point $\vec{v}$ ).
Step 6: As the blood completes the cycle and passes through the pulmonary capillaries, the $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ falls from 46 mm Hg to its original value of 40 mm Hg as 100 mL of $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ diffuses from the pulmonary capillaries to the alveoli each minute in each lung. Simultaneously, the $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ content falls from 52 to $48 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{dL}$ as $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ evolves into the alveolar air at the rate of $100 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{min}$ in each lung ( $200 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{min}$, total).

## $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ Handling

Now consider the fate of $\mathrm{O}_{2}$, illustrated in six steps in Figure 31-13B. The situation for $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ is comparable to that for $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$, although the fluxes are all in a direction opposite to those for $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$.
Step 1: Each lung must take up half of the $250 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{min} \mathrm{O}_{2}$ required by body metabolism, that is, $125 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{min}$ for each lung.
Step 2: For a $\mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ of 40 mm Hg and an RQ (see Equation 31-14) of 0.8 , the alveolar gas equation (see Equation 31-17) predicts a $\mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ of -100 mm Hg for each lung.
Step 3: The transport of $\mathrm{O}_{2}$, like that of $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$, is perfusion limited (see pp. 671-673), so that the blood emerging from each lung has a $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ of -100 mm Hg . According to the $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ dissociation curve, a $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ of 100 mm Hg corresponds to an arterial $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ content of $20 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{dL}$ (see lower panel of Fig. 31-13B, point a).
Step 4: As the blood flows through the systemic capillaries, it must give up $250 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{min}$ of $\mathrm{O}_{2}$, which will be consumed by metabolism. According to the Fick principle, the decrease in $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ content that occurs as the blood passes through the systemic capillaries is

$$
\Delta \mathrm{C}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}=\frac{\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}}{\dot{\mathrm{Q}}}=\frac{250 \mathrm{~mL} \mathrm{O}_{2} / \mathrm{min}}{5 \mathrm{~L} \text { blood } / \mathrm{min}}=50 \mathrm{~mL} \mathrm{O}_{2} / \mathrm{L}=5 \mathrm{~mL} \mathrm{O}_{2} / \mathrm{dL}
$$

(NE 31-43)
Thus, the mixed-venous $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ content must be $20-5=$ $15 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{dL}$.
Step 5: From the $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ dissociation curve, we see that a mixed-venous $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ content of $15 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{dL}$ corresponds to a mixed-venous $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ of 40 mm Hg (see lower panel of Fig. 31-13B, point $\vec{v}$ ).
Step 6: As the blood completes the cycle and passes through the lungs, it must pick up $125 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{min}$ of $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ in each lung. This influx of $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ causes the $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ content to increase from 15 to $20 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{dL}$, as the blood $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ increases from 40 to 100 mm Hg .

# N31-19 Analysis of $\dot{V}_{1} \dot{Q}$ Patterns in Figure 31-14 

## Contributed by Emile Boulpaep and Walter Boron

The following is a discussion of Figure 31-14 that is more complete than the material presented in the subsection "Alveolar DeadSpace Ventilation Affecting One Lung" (see pp. 693-696).

Figure 31-14 is a highly simplified example that illustrates the principles underlying how alveolar dead-space ventilation affects the handling of $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ and $\mathrm{O}_{2}$. Except as noted below in this paragraph, all assumptions and parameter values for this example are the same as for the normal case in Figure 31-13. 0 N3118 The key difference in the example in Figure 31-14 is that the left lung receives no perfusion-alveolar dead-space ventilation. The total $\dot{Q}$ remains at $5 \mathrm{~L} / \mathrm{min}$, but all of this perfusion goes to the right lung. Notice that the $\dot{V}_{A}$ of $4.2 \mathrm{~L} / \mathrm{min}$ is evenly distributed between the two lungs. Thus, the $\dot{V}_{A} / \dot{Q}$ of the left lung is $2.1 / 0$ or $\infty$, whereas the $\dot{V}_{A} / \dot{Q}$ of the right lung is $2.1 / 5$ or 0.42 . The overall $\dot{V}_{A} / \dot{Q}$ remains 0.84 . Can the low $\dot{V}_{A} / \dot{Q}$ of the normal lung make up for the high $\dot{V}_{A} / \dot{Q}$ of the abnormal lung?

## $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ Handling

First consider the fate of $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ in the following seven steps (see Fig. 31-14):
Step 1: In the steady state, the normal right lung must eliminate the entire complement of metabolically produced $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ production, which is $200 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{min}$. This rate of $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ delivery to the right lung is twice its normal level.
Step 2: In the right lung, the normal $\dot{V}_{A}$ of $2.1 \mathrm{~L} / \mathrm{min}$ must now dilute twice the normal amount of $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$. Thus, alveolar $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ in this right lung must be twice normal (i.e., $\sim 80 \mathrm{~mm} \mathrm{Hg}$ ).
Step 3: Because no blood is flowing to the left lung, its alveolar $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ is the same as the $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ of inspired air (i.e., $\sim 0 \mathrm{~mm} \mathrm{Hg}$ ). Half of the alveolar air that the subject expires comes from the right lung, and half comes from the left lung. Thus, the mean $\mathbf{P A}_{\mathbf{CO}_{2}}$ is $(80+0) / 2=40 \mathrm{~mm} \mathrm{Hg}$, which is normal! As we will see below, this mean alveolar $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ is important for computing the alveolar-arterial (A-a) difference (see Box 31-1).
Step 4: Because the transport of $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ across the blood-gas barrier is perfusion limited (see pp. 671-673), the blood leaving the right lung has a $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ that is the same as the alveolar $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ of the right lung, 80 mm Hg . Because no blood exits from the left lung, the systemic arterial blood also has a $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ of 80 mm Hg . According to the $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ dissociation curve, a $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ of 80 mm Hg (which represents a substantial degree of respiratory acidosis) corresponds to a $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ content of $\sim 67 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{dL}$ (see lower panel of Fig. 31-14A, point al.
Step 5: In the systemic capillaries, metabolically produced $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ enters the blood at the rate of $200 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{min}$. As we already know from our analysis of the normal condition in Figure 31-13A, adding $200 \mathrm{~mL} \mathrm{CO}_{2}$ to 5 liters of blood increases the $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ content by $4 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{dL}$ :

$$
\Delta \mathrm{C}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}=\frac{\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}}{\dot{\mathrm{Q}}}=\frac{200 \mathrm{~mL} \mathrm{CO}_{2} / \mathrm{min}}{5 \mathrm{~L} \text { blood } / \mathrm{min}}=40 \mathrm{~mLCO}_{2} / \mathrm{L}=4 \mathrm{~mLCO}_{2} / \mathrm{dL}
$$

$(\mathrm{NE} 31-44)$
Thus, the $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ content of the blood at the end of the systemic capillaries will be $67+4=71 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{dL}$.
Step 6: According to the $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ dissociation curve, when the $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ content of the blood is $71 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{dL}$, the mixed-venous $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ must be 87 mm Hg (see lower panel of Fig. 31-14A, point $\vec{v}$ ).
Step 7: As the blood completes the cycle and passes through the pulmonary capillaries of the right lung, the $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ falls from 87 mm Hg back to its original value of 80 mm Hg . Simultaneously, the $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ content falls from $71 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{dL}$ to its original value of $67 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{dL}$ as $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ evolves into the alveolar air at the rate of $200 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{min}$.

Thus, even with a severe $\dot{V}_{A} / \dot{Q}$ abnormality, the lung is able to expel the usual $200 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{min}$ of $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$, but at a tremendous price: a very high arterial $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ and thus respiratory acidosis (see p. 633).

## $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ Handling

Now consider the fate of $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ (see Fig. 31-14B):
Step 1: The normal right lung must deliver all the $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ required by the body, $250 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{min}$. This rate of $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ abstraction from the right lung is twice normal.
Step 2: Because the $\dot{V}_{A} / \dot{Q}$ ratio of the normal right lung is so low (i.e., half normal), the alveolar $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ in the right lung must be far lower than the usual value of $\sim 100 \mathrm{~mm} \mathrm{Hg}$. Because the $\mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ in this lung is 80 mm Hg and $\mathrm{RQ}_{\mathrm{I}}$ is 0.8 , we use the alveolar gas equation (see Equation 31-17) to compute a $\mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ of 51 mm Hg .
Step 3: Because no blood flow goes to the left lung, its $\dot{V}_{A} / \dot{Q}$ is $\infty$, and thus its alveolar $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ is the same as the $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ of inspired air (i.e., 149 mm Hg ). The mean $\mathbf{P A}_{\mathbf{O}_{2}}$ of air expired from the two lungs is $(51+149) / 2=100 \mathrm{~mm} \mathrm{Hg}$ and thus is normal-just as the mean $\mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ was normal.
Step 4: Because the transport of $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ across the blood-gas barrier is perfusion limited (see pp. 671-673), the $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ of the blood leaving the right lung is the same as the alveolar $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ of the right lung, 51 mm Hg . Because no blood exits from the left lung, the systemic arterial blood has the same $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ as the blood leaving the right lung, namely 51 mm Hg . From the $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ dissociation curve, we see that a $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ of 51 mm Hg dictates an $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ content of only $14 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{dL}$ (see lower panel of Fig. 31-14B, point al.
Step 5: In the systemic capillaries, $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ leaves the blood at the rate of $250 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{min}$. As we already know from our analysis of Figure 31-13B, removing 250 mL of $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ from 5 L of blood decreases the $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ content by $5 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{dL}$ :

$$
\Delta \mathrm{C}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}=\frac{\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}}{\dot{\mathrm{Q}}}=\frac{250 \mathrm{~mL} \mathrm{O}_{2} / \mathrm{min}}{5 \mathrm{~L} \text { blood } / \mathrm{min}}=50 \mathrm{~mL} \mathrm{O}_{2} / \mathrm{L}=5 \mathrm{~mL} \mathrm{O}_{2} / \mathrm{dL}
$$

$(\mathrm{NE} 31-45)$
Thus, the $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ content of the blood at the end of the systemic capillaries will be $14-5=9 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{dL}$.
Step 6: According to the $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ dissociation curve, when the $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ content is $9 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{dL}$, the mixed-venous $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ must be 37 mm Hg (see lower panel of Fig. 31-14B, point $\vec{v}$ ).
Step 7: As the blood completes the cycle and passes through the pulmonary capillaries of the right lung, the $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ rises from 37 to 51 mm Hg , and the $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ content rise from 9 to $14 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{dL}$ as $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ enters from the alveolar air at the rate of $250 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{min}$.
Thus, even with a severe $\dot{V}_{A} / \dot{Q}$ abnormality, the lung is able to import the usual $250 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{min}$ of $\mathrm{O}_{2}$, but at a tremendous price: a very low arterial $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ (hypoxia).

Beware that this analysis, as complicated as it may seem, is highly oversimplified. For example, in step 4 and step 6 of the

# N31-19 Analysis of $\dot{V} / \dot{Q}$ Patterns in Figure 31-14-cont'd 

$\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ analysis, we used a standard $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ dissociation curve. However, we know that hypoxia will increase the $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$-carrying capacity of blood (Haldane effect; see p. 657). Similarly, in step 4 and step 6 of the $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ analysis, we used a standard $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ dissociation curve. However, we know that respiratory acidosis will decrease the $\mathrm{O}_{2}$-carrying capacity of blood (the Bohr effect; see p. 652). Nevertheless, the take-home message is clear. Even if
overall $\dot{V}_{\mathrm{A}}$ and $\dot{\mathrm{Q}}$ are normal, an uneven distribution of perfusion leads to respiratory acidosis and hypoxia. Thus, the hyperperfused "good" lung cannot make up for the deficit incurred by the hypoperfused "bad" lung. In our example, the fundamental problem is that ventilating unperfused alveoli in the left lung reduces the effective alveolar ventilation by half.

## N31-20 Analysis of V/Q Patterns in Figure 31-15

## Contributed by Emile Boulpaep and Walter Boron

The following is a discussion of Figure 31-15 that is more complete than the material presented in the subsection "Shunt Affecting One Lung" (see pp. 696-698).

Figure 31-15 is a highly simplified example that illustrates the principles underlying how a shunt affects the handling of $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ and $\mathrm{O}_{2}$. Except as noted below in this paragraph, all assumptions and parameter values for this example are the same as for the normal case in Figure 31-13. N31-18 The key difference in the example in Figure 31-15 is that the left lung receives no ventilation-shunt. The total $\dot{V}_{\mathrm{A}}$ remains $4.2 \mathrm{~L} / \mathrm{min}$, but all of this ventilation goes to the right lung. Notice that the $\dot{\mathrm{Q}}$ of $5 \mathrm{~L} / \mathrm{min}$ is evenly distributed between the two lungs. Thus, the $\dot{V}_{\mathrm{A}} / \dot{\mathrm{Q}}$ of the left lung is $0 / 2.5$ or 0 , whereas the $\dot{V}_{\mathrm{A}} / \dot{\mathrm{Q}}$ of the right lung is $4.2 / 2.5$ or 1.68. Can the high $\dot{V}_{\mathrm{A}} / \dot{\mathrm{Q}}$ of the hyperventilated right lung make up for the low $\dot{V}_{\mathrm{A}} / \dot{\mathrm{Q}}$ of the unventilated left lung?

## $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ Handling

First, we will consider the fate of $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ in the following nine steps (see Fig. 31-15A):
Step 1: In the steady state, the normal right lung must eliminate the entire metabolic production of $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$, $200 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{min}$. This rate of $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ delivery to the right lung is twice normal.
Step 2: The twice-normal amount of $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ is diluted into a twice-normal $\dot{V}_{\mathrm{A}}$ of $4.2 \mathrm{~L} / \mathrm{min}$, so that the alveolar $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ in this right lung must be normal (i.e., 40 mm Hg ).
Step 3: Because no air flow goes to the left lung, the air distal to the obstruction in the left lung will have the same $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ and the same $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ as mixed-venous blood. However, because this air is not in direct communication with atmosphere, it does not contribute to the mean alveolar $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ of the expired air. Thus, the mean $\mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ is simply the $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ of the alveolar air in the right lung, 40 mm Hg , which is normal!
Step 4: Because the transport of $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ across the blood-gas barrier is perfusion limited (see pp. 671-673), the blood leaving the right lung has a $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ that is the same as the alveolar $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ on the right, 40 mm Hg . According to the $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ dissociation curve, when the blood has a $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ of 40 mm Hg , it must have a $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ content of $48 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{dL}$ (see lower panel of Fig. 31-15A, point $c$ ). This is the $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ content after the right lung has extracted $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ at the rate of $200 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{min}$.
Step 5: What was the $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ content of the blood before the $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ was extracted? That is, what was the mixed-venous $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ ? According to the Fick principle, the change in $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ content
as the blood passes through the right lung is simply the rate of $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ extraction divided by the blood flow:
$\Delta \mathrm{C}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}=\frac{\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}}{\dot{\mathrm{Q}}}=\frac{200 \mathrm{~mL} \mathrm{CO}_{2} / \mathrm{min}}{2.5 \mathrm{~L} \text { blood } / \mathrm{min}}=80 \mathrm{~mL} \mathrm{CO}_{2} / \mathrm{L}=8 \mathrm{~mL} \mathrm{CO}_{2} / \mathrm{dL}$
(NE 31-46)
Normally (see Fig. 31-13A), the $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ content of the pulmonary-capillary blood would fall by only $4 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{dL}$ as it equilibrated with the alveolar air. However, because the rate of $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ extraction is twice normal, the decrease in $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ content is also twice normal. Thus, the mixed-venous $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ content must be $48+8=56 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{dL}$. From the $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ dissociation curve, we see that this $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ content corresponds to a mixed-venous $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ of 51 mm Hg (see lower panel of Fig. 31-15A, point $\vec{v}$ ). Notice that the alveolar air in the obstructed left lung has a $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ of 51 mm Hg , the same as that of the mixed-venous blood.
Step 6: What happens when the blood from the hyperventilated right lung mixes with the shunted blood from the unventilated left lung? Although one might be tempted to average the two $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ values (i.e., 40 and 51 mm Hg ), this is a mistaken instinct. Instead, one must average the two $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ contents. As we noted above, the $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ content of blood emerging from the right lung is $48 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{dL}$. The $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ content of blood emerging from the left lung is the same as for the mixed-venous blood we examined in the previous step, $56 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{dL}$. Thus, the mixed arterial $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ content is $(48+56) / 2=52 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{dL}$ (see lower panel of Fig. 31-15A, point a).
Step 7: According to the $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ dissociation curve, a $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ content of $52 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{dL}$ corresponds to an arterial $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ of 46 mm Hg (see lower panel of Fig. 31-15A, point a).
Step 8: In the systemic capillaries, adding $200 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{min}$ of $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ to 5 L of blood increases the $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ content by $4 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{dL}$, raising the $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ content from $52 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{dL}$ (lower panel of Fig. 31-15A, point a) to $56 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{dL}$ (see lower panel of Fig. 31-15A, point $\vec{v}$ ). Notice that $56 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{dL}$ is the mixed-venous value we arrived at above in step 5.
Step 9: According to the $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ dissociation curve, a $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ content of $56 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{dL}$ corresponds to a mixed-venous $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ of 51 mm Hg (see lower panel of Fig. 31-15A, point $\vec{v}$ ). This is the same value that we arrived at above in step 5.
Thus, even with the severe $\dot{V}_{\mathrm{A}} / \dot{\mathrm{Q}}$ abnormality represented by shunt, the lung is once again able to expel the usual $200 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{min}$ of $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$, but once again at a price: a high arterial $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ (respiratory

# N31-20 Analysis of $\dot{V} / \dot{\mathrm{O}}$ Patterns in Figure 31-15-cont'd 

acidosis). Because there is a $\dot{V}_{A} / \dot{Q}$ mismatch in this example, we expect there to be an alveolar-arterial (A-a) difference for $\mathbf{P}_{\mathbf{O}_{2},} \boldsymbol{\nabla}$ N31-17 Because the mean alveolar $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ is 40 mm Hg , and the arterial $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ is 46 mm Hg in our example, the A-a difference is $40-46$ or -6 mm Hg .

## $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ Handling

The changes in $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ handling are more extreme (see Fig. 31-15B). Step 1: The normal right lung must deliver all the $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ required by the body, $250 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{min}$. This rate of $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ abstraction from the right lung is twice normal.
Step 2: Because the $\dot{V}_{A} / \dot{Q}$ ratio of the normal right lung is twice normal, and the $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ extraction rate is twice normal, we would expect the alveolar $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ in the right lung to be the normal value of -100 mm Hg. Put differently, because the inspired $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ (i.e., 149 mm Hg ), the alveolar $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ of the right lung (i.e., 40 mm Hg ), and the RQ (i.e., 0.8 ) are all normal, the alveolar gas equation (see Equation 31-17) yields a normal $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ for the right lung, -100 mm Hg .
Step 3: Of course, the $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ in the unventilated left lung $\left(\dot{V}_{A} / \dot{Q}=0\right.$ ) is the same as the $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ of mixed-venous blood. However, because this air is not in direct communication with atmosphere, it does not contribute to the mean alveolar $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ of the expired air. Thus, the mean $\mathbf{P A}_{\mathbf{O}_{2}}$ is simply the $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ of the alveolar air in the right lung, -100 mm Hg , which is normal!
Step 4: Because the transport of $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ across the blood-gas barrier is perfusion limited (see pp. 671-673), the $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ of the blood leaving the right lung is the same as the $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ in the right lung, -100 mm Hg . Thus, according to the $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ dissociation curve, the $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ content of this blood must be $20 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{dL}$ (see lower panel of Fig. 31-15B, point $d$ ). This value is the $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ content after the right lung has added $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ at the rate of $250 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{min}$.
Step 5: What was the $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ content of the mixed-venous blood? According to the Fick principle, the increase in $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ content as the blood passes through the right lung is simply the rate of $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ extraction divided by the blood flow. Normally (see Fig. 31-15B), the $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ content would rise by only $5 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{dL}$ as the blood equilibrated with the alveolar air. However, because the rate of $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ extraction from the lung is twice normal, the increase in $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ content is also twice normal:
$\Delta \mathrm{C}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}=\frac{\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}}{\dot{\mathrm{Q}}}=\frac{250 \mathrm{~mL} \mathrm{O}_{2} / \mathrm{min}}{2.5 \mathrm{~L} \text { blood } / \mathrm{min}}=100 \mathrm{~mL} \mathrm{O}_{2} / \mathrm{L}=10 \mathrm{~mL} \mathrm{O}_{2} / \mathrm{dL}$
(NE 31-47)
Thus, the mixed-venous $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ content must be $10 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{dL}$ less than the $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ content of the end-capillary blood (i.e., $20 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{dL}$ ). The mixed-venous $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ content is thus $20-10=$ $10 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{dL}$. From the $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ dissociation curve, we see that this $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ content corresponds to a mixed-venous $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ of 29 mm Hg (see lower panel of Fig. 31-15B, point $\vec{v}$ ). The alveolar air in the obstructed left lung has a $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ of 29 mm Hg , the same as that of the mixed-venous blood.

Step 6: What happens when the blood from the hyperventilated right lung mixes with the shunted blood from the unventilated left lung? One must average the $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ contents of the blood emerging from the right and left lungs. As we noted above, the $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ content of blood emerging from the right lung is $20 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{dL}$. The $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ content of blood emerging from the left lung is the same as for the mixed-venous blood we examined in the previous step, $10 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{dL}$. Thus, the mixed arterial $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ content is $(20+$ $10) / 2=15 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{dL}$ (see lower panel of Fig. 31-15B, point a).
Step 7: From the $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ dissociation curve, we see that a mixed-arterial $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ content of $15 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{dL}$ corresponds to a $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ of -40 mm Hg (see lower panel of Fig. 31-15B, point a).
Step 8: In the systemic capillaries, the tissues extract 250 $\mathrm{mL} / \mathrm{min}$ of $\mathrm{O}_{2}$. Removing 250 mL of $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ from 5 L of blood decreases the $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ content by $5 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{dL}$. Thus, the $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ content of the blood at the end of the systemic capillaries will be $15-5=10 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{dL}$. This is the same value we arrived at in step 5 (see lower panel of Fig. 31-15B, point $\vec{v}$ ).
Step 9: According to the $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ dissociation curve, an $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ content of $10 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{dL}$ corresponds to a mixed-venous $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ of 29 mm Hg (see lower panel of Fig. 31-15B, point $\vec{v}$ ). This is the same figure we arrived at in step 5.
Thus, even with the severe $\dot{V}_{A} / \dot{Q}$ abnormality represented by shunt, the lung is able to import the usual $250 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{min}$ of $\mathrm{O}_{2}$, but at a price: an extremely low arterial $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ (hypoxia).

The A-a difference for $\mathbf{P}_{\mathbf{O}_{2}}$ in this example is $100-40=$ 60 mm Hg , which indicates a $\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{A}} / \dot{\mathrm{Q}}$ mismatch of considerable magnitude. In this extreme example, we entirely eliminated airflow to one lung. Less extreme instances of uneven distribution of airflow will lead to less severe degrees of respiratory acidosis and hypoxia.

Again, beware that this example, like the ones in Figure 31-13 and Figure 31-14, is highly oversimplified. Nevertheless, it is clear that even if overall $\dot{V}_{A} / \dot{Q}$ is normal, and even if the distribution of perfusion is normal, an uneven distribution of ventilation leads to respiratory acidosis and hypoxia. Thus, the overventilated "good" lung cannot make up for the deficit incurred by the underventilated "bad" lung. One fundamental problem is that blood with a normal gas composition from the hyperventilated good lung mixes with shunted blood with a high $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ and low $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$. The consequences are perhaps easiest to grasp for $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ transport. The blood leaving the normal right lung has a normal $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ content. The shunted blood leaving the unventilated left lung has a very low $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ content, which is the same as that of mixed-venous blood. Thus, the mixed-arterial blood must have a low $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ content and must be hypoxic.

A second fundamental problem is that-because of the shape of the $\mathrm{Hb}-\mathrm{O}_{2}$ dissociation curve-hyperventilating a good lung cannot proportionally increase the $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ content of the blood leaving that lung. What would happen if the subject were to hyperventilate? Not much. It is true that increasing alveolar ventilation might increase the right alveolar $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ above its starting value of 100 mm Hg . However, because the Hb is already $97.5 \%$ saturated with $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ at a $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ of 100 mm Hg , the added ventilation would do little good. The subject would remain hypoxic.

![bo130465312c5](bo130465312c5.jpg)

Figure 31-14 Alveolar dead-space ventilation. (C) N31-19 The numbers in this idealized example refer to a time after the individual has achieved a new steady state. In the upper panels of $\mathbf{A}$ and $\mathbf{B}$, the light beige boxes give the alveolar ventilation to each lung as well as the total alveolar ventilation $\left(\dot{V}_{\mathrm{A}}\right)$. The pink boxes give the blood flow to each lung as well as the total cardiac output ( $\dot{\mathrm{Q}}$ ). The white boxes give the rates of $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ or $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ transport (in $\mathrm{mL} / \mathrm{min}$ ) at either the pulmonary or systemic capillaries. The blue boxes give the $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ and $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ partial pressures (in mm Hg ) in alveolar air. The lavender boxes give the $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ partial pressure (in mm Hg ) and $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ content (in $\mathrm{mL} / \mathrm{dL}$ ) in the mixed-venous blood, in the blood leaving each of the lungs, and in the mixed-arterial blood. The dark beige boxes give the same information for $\mathrm{O}_{2}$. The lower panel of A shows plots of how total $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ content in this example varies with $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ at the $\mathrm{Hb}-\mathrm{O}_{2}$ saturations of arterial blood (red line) and mixed-venous blood (violet line). Despite the severe respiratory acidosis, the decrease in total $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ content between the mixed-venous and arterial blood is normal ( $4 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{dL}$ ). The lower panel of B shows comparable plots for how total $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ content varies with $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ at the pH and $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ values of mixed-venous blood (violet curve) and arterial blood (red curve). Despite the severe hypoxia, the increase in total $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ content between the mixed-venous and arterial blood is normal ( $5 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{dL}$ ).

Figure 31-15 Shunt. (T) N21-20 The numbers in this idealized example refer to a time after the individual has achieved a new steady state. In the upper panels of $\mathbf{A}$ and $\mathbf{B}$, the light beige boxes give the alveolar ventilation to each lung as well as the total alveolar ventilation $\left(\dot{V}_{\mathrm{A}}\right)$. The pink boxes give the blood flow to each lung as well as the total cardiac output ( $\dot{\mathrm{Q}}$ ). The white boxes give the rates of $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ or $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ transport (in $\mathrm{mL} / \mathrm{min}$ ) at either the pulmonary or systemic capillaries. The blue boxes give the $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ and $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ partial pressures (in mm Hg ) in alveolar air. The dark beige boxes give the same information for $\mathrm{O}_{2}$. #### Abstract

lung is $2.1 / 0$ or $\infty$, whereas the $\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{A}} / \dot{\mathrm{Q}}$ to the right lung is $2.1 / 5$ or 0.42 . The overall $\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{A}} / \dot{\mathrm{Q}}$ is normal, 0.84 . The key question is whether a combination of a high $\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{A}} / \dot{\mathrm{Q}}$ in the abnormal lung and a low $\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{A}} / \dot{\mathrm{Q}}$ in the normal lung can yield normal blood-gas levels.

The normal right lung must now eliminate all of the $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ that the body produces-that is, the right lung must eliminate $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ at twice the rate that it normally does. However, the right lung has its usual $\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{A}}$ of $2.1 \mathrm{~L} / \mathrm{min}$. Because a normal amount of alveolar air must carry away twice as much $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ in the new steady state, the right lung's alveolar $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ doubles to $\sim 80 \mathrm{~mm} \mathrm{Hg}$ (see Fig. 31-14A). Because the entire cardiac output perfuses the normal right lung, arterial $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ is also $\sim 80 \mathrm{~mm} \mathrm{Hg}$. Thus, even with the severe $\dot{V}_{\mathrm{A}} / \dot{\mathrm{Q}}$ abnormality produced by alveolar dead-space ventilation, the lung is able to expel the usual $200 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{min}$ of $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$, but at a tremendous price: a very high arterial $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ and thus respiratory acidosis.

The normal right lung must also supply all of the body's $\mathrm{O}_{2}$-delivering $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ to the blood at twice its normal rate. However, because the right lung still has its normal $\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{A}}$ of $2.1 \mathrm{~L} / \mathrm{min}$, its alveolar $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ falls to $\sim 51 \mathrm{~mm} \mathrm{Hg}$ in the new steady state. The blood leaving the right lung, which is identical to systemic arterial blood, also has a $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ of $\sim 51 \mathrm{~mm} \mathrm{Hg}$. Thus, even with a severe $\dot{V}_{\mathrm{A}} / \dot{\mathrm{Q}}$ abnormality, the lung is able to import the usual $250 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{min}$ of $\mathrm{O}_{2}$, but at a tremendous price: a very low arterial $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ (hypoxia).

We can now answer the question that we posed above. The hyperperfused "good" lung cannot make up for the deficit incurred by the hypoperfused "bad" lung. In our example, the fundamental problem was that the ventilation of unperfused alveoli in the left lung effectively reduced the alveolar ventilation by half. In real life, the body would have compensated both locally and systemically. Locally, bronchiolar constriction and decreased surfactant production in the abnormal left lung (see Fig. 31-11B) would diminish alveolar dead-space ventilation and increase the effective alveolar ventilation to the normal right lung. Systemically, as we discuss in the next chapter, the respiratory acidosis and hypoxia would stimulate chemoreceptors to increase ventilation. If the body could double $\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{A}}$ to the right lungand if this right lung has a normal diffusing capacity-then it would be matching the doubled perfusion of the right lung with a doubled ventilation, and all resting blood-gas parameters would return to normal. (T) N31-16


A massive pulmonary embolism that obstructs the left pulmonary artery is superficially similar to the example that we have just discussed. However, other associated problems (e.g., right heart failure secondary to an increase in pulmonary vascular resistance, release of vasoactive agents) make the pulmonary embolism potentially fatal.

Shunt Affecting One Lung Imagine that an object occludes the left mainstem bronchus, eliminating all ventilation to the left lung. Total $\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{A}}$ remains at $4.2 \mathrm{~L} / \mathrm{min}$, but all ventilation goes to the right lung. Thus, the $\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{A}} / \dot{\mathrm{Q}}$ to the left lung is $0 / 2.5$ or 0 , whereas the $\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{A}} / \dot{\mathrm{Q}}$ to the right lung is $4.2 / 2.5$ or 1.68. The overall $\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{A}} / \dot{\mathrm{Q}}$ is normal, 0.84 . Again, the key question is whether a combination of a low $\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{A}} / \dot{\mathrm{Q}}$ in the abnormal lung and a high $\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{A}} / \dot{\mathrm{Q}}$ in the normal lung can yield normal blood-gas levels.

The normal right lung must now eliminate $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ at twice its normal rate. However, the right lung also has twice its normal $\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{A}}$. Because twice the normal amount of alveolar air carries away twice the normal amount of $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$, the right lung's new steady-state alveolar $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ is normal, $\sim 40 \mathrm{~mm} \mathrm{Hg}$ (Fig. 31-15A). The blood leaving the normal lung also has a $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ of $\sim 40 \mathrm{~mm} \mathrm{Hg}$. However, the unventilated lung has the $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ of mixed-venous blood, $\sim 51 \mathrm{~mm} \mathrm{Hg}$ in this example. After the two streams of blood min-known as venous admixture, because venous blood combines with blood from ventilated alveoli-arterial blood in the left ventricle has a $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ content that is midway between the $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ contents of the two streams of blood, corresponding to an arterial $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ of $\sim 46 \mathrm{~mm} \mathrm{Hg}$. Thus, even with the severe $\dot{V}_{\mathrm{A}} / \dot{\mathrm{Q}}$ abnormality produced by shunt, the lung is once again able to expel the usual $200 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{min}$ of $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$, but once again at a price: a high arterial $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ (respiratory acidosis).

The normal right lung must also deliver $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ to the blood at twice the normal rate. However, because the right lung's $\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{A}}$ is also twice its usual value, its new steady-state alveolar $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ is normal, $\sim 100 \mathrm{~mm} \mathrm{Hg}$. The blood leaving the right lung also has a $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ of $\sim 100 \mathrm{~mm} \mathrm{Hg}$, a hemoglobin $(\mathrm{Hb})$ saturation $\left(\mathrm{S}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}\right)$ of $\sim 97.5 \%$, and an $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ content of $\sim 20 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{dL}$. However, the unventilated lung has a $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ of mixed-venous blood, $\sim 29 \mathrm{~mm} \mathrm{Hg}$ in this example. Thus, the blood leaving the shunted lung has an $\mathrm{S}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ of $\sim 49 \%$ and an $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ content of $\sim 10 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{dL}$. After venous admixture, arterial $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ content is $(20+10) / 2$ or $15 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{dL}$, which corresponds to an arterial

# N31-16 Surgical Removal of One Lung 

## Contributed by Walter Boron

As we saw in Figure 30-10, $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ reaches diffusion equilibrium about one third of the way down the pulmonary capillary of a healthy young adult at rest. If one of the two lungs were removed surgically, the remaining lung would receive twice its normal complement of blood flow. However, provided that alveolar ventilation, $\mathrm{Fi}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}, \mathrm{~Pa}$, and $\mathrm{D}_{\mathrm{L}}$ were normal, diffusion equilibrium for $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ would still occur before the end of the pulmonary capillary. If the ventilation-perfusion relationship were also normal for this subject, then arterial $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ would be normal-at least at rest. However, during exercise, $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ transport could well become diffusion limited. Moreover, if $\mathrm{D}_{\mathrm{L}}$ were substantially below normal, or if $\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{A}} / \overline{\mathrm{Q}}$ relationships were problematic, then the subject could exhibit arterial hypoxemia even at rest. Therefore, in patients considered for total pneumonectomy, it is critical to evaluate pulmonary function preoperatively to confirm that the patient would be able to survive postoperatively with the remaining lung.

# BOX 31-1 Clinical Approaches for Diagnosing a $\dot{V}_{\mathrm{A}} / \dot{\mathrm{Q}}$ Mismatch 

Rither simple diagnostic methods are available for detecting the presence of a $\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{A}} / \dot{\mathrm{Q}}$ mismatch, assessing its severity, and identifying a shunt.

## Diagnosis of Exclusion

The physician can often diagnose a pathological nonuniformity of $\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{A}} / \dot{\mathrm{Q}}$ by excluding other possibilities. In general, low arterial $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ -under basal metabolic conditions-could be due to a reduced inspired $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ (e.g., high altitude), reduced alveolar ventilation, decreased diffusing capacity $\left(D_{L}\right)$, or $\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{A}} / \dot{\mathrm{Q}}$ mismatch. Let us assume that arterial $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ is appropriate for the altitude (see p. 681) and that results of simple spirometric tests indicate that respiratory mechanics are normal. Because $D_{L}$ is normally about 3 -fold greater than necessary for achieving diffusion equilibrium for $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ and $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ (see pp. 671-672), a problem with $\mathrm{D}_{L}$ is unlikely in the absence of a positive history. By default, the most likely cause is a $\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{A}} / \dot{\mathrm{Q}}$ defect.

## Alveolar-Arterial Gradient for $\mathrm{O}_{2}$

The difference between the mean alveolar $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ and the systemic arterial $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ is known as the alveolar-arterial (A-a) gradient for $\mathbf{P}_{\mathbf{O}_{2}}$. In our "normal" example in Figure 31-13B, both mean alveolar $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ and arterial $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ were 100 mm Hg . In real life, however, physiological $\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{A}} / \dot{\mathrm{Q}}$ mismatches cause arterial $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ to be 5 to 15 mm Hg below the mean alveolar value.

A defining characteristic of $\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{A}} / \dot{\mathrm{Q}}$ mismatches is that they widen the A-a $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ gradient. In our example of alveolar dead space in Figure 31-14B, the mean alveolar $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ was $(51+149) / 2=$ 100 mm Hg , whereas the systemic arterial $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ was 51 mm Hg , for an A-a gradient of 49 mm Hg . In our example of shunt in Figure 31-15B, the mean alveolar $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ was $100 \mathrm{~mm} \mathrm{Hg}$, whereas the arterial $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ was only 40 mm Hg -an A-a gradient of 60 mm Hg .

Because the A-a gradient for $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ is an index of the severity of the $\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{A}} / \dot{\mathrm{Q}}$ mismatch, physicians routinely estimate the A-a gradient in the intensive care unit. The approach is to (1) obtain the arterial blood-gas levels, which include $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{NO}_{2}}$ and $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{NO}_{2}}$, (2) assume that the mean $\mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ is the same as the measured $\mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$, (3) use the alveolar gas equation (see Equation 31-17) to compute the mean $\mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ from mean $\mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$; and (4) compute the difference $\mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ $-\mathrm{PA}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$. However, the assumption in point 2 is not entirely true because $\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{A}} / \dot{\mathrm{Q}}$ mismatches cause an A-a gradient for $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ just as they do for $\mathrm{O}_{2} . \quad \text { 1 N31-17 }$

## Effect of Breathing $100 \% \mathrm{O}_{2}$

Once a $\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{A}} / \dot{\mathrm{Q}}$ mismatch has been identified, it is important to distinguish between a shunt, which might be corrected surgically, and other causes. Imagine two patients with similar degrees of hypoxia. In the first patient, one lung is relatively hypoventilated (low $\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{A}} / \dot{\mathrm{Q}}$ ) and the other lung is relatively hyperventilated (high $\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{A}} / \dot{\mathrm{Q}}$ ). However, no alveoli are shunted. In the second patient, a complete shunting of blood makes one lung totally unventilated (a $\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{A}} / \dot{\mathrm{Q}}$ of zero, as in Figure 31-15). The normal lung has a high $\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{A}} / \dot{\mathrm{Q}}$. We can distinguish between the two cases by having both subjects inspire $100 \% \mathrm{O}_{2}$.

When the patient without predominant shunt breathes $100 \%$ $\mathrm{O}_{2}$, the $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ of the blood leaving both lungs will be far higher than normal (Fig. 31-16A). Blood leaving the hypoventilated lung has a $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ somewhat lower than that of the blood leaving the hyperventilated lung. However, because the $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ is on the flat part of the $\mathrm{Hb}-\mathrm{O}_{2}$ dissociation curve in both cases, the $\mathrm{S}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ values are virtually identical. The miniscule difference in $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ contents between the two streams of blood is due to a difference in dissolved $\mathrm{O}_{2}$. Thus, the mixed systemic arterial blood will have a slightly elevated $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ content, an $\mathrm{S}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ of $\sim 100 \%$, and a markedly elevated $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$.

The situation is very different in the patient with a severe shunt (see Fig. 31-16B). Because blood leaving the shunted lung does not equilibrate with the alveoli ventilated with $100 \% \mathrm{O}_{2}$, it has the low $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}, \mathrm{~S}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$, and $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ content characteristic of mixed-venous blood. Although blood leaving the normal lung will have an extremely high $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$, both the $\mathrm{S}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ and $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ content will be only slightly above normal-like the hyperventilated lung in Figure 31-16A. Thus, when one stream of blood with a slightly increased $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ content ("normal" lung) mixes with another stream with a markedly decreased $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ content ("shunted" lung), the mixed-systemic arterial blood has a lower-thannormal $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ content. This low $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ content translates to a low $\mathrm{S}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ and thus to a low systemic arterial $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$. Thus, unlike subjects with other kinds of $\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{A}} / \dot{\mathrm{Q}}$ mismatches that do not include substantial shunt (e.g., alveolar dead-space ventilation in Fig. 31-14), those with substantial shunt have low arterial $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ values, even while breathing $100 \% \mathrm{O}_{2}$. Because breathing $100 \% \mathrm{O}_{2}$ greatly increases mean alveolar $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ without substantially increasing arterial $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$, this maneuver greatly exaggerates the A-a difference for $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$.
$\mathrm{S}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}\left(\mathrm{Sa}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}\right)$ of 73 and a $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ of $\sim 40 \mathrm{~mm} \mathrm{Hg}$. Thus, even with the severe $\dot{V}_{A} / \dot{Q}$ abnormality caused by shunt, the lung is able to import the usual $250 \mathrm{~mL} / \mathrm{min}$ of $\mathrm{O}_{2}$, but at the price of an extremely low arterial $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ (hypoxia).

Why did the $\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{A}} / \dot{\mathrm{Q}}$ mismatch caused by shunt lead to only a mild respiratory acidosis but a severe hypoxia? The fundamental problem is that the $\mathrm{Hb}-\mathrm{O}_{2}$ dissociation curve (see Fig. 29-3) is nearly saturated at the normal arterial $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$. If $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ content were proportional to $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$, then mixing unshunted blood $\left(\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}=100 \mathrm{~mm} \mathrm{Hg}\right)$ with shunted blood $\left(\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}=29 \mathrm{~mm} \mathrm{Hg}\right)$ would have yielded arterial blood with a $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ of $(100+29) / 2=\sim 65 \mathrm{~mm} \mathrm{Hg}$, which is far higher than the arterial $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ of $\sim 40 \mathrm{~mm} \mathrm{Hg}$ in our example.

In our example, shunt would trigger compensation at two levels. Locally, hypoxic vasoconstriction would divert blood to well-ventilated alveoli. Systemically, an increase in $\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{A}}$ would lower $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ and raise $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ in the normal right lung. In fact, even a modest increase in $\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{A}}$ would be sufficient to lower arterial $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ to 40 mm Hg . However, even if $\dot{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{A}}$ approached infinity (raising the right lung's alveolar $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ to the inspired $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ of $\sim 149 \mathrm{~mm} \mathrm{Hg}$ ), arterial $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ would still be well under 100 mm Hg . In fact, even if the inspired air were $100 \% \mathrm{O}_{2}$, the arterial $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ would still fail to reach 100 mm Hg ; because of the shape of the $\mathrm{Hb}-\mathrm{O}_{2}$ dissociation curve, an increased alveolar $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ can increase the $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ content of arterial blood only marginally (Box 31-1).

# N31-17 Alveolar-Arterial Differences for $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ (Robin Test) 

## Contributed by Emile Boulpaep and Walter Boron

$\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{x}} / \mathrm{Q}$ mismatches lead not only to an alveolar-arterial (A-a) difference in $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$, but also to an A-a difference in the $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ partial pressure (Robin test). In our example of alveolar dead space (see Fig. 31-14A), the A-a difference for $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ was $40-80$ or -40 mm Hg , and in our example of shunt (see Fig. 31-15A), it was $40-46$ or -6 mm Hg . A-a differences for $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ are smaller than those for $\mathrm{O}_{2}$ both because alveolar $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ values are lower and because the $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ dissociation curve is more linear. Clinicians generally do not use the $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}} \mathrm{~A}$-a difference as an index of a $\mathrm{V}_{\mathrm{x}} / \mathrm{Q}$ mismatch. In fact, they generally assume that the mean alveolar $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ is identical to the arterial $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$, and they use this value in the alveolar gas equation (see Equation 31-17) for computing the mean alveolar $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ needed to determine the A-a difference for $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$.

![bo135465312c5](bo135465312c5.jpg)

Figure 31-16 Analyzing $\dot{V}_{A} / \dot{Q}$ mismatch by administering $100 \% \mathrm{O}_{2}$. Sat, saturation. The $P_{O_{2}}$ values are in mm Hg , and the $C_{O_{2}}$ values are in $\mathrm{mL} / \mathrm{dL}$.

Mixed $\dot{V}_{A} / \dot{Q}$ Mismatches Pathological $\dot{V}_{A} / \dot{Q}$ mismatches cause the range of $\dot{V}_{A} / \dot{Q}$ ratios to broaden beyond the physiological range. Some alveoli may be true alveolar dead space (i.e., perfusion absent, $\dot{V}_{A} / \dot{Q}=\infty$ ), but others may be more modestly underperfused. Some alveoli may be totally shunted (i.e., ventilation absent, $\dot{V}_{A} / \dot{Q}=0$ ), but others may be more modestly underventilated. Thus, the left ventricle receives a mixture of blood from alveoli with $\dot{V}_{A} / \dot{Q}$ ratios from $\infty$ to zero, corresponding to all of the points along the $\mathrm{O}_{2}-\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ diagram in Figure 31-10B. What is the composition of this mixed blood? The principles that we developed in our simplified examples of alveolar dead-space ventilation and shunt still hold. Even if total $\dot{V}_{A}$ and total $\dot{Q}$ remain normal, pathologically high $\dot{V}_{A} / \dot{Q}$ ratios in some alveoli cannot make up for pathologically low ratios in others, and vice versa. The result of uncompensated pathological $\dot{V}_{A} / \dot{Q}$ mismatching is always respiratory acidosis and hypoxia.

# CONTROL OF VENTILATION 

George B. Richerson and Walter F. Boron

## OVERVIEW OF THE RESPIRATORY CONTROL SYSTEM

Breathing is one of those things in life that you almost never think about until something goes wrong with it. However, those with pulmonary disease become intensely aware of breathing, as do people who overexert themselves, especially at high altitude. The feeling of dyspnea that they experience (see p. 701) is one of the most unpleasant sensations in life (Box 32-1). Swimmers and SCUBA divers, musicians who sing or play wind instruments, Lamaze practitioners, and anyone with a bed partner who snores also focus intensely on breathing. It is common for respiratory output to be the last brain function to be lost in comatose patients, in which case its cessation marks the onset of brain death. ( $\mathbf{\text { N32-1 }}$ Thus, despite our common tendency to ignore breathing, control of ventilation is one of the most important of all brain functions.

The ventilatory control mechanism must accomplish two tasks. First, it must establish the automatic rhythm for contraction of respiratory muscles. Second, it must adjust this rhythm to accommodate changing metabolic demands (as reflected by changes in blood $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}, \mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$, and pH ), varying mechanical conditions (e.g., changing posture), and a range of episodic nonventilatory behaviors (e.g., speaking, sniffing, eating).

## Automatic centers in the brainstem activate the respiratory muscles rhythmically and subconsciously

The rhythmic output of the central nervous system (CNS) to muscles of ventilation normally occurs automatically, without any conscious effort. This output depends upon a vast array of interconnected neurons-located primarily in the medulla oblongata, but also in the pons and other brainstem regions. These neurons are called respiratory-related neurons (RRNs) because they fire more action potentials during specific parts of the respiratory cycle. For example, some neurons have peak activity during inspiration, and others, during expiration. Some RRNs are interneurons (i.e., they make local connections), others are premotor neurons (i.e., they innervate motor neurons), and still others are motor neurons (i.e., they innervate muscles of respiration). A subset of these neurons, thought to be in the medulla oblongata, is able to independently generate a respiratory
rhythm-and is known as the central pattern generator (CPG; see p. 396). Together the neurons of the respiratory network distribute signals appropriately to various pools of cranial and spinal motor neurons (see pp. 241-242), which directly innervate the respiratory muscles (Fig. 32-1).

The most important respiratory motor neurons are those that send axons via the phrenic nerve to innervate the diaphragm (Table 32-1), one of the primary muscles of inspiration (see p. 607). When respiratory output increases (e.g., during exercise), activity also appears in motor neurons that innervate a wide variety of accessory muscles of inspiration and expiration (see p. 607).

Each of these muscles is active at different times within the respiratory cycle, and the brain can alter this timing depending on prevailing conditions. It is the job of the premotor neurons to orchestrate the appropriate patterns of activity among the different pools of motor neurons. The pattern of alternating inspiratory and expiratory activity that occurs under normal conditions during non-rapid eye movement (NREM) sleep, at rest, and during mild exercise is called eupnea. During eupnea, neural output to respiratory muscles is highly regular, with rhythmic bursts of activity during inspiration only to the diaphragm and certain intercostal muscles. Expiration occurs purely as a result of cessation of inspiration and passive elastic recoil (see p. 606) of the chest wall and lungs. During more intense exercise, the amplitude and frequency of phrenic nerve activity increase, and additional activity appears in nerves that supply accessory muscles of inspiration. With this increased effort, the accessory muscles of expiration also become active (see p. 608), thereby producing more rapid exhalation and permitting the next inspiration to begin sooner (i.e., increasing respiratory frequency).

## Peripheral and central chemoreceptors-which sense $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}, \mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$, and pH -drive the CPG

The CPG for breathing is the clock that times the automatic cycling of inspiration and expiration. In some cases, the CPG stops "ticking" in the absence of tonic drive inputs, which results in the absence of ventilation, or apnea. Although this tonic drive comes from many sources, the most important are the central and peripheral chemoreceptors, which monitor the arterial blood gas parameters- $\mathrm{O}_{2}, \mathrm{CO}_{2}$, and pH levels. Unlike the frequency of a clock, that of the

# N32-1 Brain Death 

## Contributed by George Richerson

As stated in the text, respiratory output is often the last brain function to be lost in comatose patients, in which case its cessation marks the onset of brain death. In the United States, the legal definition of "brain death" is the irreversible loss of clinical function of the entire brain-which does not include the spinal cord. Brain death is legally equivalent to other forms of death. The declaration of brain death requires a careful neurological examination testing all reflexes mediated by cranial nerves, evaluating the patient for evidence of behaviors that require brain function, and ruling out any reversible cause such as hypothermia or drug overdose.

respiratory CPG changes with the strength of the drive from the chemoreceptors, resulting in changes in both depth and frequency of ventilation.

The peripheral chemoreceptors, located in the carotid bodies in the neck and aortic bodies in the thorax, are primarily sensitive to decreases in arterial $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$, although high $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ and low pH also stimulate them and enhance their sensitivity to hypoxia. They convey their sensory information to the medulla via the glossopharyngeal nerve (cranial nerve [CN] IX) and vagus nerve (CN X). The central chemoreceptors, located on the brain side of the blood-brain barrier (see pp. 284-287), sense increases in arterial $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ and-much more slowly-decreases in arterial pH , but not arterial $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$. All three signals trigger an increase in alveolar ventilation that tends to return these arterial blood-gas parameters to normal. Thus, the chemoreceptors, in addition to supplying tonic drive to the CPG, form the critical sensory end of a negative-feedback system that uses respiratory output to stabilize arterial $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}, \mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$, and pH (see Fig. 32-1).

## BOX 32-1 Dyspnea

Dyspnea is the feeling of being short of breath, or the unpleasant conscious awareness of difficulty in breathing. In some cases, dyspnea is an adaptive response. For example, when arterial $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ falls or $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ rises from breath holding, asphyxia, or pulmonary disease, dyspnea leads to efforts to increase ventilation and thus to restore arterial blood gas levels to normal. However, dyspnea can occur even with a normal arterial $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ and $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$. For example, increased airway resistance can cause dyspnea, even if arterial blood gas levels do not change. Exercise also causes dyspnea, even though $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$ is usually normal and $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$ falls. Other causes of dyspnea seem maladaptive. For example, claustrophobia and panic attacks can induce the feeling of suffocation-that is, dyspnea-despite normal ventilatory parameters or even a drop in $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{CO}_{2}}$. The central neural mechanisms and pathways responsible for dyspnea are unknown, although many of the forebrain regions involved have been identified.

## Other receptors as well as higher brain centers also modulate ventilation

Left alone, the respiratory CPG would tick regularly for an indefinite period. However, many inputs to the CPG cause the clock to speed up or slow down. For example, respiratory output is often highly irregular during many behaviors that use the respiratory muscles (e.g., eating, talking, and yawning). During NREM sleep or quiet wakefulness, and with anesthesia, the CPG is unperturbed and does run regularly; it is under these conditions that neuroscientists usually study mechanisms of respiratory control.

A variety of receptors in the lungs and airways provide sensory feedback that the medulla integrates and uses to alter respiratory output. Stretch receptors monitor pulmonary mechanics (e.g., lung volume, muscle length) and may help optimize breathing parameters during changes in posture or activity. Activation of pulmonary stretch receptors also can terminate inspiratory efforts, thereby preventing overinflation. Other sensors that detect the presence of foreign bodies or chemicals in the airways are important for protecting the lungs by triggering a cough or a sneeze. Still others detect the movement of joints, which may be important for raising ventilation with exercise. The mechanoreceptors and chemoreceptors in the lungs and lower (i.e., distal) airways send their sensory information to the respiratory neurons of the medulla via CN X , and those in the upper airways send information via CN IX.

Nonrespiratory brainstem nuclei and higher centers in the CNS also interact with respiratory control centers, which allows the ventilatory system to accommodate such activities as speaking, playing a musical instrument, swallowing, and vomiting. These interconnections also allow respiratory control to be highly integrated with the autonomic nervous system, the sleep-wake cycle, emotions, and other aspects of brain function.

In the remainder of this chapter, we examine (1) respiratory neurons, (2) how these neurons generate the automatic rhythm of ventilation, (3) the control of ventilation by arterial blood-gas levels, and (4) how afferent feedback and higher CNS centers modulate ventilation.

## NEURONS THAT CONTROL VENTILATION

## The neurons that generate the respiratory rhythm are located in the medulla

A classical method for determining which parts of the CNS (1) N32-2 are responsible for controlling respiratory output is to transect the neuraxis at different levels and to observe changes in breathing. Using this approach in the second century, Galen was the first to determine the location of the respiratory controller. As a physician for gladiators in the Greek city of Pergamon, he observed that breathing stopped after a sword blow to the high cervical spine. A similar blow to the lower cervical spine paralyzed the arms and legs, but allowed respiration to continue. He reproduced these lesions in live animals and correctly concluded that the brain sends information via the midcervical spinal cord to the diaphragm.

Eighteen centuries later, Lumsden used a similar approach in cats. He found that transection of the CNS between the medulla and spinal cord (Fig. 32-2, spinomedullary transection) causes ventilation to cease as a result of loss of the descending input to phrenic and intercostal motor neurons in the spinal cord. However, even after a spinomedullary transection, respiratory activity continues in muscles innervated by motor neurons whose cell bodies reside in the brainstem. During the period that would have been an inspiration, the nostrils continue to flare, and the muscles of the tongue, pharynx, and larynx continue to maximize airway caliber-although this respiratory activity cannot sustain life. Thus, spinomedullary transection blocks ventilation by interrupting output to the diaphragm, not by eliminating the respiratory rhythm. We can conclude that the neural machinery driving ventilation lies above the spinal cord.

When Lumsden, in the 1940s, made a transection between the pons and the medulla (see Fig. 32-2, pontomedullary transection), he noticed that breathing continued, but with an abnormal "gasping" pattern. Others have since observed relatively normal breathing after a transection at this level, and concluded that the gasping seen by Lumsden was due to surgical damage to the respiratory CPG in the rostral medulla. Today, most respiratory neurophysiologists believe that the respiratory CPG is located in the medulla and that other sites, including the pons, only shape the respiratory output to produce the normal pattern. (1) N32-3

## The pons modulates-but is not essential for-respiratory output

Although the medulla alone can generate a basic respiratory rhythm, both higher CNS centers and sensory inputs finetune this rhythm. For example, the pons contains neurons that affect respiratory output.

Lumsden found that a midpons transection has only a modest effect: an increase in tidal volume and a slight decrease in respiratory rate. A bilateral vagotomy-interrupting the two vagus nerves, which carry sensory information from pulmonary stretch receptors-has a similar but smaller effect. However, combining a midpons transection with a bilateral vagotomy causes the animal to make a prolonged inspiratory effort-inspiratory apneusis-that is terminated by a brief expiration. A brainstem transection above the pons does not alter the basic respiratory pattern of eupnea. These observations led Lumsden to propose that (1) the caudal pons contains an apneustic center (i.e., it can cause apneusis), and (2) the rostral pons contains a pneumotaxic center that prevents apneusis (i.e., it promotes coordinated respirations). He believed that these two regions and the medulla are required for normal breathing. (1) N32-4 Although this viewpoint is common in current literature, it is held by only a minority of respiratory physiologists.

What is the modern view? We now appreciate that the apneustic center is not a specific nucleus but is distributed diffusely throughout the caudal pons. The pneumotaxic center is located in the nucleus parabrachialis medialis and adjacent Kölliker-Fuse nucleus in the rostral pons. However, the pneumotaxic center is not unique in preventing apneusis because simply increasing the temperature of the animal can reverse apneusis induced by lesions in the pneumotaxic

# N32-2 Experimental Preparations for Studying the Neural Control of Ventilation 

## Contributed by George Richerson

One of the most difficult challenges in neurobiology today is to understand how neurons function within neural networks to generate normal behaviors. Although it is one of the most primitive in the mammalian brain, the neural network controlling respiration is still highly complex. The experimental preparations and techniques used to study respiration are also shared by neuroscientists studying other neural networks and include the following:

Intact, awake, behaving animals and humans are used to permit direct correlation of results with behavior. Investigators primarily use this approach to measure body movements, lung volume changes, or electrical activity in muscles or peripheral nerves.

Anesthetized, paralyzed, mechanically ventilated animals are used to permit better control of experimental variables and more intensive surgical techniques. Paralysis reduces movement of the brain so that individual neurons can be studied using extracellular recordings of single neurons.

The whole brain or large portions of the brain can be isolated in vitro by perfusing the brain via the arterial system, or by removing the spinal cord and lower brainstem of a neonatal rat from the body and keeping them alive submerged in artificial CSF. Because the brainstem circuitry is intact, a respiratory rhythm may still be produced. Elimination of the lungs and heart results in reduction of movement, which makes intracellular recording easier.

Brain slices are prepared by cutting the medulla into thin slices and are kept alive in artificial CSF. These slices can be used
to study individual neurons in relative isolation; for example, with intracellular microelectrodes or patch-clamp recordings. Simple synaptic connections, limited to a restricted subset of those present in vivo, can also be studied.

Dissociated neurons can be studied immediately or after days or weeks in tissue culture. These approaches isolate neurons from synaptic input and permit highly stable recordings so that their biophysical properties can be studied with a high degree of precision and control.

These approaches to studying neural networks are at the same time very powerful and very limited. Results can vary depending on the species studied, on age, and on whether the subject is awake or anesthetized. Each experimental preparation has advantages for answering specific questions, as well as disadvantages. For example, it is not possible to define the properties of individual neurons while they are still part of a complex neural network. On the other hand, the respiratory rhythm is usually no longer present in "reduced" preparations, which makes it difficult to know if a given neuron is actually involved in respiratory control. All of these approaches suffer, more or less, from effects due to measurement of the responses-the biological equivalent of the Heisenberg uncertainty principle. There is ischemia in the center of en-bloc tissue, traumatic damage of neurons in brain slices, dedifferentiation of some neuronal properties in culture, and effects of anesthetics and prolonged surgery in vivo. For this reason, it is important that each result be verified and each theory be tested using many of these approaches, instead of relying on only one.

## N32-3 Evolution of the Respiratory Central Pattern Generator

## Contributed by George Richerson

The medulla actually has two identical respiratory CPGs-one on each side. All of the elements of the respiratory controllerdrive inputs, sensory feedback, RRNs, and motor neuronsare bilateral. Thus, after a midsagittal transection, each side of the medulla generates an independent respiratory rhythm. As noted in the textbook, the location of the respiratory CPG is not universally agreed upon.

The respiratory CPG probably evolved in fish, where bloodgas exchange across the gills requires pumping by branchial structures; this necessity explains why the CPG is located in the medulla. CPGs generate all repetitive motor activities (see pp. 396-397). In invertebrates, these include swimming in sea slugs and movements of the stomach in lobsters. The mechanisms of rhythm generation discovered in these systems have led to establishment of general principles that have been valuable in promoting understanding of CPGs in mammals.

## N32-4 Role of the Pons in the Control of Ventilation

## Contributed by George Richerson

A dorsal view of the brainstem and spinal cord, with the cerebellum removed, and records of integrated nerve activity during one respiratory cycle following the indicated transection. center. Moreover, lesions in many locations outside the pneumotaxic center can also induce apneusis. Today we still do not understand the role of the apneustic center, and the consensus is that the pneumotaxic center plays a general role in a variety of brainstem functions-including breathingbut is not required for eupnea (Box 32-2). Thus, the terms apneustic center and pneumotaxic center are generally of only historical significance.

## The dorsal and ventral respiratory groups contain many neurons that fire in phase with respiratory motor output

In the 1930s, Gesell (ㄱ) N32-5 and colleagues used extracellular microelectrode recordings to monitor single neurons, finding that many neurons within the medulla increase their firing rate during one of the phases of the respiratory cycle. Some of these neurons fire more frequently during inspira-tion-inspiratory neurons-whereas others fire more often during expiration-expiratory neurons.

Not all neurons that fire in phase with the respiratory cycle are involved in control of breathing. For example, because they are located within the chest cavity, aortic baroreceptors (see p. 534) produce an output that varies with
lung inflation, but they are primarily involved in the control of cardiovascular-not respiratory-function. Conversely, some neurons whose firing does not correlate with the respiratory cycle may be essential for respiratory control. For example, central chemoreceptors may fire tonically (i.e., they do not burst during inspiration) and yet are critical for maintaining respiratory output by providing tonic drive (see pp. 700-701). Thus, not all RRNs (e.g., those stimulated by the aortic baroreceptors) play a direct role in respiration, and respiratory control involves more than just RRNs (e.g., chemoreceptor neurons).

Although electrical recordings from RRNs cannot identify all neurons necessary for producing respiratory output, this mapping has proved very useful in defining neurons that are candidates for controlling ventilation. On each side of the medulla, two large concentrations of RRNs-the dorsal and ventral respiratory groups-are grossly organized into sausage-shaped columns, oriented along the long axis of the medulla (Fig. 32-4). Many neurons of these two regions tend to fire exclusively during either inspiration or expiration.

The pons also contains RRNs. (O) N32-4 Although, as discussed above, pontine neurons may not be needed to

# BOX 32-2 Normal and Abnormal Respiratory Patterns 

Respiratory output can change for a variety of reasons. Many patterns, both normal and abnormal, have recognizable characteristics summarized below. Figure 32-3 illustrates some of these.

Eupnea Normal breathing.
Sighs Larger than normal breaths (see Box 32-4) that occur automatically at regular intervals in normal subjects, possibly to counteract collapse of alveoli (atelectasis).

Yawn An exaggerated sigh (see Box 32-4).
Tachypnea An increase in respiratory rate.
Hyperventilation An increase in alveolar ventilation (see pp. 675-676)—caused by an increase in respiratory frequency or an increase in tidal volume-that decreases arterial $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$. Seen in pregnancy and liver cirrhosis (due to increased progesterone), in panic attacks, and as a compensation to metabolic acidosis (see p. 642).

Kussmaul Breathing Extremely deep, rapid breathing seen with metabolic acidosis, such as in diabetic ketoacidosis (see p. 1185).

Central Neurogenic Hyperventilation Rapid, deep breathing causing a decrease in arterial $\mathrm{P}_{\mathrm{O}_{2}}$. Although first described in a small number of patients with focal brain lesions, it is now believed that this pattern reflected coexisting lung disease or other systemic illness in the majority of these patients.

Cheyne-Stokes Respiration A benign respiratory pattern. Cycles of a gradual increase in tidal volume, followed by a gradual decrease in tidal volume, and then a period of apnea. Seen with bilateral cortical disease or congestive heart failure, or in healthy people during sleep at high altitude.

Gasping Maximal, brief inspiratory efforts separated by long periods of expiration. Seen in severe anoxia, as well as in terminal, agonal breathing exhibited by patients with brainstem lesions or cardiac arrest.

Apneusis (Inspiratory) Prolonged inspirations separated by brief expirations, typically seen in animals with lesions of the rostral pons plus bilateral vagotomy. Rarely seen in humans.

Apnea Cessation of respiration.
Vagal Breathing Slow, deep inspirations caused by interruption of vagus nerve input to the brainstem. Rarely seen in humans.

Ataxic Breathing Highly irregular inspirations, often separated by long periods of apnea. Seen mainly with medullary lesions.

Cluster Breathing Similar to ataxic breathing, with groups of breaths, often of differing amplitude, separated by long periods of apnea. Seen with medullary or pontine lesions.

Biot Breathing First described in patients with meningitis by Biot (in 1876), with breaths of nearly equal volume separated by periods of apnea. Biot breathing is also considered to be a variant of ataxic or cluster breathing.
